HEADLINE: U.S.to Auction Bills and Notes This Week

 BODY:
The tentative schedule of Treasury financings this week includes today's weekly auction of three- and six-month bills, followed tomorrow by an auction of two-year notes and Wednesday by an auction of five-year notes.
By late Friday the outstanding three- and six-month bills were bid at 7.03 percent and 6.98 percent, respectively. In when-issued trading the two-year note was offered at a price to yield 7.47 percent, while the medium term five-year note was offered at a price to yield 7.90 percent. 
 Among Government agency issues expected this week is a financing by the Federal Farm Credit Banks Funding Corporation, which plans to announce on Wednesday the interest rates for a five-part offering totaling $2.98 billion. Maturities are three months, six months, one year, two years and 10 years.
The following new fixed-income issues are scheduled this week:

HEADLINE: Mexicans Hopeful as They Await Bush Today

BYLINE: By MARK A. UHLIG, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MONTERREY, Mexico, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Seeking to capitalize on the warmest official American-Mexican relations in recent memory, Mexican authorities prepared for the arrival of President Bush here on Monday for a two-day state visit that is expected to focus largely on trade issues.
The visit, which will be the sixth meeting between President Bush and President Carlos Salinas de Gortari, is seen here as a confirmation of the good personal ties between the two leaders, who held their first talks even before Mr. Bush was sworn in as President.That theme has dominated descriptions of the meeting by aides to Mr. Salinas, who has staked much of his personal and political success on his ability to work as an equal with Mr. Bush in improving relations between the two countries. 
 But officials familiar with the agenda said that the meeting would also include substantive talks, dominated by efforts to eliminate potential obstacles to a free-trade agreement that would link North American economies in a giant new trading partnership.
They said the two leaders would also confront a variety of more divisiveissues, ranging from drug enforcement efforts to violence against illegal Mexican immigrants entering the United States near San Diego, where ethnic tensions have flared in recent months.
 
Preventing Problems
 "This is not so much a meeting to solve problems as to prevent them," said a Mexican official involved in preparations for the visit. "We believe we have better relations with this Administration than with any other in years, and we don't want to lose the chance to keep things going forward."
Preliminary discussions toward a Mexican-American free-trade agreement have been under way since the two Governments formally accepted the idea during a visit by Mr. Salinas to Washington in June.
Since then, however, the environment for direct negotiations has been sharply affected by a series of international developments, ranging from the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait to the stalemate of the Uruguay Round of international trade talks in Geneva.
Among the major outstanding issues in the talks is the degree to which any free-trade agreement would cover Mexico's large oil sector, which Mr. Salinas has repeatedly said must be excluded from negotiation. American interest in that issue has grown with the potential for conflict in the Persian Gulf, but senior aides to Mr. Salinas asserted again last week that he would not accept oil as part of the trade package.
Other concerns, officials said, include efforts in both countries to allay domestic opposition to the pact, which must overcome sharp differences in everything from wages to environmental restrictions. Diplomats and officials on both sides have also expressed worry over the potential political fallout from continued charges of fraud against Mr. Salinas's governing party, most recently during local elections Nov. 11 in the state of Mexico, just outside the capital.
 
Canada's Role Is Issue
 The format of the trade negotiations themselves has also been thrown into question by the proposed participation of Canada, whose inclusion in any formal agreement would create a trading zone some 25 percent larger than theEuropean Community.
A free-trade pact between the United States and Canada went into effect last year. But the announcement of negotiations toward a similar agreement between the United States and Mexico prompted immediate Canadian demands for some sort of participation or representation in the new talks.
The idea of permitting a new participant at the negotiating table has been opposed by many proponents of the agreement, who have argued that it would slow the progress of the talks, which both sides hope to complete before the end of Mr. Bush's term in 1992.
But senior Mexican officials now say that some degree of Canadian participation appears inevitable, if only in the form of an invited observer or other special status.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
An Ailing Buyout Tries a Buyback

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
WHEN Supermarkets General went private in 1987, in a deal completed just before the stock market crash, the company's expectations were for steady growth that would provide more than enough cash to meet its debt payments.
Three years later, results are not up to the rosy forecasts, the company is in violation of its loan agreements with banks, and it is refinancing. A crucial part of that refinancing is a proposal to buy back securities at a small fraction of their value when they were issued three years ago. 
 Part of the company's problems concern its regional concentration in New England, with Purity Supreme supermarkets, and in the New York area, with Pathmark supermarkets. Both regions have weak economies, and the company said sales in the latest quarter, ended Nov. 3, were down from a year ago. It has also been set back by problems at its Rickel home centers subsidiary, which led to a $31.4 million charge last year to cover the costs of closing eight stores and writing down excess inventories.
The company now forecasts that it will have sales of $6.2 billion in the 1991 fiscal year, which ends Feb. 2, 20 percent below the $7.8 billion it expected for that year back in 1987. Operating profits are expected to be $182 million, 35 percent below the forecast of $279 million. Now, not until 1996 does the company expect to have sales as high as it had once projected for 1991.
The figures for operating profits do not include interest payments, which have plunged the company into red ink. It lost $31 million in the first halfof this year, compared with a loss of $38 million in the same period a year earlier.
Company officials say the proposal to buy back some securities should be viewed not as a sign of distress but as "an opportunity to improve the balance sheet and a first step toward unleveraging the company," said Harvey Gutman, a company spokesman. He added, "It is not a step we have to take, or need to take."
But if that particular step is not required, some action is. The company has reported to the Securities and Exchange Commission that it will soon be in violation of its bank lending agreements, because its financial reports will show that its operating profit and net worth are below minimum acceptable levels. As part of negotiations with the banks, the company agreed to come up with additional capital.
The plan is for institutional investors, including the Equitable Life Assurance Society and partnerships organized by Merrill Lynch, both of which were involved in the buyout, to buy new preferred shares. A fund run by Adler & Shaykin, another buyout group, has also agreed to participate. In all, about $80 million is to be raised, the company said.
The money will mostly be used to repurchase the "cram down securities," in the jargon of Wall Street, that public shareholders received for some of their holdings in 1987. Shareholders got a package of bonds and preferred stock valued at $45.26 a common share. In the intervening years, holdershave received no cash dividends or interest, and now are being asked to sell the securities back for a total of $19.41 a share, a 57 percent haircut.
Those prices, as low as they are, are much better than the market valuations before the exchange offer was announced earlier this month. Then, the bonds were trading at 20 cents for each dollar of principal value, while the preferred shares were quoted at about $3.50 each for those with an eventual redemption value of $25. The prices being offered, for half the outstanding bonds and a quarter of the preferred shares, are 27 cents on the dollar for the bonds and $5 for each preferred share.
If Supermarkets General is eventually able to honor its obligations, the annual rate of return on its bonds, for anyone who buys at 27 and holds to maturity, will be more than 30 percent. The return on the preferred will be even higher. Both securities are supposed to start paying cash to holders in 1993.
One Wall Street analyst, who insisted that he not be quoted by name, said he was recommending that investors not tender, in large part because he thinks they would get more money in a 1993 restructuring.
The presence of Adler & Shaykin may lend credence to that sentiment. Alone among the buyers of the new preferred shares, it is not a current investor in the company, and thus presumably is making an investment based solely on current conditions. It is investing $10 million and is promised an 11.5 percent annual return, counting both dividends and a consulting agreement that the company has signed. In addition, the investment is convertible into common stock, offering a big upside if the company prospers.
For Adler & Shaykin to get that money, it will be necessary for other holders to be paid off, too. If its investment is wise, then those who sell their securities now would seem to be getting a poor price.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 29, 1990, Saturday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
The Market Place column in Business Day on Monday referred incorrectly to the status of the Supermarkets General Corporation's bank loan agreements. The company says it will be in violation of its bank loan agreements when it files financial reports for its current fiscal year, which ends on Feb. 2, but it is not in violation now. The article also misstated the 1987 value of a group of securities issued as part of a leveraged buyout. It was $34.66, not $45.26. The price being offered by the company for those securities now, $19.41, is 44 percent below that figure, not 57 percent.

HEADLINE: Bush Adviser Offers Outlook

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
The White House chief economic adviser, Michael J. Boskin, said today that the United States economy would probably rebound from its current slowdown by the middle of next year once oil prices decline.
But a drop in oil prices depends on resolving the Persian Gulf crisis, Mr. Boskin said on the "Newsmakers" program on Cable News Network's
"When oil prices decline from where they are now and for other reasons, we will come out of it sometime in the middle of next year," he said.
Oil prices have doubled since Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2 and remain high and volatile as the standoff in Saudi Arabia continues.
While many economists say the economy is in more dire straights than a mere slowdown, Mr. Boskin repeated his view that it was too early to tell whether the United States had entered its first recession in eight years, defined as six months of shrinking output.
In a separate interview on NBC-TV today, Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher was mostly upbeat. "We have a lot of down sides, but we still have some up sides," he said, pointing to strength in the agricultural, medical supplies, chemicals and pharmaceutical sectors.

HEADLINE: Bush Visit to Highlight Caracas Chief's Role

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
If there is a world leader who works the international telephone operators harder than President Bush, it is President Carlos Andres Perez of Venezuela.
The two have little else in common. One is a blue-blood Republican, the other a charismatic Social Democrat. But their instinct to reach for the Rolodex when crisis strikes has helped make them uncommonly close allies. 
 Administration officials say Mr. Bush's visit to Caracas on Dec. 7 as the final leg of his South American tour will highlight Mr. Perez's central rolein Latin American politics. The two leaders plan to discuss how to reinvigorate the stalled Salvador peace negotiations, how to insure that the coming elections in Haiti are peaceful and fair, and how to cushion the international oil market from the gyrations of the Persian Gulf crisis.
Mr. Bush's talks with Mr. Perez, a State Department official predicted, "will have a nice personal glow."
Their relationship blossomed two days after the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait, when Mr. Bush called Mr. Perez on the telephone to express his concern that an explosion in oil prices could destabilize the Western economy.In a formal note delivered to Mr. Perez by Vice President Quayle four days later, Mr. Bush asked Venezuela, a member of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, to increase its oil exports. Without hesitating, Mr. Perez said he would expand production as quickly as possible, and with the help of King Fahd of Saudi Arabia he lobbied for weeks to persuade OPEC members to sustain production levels.
"He's a real operator," said a White House official, who noted that Mr. Perez's promise to help ease the oil crunch was "the first bit of really good news the Administration had received internationally after the invasion.""His word was his bond," he said. "He delivered."
Venezuela, which has the largest oil reserves in the Western Hemisphere, has increased its production since the invasion from 2 million to more than 2.4 million barrels a day. The nation plans to boost production by 100,000 barrels more by January.
"I have told President Bush, and I think he understands, that the potential reserves of Venezuela can take care of all the energy needs of the hemisphere for the forseeable future," Mr. Perez said in a telephone interview.
What makes the emerging close relationship between the two Presidents all the more remarkable is Mr. Perez's populist record from his first term as President in the 1970's. Then he used his country's oil wealth to expand the role of the state in the economy and tohelp finance and arm the Sandinista revolution in Nicaragua. But today a mellower Mr. Perez is a promoters of a more conservative school of thought that seeks to encourage private enterprise and foreign investment.
 
'Clear Latin American Lead'
 "Venezuela and Carlos Andres Perez have taken the clear Latin American lead," said Michael M. Skol, the American Ambassador in Venezuela. "Venezuela has influence and has used it responsibly."
Recognizing Venezuela's role as the most influential nation in the Caribbean basin, Mr. Bush urged Mr. Perez at their last meeting in September to keep a close eye on the Haitian presidential campaign. The Venezuelan needed little encouragement, and in loose coordination with Washington, hehas pursued an aggressive policy to insure that elections next month are fair.
Mr. Perez said Venezuela was sending 15 military advisers to help insure the security of the balloting in Haiti, and is sending 300,000 barrels of oil at preferential rates during the campaign to ease the country's import burden. Venezuela is also sponsoring seminars for all seven presidential candidates to teach them how to campaign and get out their supporters on election day.
Mr. Perez said he had spoken with Mr. Bush on the telephone twice in recent weeks about developments in Haiti, Nicaragua and El Salvador. In those conversations, he said that he had also proposed that Venezuela, the United States, Mexico and Spain join in developing an economic aid package for Central America, and that Mr. Bush agreed that it would be a good idea.

HEADLINE: Taiwan Aims for High-Tech Niches

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HSINCHU, Taiwan

 BODY:
In the glass and steel buildings beyond the green lawns of this science park, Taiwan is trying to stage a new industrial revolution.
Instead of toys, companies here are making personal computers. Instead of electronic gadgets, they are making satellite receivers. Taiwan is trying to abandon the low-technology products that made it rich to develop high-technology niches that will enable it to compete with American and Japanese companies. 
 The Government's science park is leading the movement. It has brought back 600 Taiwan-born American-trained scientists and engineers, reversing a brain drain over the last two decades that saw Taiwan's best and brightest beat a path to America, work their way through graduate schools and then find jobs as engineers at electronics companies in Silicon Valley in California and along Route 128 in Massachusetts.
Now, the Government is trying to use these experts to replace the country's copycat strategy with one based on innovation, and while critics say progress is slow, 107 companies already are operating at the science park, and there is little space left.
Taiwan has been able to advance its expertise in computers. It now has a sizable share -- 20 percent by some counts -- of the world personal computer market. While it still buys the basic chip from the Intel Corporation, Taiwanese companies can build almost everything else. Its largest personal computer maker, Acer Inc., is expanding rapidly and had sales of $480 million last year.
Even so, establishing a high-technology base is a process that requires patience in a country where little is spent on basic research and development, which can lead to the biggest breakthroughs. And scientists have not always been eager to give up their high salaries and easy way of life in America to return home.
For Taiwan, the change in strategy is a matter of survival. For two decades, the country has relied on its cheap labor to reproduce low-technology electronic products like toys and computer keyboards accurately and swiftly. But with labor costs rising as its economy matures, Taiwan, like many rapidly developing Asian countries, is seeing its traditional markets eroded by countries with cheaper labor like China. It is being forced to move into more sophisticated frontiers to survive.
"A couple of years ago, Taiwan found a niche in low-end products like monitors, but now, with rising wages, these niches have vanished," said Benjamin H. W. Chen, an analyst at Baring Research (Taiwan) Ltd. "The new niche will be at the medium-tech level, like telecommunications products."
Taiwan has hundreds of small concerns that are moving into the world of technology. It makes about 30 percent of all traditional computer monitors used in the world, more than the share made in the United States.
 
 Silicon Valley a Model
 But Taiwan still has no company that even approaches the global might of the International Business Machines Corporation or Fujitsu Ltd., one reason that the Government created the science park. Using Silicon Valley as a model, the park hopes to provide the infrastructure, the manpower and an atmosphere for closet entrepreneurs who will help create one or two flagship companies that will become household names around the world.
"I don't think we can ever be on top, but we'd be proud to be second or third," said H. Steve Hsieh, director general of the Science Park Administration, who returned to Taiwan in 1982 after 13 years in America.
The park has stiff guidelines including a requirement that its tenants spend 5 percent of their revenues on research and development. "Nothinghere is at the forefront of research and development, but at least the direction is right," said Sydney Merritt, an adviser to the United Microelectronics Corporation, a company in the park that is one of Taiwan's largest makers of integrated circuits.
 
 Tax Breaks and Loans
 Companies in the park also receive a variety of tax incentives and low-interest loans. The park promotes exchanges with two nearby universities. The Industrial Technology Research Institute, which is affiliated with the park, is mapping out strategies for industrial development and is trying to start ventures in new fields like aerospace.
Yet critics say that Taiwan's attempt to create its own version of Silicon Valley at the science park is progressing slowly. Park planners hope that by 1996 its companies will generate a total of $8 billion in annual revenues and devote $600 million a year to research and development.
This is an ambitious target, considering that at the end of last year, the companies in the park brought in $2 billion in revenues and invested only $70 million in research.
The park could also have trouble attracting investment because of slower economic growth expected by Taiwan for the next couple of years and the collapse of the stock market here. The majority of investment in the park comes from Taiwan companies and individuals.
 
 Difficult Supply Situation
 Although conditions at the park have improved considerably, they still are not on a par with Silicon Valley. When Matthew F. C. Miau, chairman of the Mitac Computers Group, returned to Taiwan from Silicon Valley in 1976, he could not find the plastic he needed to make disks for his personal computers and a machine shop to grind the materials to make the disk drives. After working for Intel for five years helping to develop the first generation of microprocessors, he was used to having suppliers at his doorstep.
These days, logistical constraints in the science park still make it difficult for companies to expand. "In Silicon Valley, it is so easy to lease things," said Mr. Miau, who has had to fight to bring a gas supplier into the park because the company does not qualify as a high-technology one. Such support systems are essential, he said.
Most Taiwanese-Americans return because of the career prospects, the opportunity to be their own bosses or to play out their entrepreneurial dreams. They often return with American passports, and because of Taiwan's rapid economic development, many say the quality of the Taiwanese way of life makes returning home appealing.
But Taiwan companies cannot always offer scientists and engineers the same salaries they get in America. Taiwan's engineers still earn only a third to one-half the comparable wage in the United States. Management styles in Taiwan are also different.
"We think about endurance, loyalty and how someone develops," said C. K. Liu, president of the Tecom Company, which makes sophisticated telephone systems in the science park. "People work more like a family because people here are less sophisticated."
 
 Gaining a Vital Base
 But slowly, the park is gaining a vital base. Among the companies here is Microelectronics Technology Inc. In 1983, Chi C. Hsieh, the company's president, and seven of his colleagues from the communications field left Silicon Valley and returned to Taiwan to develop microwave technology. The company, with 600 employees, had sales of $48 million last year.
It manufactures suitcase-sized satellite communications systems that allow people to place telephone calls from the middle of nowhere. Microelectronics says that one of its systems, which is tailored for ships, was used during the summit meeting off Malta in December between President Bush and President Mikhail S. Gorbachev.
The company has been making 15 of its portable satellite communications systems each month. But it is now benefiting from the Middle East crisis: it has doubled production and sold more than a dozen to business executives and officials who want to use them in the region.
"Before we developed this microwave industry, they all were doing lower-tech TV's and other consumer products," said Mr. Hsieh, who spent 17 years in America, at graduate school and at the Harris Corporation.
 
 $260 Million Plant Completed
 United Microelectronics says that at the moment it is equipped with the world's most precise manufacturing capability in its field. It has just completed a $260 million plant, its second, which it says will eventually be able to build integrated circuits with a degree of precision as thin as 0.65 microns, or a tiny fraction of the width of a human hair.
While Taiwanese companies have traditionally been too small to acquire American corporations, in February, Mitac purchased Wyse Technology Inc, an American maker of computer terminals, taking it private in a $270 million transaction.
While it is not as large as Acer, Mitac, with $346 million in sales last year, is one of Taiwan's largest makers of personal computers. The purchase might have been the first of a wave of acquisitions by Taiwan companies if not for the plunge in the Taiwan stock market, a major source of money for large companies here.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Chi C. Hsieh, left, the president of Microelectronics Technology Inc., demonstrating the use of a suitcase-sized portable satellite communications system at the company's headquarters in Taiwan. (pg. D1);
"We think about endurance, loyalty and how someone develops," said C. K. Liu, president of Tecom, a maker of phone systems, in discussing management styles in Taiwan, which are different from American ones. (pg. D7) (Sheryl WuDunn/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
A Struggling Czech Retailer Delivers the Goods

BYLINE: By BURTON BOLLAG, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PRAGUE

 BODY:
A full year after the democratic revolution in Czechoslovakia, this country's leading department store is still struggling almost daily to stock Western products eagerly sought by shoppers.
Even so, the store, Kotva, is a leader in the drive by Eastern European enterprises to modernize their way of doing business. Kotva's executives have used energy and imagination to overcome many of the obstacles, like the scarcity of easily convertible, or hard, currency that still hobbles many Eastern European enterprises. 
The store is still state-owned, but its shelves hold a growing selection of Western goods: Swiss chocolates, American cigarettes and West German skateboards. In the past, the few Eastern European stores carrying such products accepted only hard foreign currency like dollars or German marks. Now, Kotva sells all its wares in the local currency, the crown.
 
 Struggling to Improve
But the Kotva scenes of people crowding around special stands selling Lego toys and Nina Ricci perfumes or having their photos developed overnight at the Fuji film counter are still rare in Czechoslovakia.
Many stores, shops and consumer services are still struggling to improve, hindered not only by a lack of currency needed to import Western wares but also by vestiges of strict Government controls. As a result, many remain old-fashioned, unattractive and unresponsive distribution points for shoddy products.
Kotva's accomplishments are even greater than they might first appear. The store's managers say they must fight against the sloth and lack of initiative that many workers acquired during four decades of Communist rule.
Goods imported from non-socialist countries account for 30 percent of Kotva's sales, up from 7 percent last year, store officials say.
 
 Bartering for West's Goods
 To stock the fashions and other Western products it sells, Kotva has developed extensive bartering arrangements.
Kotva opened in 1975; it has 65,000 square feet of floor space, and its officials say it is the largest department store in Central and Eastern Europe.
"Kotva is led by young people," said Martin Svehla, spokesman for the State Bank of Czechoslovakia, "and we think it is one of the best in services and goods offered."
Bankers say Kotva began hiring younger, more progressive managers after the Communist Government liberalized its regulations on commercial trade in the mid-1980's, giving retail stores more leeway in seeking their own goods abroad.
Czechoslovakia's new Government has not yet decided exactly how it will carry out its changes in the economy. But Kotva's general director, Franisek Skok, told the Czech newspaper Mlady Svet in a recent interview that next year he would like to start buying out ownership of the store from the Government and distributing stock among the 1,700 employees.
 
 Elected by the Staff
 Mr. Skok was elected to his post by Kotva's staff in April 1989.
A leader in the struggle to modernize the department store is Lubomir Staffen, who is 38 years old and a Kotva vice president. He says that each morning he asks his staff of buyers three questions: "What was our profit? What was the price of the competition? What are you doing to beat the competition?"
Mr. Staffen had a career in Czechoslovakia's foreign trade ministry, which included periodic trips to Geneva with his country's delegation to the multilateral trade talks there, before taking his current job a little more than a year ago.
Mr. Staffen is largely responsible for the sharp increase in the store's selection of Western goods. "Now people go abroad," he said. "They're demanding new things, but I'm limited by what I can get through counter trade," a form of barter.
When he took his job, he created a staff of 10 just to barter for better goods. The group got the Lego toys from Denmark by exchanging Czechoslovak paper. Italian vermouth, selling in the store's basement supermarket, came in return for Czechoslovak cheese.
 
 Government Influence
 To Mr. Staffen's regret, the Government still has a big hand in business. Barter deals have to go through state-owned or licensed "trading companies." Moreover, the store's tax burden remains unchanged at 65 percent of gross profits.
To better train and motivate his staff of 56, Mr. Staffen holds classes on modern business practices. Using mimeographed sheets, he teaches ways to improve in areas long ignored, like marketing, pricing and timely deliveries.
He has also been trying to increase productivity by paying an additional half salary to buyers able to take over the duties of a colleague dismissed as inefficient. Four jobs have been eliminated in this way.
 
 100,000 Customers a Day
 Kotva, like most stores and restaurants in the region, still suffers from overcrowding and frequently dirty floors. An average of 100,000 customers visit Kotva each day and they line up for a few minutes to a quarter-hour to get to a sales counter or into the basement supermarket.
Czechoslovakia's new Government is unambiguously steering the country to a market economy. But it will take months, perhaps years, before local industry will produce enough exportable goods to allow for full convertibility of the crown. For this reason Mr. Staffen expects to have to rely on cumbersome bartering for some time.
Counter trade was used before the revolution here a year ago. But state barter did not provide goods that consumers really wanted.
"The old philosophy was fulfilling the Government plan," he said. "Mine is making money."

HEADLINE: Metro Matters;
Westway's Ghost Pays Off, Proving Skeptics Wrong

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS

 BODY:
If the road to hell is paved with good intentions, just imagine where Manhattan's West Side Highway would lead.
Two decades have gone by since a cadre of urban planners conceived an underground thoroughfare, buried beneath parks, apartments and commercial development built on landfill in the Hudson River. That ambitious proposal, called Westway, collapsed of its own bureaucratic weight five years ago this fall after an investment of $250 million -- nearly a tenth of its projected budget. 
 Now another ground-breaking has been very tentatively scheduled -- this one, for a more modest replacement highway. The road is unlikely to be finished before the millenium. Officials are reviewing plans for a companion riverfront park, although the defeat of New York's proposed environmental bond issue this month cost the park project $100 million, or about one-fifth of its estimated budget.
Yet New Yorkers have collected a generous fringe benefit from Westway's defeat.
Congress had empowered localities to substitute money committed to unbuilt interstate highway system segments, like Westway, for an equivalent amount of Federal funds to be spent on mass transit and on a cheaper substi tute road. The trade- in legislation armed Westway foes, who had been dismissed as obstructionists, with a constructive alternative. Eventu ally, their loose coali tion of environmen talists, mass-transit advocates and fiscal conservatives suc ceeded in the courts and in Congress.
 Westway diehards had predicted that the trade-in wouldn't work. They warned that Washington would never relinquish the full $1.7 billion or so, or that inflation would severely devalue it. But unwilling to gamble on another environmental review after Congress refused to extend a trade-in deadline, the governor and mayor declared Westway dead. Since then the skeptics have generally been proved wrong.
The swap earmarked $690 million for a replacement highway; another $690 million for the city's own highway budget, freeing an equivalent amount of city money for mass transit, and $345 million directly to the Metropolitan Transportation Authority.
"That complicated agreement has basically worked," said Mortimer L. Downey, the M.T.A.'s executive director.
"A surprising amount has ended up in mass transit," said Marcy Benstock, the Clean Air Campaign director who was instrumental in defeating Westway.
Mr. Downey said the transit agency had received $694 million of the $1.035 billion from both sources and had invested most of it in subway car overhauls and track and signal repairs.
By the end of next year, the agency expects, it will have collected the rest of the $690 million that was to be funneled through the city under the Westway shuffle and most of the direct mass-transit money -- which has since grown to $380 million. (The city recently rejected Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato's proposal to spend trade-in money to hire more police officers; he had warned that fearful New Yorkers would use the new highway to leave town.)
Not surprisingly, plans for the substitute highway, from the Battery to 59th Street, are only inching along. Several alternatives for the Route 9A Reconstruction Project, as it has modestly been called, range between six and eight lanes in width and between $300 million and $1 billion in cost. A draft environmental impact report is to be completed next spring. Barring other impediments, construction could start late in 1992. It could take about six to eight years to replace the existing temporary roadway -- itself a substitute for the elevated West Side Highway, which was razed.
"We've made a lot of progress," said Richard A. Maitino, project director for the State Transportation Department, "in that most of the people we talk to, instead of asking why should the road be built, are asking how the road should be built."
Since 1985 roughly $40 million has been obligated for West Side Highway purposes under the trade-in program, which expires next September. Still unresolved are how much the city will be reimbursed for the old Westway right of way and where the viaduct north of 59th Street will be rebuilt. Also, how much, if any, of the proposed waterfront park will be endorsed by Mayor David N. Dinkins and Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and whether they will find another source for the $100 million that would have come from the defeated environmental bond issue.
Five years ago the governor predicted that Westway would soon be forgotten like "a walnut in the batter of eternity." It has been. But on the surface, the ripples remain.

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Rose 1.9% Last Month

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS

 BODY:
Machine tool orders increased nearly 1.9 percent in October from the September level, the industry's trade association reported in its monthly survey. Some analysts said the increase indicated that the nation's economic slowdown had not yet prompted manufacturers in the United States to sharply reduce spending for new equipment.
The level of new orders for American-made machine tools reached $269.3 million, compared with $264.3 in September, the Association for Manufacturing Technology said in its report, which is scheduled to be released today. The October figure, which was one of the three highest monthly totals in the last 12 months, represented a 31.4 percent increase from October 1989. For the first 10 months of this year, machine tool orders were 2.1 percent higher than in the comparable period a year earlier. 
 Machine tools are power-driven devices that cut or shape metal parts used in products ranging from appliances and vehicles to aircraft and construction materials. The strength of tool orders generally reflects the strength of industrial production, and economists consider patterns of machine tool sales to be important indicators of capital spending by manufacturers.
 
 Manufacturing Strength
 "The rise in new orders indicates that -- despite gloomy prospects elsewhere in the economy -- the level of business activity in the manufacturing technology industries remains firm," said Albert W. Moore, president of the association, which is based in McLean, Va.
As has been the case for much of this year, foreign demand for machine tools continued to be strong, with overseas orders increasing 22.7 percent in October from the previous month. The level of orders from foreign customers in October was up 52.8 percent from October 1989.
Domestic orders were down one-sixth of 1 percent in October from the September level, but were 28.8 percent higher than in October 1989.
 
 'Economic Turning Point'
 "The United States is at an economic turning point," Mr. Moore said. "In spite of caution on the consumer side of the economy, United States manufacturers need to look to the future. They have to take the long view, and that means they must continue to upgrade their manufacturing operations."
Shipments of machine tools fell 14.9 percent in October, to $254.3 million, from the September level. The association said the September figure reflected the surge in shipments that typically occurs at the end of each quarter.
Analysts said orders for machine tools from the aerospace industry had remained firm and the decline in orders from auto makers had not been as pronounced as many had feared. Auto makers typically account for more than a third of overall orders, and sluggishness in the auto industry has caused machine tool orders to slow in several months this year.
 
 Good Short-Term Prospects
 "At the moment, things are looking relatively good," said James J. Paper, a machine tool analyst with Kirkpatrick, Pettis, Smith Inc. in Omaha. "Orders are coming in well from a diverse base of industries, and the inquiry rate is strong from potential customers, indicating that the prospects, at least in the short term, are good."
Despite the stronger results for October, Mr. Paper and other analysts predicted that orders would fall if a prolonged economic downturn occurred. "The risk is, of course, that the whole economy will go through a downturn and stay down six months to a year or longer," Mr. Paper said. "That will affect manufacturers' operating rates, and their future plans for capital spending are sure to be affected."
Orders for metal-cutting tools, which include boring mills and lathes, dropped 12.1 percent in October from September's level. That segment of the industry typically accounts for three-fourths of new orders. Orders for metal-forming tools increased 55.3 percent in October from September's level, helped by a surge in foreign orders.
The backlog in orders stood at $1.58 billion in October, up from $1.57 billion in September.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Inflation Fear Is Said To Subside

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Many credit market analysts say inflationary pressures have begun to ease significantly, even though oil prices remain substantially above the levels before Iraq invaded Kuwait and could go higher still if war erupts in the Persian Gulf.
The moderating trend has come as a welcome surprise to many credit market participants, who feared that rapidly rising oil prices would in turn push up the prices of other goods and services. 
 Price pressures are still evident in the service sector, but producers of goods are finding it difficult, if not impossible, to raise prices. Since early August non-oil commodity prices have declined significantly.
 
 Modest Rises Lately
 The drop has pushed the Commodity Research Bureau's index to levels not seen since the summer of 1989. After rising sharply in August and September, consumer prices excluding food and energy have posted modest increases of three-tenths of a percentage point in each of the last two months.
Fears about an oil-generated inflationary spiral have not been borne out because the economy was much weaker than most people realized at the time of the Iraqi invasion, analysts said. That weakness has intensified since, further crimping demand.
"We have resolved a lot of doubts about the economy," said Richard Berner, an economist at Salomon Brothers, "particularly about how fast inflation will come down. We are reversing perceptions that inflation will be staying at extraordinarily high levels."
 
 Expectations Exceeded
 Rudolf Thunberg, an economist at Ried Thunberg & Company, an investment advisory firm in Westport, Conn., said the declines in non-oil commodity prices "reflect a slowdown in demand for basic commodities, one which has exceeded my expectations."
"The rate of inflation is likely to diminish over the next several quarters as the economy sinks into recession," he added.
As a result of economic weakness, Mr. Thunberg is forecasting that for the 12 months ending in December 1991 consumer prices will rise at a 4 percent rate, down from a 6.4 percent rate for this year.
The grudging awareness among credit market participants that inflationary pressures seem to be easing is something of a testament to the Federal Reserve, analysts said.
 
 Reputation Reinforced
 The fact that the central bank chose not to push interest rates lower in the early stages of the Persian Gulf crisis may prove to be the deciding factor in hastening an economic downturn, but the delay also reinforced the Fed's reputation as an inflation fighter.
'Financial markets were a little bit slow to recognize that the Fed was very reluctant to allow commodity prices to run up, too," said Donald Ratajczak, director of the economic forecasting project at Georgia State University. "I could make a case that the underlying inflationary trend is a little bit lower now than it was in July."
The sense in the credit markets that the inflation threat is receding is seen in bond yields, which have declined over the course of November despite the fact that the Fed has moved to lower the overnight Federal funds rate twice. Very often a reduction in short-term interest rates by the Fed will be poorly received in the bond market, which is highly sensitive to any steps that could stimulate demand and thereby ignite price pressures.
 
 Move Called Impressive
 The movement in bond yields in the wake of the Fed's moves "is very impressive to me," said Paul L. Kasriel, a vice president and money market economist at the Northern Trust Company in Chicago. "The fact that the bond market has rallied on those cuts in the funds rate suggests that the economy is weakening at a faster rate than the Fed is easing. The Fed is winning its battle against inflation, and its move to ease monetary policy is not over."
Market participants will continue to look for, and expect, additional evidence of economic weakness this week. But a significant surprise could come on Wednesday, when the Commerce Department releases revised estimates of third-quarter economic growth.
In late October most analysts dismissed the department's preliminary growth estimate of 1.8 percent as too high, adding that it would almost surely be revised downward later on. A month later, that view has changed. Most economists now expect that the figure will be revised upward, perhaps substantially.
 
 Huge Revision Predicted
 The revision in the gross national product figures will be huge, said Robert Giordano, a partner and director of economic research at Goldman, Sachs & Company. Because of increases in inventories, consumer spending and foreign trade, he expects the third-quarter output figure to be raised to 2.5 percent.
Economic growth of that magnitude would seem to cast doubt on just how weak the economy truly was at the time of the gulf crisis. But Mr. Giordano and others assert that stronger third-quarter numbers have no bearing on current conditions.
"Much of the gain in G.N.P. came before confidence plummeted in the wake of the crisis," he said. "Right now the economy is declining, and the Fed can't afford to conduct policy on the basis of history. A healthier looking third quarter may lead to some political crowing in Washington, but it will have no impact on monetary policy."

HEADLINE: JAPAN WEIGHS AID TO NORTH KOREANS

BYLINE: By STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Nearly a half century after the end of its brutal subjugation of the Korean Peninsula, Japan is starting to think about possible reparations for North Korea but finding the decision-making difficult because of Koreans' extreme wariness of Japanese motives.
Japanese officials said last week that talks on a compensation package could begin after the first of next year, most probably in Pyongyang, with North Korea demanding at least as much as the $500 million that South Korea received when it normalized relations with Japan in 1965. 
 In theory, that package of loans and grants would be worth several billion dollars today, but Japanese officials acknowledge that coming up with such a large sum would stir anger in South Korea and perhaps the United States.
"We're at an impasse on the issue of compensation," said a Japanese official. "The problem is that this is not simply an issue between Japan and North Korea alone. There are other interested parties."
 
Prime Ministers Meet
 The discussion over an aid package with North Korea offers an intriguing glimpse into diplomacy in post-cold-war northeast Asia, where the rules of alliances are being rewritten in a far less visible fashion than in Europe.
At the center is an incipient reconciliation between North and South Korea, reflected in the recent meetings between the Prime Ministers of the two countries, the highest level of contacts since the end of the Korean War in 1953.
The first two rounds of talks in September and October were marked by speeches reiterating familiar charges and countercharges and a general failure to narrow longstanding differences. The next round of talks is to begin Dec. 11.
But North and South Korea have also engaged in a ballet of diplomatic maneuvering with other countries, particularly China, Japan, the Soviet Union and the United States.
What has emerged is a new regional game of "two plus four" relating to the Korean Peninsula, much as German reunification was settled by negotiations involving East and West Germany plus the United States, the Soviet Union, France and Britain.
 
Wooing Moscow and Beijing
 The four powers outside the Korean Peninsula have all made clear that they hope North and South Korea are successful in trying to solve their problems, easing tensions in a heavily armed region that remains an anomalous relic of East-West confrontation.
But most diplomats agree that the current discussions between North and South Korea are generally seen as the fruit of two years of assiduous courtship of North Korea's longtime patrons, the Soviet Union and China, by President Roh Tae Woo of South Korea.
For Mr. Roh, the payoff has been an agreement to establish diplomatic relations with Moscow, including a possible trip to the Soviet capital himself, and the opening of Chinese and South Korean trade and liaison offices in Seoul and Beijing.
Moscow especially is putting pressure on Pyongyang, demanding that it repair its international image as a sponsor of terrorism and give up its belligerent posture toward Seoul.
To counter these presures, North Korea succeeded this fall in opening up a dialogue with Japan, which could help it out of its economic stagnation.
Somewhat to the dismay of both American and South Korean officials, Japan opened this new avenue by abruptly sending a delegation to North Korea in September to begin negotiations to establish diplomatic ties.
 
Seoul Is Incensed
 The Japanese step incensed Mr. Roh's Government, which accused Japan of advancing its economic interests, heedless about the concern that doing so would undercut Seoul's careful drive to isolate Pyongyang and force it into a more reasonable position. "We have doubts today about the strength, consistency and even the dignity of Japanese foreign policy," a special adviser to Mr. Roh said last month.
Since then, however, Japan has taken a much more cautious approach to North Korea. Senior Japanese officials said in interviews that Tokyo would not do anything to undercut the North-South dialogue.
In recent weeks, Japan has been reaching out to South Korea, deciding, for instance, to eliminate fingerprinting requirement for many Koreans living in Japan for several generations, always a sore subject among Korean people.
South Korean officials said they were somewhat reassured about Japanese intentions. "We feel much more at ease than before," one said.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
U.S. Aides Press Iraqi Nuclear Threat

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
The Bush Administration today stepped up its effort to raise American concern about Iraq's ability to develop nuclear weapons, saying Baghdad might be able to produce a crude nuclear device within a year.
Defense Secretary Dick Cheney and Brent Scowcroft, the national security adviser, said in separate appearances that the Iraqi nuclear weapons program was advancing despite the embargo, and they provided intelligence estimates intended to buttress President Bush's statements about Iraqi's emerging nuclear ability made during his Thanksgiving visit to Saudi Arabia. 
 Mr. Bush's statements, and the remarks today by the two officials, were clearly intended to add weight to the Administration's argument that the situation in the Persian Gulf must be brought to a decisive turn this winter.
The Iraqi nuclear program is not a new concern for Washington, and the assertion today that Iraq might build a rudimentary nuclear device within a year appeared to be an attempt to counter Democratic lawmakers who have suggested that the United States should give sanctions a year or more to work so war might be avoided, as well as to influence the United Nations Security Council meeting on the gulf crisis to be held on Thursday.
 
Hearing Due This Week
 Senator Sam Nunn, Democrat of Georgia, is scheduled to lead Armed Services Committee hearings this week that are expected to become a forum for challenging the Administration's decision to speed up its timetable for resolving the crisis caused by the invasion of Kuwait by Iraq on Aug. 2.
In recent weeks the Administration cited a variety of reasons for forcing a resolution of the gulf crisis, including destruction in Kuwait, the holding of hostages and threats to the economy of the United States and the world.
In his appearance today on the ABC News program "This Week," Mr. Scowcroft said the Administration was also motivated by concern that the coalition confronting Iraq might dissolve if the crisis was prolonged. It was the first time a senior official has publicly acknowledged that factor.
"This international coalition is a disparate group of people," Mr. Scowcroft said. "There are all sorts of tensions and pressures one way or another." He added that there was a "common feeling" within the coaltion that the crisis "needs to be brought to an end."
 
Poll Results Cited
 None of those rationales for possible military action against Iraq appears to to have decisively swayed public opinion. In contrast, in a recent New York Times/CBS Poll, 54 percent of the respondents said they believed that preventing Iraq from acquiring nuclear weapons was a good enough reason to take military action against Iraq.
An Administration official said the survey results had been noted by the White House, which is also looking for an argument that will sway the public. Another Government official, who insisted that he not be identified, said the Administration's statements today that Iraq could produce a bomb in a relatively short period was unduly alarmist, considering the difficulties of the technical processes.
While Mr. Bush gave new emphasis to Iraq's nuclear potential during his Thanksgiving visit to American troops, he did not provide intelligence estimates about how long it might take Iraq to develop a bomb.
 
More Detailed Estimates
 Today, Mr. Scowcroft and Mr. Cheney sought to buttress Mr. Bush's statements by providing more detailed estimates about Iraq's nuclear potential. Administration officials said the remarks by Mr. Cheney and Mr. Scowcroft reflected a classified intelligence estimate that Iraq could develop a nuclear bomb in six months to a year, but probably longer, even if it conducts a crash program.
Such an effort would involve using about 27 pounds of highly enriched uranium that has been supplied by France and was recently inspected by officials from the International Atomic Energy Agency, experts say. In addition, Iraq might also use a small quantity of less highly enriched uranium that it has acquired from the Soviet Union.
"If he were to take that material, he could produce a crude device with it," Mr. Cheney said on the CBS News program "Face the Nation," adding that a "worst-case assumption held that such a weapon might be developed in a year or less.
"It wouldn't be anything you could deliver from an airplane," Mr. Cheney said. "It wouldn't be anything that would be weaponized in the sense that we think of a nuclear weapon. But it would be capable, for example, of doing some damage, of producing some kind of a yield -- a very crude system."
The Defense Secretary asserted that the Iraqis could build a more sophisticated weapon in 5 to 10 years by developing the ability to refine crude uranium available in Iraq.
Mr. Scowcroft said Government estimates about when Iraq might acquire a nuclear weapon ranged from "months to as much as 10 years." That estimate includes the possibility that Iraq might conduct a crash program to develop a crude bomb as well as its ongoing, longer-term effort to build a more sophisticated weapon that could be delivered by a missile or plane.
The national security adviser then drew a direct link between the prospective Iraqi nuclear threat and the need to resolve the gulf crisis in a short period of months.
Waiting a year or two to see if sanctions will work, as some Democratic lawmakers suggest, Mr. Scowcroft said, "raises the possibility that we could face an Iraq armed with nuclear weapons, which would dramatically change the character of any conflict."
 
Danger Called Exaggerated
 Gary Milhollin, an expert on the spread of nuclear weapons, said the Administration was exaggerating the danger that Iraq could develop a nuclear weapon in a short period to buttress its case for considering a war this winter.
"What are you going to do with one untested, unwieldly bomb when facing three nuclear weapons powers -- the United States, France and England -- that have already deployed troops and a fourth nuclear power Israel that is not far away?" Mr. Milhollin said.
"I would think it would be a reckless, desperate act to threaten four nuclear powers with one untested bomb," Mr. Milhollin added.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk

HEADLINE: Middle-Class Blacks Try to Grip A Ladder While Lending a Hand

SERIES: Scraping By: Second of Three Articles.

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON

 BODY:
Joyce Ford, the daughter of laborers, fled the hand-to-mouth world of Washington's black housing projects and now has amenities of the middle class: a good job in the Government, an office across the street from the White House, a merit raise and an American Express card. But she still has the bone-deep worries of the poor.
Sitting at her desk the other day, she got yet another call from a frantic relative with bad news and high expectations. "Your brother's sick," the caller said. "He needs to see somebody. What are you going to do?" 
 What else could she do? She spent the better part of the day trying to figure out where her out-of-work brother should go for treatment, and how to pay for it.
Mrs. Ford, 44 years old, is among tens of thousands of black people who are the first in their families to break out of poverty and take a tenuous place in the American middle class. Many are scaling the economic ladder with one hand on the middle rung and the other still outstretched to the relatives who need them.
 
The Peril of Falling Back
 To the outside world, Mrs. Ford and those like her are examples of how far this nation has come since the civil rights movement opened up legions of professional jobs previously closed to black people. To their families, they are the American dream come true, showpieces to brag about.
But even in the best of times, these newly arrived blacks have a loose grasp on prosperity. And as a faltering economy tumbles toward recession, they may slip out of the middle class altogether, many economists and sociologists fear, creating an even deeper well of need among impoverished blacks to whom they now lend support.
Two main things tend to distinguish black middle-class people from middle-class whites. One is the likelihood that many more of their relatives will come to them for help. The other is that they tend to lack the resources of people who started life in the middle class.
There is no clear-cut definition of "middle class," a concept that can embrace not just how much money people make but the way they make it: usually not with their hands. Most economists and sociologists agree that the middle class includes mainly professionals, skilled service and clerical workers and entrepreneurs, each one earning $25,000 to about $50,000 a year, or more if the person lives in an expensive city or has a lot of children.
Under those criteria, about 40 percent of blacks fall into the category of middle class or above, while 30 percent are blue-collar working class and 30 percent are the unskilled poor.
But unlike the white middle and upper classes, which include nearly 70 percent of all white households and have been a fixture for three or four generations, the vast majority of the black middle class is starting from scratch.
Census Bureau figures for 1989 show that in median annual income, the households of black college graduates trailed those of white college graduates by more than a third: $37,958 for blacks as against $48,862 for whites.
And while all couples earn more if both husband and wife work, a second income for black families is usually what allows them to be middle class.
When both spouses work, the median income of black couples is $37,787, according to 1989 census figures. But when one spouse did not bring home a paycheck, the black couple's median income dropped to $18,727 -- out of the middle class. White couples, by contrast, tend to keep their middle-class status even when one spouse does not work. Last year, white couples earned a median figure of $45,803 with two paychecks and $29,689 with one.
Perhaps equally important, economists say, black people often enter the middle class with a slight fraction of the financial assets of middle-class whites whose parents and grandparents were middle class. So blacks lack the reserve of money and property needed to buy a house, to finance a college education, to weather a personal catastrophe or a national recession.
"Middle-class blacks are much more vulnerable than whites because they tend to be new to their positions and don't have the seniority or accumulated wealth," said William Julius Wilson, a professor of sociology at the University of Chicago.
 
Reminders of Desperation
 Unlike most middle-class whites, they live with daily, personal reminders of the poverty and desperation they are trying to put behind them, as the loved ones back home who have not fared so well turn to them for help with the rent, use of a car, a place to stay.
"We're all tied together because upward mobility is recent for us," said Dr. Andrew Billingsly, chairman of the Department of Family and Community Development at the University of Maryland and a leading researcher on the black middle class.
The topic can be painful. Many middle-class black people were reluctant to talk to a reporter about their experiences out of a sense of duty and protectiveness toward their loved ones. Some did not want to take credit or appear to be holding themselves above the people they once played stickball with.
And in a society that in many ways judges people not only by where they are but by where they came from, many feared that their backgrounds and family responsibilities would subject them to unwanted scrutiny at work, or even be held against them.
In dozens of interviews, middle-class black people said they were proud to be the ones able to help their families, but were frustrated by their inability to get ahead.
"You can never accomplish anything," said Mrs. Ford, the Government worker in Washington. "As soon as you get anything, there's something there to take it away. I often wonder what will happen if something happens to me. Who'll be there for me?"
 
A Big Family
 
Some Dependents Aren't Declared
Donald Sheppard, an assistant professor of social work at Texas Southern University in Houston, has a wife and five children, but they are just the dependents the Internal Revenue Service gives him credit for.
When a brother needs a loan, when a sister needs a job, when a nephew needs a car, when a cousin needs a credit card or a friend needs a place to stay, they come to him.
"I know that when I get paid on the first of the month, I'm going to get a call," Mr. Sheppard, 40, said. "It's expected that I have it and that I'm going to give it. I feel I'm working for the whole extended family. It's not a question of if they're going to need it. I know they're going to need it."
His relatives have looked to him for help since he was in grade school. "When the television would get those horizontal lines, they would always call on me," Mr. Sheppard recalled. "I didn't know what to do any more than they did. But I'd turn some screw and it would work. Even as a child, people expected me to do everything."
By the time he was 11, he was catching the bus to go pay the family's utility bills. His father, a wool presser at a Houston dry cleaners with not a day of high school, could not dare ask for the time off; his mother, a maid, had suffered a stroke and was too sick. So she kept him out of school to pay the bills. He remembers being stopped by a truant officer and telling him, "Sir, my mama's sick. I'm going to go to school as soon as I pay the bills."
Mr. Sheppard became the only one of eight children to get a college degree and carry a briefcase to work. A sister once told him, "You're almost like a god in our family."
He and his wife, Jina, both social workers, earned their master's degrees at the University of Chicago in the mid-1980's by packing their five children in their 1971 station wagon and all their belongings in a U-Haul truck, driving north and feeding seven mouths on what was left of their scholarship money.
 
Sometimes He Gets Burned
 They returned to Houston to an extended family of relatives and friends who needed them. Mr. Sheppard has gone into the refrigerator to get chicken broilers and T-bone steaks for them; he has had to choose between helping a relative out with the rent or fixing the boiler at his house. The family always wins.
Lately, he has been trying to provide services rather than money. He recently got one of his nephews a maintenance job at the university. "I have to do it for me," Mr. Sheppard said. "If I get them a job, they won't have to be asking me for something."
Last August, a childhood friend of Mr. Sheppard's who was down on his luck and recently evicted asked if he could move his family in with Mr. Sheppard and his family. The answer was yes, and Mr. Sheppard's children slept on the floor to make room. They stayed for five weeks.
"Every day, I had to buy milk and bread and cereal," Mr. Sheppard said. "I had 12 people in the house, and I was the provider."
Some of his investments have begun to pay off. For instance, he saw promise in the young son of one of his sisters, who was raising the boy as a single parent. Mr. Sheppard signed the boy up for Little League Baseball, coached him and counseled him as a surrogate father. The boy is now a senior in high school, at the top of his class and a drum major in the band.
Mr. Sheppard is still paying for the help he has given. His credit cards are up to their limit and the car needs fixing. Even on his and his wife's combined income of $60,000 a year, they have only a $5,000 cushion for their retirement and have not been able to save for their children's college education.
"In my family's eyesight, we've made it," Mr. Sheppard said. "But we're living paycheck to paycheck. To lavish and wallow in the status of the middle class, we can't do it. And I can't give like I used to. But it doesn't seem to register. People still come up to me and say, 'I know it's rough on you, but it's rougher on me, so can you give me this?' "
His goal is to build up reserves so he can provide for relatives without depleting his own money. But he is a long way from that. "When I think about it, it really kind of frightens me," he said. "Sometimes I find myself frustrated and depressed. I'm called on by my children, my wife, family members, students, colleagues, friends, needing, asking, wanting. The cycle never ends."
 
Pathfinders
 
An Easier Road For Immigrants?
The role of family pathfinder is not new. Immigrants who flocked here from Europe early in this century typically relied on the first child who got an education and a foothold to serve as ambassador and teacher for the rest of the family.
But there are key differences for today's black people. For one, while many unskilled immigrants were able to get relatively good-paying factory jobs or make connections with local political machines to vault their children into the middle class, discrimination prohibited the parents and grandparents of most of today's black middle class from nearly all but menial positions in industry or politics. That meant black families had far less than white families to pass on.
Soon after civil rights measures created a more even playing field, the economy changed: once-dependable, relatively lucrative factory jobs started to vanish just as they began opening up to blacks.
Sociologists, however, point to the effects of lingering racial discrimination as the main difference. Dr. Joe R. Feagin, a professor of sociology at the University of Florida who is completing a book on racial discrimination and the black middle class, contrasts his experiences as an Irish-American with those of black Americans.
"My great-great-grandparents saw signs that said, 'No Irish or dogs allowed,' " Dr. Feagin said. "But once we lost the brogue and the look of the Irish, we could pass. People couldn't single us out for discrimination anymore. That has not been the case for blacks, no matter what their status."
Indeed, the civil rights gains of the last 20 years have done little to even out the huge gaps in white and black wealth. Census figures from 1984, the most recent available for this category, showed that for black households, the median net worth, or total value of what they own (cars, houses, stocks, equity in a business, for example) minus what they owe, such as an outstanding mortgage or education loan, was $3,397. For white households, it was $39,135.
More disturbing, perhaps, nearly a third of blacks have zero or negative net worth, meaning they owe more than they own, Only 8 percent of whites are in that category.
 
An Anchor
 
Giving Help, Giving Space
When Marjorie Ellis, 38, is not punching blueprint data into a computer as a drafting clerk at the Southern Bell Telephone Company, she runs a boardinghouse -- for her own family. Her two-bedroom ranch house just south of Atlanta bustles with the banter and footsteps of her sister, her sister's children, her elderly aunt and her mother.
They are all in transition. Her sister has a job as a civilian worker in the military and is trying to get on her feet, one of the nephews is in college, the aunt is recovering from hip surgery and her mother is struggling on a pension. They all turned to Miss Ellis when they needed help and now share space in the house, chipping in whenever they can.
Their fortune is tied to her fortune. "If there's a middle class, I'm in the poor section," said Miss Ellis, who earns about $30,000 a year.
In the months since her relatives moved in, she has depleted her savings and fallen behind on her house payments.
She doesn't have the $2,500 she needs to get the engine fixed for her 1984 Thunderbird. The car sits cold and idle in her driveway as she leaves to catch the 5:30 bus each dark morning to get to work by 7.
"I'm behind and it's hard to catch up," Miss Ellis said. "I pay one bill one week, the others when I can. I might be late a month. That doesn't mess your credit up too bad. Sometimes I sit and pray and cry. Then I figure it'll be all right somehow. I'm not going to let any of my relatives go wanting if I can help them."
 
A Wreck Is Disastrous
 A few years ago, when her nephew needed a car to get to school, for instance, she bought a car, for which he agreed to make the payments if she covered the down payment and insurance. He never got the money. Worse, the car was in an accident and she ended up owing $1,300 on it. She is still paying for the car, which neither of them can use.
Miss Ellis's father was a cement finisher who died when she was in grade school; her mother was a maid. She and her sister were the first to graduate from high school and work behind a desk.
She remembers her minister's words when she got her house: "You're coming up in the world. You're coming up slow, but you're coming up in the world."
Between the economy and her family responsibilities, she doesn't feel that way anymore. She sleeps on the speckled beige sofa in the den, beneath pictures of the assorted relatives who rely on her. There is also a dog-eared picture of her grandmother, posing not with any of the 25 children she bore but with one of the white children she tended to for a living.
Under the strain, Miss Ellis has developed a bleeding ulcer, diabetes and chronic depression for which she takes regular medication. Her nightmares are not of ghosts and gremlins but of becoming homeless.
Her mother has begun to catch on. She has seen the somber-looking letters from the Department of Housing and Urban Development, which backed her mortgage. But Miss Ellis doesn't want to let on to her situation. Her relatives look up to her. She wouldn't want to let them down.
 "What are you studying about?" her mother will ask in rural Georgiaspeak when she sees her daughter thinking of the weight on her shoulders.
"Nothing," Miss Ellis will answer, and smile not very convincingly. "I'm fine."
 
Tough Choices
 
Saying 'No' To Relatives
Glen Giles of Atlanta doesn't go out for lunch or take his family on trips to Florida anymore. And for the first time in his life, he must sometimes say no when relatives need money. It is not easy for a man who, after he got his degree, sent $25 every week to the next sibling in line to go to college.
But he is an insurance account agent with an office to run and a commission to earn at a time when people are not buying houses or cars, two of the biggest things people insure.
Since the first of the year, his business has been off 50 percent as the housing market in Atlanta has slowed. Last summer he had to lay off his entire office staff: a secretary and two other agents.
Now the shiny new desks sit empty in his gray-carpeted office, and he answers the phone, takes orders and messages himself.
So when relatives need help with the electric bill or the rent, he can't help them as he used to. One brother-in-law spent several nights in jail on a traffic violation because no one in the family could bail him out.
"I don't have it to give," said Mr. Giles, 43. "It takes everything we make to pay the bills. I have to dip into my own money to pay the office bills. Some bills go lacking as long as they can. We're borrowed out."
He is the third of 10 children born to textile workers in South Carolina, and even though his siblings are all college graduates and all working, they have limited resources just as he does. So he has no place to turn. "It's just me and whatever financial institution that will help me," Mr. Giles said.
 
Starting Late
 
Decades Behind In Prosperity
Jared Samples presumably went from working poor to middle class overnight when he won a seat on the Atlanta City Council last year. One of the first things he did when he got his first paycheck was to move out of the Perry Homes housing project where he grew up. The next thing he wants to do is get his mother out.
He is saving for the house he wants to buy for her, but he is finding that the $25,000 he makes as a full-time public servant doesn't go very far, especially when he still tries to give money to his nephew in college or a sister to pay her car insurance when he can.
He drives a 1975 Dodge. "My family knows I'm still poor," he said. "I might be able to loan somebody a little money, but I need my money back. We're four generations out of slavery and one generation out of the projects."
He knows he is starting from a deficit. "I'm working for my nephews and nieces," he said. "My life is pretty much set. I can work every day but I can't get but so far."
 
Waiting for the Century?
 Many economists say it will take much of the next century to close the gap between the security levels of the black and white middle class. "Blacks can try to do better than in the past and save up, but the best they can do is to try to provide an education for their children so that by the second or third generation, perhaps there will be parity," said William D. Bradford, a professor of finance at the University of Maryland who has written extensively on race and wealth. "I don't see anything in the next 20 years. We're looking at 40 to 60 years."
And many public policy analysts say that such an outcome will require continuing Government intervention. "Blacks are dependent on the Government opening doors and keeping those doors open," said Dr. Walter Allen, a professor of sociology at the University of California at Los Angeles.
Much is at stake for the entire country, sociologists say. "If the black middle class is not stable and secure, they will fall into the lower classes and not be able to pay their bills, their fair share of taxes, and will use a larger share of public services," Dr. Billingsly of the University of Maryland said.
Black people say they are doing what they can to help themselves. Betty Brown Chappell, a social worker and a doctoral candidate at the University of Chicago, remembers her tough freshman year at the University of Michigan, when she arrived on campus with not even enough money for books and food. She remembers how her father, a factory worker, sent what he had -- a $10 bill. She gave $5 of it to one sister in college, who then sent $2.50 to another sister in college.
Mrs. Chappell wanted things to be different for the next generation. So when her niece, Alea, was born 18 years ago, she bought $150 in savings bonds for her. Last September, Alea enrolled at the University of Michigan. The savings bonds had doubled in value. She used the money to buy her books.
