SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 4; National Desk

LENGTH: 1549 words

HEADLINE: Traffic in Fake Documents Is Blamed For New Rise in Illegal Immigration

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HOUSTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Four years after a new immigration law brought about a steady decline in the number of illegal aliens caught crossing the southern border, the number has turned sharply upward as illegal aliens and those who help smuggle them have widely circumvented the law with counterfeit documents.
The 1986 law, the Immigration Reform and Control Act, sought to weaken the economic magnet that draws illegal aliens to the United States by prohibiting the employment of people who cannot document their immigration or citizenship status. The law was backed up with stiff fines on employers who hire illegal aliens. 
 But a growing number of scholars and policy makers have recently concluded that the law's deterrent effect was only temporary and that its impact has been rapidly wearing off this year.
 
'Just One More Hurdle'
 "Employer sanctions may have seemed a real barrier at first, but now they are just one more hurdle to overcome," said Wayne A. Cornelius, director of the Center for United States-Mexican Studies at the University of California at San Diego. "We seemed to have turned a corner with that perception sometime last year, and the widespread use of fraudulent documents appears to be a major factor."
Before the law was enacted, detentions of people crossing the southern border reached more than 1.6 million a year, provoking the Reagan Administration to declare the border "out of control." After three years of steadily declining, the number detained rose to about 850,000 in the fiscal year that ended in September 1989.
Recently released statistics for the 12 months ending this September showed that more than 1 million illegal aliens were caught crossing the border, an increase of more than 22 percent over the previous year. Although the numbers are subject to varying interpretations, they are widely regarded as an indicator of the surreptitious human traffic across the border.
The Commissioner of the Immigration and Naturalization Service, Gene McNary, said in a telephone interview that part of the increase in apprehensions was attributable to an increased law enforcement effort along the border. "That's one explanation," he said. "I don't believe it explains everything because we know that we've got more fraudulent documents and we know that we have more pressure because of the economic situation in Mexico."
 
Renewed Discussions on Policy
 In recent months discussions have begun in Congress, the immigration service and elsewhere in Washington over whether the 1986 law is fundamentally flawed or whether it simply needs to be enforced differently.
The rising figures on detentions are only one reason that sanctions against employers are coming under renewed scrutiny. Last March the General Accounting Office, the investigative arm of Congress, issued a report finding that the sanctions had caused "a widespread pattern of discrimination" against people who look or sound like foreigners. Many employers admitted to being so confused about the law that they simply did not hire anyone who might be an alien.
For the people who police the border there is little doubt about where to find the major weakness of the 1986 law. Many of the aliens they apprehend carry it in their hip pockets or purses.
"We've been seeing the apprehension numbers going up, and at the same time we've been seeing more and more people carrying fraudulent documents," said Dale A. Musegades, chief agent for the Border Patrol's El Paso sector. "And the quality of the documents has been improving, too.
"It's no different than when we put manpower in one spot and they simply move someplace else. They've adapted to employer sanctions and found a way around it."
 
Adaptation to New Requirements
 For the most part the sanctions against employers, usually in the form of fines, have been imposed when the immigration service believes that an employer knowingly and repeatedly hired illegal aliens.
Since the law allows workers to use 17 types of documents to prove their legitimate status and an employer need not verify the authenticity of the papers, "an illegal can present a good set of fake ID and an employer will be fairly blameless in hiring him," Mr. Musegades said.
As a measure of how sophisticated the market for fraudulent papers has become, officials pointed to a counterfeiting operation uncovered in Harlingen, Tex., earlier this month. Joseph De La Cruz, an agent in the Border Patrol station in Laredo, said a laser scanning device had been used to produce nearly perfect copies of several kinds of documents, including Social Security cards that sold for $20 apiece in bulk.
 
Counterfeits in Quantity
 The size of the market was demonstrated by the seizure of single cache of more than 25,000 fraudulent documents in Houston last January, Mr. De La Cruz said.
The increased use of fraudulent documents to circumvent sanctions against employers has renewed a longstanding debate over whether the Government needs some means to check the identities of all the people living in this country.
"I don't think that we can give up on employer sanctions yet, but we better start realizing that eventually we are going to have to face up to the document issue," said Doris Meissner, an immigration specialist at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, a research institute in Washington.
In the initial debate over the 1986 law, Congress overwhelmingly rejected national identity documents as a step toward totalitarian controls. This year more modest proposals encountered equally fierce resistance.
 
Resistance From Many Quarters
 For example, there have been numerous proposals for some kind of telephone verification system that would allow employers to check the validity of an identity document just as credit cards are often checked during a transaction. But so far, the Bush Administration has opposed such widespread public use of Government data.
Meanwhile, some important lawmakers have begun to question whether the sweeping program to revise immigration policy that began with the 1986 law is suffering a major setback just as it reaches its conclusion.
By sharply reducing illegal immigration the 1986 law was intended to lay the groundwork for the measure adopted by Congress just last month, which increased legal immigration by about 40 percent with most of the new visas going to people valued for their job skills.
During the debate on the most recent bill, Senator Alan K. Simpson, Republican of Wyoming, who helped draft both immigration laws, pointed to the increased number of apprehensions as showing problems with the program. "We must face the fact that we have not yet closed that back door," he said. "In fact, we are still leaving the back door open while considering prying open the front door much wider."
At a Border Patrol detention office along the Rio Grande in El Paso, sanctions against employers seemed to be accepted as a fact of life by the men and women who were brought in one day this month after being caught crossing the river illegally.
Smiling and rubbing the dark stubble on his cheeks, a young man who identified himself as Carlos Garcia, said, "I know about the law, but it doesn't bother me." Waiting in a holding cell with about a dozen others to be sent back across the bridge to Mexico, he said that he could not remember how many border crossings he had made but that there had been dozens. This was the third time he had been caught, he said.
Whenever he went north, he said, he always managed to find work of some kind. "If you have friends you can mow lawns or something like that, and if you want something better you buy an ID to show the boss," he said.
 
Numbers Open to Speculation
 Just how many people are coming through the back door is a subject of much analysis and speculation.
There is no way to count the number of people who cross the border surreptitiously, and the number of people caught can fluctuate because of many factors aside from the actual number illegally crossing, including changes in staffing levels by the Border Patrol.
Studies published this year by two research organizations, the Rand Corporation and the Urban Institute, concluded from analyses of the apprehension figures that sanctions against employers could be credited with reducing the number of illegal entries by roughly 20 percent to 25 percent in first three years after enactment.
"Since then, and especially this year, we've seen the trend change and now the flow seems to be increasing," said Frank D. Bean, a sociologist who helped conduct the Urban Institute Study. "This calls into question whether sanctions have had any permanent impact."
But Representative Bruce A. Morrison, Democrat of Connecticut, who sponsored the House version of this year's immigration law, was reluctant to draw any conclusions from the recent rise in apprehensions. "It is hard to tell whether changes in those numbers reflect changes in reality or changes in policy at the I.N.S.," he said. "They are too easy to manipulate."
Nonetheless, Mr. Morrison questioned the effectiveness of the 1986 law. "It's not easy to know the potential of employer sanctions because they've never been effectively enforced by the I.N.S.," he said.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
U.S. Is Reported To Win Support For Use Of Force

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Bush Administration officials and foreign diplomats said today that the United States has mustered enough support in the United Nations Security Council to gain approval of a resolution that would authorize the use of military force against Iraq if it did not withdraw its forces from Kuwait by the end of January.
The final wording of the resolution has not been decided, but the officials said it would be debated and approved on Thursday. Officials indicated that the resolution was unlikely to authorize "military force" in those words, but would opt for some other formulation like "all necessary means." 
 A senior United States official said today that the Bush Administration would interpret such language as authorizing the use of force, just as it did after the Aug. 25 resolution authorizing a naval blockade of Iraq.
 
U.S. Seeks Jan. 1 Deadline
 The Bush Administration is pushing for a Jan. 1 deadline but diplomats cautioned tonight that no final decision has been made. They said the Council may end up setting the deadline toward the end of the month to allow more time for last-minute attempts to promote a peaceful settlement.
The resolution, if passed, would represent only a partial victory for the Administration. While authorizing force and warning President Saddam Hussein that Iraq must leave Kuwait without conditions, it would also give opponents of a military solution more time to make a case against an attack in gulf. There is already opposition to military force in Congress and among some of Washington's Arab allies ; anti-war demonstrations have also begun to crop up around the United States.
In Washington, the Bush Administration stepped up its effort to raise concern among Americans about Iraq's ability to develop nuclear weapons, saying Baghdad might be able to produce a crude nuclear device within a year. [Page A13.]
 
Strenuous Lobbying Effort
 The United States gained promises of support for the resolution as the result of a strenuous lobbying of Security Council members by Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and repeated public statements by President Bush that, while not directly threatening war, emphasized the need to consider the use of force against Iraq. The Administration had hoped for unanimous support among the five permanent members of the Security Council for a resolution authorizing the use of force without linking it to a warning that would in effect delay military action against Iraq.
What it got was agreement to the new resolution from three of the other four permanent Security Council members with the power to veto decisions -- Britain, France and the Soviet Union. The fifth, China, said it will not block the resolution but has yet to decide whether to vote in favor or abstain.
A further 6 of the Council's 10 rotating members -- Canada, Finland, Ethiopia, Ivory Coast, Zaire and Romania -- have also indicated they will vote in favor of the proposed resolution, ensuring that it will get at least 10 votes.
Four other rotating members -- Malaysia, Colombia, Cuba and Yemen -- remain undecided, American officials said. But they added that Colombia, and perhaps Malaysia too, may well end up voting in favor, though Cuba and Yemen will probably vote against or abstain.
 
Warning for Baghdad
 Approval of the resolution would enable the Bush Administration to wind up its monthlong presidency of the Security Council with a partial victory that sends Baghdad a serious warning and demonstrates the cohesion of the international alliance the Bush Administration has built up against President Hussein in the gulf.
The Security Council has approved 10 resolutions so far during the gulf crisis, declaring Kuwait's annexation null and void and imposing far-reaching economic sanctions on Iraq. So far all of these resolutions have won the support of at least 13 Council members, with Yemen and Cuba the only 2 to vote against or abstain.
Approval of the new resolution would mark only the second occasion in the history of the United Nations that it has agreed to invoke the provisions of its charter allowing the organization to reverse aggression by military force if economic embargoes and other less forceful actions fail. The first was the series of decisions in 1950 that were taken in response to the North Korean invasion of South Korea.
The Security Council has also twice approved the use of force to impose an economic blockade against a country deemed in breach of the charter, authorizing Britain to cut oil supplies to Rhodesia's breakaway white seccessionist Government in 1968 and allowing naval enforcement of the trade embargo it imposed on Iraq after the Aug. 2 invasion of Kuwait.
 
Baker Will Preside
 Mr. Baker, who will preside over the Thursday meeting, announced his intention to convene the Security Council early this morning in Houston. He had just returned from lightning visits to Colombia to garner support for the resolution as well as to Los Angeles for similar talks with Malaysia's Foreign Minister, Abu Hassan Omar.
Mr. Baker said during his return flight, "I think the Council will want to explore a resolution that would make it very clear that members could utilize all necessary means after a certain date to implement the previous resolutions."
"We're talking about a resolution that would lay the political foundation for a possible use of force," Mr. Baker added. "The clear message of such a resolution to Saddam Hussein would be that there is still a chance to resolve this matter peacefully and politically within that time frame. We think this offers the very best hope for a peaceful and political settlement."
 
Soviets Forced Delay
 During all the Administration's consultations, the Soviet Union has not waivered in its insistence that Iraq must obey the Security Council and get out of Kuwait without any preconditions. But the Soviets have also appeared hesitant about the use of force, repeatedly stressing the immense and incalculable dangers inherent in a Middle East war and pleading for more time to find a peaceful resolution.
As a result, diplomats say the United States has been forced to agree to a delay of about two months before resorting to force in order to give Mr. Hussein more time for reflection and other countries a last chance to persuade him to surrender Kuwait without a fight.
Officials say this was not the Administration's preferred approach because the delay gives the anti-war movement in the United States and elsewhere more time to organize and also helps those in Congress who believe that only they can authorize the use of American soldiers in battle.
China has appeared even more reluctant to envision the use of force, repeatedly saying that it believes all international disputes should be resolved peacefully. But American officials say Beijing has given assurances it will not block the kind of resolution the United States is proposing. They also say that China came under heavy Soviet pressure to vote for it at a hurried meeting last week in northwestern China between the Foreign Ministers of the two countries.
 
No Agreement on Text
 While a majority of Security Council members now favor a new resolution authorizing force after a further delay, American officials warn that there is still no agreement on the actual text. Representatives of the five permanent Council members met in New York today to discuss exactly what the resolution might say.
Officials say the draft resolution is unlikely to use the phrase "military force," in large measure to avoid Chinese objections. But Mr. Baker spoke of the Council allowing countries to "use all necessary means" to expel Iraq from Kuwait.
The Council also avoided any direct reference to force on Aug. 25 when it approved Resolution 665, which allows countries with naval forces in the gulf to intercept and board cargo vessels trying to break the trade embargo against Iraq.
The resolution is also expected to contain a last appeal to the Iraqi President to reach a peaceful settlement on the basis of previous Security Council resolutions.

HEADLINE: California, the Congressional Giant, Has the Numbers but Not the Solidarity

BYLINE: By SUSAN F. RASKY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
If numbers were all that mattered, the most powerful delegation in the House of Representatives would be California's.
With 45 members, and the expectation of seven more after Congressional districts are redrawn for 1992, the California contingent would become the largest state delegation in American history, holding a 19-seat advantage over its closest rival, New York. 
 But as California Representatives are the first to note, their collective clout may be more myth than reality. Poised for what would seem to be an opportunity to dominate Congress well into the next century, they see their delegation hobbled by ideological and regional divisions that threaten to significantly dilute its numerical strength.
"Some people would argue that we are the giant easily tied down by the Lilliputians," said Representative Vic Fazio, a Democrat from the Sacramento area, who will be the next chairman of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee.
 
'At Each Other's Throats'
 Representative Jerry Lewis, a Republican from Redlands who heads the House Republican Conference, put it more bluntly. "We are a self-neutered giant," he said. "We represent over 10 percent of the House, and it doesn't matter what the issue is, we are constantly at each other's throats."
Politically, the delegation is as eclectic as the state itself, so profoundly split that Democrats and Republicans no longer even try to hold the kind of regular bipartisan meetings that unify other large delegations like those of Illinois, Texas or New York. California's members run the gamut from ultra-liberal Democrats like Ronald V. Dellums of Berkeley, who just filed suit to prevent the Bush Administration from going to war in the Persian Gulf without Congress's consent, to ultra-conservative Republicans like William E. Dannemeyer of Orange County, who argues that homosexual conduct is a sin and that AIDS victims "emit spores" that can spread the disease.
Eternally baffled by the politics of a state that could produce such extremes, the former House Speaker, Thomas P. O'Neill of Massachusetts, used to shake his head and refer to the Californians simply as "the distinguished members from Disneyland."
 
Success With Quake Aid
 Measured in simple terms of bringing home the bacon, the Californians worry that they do not deliver what their numbers should entitle them to.
Although the delegation pulled off a stunning performance last year in ramming more than $3.5 billion in disaster aid through the House and Senate after the Bay Area earthquake, that kind of effort is the exception.
Three years ago, a national earthquake monitoring center, for which California would have made a logical home, went instead to Buffalo. And Representative Lewis is still fuming over the failure of California to make a serious bid for the Superconducting Supercollider, the research center that went to Texas.
In both of those cases, Mr. Lewis said, the inability of the delegation to come together was compounded by a curious sort of indifference to Washington from state politicians and constituents back home. "People in California don't think about Congress or Washington," he said. "It's more than just 3,000 miles that separates us."
 
Eyes Toward Home
 Applying broader criteria, like influence over national policy and the ability to shape major legislation, the Californians are well placed. Members have held leadership positions or had quiet influence on panels that handle health policy, immigration, transportation, appropriations and the budget.
But there are significant gaps. There is no Californian of national stature in Congress who speaks on issues like military and foreign affairs, trade or tax policy. And California's Senators, unlike those from other large states, have historically been less oncerned with making national names for themselves than tending to their constituencies.
"It depends how you judge them," said Sherry Bebitch Jeffe, a senior associate at the Claremont Graduate School's Center for Politics and Policy in Southern California. "They are in many ways very responsive to their California constituencies, and it happens that those are not necessarily the areas their colleagues in Washington perceive to be the most glitzy."
The delegation's divisions are by no means simply partisan. Democrats have feuded over such seemingly clear cut state issues as taxes on the wine industry and Federal water subsidies.
 
Ideological Divisions
 On the Republican side, the divisions are ideological. Because of disagreements over the budget, for instance, Representative Dana Rohrabacher, a conservative Republican from Torrance, plans to vote against his fellow Californian, Mr. Lewis, in a contest for chairmanship of the House Republican conference next week.
Conceding that such a vote might cost the state the third-ranking slot in the House Republican hierarchy, Mr. Rohrabacher said, "When it comes down to philosophical compatibility versus pure California interests, my instinct is to go with compatibility."
Representative Robert T. Matsui, a Sacramento Democrat who serves on the Ways and Means Committee, often finds himself torn among the state's conflicting ideologies and competing urban, agricultural and high technology interests.
"I think the wise members outside the delegation know our level of incompetence and know how to exploit it," he said. "It's easy to divide us."
 
Districts Are Split
 Even the districts are divided. Representative Douglas H. Bosco, a Democrat whose district in Northern California includes some of the most spectacular forest land in the nation, is fond of explaining this to colleagues: "All of my constituents like trees." he said. "The problem is, 50 percent like them vertically and the other 50 percent like them horizontally."
Mr. Bosco was defeated earlier this month by Frank Riggs, a Republican, putting the delegation lineup in the House at 26 Democrats and 19 Republicans for the next Congress. California's two Senate seats, occupied by Alan Cranston, a Democrat, and Pete Wilson, a Republican who was just elected Governor, have been split between the parties since 1977.
Mr. Wilson's seat is known in California as "the crazy seat," having changed hands six times since 1958, and Mr. Wilson's departure will leave the state with yet another freshman in the Senate, denying California a chance to build seniority on a major committee.
The situation in the Senate is likely to get worse. Mr. Wilson will name a successor to fill his seat until 1992, and whoever is elected to it then to fill out Mr. Wilson's unfinished term will have to run again in 1994. Mr. Cranston's seat will also be vacant in 1992. The ailing Senator, currently under an ethics investigation on charges of seeking favors for a troubled California savings and loan instutition, has decided not to run for re-election and to step down from his position as Democratic whip.
 
Different Kind of Clout
 Representative Thomas J. Downey, a New York Democrat who affectionately describes his liberal California colleagues as the "bran muffin and grapefruit set," agrees that they are not as politically adept as their Eastern cousins. But he finds the hand wringing about a lack of California clout amusing.
"I look at New York now as the aging British Empire of American politics," he said with a sigh. "We are still the repository of culture and finance, thank God, but we have to rely less on numbers than on cunning, intelligence, subtlety and inveiglement."
As for the Californians, he said, they should relax a little. "Their state is so big, so rich and so economically powerful, that they don't have to exercise their political muscle to be felt."

GRAPHIC: Photos: "Some people would argue that we are the giant easily tied down by the Lilliputians," said Representative Vic Fazio, a Democrat from the Sacramento area.; "It doesn't matter what the issue is, we are constantly at each other's throats," said Representative Jerry Lewis, who heads the House Republican Conference. (Paul Conklin)

HEADLINE: Business Digest

 BODY:

Economy
Bond traders say inflationary pressures have eased, even though oil prices remain significantly above levels seen before Iraq invaded Kuwait, and could go higher if war erupts. [Page D1.]
Retailers are urging affluent consumers to buy expensive goods like cars, boats, jewelry and furs before the new Federal "luxury tax" takes effect on Jan. 1. The deficit-reduction bill established a 10 percent excise tax that applies to the portion of retail prices that exceeds $30,000 for automobiles, $100,000 for private boats and yachts, $10,000 for furs and jewelry, including watches, and $250,000 for private airplanes. [D1.]
Many of the blacks who have taken a tenuous place in the middle class are scaling the economic ladder with one hand on the rung and the other outstretched to relatives who need them. [A1.]
Cape Canaveral will not be able to fend off foreign competitors in the rocket-launching business unless the base in Florida is revamped. State officials have a plan involving Federal grants and tax incentives to commercial space ventures. [D1.]
Machine tool orders increased nearly 1.9 percent in October from the September level, the industry's trade association said in its monthly survey. [D2.]
 
Companies
The deal for MCA, owner of Universal Studios, seems near, but the details of the takeover by Matsushita were not yet completed last night. One participant said no hurdle had arisen to block the deal, which is valued at almost almost $7 billion. [D2.]
 
The Media Business
Ninja Turtles have conquered yet another world: Saturday morning television ratings. With its "Teen-age Mutant Ninja Turtles," CBS seems likely to take the lead in the prime time for children for the first time in almost a decade. [D1.]
A proposed category for mail would cost newspapers millions of dollars in lost business. The Postal Service wants to create a new third-class category that would give direct-mail companies, which compete with newspapers, a 20 percent discount. [D1.]
Publishers like Farrar, Straus & Giroux see great potential in Eastern Europe. Edwin McDowell: Publishing. [D8.]
Mirabella and Lear's magazines are succeeding in attracting sophisticated, affluent, middle-aged women. [D8.]
Advertisers often feel their agencies ignore them, according to a survey conducted by a consulting firm. But many brand managers and marketing executives said they were not thinking of switching agencies. Kim Foltz: Advertising. [D9.]
 
 International
Taiwan is trying to accomplish a new industrial revolution by becoming a high-technology manufacturer of computers instead of toys, and satellite equipment instead of gadgets. [D1.]
Czechoslovakia's Kotva department store is a leader in the Eastern European drive to modernize. Its executives have overcome many obstacles, like the scarcity of hard currency, but they still struggle to keep the shelves stocked. [D7.]
Caribbean banana farmers are worried that the economic changes in Europe could kill their industry by ending the preferences they rely upon to compete with Latin American rivals. [D7.]
 
Today's Columns
Faster, prepackaged bankruptcies are becoming more popular, as more overleveraged companies admit that they cannot make the interest payments on high-yield securities they issued in the 1980's. Some troubled corporations are trying to cut both the time and expense of reorganizations. Business and the Law. [D2.]
Supermarkets General wants to unleverage the company it took private before the stock market crash in 1987. It is trying buy back some of the securities it issued but says the refinancing is not a sign of distress. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D4.]

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Arafat Stresses Linkage in Settling Gulf Crisis

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TUNIS, Nov. 22

 BODY:
The Palestine Liberation Organization chairman, Yasir Arafat, has said he strongly supports President Saddam Hussein's assertions that a withdrawal of Iraqi troops from Kuwait must be tied to an overall settlement of conflicts in the region, including the 40-year-old Palestinian-Israeli conflict.
Mr. Arafat, who left on one of his frequent visits to Baghdad just after being interviewed early Thursday, said that based on what he knows about the Iraqi armed forces, a war would be so devastating that it would last a long time, cause tens of thousands of casualties, and leave the region in economic and political instability. 
 For this reason, he said, there was no real alternative to negotiations between Mr. Hussein and those nations opposing his occupation of Kuwait.
Mr. Arafat, whose support of Iraq has been strongly denounced by Saudi Arabia, its Arab gulf allies and Egypt, dismissed predictions of a lasting and deep rupture between the P.L.O. and pro-Western Arab nations, saying the disputes would dissapear with the settlement of the crisis.
"We have had many splits before in the Arab camp," he said. "This is not the first crisis for the Arab nation, and it will not be the last."
 
Few Regrets About U.S.
 He also expressed few regrets over the effect of the crisis on the P.L.O.'s relationship with the United States, the leader of the military forces arrayed against Iraq. He noted that the United States had ended its dialogue with the P.L.O. even before the gulf crisis.
In an interview that began after midnight and lasted 90 minutes, he insisted that the resolution of the crisis could not be achieved without a simultaneous settlement of the Arab-Israeli issue, saying the two have become inextricably linked in the minds of most Arabs and in the view of many foreign governments.
"China, Japan, Germany, the European Community and even President Bush have said so," Mr. Arafat said in a reference to the Mr. Bush's assertions early in the crisis that the world must eventually address the issue of the Palestinians.
But he acknowledged that his stand has cost the Palestinians and his organization dearly. He estimated the total cost to Palestinians at $10 billion, including savings lost by about 400,000 Palestinians who lived and worked in Kuwait and the cutoff of financial aid to the P.L.O. from Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, the United Arab Emirates and Qatar, which have been angered by the P.L.O.'s support of Iraq.
 
Principle Is Defended
 Mr. Arafat said his positions were a matter of principle that could not be changed in return for "a few coins or a few petrodollars." He dismissed talk by his Arab opponents about putting pressure on the P.L.O. to remove him as a leader of the organization, saying with a faint smile that "it is our people who chose, not anyone else" and adding that some of these Arab leaders' hold on power is less sure than his own.
He argued that a war would produce such huge casualties among Arabs in Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, Iraq and elsewhere in the Middle East that it would lead to the overthrow of many an Arab government. "There is not a single Arab regime that can govern or exist over the bodies and skeletons of other Arabs," he said.
Contradicting assertions by American military officials as well as military experts in Saudi Arabia and Egypt that a war with Iraq will be quickly and decisively won by the better-armed allied forces, Mr. Arafat said Iraqi troops could withstand an assault and fight on for many months, perhaps as long as three years. He said he based that assessment on his knowledge of the Iraqi Army's armaments and ammunition, including "weapons of mass destruction,"
He placed the largest responsibility for the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait on the Kuwaiti ruling family, the Sabahs. He said that days before the invasion, he repeatedly urged the Emir of Kuwait, Sheik Jaber al-Ahmed al-Sabah, to show some flexibility toward Iraq's demands that Baghdad's $11 billion debt to Kuwait be forgiven, and toward territorial concessions along the Kuwaiti-Iraqi border.
 
His Advice to the Kuwaitis
 In a moment of self-criticism, Mr. Arafat said that in trying to persuade the Kuwaitis to compromise, he told the Emir not to become entrapped by what he described as "useless and meaningless" United Nations and Arab League resolutions against Mr. Hussein's claims.
"I told them, 'Learn from us, the Palestinians,' " Mr. Arafat said. "We have so many resolutions and decisions, but we still don't have a solution. I told them, 'Don't be fooled by resolutions and decisions. Go for the solution if there is a chance for a solution.' "
He said the P.L.O. was pursuing an eight-point settlement that it has proposed to all the nations involved in the gulf crisis. It includes a partial withdrawal of Iraqi troops from Kuwait, negotiations on a financial settlement between Iraq, Kuwait, the P.L.O. and other Arab countries affected by the crisis and the replacement of foreign troops in the region by Arab troops.
He stressed that his plan put Palestinian demands for a settlement with Israel on the same footing as the United Nations demands for an Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait.
"One thing has to be understood clearly," he said. "We, the Palestinians, are not just a number in the equation. We are the difficult number. There can be no solution in the gulf crisis without a solution to the Palestinian problem."
 
Always the 'Principal Issue'
 He added that when it comes to the Middle East, "the Palestinian question has always been the principal issue" and that it "will remain the principal issue" after the gulf crisis.
Mr. Arafat said that given the ominous consequences of a war, there was little choice but to avoid one.
"This war will take place, however, in the most strategic and vital oil region in the world," he said. "So from the moment the first bullet is fired, there will be an economic panic. The barrel of oil will reach $100 or more. You know that oil wells and facilities on both sides will be damaged. That is in addition to the use of weapons of total destruction. And Israel, which is unquestionably a part of the plan, will be involved."
"Victory in this war will be ashes in the mouth of the victor," he said.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1537 words

HEADLINE: Ulster County Warns Albany of a Tax Revolt

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES

 BODY:
In bucolic Ulster County, some 90 miles north of New York City, there are rumblings of a tax revolt. The stirrings began three weeks ago, when the county unveiled its proposed 1991 budget -- and a proposed county property-tax rise of 82 percent.
For many of Ulster County's 168,000 residents, who have already endured a 42 percent tax rise this fiscal year, the proposal is a sign that it is time to draw the line. Groups have organized and petitions have been circulated to cut back the county budget. And some are talking of spreading the revolt, by taking the message to other counties in the mid-Hudson Valley. 
 Members of the Ulster County Legislature, who are already feeling the heat, say the anger should be directed farther up the Hudson -- to Albany. Like local government officials around the country, they say they are caught between soaring social-service costs for required programs and dwindling aid from the Federal Government and from states that see their own revenues falling.
"The first thing we're going to do in Ulster is cut the hell out of the budget because no way can we come in with an 82 percent tax increase," said Danny Alfonso, the County Legislature's finance chairman. "That's by Dec. 10. The second thing we're going to do is get together with leaders from Dutchess, Putnam, Sullivan, Columbia, Greene and Orange to put together a list" of demands on the state that will be presented in January.
Then, said Mr. Alfonso, the seven counties plan to sue the Governor. "Every time we have a public meeting, everybody says sue the state," he said. "So we're going to sue the state."
For residents already hard-pressed by the recent hard times, the tax increases, no matter whose fault, seem too much to bear. Joe and Mary Ann Marchetti, who bought a $65,000 handyman special in Kingston five years ago, paid $2,000 in county property taxes this year.
"And we're lucky," Mrs. Marchetti said. "We know some people paying $5,000 and $6,000. People talk about moving away, but with the economy the way it is, who are you going to get to buy your house?"
Instead of moving, last week at a public meeting with 33 county legislators, the Marchettis announced they had gathered 200 families -- and were gathering 200 others -- to plan a tax boycott if any substantial tax increase is passed.
 
Reagan's Cuts in Federal Aid
 In some ways, the situation in Ulster is like that faced by counties around the state. But none other faces such a steep tax increase -- in large part, officials say, because the county did not raise the tax rate for nine years before last year's rise, and the current rate of $3.50 per $1,000 of assessed value is still low by statewide standards. Neighboring Dutchess County, for instance, has a rate of $6.18 per $1,000. The projected 82 percent tax increase would give Ulster a rate of about $6.37 per $1,000.
In Ulster, as in counties throughout the state and the country, officials attribute much of their current woes to deep cuts in Federal aid for social programs. The cuts, which started during President Ronald Reagan's administration, sent state governments reeling, and the pain, they say, eventually landed lower down.
G. Thomas Goodman, a spokesman for the National Association of Counties in Washington, says the problem threatens to grow as state and Federal governments continue to pass laws mandating services, mainly in health, human services and environmental protection, without providing money for them.
"The counties are facing demands for new services from their residents but are limited in the kinds of taxes they can levy to pay for these services," Mr. Goodman said. "And all this is taking place as the nation's economy weakens and the resistance to new taxes by voters grows."
In New York State, the picture is almost certain to get bleaker. Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has proposed $1 billion in cuts. They would reduce outlays to county governments by 10 percent, including cuts in revenue-sharing programs, school aid and Medicaid, foster care and other social services the state dictates.
The upshot, said Edwin Crawford, chief lobbyist for the state's 62 counties, is that the state's local governments will start cutting costs by eliminating youth recreation programs, offices serving the elderly, and other non-mandated local services.
 
Parks, Roads, Arts
 "The Governor has come around -- he's saying that mandates have to be curbed," Mr. Crawford said. "But until then, what we'll see evolving in the next few years is that parks and playgrounds will close, roads won't be rebuilt, arts will suffer."
Even before the Governor's proposed cuts, localities around the state were already facing hard times.
In New York City, where Medicaid expenditures are over $1 billion, residents face a slew of increased costs, from new sales and property taxes to subway fare and toll increases. The city stands to lose between $100 million and $180 million in state funds if the Governor's proposals are passed.
Suffolk County, the largest county outside of New York City, with 1.4 million residents, has already cut 2,500 workers from the payrolls in the last three years, is cutting 200 more next year and may have to cut even more, said County Executive Patrick Halpin.
 
Most Spending Is Mandated
 Sullivan County's 90,000 residents, more than half of whom are over age 55, face a 61 percent tax rise next year, largely because of increased costs in Medicaid, foster care and Aid to Families with Dependent Children, said the Sullivan County Administrator, Paul Rouis. "More than 55 percent of our budget goes toward these state-mandated programs," he said. "I don't think we can absorb much more."
In Ulster County, Administrator William Darwak said up to 70 percent of the $151 million budget proposed for 1991 -- up from the $136 million approved for 1990 -- would pay for services mandated by the state. Next year's budget calls for $15 million in Medicaid spending, almost $5 million more than the budget dictated for 1990 -- and about $3 million more than the county spent. The county budgeted Medicaid at $190,000 a week and has spent $350,000. It is also spending $30 million in mental health services and more than $3 million in related services for children.
But though the projected budget increase of $15 million is only 11 percent, the county faces an 82 percent tax rate increase because its revenues have been dropping as well. County officials say there is a shortfall of $2 million this year in sales-tax collections and a $1.8 million drop in property-tax revenues. Federal revenue-sharing aid of $1.8 million ended in 1987, and state revenue-sharing of $750,000 ended in 1989.
"New York State is the Cadillac of social services," Mr. Alfonso said. "Other states have Volkswagens. We're getting more people moving in for more services. If Medicaid wasn't in our budget, we'd be facing a 15 percent tax decrease instead of an 82 percent tax increase."
 
Arts Funds Frozen and Falling
 County legislators have sworn to keep many county programs, including a 280-bed infirmary for the elderly that will cost $5 million this year and a county jail at capacity with 160 inmates, who each cost $75 a day. But the Unison Learning Center in New Paltz, which holds concerts, classes and arts workshops for about 6,000 county residents each year, is threatened. The arts center stopped receiving county money two years ago; recently, it was told by the state that funds were being frozen and would probably be cut, said its director, Stuart Bigley.
Members of the Ulster County Legislature, who have held three public meetings since they outlined the new budget, are well aware that voters are worried and fed up.
"The taxpayers are extremely angry," said Gerald Benjamin, the county's Senate majority leader. He said that in the last public meeting, held on Tuesday, 500 taxpayers told the Legislature they believed the proposed 82 percent figure was a ploy to make an actual increase of 40 or 50 percent more palatable. "People said it was a cynical manipulation," said Mr. Benjamin. "That's very distressing and indicative of the public view that we're dishonest. But in my view, this is the tightest budget we've proposed in nine years."
Ulster County residents say there is a new activism born of taxpayers' disgust. Two taxpayers associations have formed in the past few months, each with more than 200 members, and leaders of the two groups have formed a countywide taxpayers alliance, designed to help others in the county's 20 towns form taxpayers associations.
On their agenda is a mock Boston Tea Party on Saturday at the Kingston waterfront. "It's symbolic," said Joe Marchetti, who organized the tea party. "But the point is to tell legislators we're tired of runaway tax hikes, bureacracy and abuse."
The head of the Kingston Taxpayers Association, Gordon Ross Webb, said: "Our point is that no one can force a legislator to vote for a budget increase. We keep hearing mandates, mandates, mandates, but there is an awful lot of waste in Ulster County. There are 33 legislators. If they don't give us what we want, we have the power to change things with our votes."

SECTION: Section C; Page 13; Column 1; Cultural Desk

LENGTH: 1345 words

HEADLINE: Seeing the World Through the Eyes of 7-Year-Olds

BYLINE: By GLENN COLLINS

 BODY:
Michael Apted, the English film maker who documented the lives of 14 children in his highly praised series "7 Up" more than a quarter of a century ago, was resting in a second-grader's chair, looking bemused. A brand new "7 Up" was revving up around him, as a crew of seven film makers toiled under the direction of Phil Joanou. They were adjusting lights and loading the camera in Room 308 at Public School 97 on East Houston Street in Manhattan, as everyone waited for the first 7-year-old to arrive.
The 48-year-old Mr. Apted is the director of such feature films as "Gorillas in the Mist," "Gorky Park" and "Coal Miner's Daughter," but he is the producer of "Sevenup in America," the Yankee version of "7 Up." That's why he could afford to relax and observe Mr. Joanou at work. 
 "Philip, this is day 1 of a life's work, you know," said Mr. Apted, gray-haired, tweedy and avuncular.
"I know, I know," said Mr. Joanou.
"And," Mr. Apted continued, "I think you'll outlive me on this one!"
They both laughed. In the English series, initiated in 1964, Mr. Apted first filmed his group of youngsters when they were 7 years old and filmed them again when they were 14, 21 and 28.
 
The American Version
 The new American version -- whose title is spelled "Sevenup" so that audiences "won't confuse the film with the soft drink," Mr. Joanou said -- will follow the original's scenario. Mr. Joanou is interviewing more than 20 7-year-old boys and girls from disparate ethnic, racial, economic and social backgrounds in New York, Chicago, Los Angeles, Lincoln, Neb., and Dublin, Ga.
Six of the children are from wealthy families; six are poor, including one from a homeless family; the rest have middle-class backgrounds. The subjects are white, black, Hispanic and Asian; they are Protestant, Roman Catholic and Jewish. One child is Vietnamese and two others -- one from El Salvador, and one from Mexico -- do not speak English.
The American series is ultimately intended for theatrical release at the 28-year stage, following the pattern of the English series. Mr. Apted's "28 Up" grossed more than $1 million, he said, unusual for a documentary. Until the year 2011 -- when the young Americans will have reached age 28 -- the series will appear every seven years as an hourlong television special. CBS , which is co-producing the series with Grenada Television in England, will air the "Sevenup" special next March or April.
 
'Reality of Children's Lives'
 Why film a new version? "It seems to me that this is a rich and valuable way to look at the history of a country," Mr. Apted said. "At worst, it's a social document, and at best it's a powerful film that gains strength from its portrayal of the reality of children's lives.
"These films are really a mix of instant history, sociology and soap opera. We'll try to build a series that looks at the issues that will be important to America in the future."
Mr. Apted said that the original "7 Up," for which he was the line producer at the age of 22 in 1964, "was an accident, a one-shot," he said. "Only later did its immense popularity give us the idea to go back at seven-year intervals. If we'd known that, we'd have been more careful about choosing our 7-year-olds in the first place, and would have asked them more questions. On this series, we can finally do it right."
Doing it right, to Mr. Apted, means having a cross-cultural control group for the American series. Grenada has set loose a Russian documentary crew that is now shooting 7-year-olds in the Soviet Union. In reflecting the momentous changes there, over time, they will also serve as foils for their American counterparts.
 
Tracking Down the Originals
 Mr. Apted has not lost track of his original 14 subjects, however. In December he'll begin shooting "35 Up," a new documentary on his English cast of characters as they reach the age of 35. It will be shown on British television in May, along with Mr. Joanou's documentary on the American 7-year-olds, as well as the gestating project on the Russian 7-year-olds.
In Room 308, Mr. Joanou was going over lists of questions when his first subject bounced through the door, a streetwise whirlwind of energy wearing a Super Bowl Shuffle T-shirt. "Hello, Luis!" Mr. Joanou said.
"O.K., let's go!" said Luis -- who, like all "7 Up" children since 1964, is publicly identified only by first name.
Mr. Joanou led Luis across the scuffed red linoleum to a battered second-grader's desk, and as the camera rolled, the director asked him questions about his school, his family, his neighborhood and the life he leads as the child of a homeless family.
Despite the hot lights and the crowd of technicians around him, Luis answered Mr. Joanou's questions volubly, displaying impatience at times, prodding his questioner, "Next? Next?" When the camera stopped rolling to reload, he trained his huge, canny eyes at Mr. Joanou and said, "So -- how was I?" Mr. Joanou said he was great.
The next morning the crew traveled to an utterly different setting: the elegant precincts of the Nightingale-Bamford School off Park Avenue on the Upper East Side of Manhattan. The subjects, dressed in pressed uniforms and crisp white blouses, were three lively second-graders named Lucy, Kate and Alexis. They sat on a beige couch between blue accent pillows. A blue-and-white Oriental covered the floor.
 
7-Year-Old Wisdom
 Though they were just as much at ease before the camera as Luis, the girls' mode of expression reflected their upbringing: "Some nannies, I just wouldn't leave my child alone with," said 7-year-old Lucy, when asked about her future. "You have to be careful."
Mr. Joanou (whose name is pronounced Joe-ON-oh), is the director of "State of Grace," which starred Sean Penn, Gary Oldman and Ed Harris in a tale of Irish-American gangsters in the Hell's Kitchen section of Manhattan. His previous feature was "Three O'Clock High," but the film that recommended him to Mr. Apted was the documentary "U2 Rattle and Hum."
"I chose Phil not only because he's an accomplished film maker, but also because I know he'll be around for the next 30 years," said Mr. Apted. "He's 28, so he gets on well with the 7-year-olds and can grow old gracefully with them."
Mr. Apted also likes the idea "that Phil has a feature-film career, too," he said.
"Because the way things are going these days," he added, "a documentary career can be a frail reed indeed, and you never know whether documentary directors will give up. I wanted Phil to be in the business three decades from now."
 
'Kids Think It's Fun'
 The children of "Sevenup" and their parents will not be paid for their contribution to the film. Parents have agreed to participate "because they're proud of their kids and the kids think it's fun," said Vicky Bippart, the film's line producer. "But it's entirely up to them if they want to participate in the future documentaries."
Two of Mr. Apted's original 14 children dropped out of the filming in "28 Up," although they had participated at the 14- and 21-year intervals. Mr. Apted said he hoped they'd be back in the fold for the 35-year documentary.
The original English series focused on the disparities of the English class system, as played out in the children's lives. Although Mr. Joanou said he believed that America had its own class system, "I'm not going to impose an editorial point of view going in," he said.
"I'd like to present these children honestly, and let the film's themes surface in a less structured way," he added.
As Mr. Joanou looks ahead, he said he hoped he could find the emotional distance from his captivating subjects, "to be able to observe these kids as they go through life changes and dislocations, and still remain dispassionate."
"That's just essential to the project," he said.
"I think that '28 Up' was one of the greatest documentaries ever made," said Mr. Joanou. "It's going to be very difficult to live up to that." He smiled. "But I've got 21 years to get good at it."

HEADLINE: India's Hindus Try to Right a 400-Year Wrong;
Structural Reforms

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Among the varieties of leadership available in India, it is true that V. P. Singh, who has resigned as Prime Minister, was the most deserving to lead the country ("A Test of Principles in India," editorial, Nov. 8). However, his failings go beyond the issues you discuss.
V. P. Singh came to office on the specific promise of unearthing certain cases of kickbacks involving the Government of Rajiv Gandhi and a few overseas companies. But all such cases were conveniently relegated to the background in the intense jockeying for power.
Mr. Singh's revival of a dormant, ineffectual proposed "reservation-quota system" is not restricted to "low-caste Hindus," as you state. It was broadened to include the "other backward castes," many of whom are politically, socially and economically affluent.
This has become a very sensitive issue, leading dozens of youths to commit immolation in disgust. How would decision makers in this country respond if positions under affirmative action plans were usurped by the sons and daughters of a David Dinkins or a Douglas Wilder? V. P. Singh was not prepared to concede many angularities developed over the years in the reservation-quota system.
However shrewd and scheming, Mr. Singh was a politician with a relatively clean record. People had expected him to strike at the roots of the problems afflicting India.
Apart from economic reforms, India urgently requires structural reforms, mostly electoral-judicial and administrative-legislative. If applied firmly, these reforms could have taken care of the temple-mosque controversy and the conflict-ridden states.
How can a leader of the governing party resolve basic questions, sometimes involving unpopular measures, if he fears forces opposed to him within his party (the dissidents), in collaboration with the opposition, will topple his Government in a couple of months? This ailment is going to be endemic to the world's largest democracy.
 
BINOY S. PRASAD
Columbia, Mo., Nov. 9, 1990
 
The writer is a Fulbright scholar in political science at the University of Missouri-Columbia.

HEADLINE: ABROAD AT HOME;
Democracy in the Way?

BYLINE: By Anthony Lewis

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
As President Bush intensifies the threat of war on Iraq, we are hearing complaints that criticism of that policy spoils its chance of success. Saddam Hussein would take the threat more seriously, that is, if Americans would silently support it.
The President himself is said to have complained about Senate critics of his turn toward war. The Senate Armed Services and Foreign Relations Committees open hearings on policy in the Persian Gulf this week, and the Administration is unenthusiastic about that. 
 The case against open debate of the war policy has been made with candor by a defense specialist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, Barry R. Posen. Writing in The Boston Globe, Professor Posen said:
"Since the President cannot want war, his purpose must be to frighten Saddam Hussein to leave Kuwait. This is coercive diplomacy, and its success depends on the ability of the United States to persuade Saddam that the coalition has the capability and the will to recapture Kuwait.
"Unfortunately, the multiple voices of American democracy make this very difficult. . . . Debate can only reduce the odds of success."
In other words, democracy is inconvenient. It is a familiar lament, heard at many points in American history. Is it true?
President Bush's war-like rhetoric is no doubt intended to frighten Saddam Hussein out of Kuwait without war. The democratic leaders who visited the gulf over Thanksgiving, the Senate majority leader, George Mitchell, and Speaker Thomas Foley, came back saying that the war talk was still just that: talk, not a decision to attack.
But it is not only talk. Threats are empty unless there is an intention to carry them out if talk fails.
When Mr. Bush early this month ordered up to 200,000 more Americans to the gulf, he moved into a scenario that must involve a real possibility of war. The huge U.S. force cannot stay in the gulf indefinitely. If "coercive diplomacy" does not move Saddam Hussein within a few months, there will be mounting pressure to use that force.
The President had to know that he was setting out on the path to war when he decided to raise the troop commitment to such a high level. He had to weigh fateful questions.
What was wrong with the previous policy of grinding Iraq down by economic sanctions? Was there doubt that Americans, or our allies, would have the patience for a long siege?
How would a war be fought? How many American casualties could be expected? What vital U.S. interest would be vindicated?
What would be the consequences of a war? How stable would pro-Western Arab governments prove to be? Might Israel be threatened?
Those and many other questions would have to be considered before any American President set his country on the road to war in an area as tricky as the Middle East. But we do not know what advice Mr. Bush got, or how he finally answered the questions. He has told us nothing about the reasons that made him shift to a policy of threatening war.
We are asked to take the President's policy on faith. We must believe, for example, that a war with Iraq would be relatively easy -- that U.S. casualties would be low.
But a large and growing number of Americans do not believe. They are not willing to go to war in the Persian Gulf on the President's unexplained say-so. Women especially have doubts: a fascinating point that is not adequately reflected in establishment comment and television talk shows.
Is it a weakness of the system if Americans refuse to follow a President blindly into war? I do not think so. Experience supports what Senator Bob Kerrey, Democrat of Nebraska, who won the Medal of Honor in Vietnam, said on Sept. 19:
"Policy makers in the United States dare not delude themselves into thinking that we do not need to debate what we are doing [in the gulf] and to state our disagreements where they exist. A failure to do this -- a simple and blind acceptance of everything the President as Commander in Chief does -- will lead to bad policy and the potential loss of clear public support for what we are doing."
Presidents have to lead. But they must suffer public debate because it tests the wisdom of their policy. That is our constitutional system, and it has outlasted most of the others on earth.
"Democracy is the worst form of government," Winston Churchill said, "except all those other forms that have been tried from time to time."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Baker Is Right About 'Jobs' . . .

BYLINE: By Robert Eisner; Robert Eisner is professor of economics at Northwestern University.

DATELINE: EVANSTON, Ill.

 BODY:
Secretary of State James Baker has introduced a curious new rationale for our changing Persian Gulf policies: jobs. "An economic recession worldwide, caused by the control by one nation -- one dictator if you will -- of the West's economic lifeline will result in the loss of jobs for American citizens," the Secretary tells us.
Well, whatever the public relations appeal he anticipates in using this argument, the economics are essentially wrong. Far from saving jobs, the Administration's new "offensive option" will threatens more job losses and deepening recession. 
 With the gulf crisis, as we all know, crude oil rose in price from $18 a barrel to as high as $40 before settling at about $30 a barrel. This price rise contributes to a recession by draining off consumer purchasing power.
The joker is that oil prices rose not so much in response to the brutal invasion of Kuwait itself but because of the threat of general war in the region.
The invasion of Kuwait and the consequent sanctions cut off only a small portion of the world's supply of oil, an amount that Saudi Arabia readily made up. War, however, threatens the wholesale destruction of oil installations and a possibly long-term interruption of oil supplies.
Dictator he certainly is, and a pretty mean one at that. But Saddam Hussin is hardly a lunatic. He did not go for Kuwait's oil -- nor would he want Saudi Arabia's, for that matter -- to drink it or pour it into the sea.
Any dictator controlling a significant amount of oil would want to sell it for the best possible price. Too high a price would throw the world into recession, cutting demand, and would spur a search for substitutes. A number of Western analysts have calculated that the optimum price for Saddam Hussein, if he could control the oil, would be about $25 a barrel.
Now $25 a barrel is significantly more than the $18 a barrel before the Kuwait invasion. But it is still much less in real terms than what we were paying a decade ago -- not the stuff of recession.
There is one saving grace to our military escalation, which is already threatening to cost $30 billion a year. All that Government spending, which so many want to reduce, would pour into the economy.
Between the people "employed" in the armed forces, those working in expanded defense industries and those using wartime incomes to purchase goods from our nonmilitary companies, the unemployment rate would decline. It is now close to 6 percent, compared with 1 percent in World War II and about 3 percent during the Vietnam War.
If that is what Mr. Baker has in mind, he may be right after all that our policy is aimed at saving jobs.

HEADLINE: Liberian Factions Gather to Start Peace Talks

BYLINE: By KENNETH B. NOBLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BAMAKO, Mali, Nov. 26

 BODY:
The leaders of Liberia's main political factions gathered here today in preparation for long-planned peace talks.
If the meeting begins on Tuesday as scheduled, it will be the first time all factional leaders have come together for negotiations since the country's civil war began 11 months ago. 
 Riding on these discussions is not only Liberia's political fate but also the future of the West African peacekeeping force now in Monrovia. The force, which is dominated by the Nigerian Army, installed an interim Government last week.
African and Western diplomats say the fighting appears to have reached a turning point in the last few weeks as the West African force has coordinated its moves with the remnants of the army of the late President, Samuel K. Doe, and the breakaway guerilla unit led by Prince Johnson. The three forces have pushed the main rebel group, the National Patriotic Front led by Charles Taylor, out of Monrovia with heavy artillery and air power.
The peacekeeping force was sent to Liberia last August by the 16-nation Economic Community of West African States. The intervention was conceivedas an effort to stop hostilities and organize elections. The interim Liberian Government installed by the peacekeeping force last week is led by Amos Sawyer, a political science professor and longtime opponent of the Doe Government who has lived in the United States for the last several years.
If the discussions should produce even some limited form of agreement among the Liberians, the prospects for withdrawing the peacekeeping force would be considerably brighter. If the talks should disintegrate into rancorous arguments, as lower-level talks did last August in Banjul, Gambia, then the bloody civil war is likely to continue.There are, in essence, five negotiating blocs attending the conference. The interim Government, which is backed by the peacekeeping force, is expected to be represented by Mr. Sawyer.
Representing the National Patriotic Front will be its leader, Mr. Taylor, a former bureaucrat in the Doe regime who began the rebellion last December with some 150 guerrillas, including Mr. Johnson. The conflict has caused the deaths of at least 20,000 people, mostly civilians, and the dislocations of about half of the nation's 2.4 million people.
Mr. Taylor is expected to press for the removal of the peacekeeping force, which he has accused of trying to prop up the remaining elements of the former Doe Government. He has rejected calls for cease-fire talks until the intervention force left Liberian soil. But he was forced to attend these talks, Western diplomats say, after the West African force mounted a successful assault on his main military position.
A third negotiating bloc is headed by Mr. Johnson, 38 years old, who was once a commander of Mr. Taylor's National Patriotic Front, but split with them shortly after the fighting started. Mr. Johnson's forces captured and killed Mr. Doe in late September.
A fourth bloc will be followers of the Mr. Doe, most of them members of his ethnic group, the Krahn. Attending for the group is Brig. David Nimley, commander of Mr. Doe's military forces.
A fifth bloc will be the 12,000-member peacekeeping force itself, led by Maj. Gen. Joshua Dogdnyaro, its Nigerian commander. The force comprises military units from Nigeria, Ghana, Guinea, Sierra Leone and Gambia.

HEADLINE: THE DEAL FOR MCA;
Politics and Multinational Movies

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: OSAKA, Japan, Tuesday, Nov. 27

 BODY:
When he announced Japan's largest purchase yet in the United States, the president of the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company appeared to fuel the political concerns surrounding the deal with a surprise omission: He refused to say that the Japanese consumer electronics giant would not interfere with the content of MCA's movies, records or books.
That question, more than any other, may dictate how smoothly Matsushita's president, Akio Tanii, gains acceptance for the $6.13 billion deal for MCA in Washington and Hollywood. And it is a question likely to be asked with increasing urgency now that Matsushita, the world's largest maker of consumer electronic goods, and Sony, its archrival and owner of Columbia Pictures, now control more than a quarter of the American movie market. 
 The issue of a chilling effect on movies, books or records has been particularly potent because Japanese producers and publishers exercise a degree of self-censorship in Japan that goes beyond what is usually seen in the West. Certain topics -- the power of right-wing political groups, the existence of an outcast group in Japan known as the "burakumin," Japanese atrocities during the occupation of Korea and China -- are rarely discussed openly, and rarely the subject of extensive treatment in film or music. It is not that any overt censorship takes place, but rather that the norms of a society well attuned to subtle signals make unnecessary rigid rules about what is acceptable discourse.
There is no evidence so far that those same rules will be applied outside Japan, and Japanese often complain that the same level of concern is not raised when Australians or Italians buy into Hollywood, as they have recently in acquisitions of 20th Century Fox and MGM.
While the aims expressed by Matsushita and Sony are nearly identical -- to marry Japanese "hardware," including televisions and videocassette players, with American "software," including TV programs and movies, what Mr. Tanii on Monday called "two wheels of the same car" -- their approach to the political questions engendered by their purchases seemed markedly different.
At the time of the Sony acquisition its management, responding to American fears that an important cultural asset would be controlled from abroad, promised to "keep it totally as an American company" and not to interfere with the selection of movie topics or scripts. In recent weeks MCA has hired Washington lobbyists to make the similar claims about the Osaka-based Matsushita, which is a far more traditionally Japanese company.
But when Mr. Tanii, who has been president of Matsushita for four years and commanded its revitalization as a giant electronics concern, discussed the MCA purchase for the first time on Monday, he declined several opportunities to offer such assurances himself. He did say, however, that Matsushita was planning to keep the company's current management, including the chairman, Lew R. Wasserman. He said MCA's management had "common sense and fairness in using their powers of judgment."
The contrast between Sony's and Matsushita's statements was made more vivid when Mr. Tanii answered the same question that was posed a year ago to Akio Morita, Sony's chairman: Would his company be willing to produce a movie about the wartime role of the late Emperor Hirohito, one of the many taboo subjects in Japanese society?
 
Contrast to Sony's Pledge
 Last year Mr. Morita, without hesitation, said he would never interfere with such a production, though he said he could not guarantee that theaters in Japan would be willing to show it.
On Monday, a visibly agitated Mr. Tanii said, "I could never imagine such a case, so I cannot answer such a question." He also declined to answer when asked if Matsushita would permit the making of movies that some Japanese might perceive as "Japan bashing" because they were critical of Japanese society or economic practices.
"I never dreamed of hearing such a question," he said. But later he added: "Our new cooperation with MCA is part of broader U.S.-Japan cooperation as countries. So I believe that such a movie will not be produced." He suggested that it would be needless as the two countries resolved their increasing economic friction.
Mr. Tanii's answers seemed to reflect the depth of the differences between Matsushita and Sony, and between Japan's industrial center in Osaka, which Matsushita dominates, and the international and political center in Tokyo.
 
Politics and Culture
 Sony, half Matsushita's size, has always seemed highly Americanized and politically attuned. Created just after the end of World War II, it focused its energies on products for foreign markets, and its senior executives often seem at ease in both countries. By the time it bought CBS Records in 1988 and Columbia Pictures last year -- two moves that analysts believe spurred Matsushita to buy MCA -- it had already run a joint venture in record production for two decades.
Matsushita, on the other hand, has until very recently been a creature of Japan. Though it opened offices in the United States in 1953, until five years ago only 12 percent of its production was based outside of Japan. That figure is rising rapidly, and the purchase of MCA will easily be the company's largest single holding in the United States. Still, it is a deeply traditional Japanese company, and its executives concede that it will have a more difficult time adjusting to the ways of Hollywood.
Mr. Tanii seemed to acknowledge that when he said Monday that the company would rely on present management. But he also described plans to place Matsushita's own strong management controls on the company, making movies to strict budgets the way it makes electronics.
"In Matsushita, business plans are the core of our management," he said. "We will make firm business plans, and both Matsushita and MCA will agree on these plans. And I hope MCA's management staff with follow them."
Mr. Tanii also sidestepped another political issue that has dogged Matsushita for some time: Its observance of the Arab trade embargo of Israel. While the negotiations were under way with MCA, Jewish groups in the United States asked whether they would similarly stop distribution of their movies in Israel, an event that seems highly unlikely. Nonetheless, the boycott raises the question of whether a company willing to give in to Arab pressure to break off direct contacts with Israel would also be quick to squelch movies that might complicate its trade relations with other nations that are sensitive about how they are portrayed, such as China.

HEADLINE: Huge Rebuilding Effort Awaits Kiley's Successor

BYLINE: By RICHARD LEVINE

 BODY:
Robert R. Kiley's successor as head of the Metropolitan Transportation Authority will inherit a network of rail and bus lines and bridges and tunnels on which more than $16 billion has been or will be spent in one of the most expensive rebuilding efforts ever undertaken in the New York region.
But it is also a system that will require billions of dollars more to achieve Mr. Kiley's goal of "a state of good repair," and one that will likely have to cut service or raise fares or both in the meantime. 
 The New York City Transit Authority, by far the M.T.A.'s largest subsidiary, has already said it might be forced to lengthen the waiting times in the early morning or tack another 10 cents onto a fare that rose to $1.15 from $1 in January.
People still complain about the subways, of course, and probably always will. But, Richard T. Anderson, president of the Regional Plan Association, a research organization, said yesterday that when Mr. Kiley arrived at the authority's Madison Avenue headquarters seven years ago the subways were plagued by derailments and fires and covered with scrawls.
 
'A Running Start'
 "He inherited one series of problems and he dealt with them," Mr. Anderson said, adding that the next chairman would have to deal with other kinds of problems, including crime and homelessness.
Mr. Anderson credited Mr. Kiley with building a strong staff and said he had given his successor "a running start" on innovations like automatic fare collection. Mr. Anderson also said Mr. Kiley was beginning to address what Mr. Anderson said was a politically difficult but necessary task of replacing underused and antiquated parts of the system, like elevated subway lines, so that expansion vital to the region's economic growth could take place.
"Do we invest in rail access to the airports, or do we invest in greater utilitization of the 63d Street Tunnel or do we invest in another tunnel under the Hudson?" Mr. Anderson said. "They all involve multibillion dollar investments at a time when we don't have the money."
Taking over from Richard Ravitch, who conceived the sophisticated and innovative plan that rescued the foundering authority and financed its initial rebuilding effort, Mr. Kiley presided over a period in which 90 percent of the Transit Authority's subway cars and buses were either replaced o rebuilt, as were all the cars on the Long Island Rail Road and the Metro-North Commuter Railroad. Most of the 2,000 miles of rail and subway track have also been replaced.
 
20-Year Wish List
 Even the 63d Street Tunnel, a project envisioned in the 1960's was finally opened, although the new line it was to have connected to Manhattan never materialized, all but rendering the $800 million tunnel vestigial, at least for now.
But much work will remain even after the authority's second five-year rebuilding effort ends in December 1991. The agency has issued a 20-year wish list in which it estimated that it would need $37 billion to rehabilitate its transit lines fully and provide for normal replacement of equipment.
On top of that, it listed $13 billion in new projects, including building transit links to the airports, completing the mothballed Second Avenue subway line, constructing two Hudson River crossings and rehabilitating Grand Central Terminal.
"If anything, I think the M.T.A. has a more difficult row to hoe, not that getting a toll increase is easy," said Stanley Brezenoff, executive director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, which is seeking a $1 rise in tolls on its bridges and tunnels and a 25-cent increase in the fare on its PATH trains. "They're still playing catchup."
The State Legislature, in its special session next month, is expected to renew a surcharge on corporate income in transportation authority's service region that provides $300 million a year in operating money.
But next year the authority will be back in Albany, asking for about $12 billion for its next five-year rebuilding plan. Given the state's fiscal problems, the outlook for a favorable response seems uncertain at best. One argument that may be heard in the Statehouse is that, because of all the improvements already been made, the authority no longer needs large investments.
To add to its frustration, the agency has so far been unable to develop two choice parcels of land in Manhattan, the site of the New York City Coliseum and its west side rail yards.
Still, each weekday six million people rely on the M.T.A., which has a domain that stretches from Montauk, on the tip of Long Island, to Port Jervis in Orange County to Dover Plains in Dutchess County and comprises two commuter railroads, a suburban bus line, the New York City subway and bus system, seven bridges and two tunnels.
"The case for investing in transit is strong," said Gene Russianoff, staff lawyer for the Straphangers Campaign, a transit advocacy group. "When you're in a downturn, it's time to use the assets that you've got."
Mr. Brezenoff, who served on the authority's board when he was New York City's First Deputy Mayor and has remained a member, said, "Albany has always shown a lot of interest and concern for the M.T.A."
Then he added, "It's a question of what's affordable and what the competing priorities are."
