HEADLINE: Bluff in Dominica Yields Columbus's First Colony

BYLINE: By JOHN NOBLE WILFORD

DATELINE: EL CASTILLO, Dominican Republic

 BODY:
ON a low limestone bluff lapped by turquoise waters, archeologists are scraping away five centuries of decay and covering loam to get at the ruins of the first known European colony established in the Americas, the place Christopher Columbus in 1494 described as a "well-situated rock" and, with extravagant hopes, christened La Isabela.
Archeologists have identified the stone foundations of the settlement's main structures: a large storehouse, watch tower and sentry posts, a powder house, hospital, church and the house of Columbus. The cemetery has yielded skeletons with their hands crossed over their chests in Christian burial. 
 They have also found the quarry half a mile away where colonists cut stone for the fort, an oven where lead was melted for pottery glaze or molded into lombard shot used in cannons, and a kiln where colonists fired ceramics, the first Old World manufacturing in America. Buried in the mound at the site of Columbus's house were fragments of earthenware plates and bowls he probably ate from, roofing tiles that sheltered him and the rim of what very likely was a chamber pot. Columbus's very own?
Digging at the storehouse site, archeologists accidentally released thousands of evanescent drops of mercury that glittered in sunlight as they floated away. With the look of a triumphant alchemist on his face, Dr. Jose M. Cruxent, a Venezuelan archeologist, cupped a brownish glob of the thickly liquid mercury in his hand and uttered the intoxicating word, "Gold." This was some of the mercury the Spaniards had brought for separating from the mineral matrix the pure gold they had hoped to find in abundance.
For more than three years, Dr. Cruxent has directed archeologists and residents of this isolated fishing village in the first systematic exploration of La Isabela. Excavations are being expanded with the addition of archeology teams from the University of Florida under the leadership of Dr. Kathleen Deagan, curator of anthropology at the Florida Museum of Natural History at Gainesville.
They are trying to reconstruct the outlines of the first Spanish capital in the Indies in time for the 500th anniversary of Columbus's voyage of discovery in 1492. Their ultimate aim is to learn more about how the 1,500 sailors, soldiers and settlers accompanying Columbus on his second expedition in 1493 managed in their strange surroundings at La Isabela and why their colony collapsed and was abandoned in less than five years.
"Isabela is the gem of Columbus archeology," said Dr. Deagan. "It lets us look at how the Spaniards first began to cope with the American environment and began to develop many of their concepts and methods of colonization."
 
Origin of Slave Trade
 Here the Spaniards provided the New World with the first intentional introduction of plants and animals, the first masonry houses and the first European canals. Here, too, Columbus's lieutenants led troops across the plain and into the hills to plunder and pillage, ending any hope there might have been for peaceful relations between these reunited branches of humanity. From here, a year later, Columbus loaded 500 Taino Indians into four caravels and shipped them back to Spain, inaugurating the transatlantic slave trade.
Dr. Michael V. Gannon, a historian and director of the Institute for Early Contact Period Studies at the University of Florida, said: "The town provides the first example we have of a sustained contact between Europeans and Native Americans. It was the initial experience, if you will, of a process that changed the political, economic, social and religious world of the 16th century and thereafter."
The excavations at La Isabela highlight the growing role of archeology in the study of Columbus and the period of initial contact between European and American cultures. Except for the rare discovery of a new archive or an insight gleaned from improved translations, historians have learned just about all they can from written documents of the time and are looking to archeology to fill some of the gaps in understanding this crucial period.
Archeologists, for example, are aiding Columbus scholars by searching for physical evidence of exactly where he first landed in the New World, what his ships were like and where and how his men put down their first roots on American soil.
Dr. Charles A. Hoffman, an archeologist at Northern Arizona University, digs each year in the Bahamas at San Salvador Island, searching for confirming evidence that this, and not some other contending island, is where Columbus made his historic landfall on Oct. 12, 1492. He said he had found buried there more Spanish coins, beads, ceramics and a belt buckle from the Columbus period.
"If it wasn't Columbus who was here, it was somebody shortly thereafter," Dr. Hoffman said. "Perhaps Ponce de Leon."
Expeditions from Texas A & M University's Institute of Nautical Archeology and Ships of Discovery, an archeology foundation, are in the field this fall, trying to find and recover sunken remains of Columbus ships in Jamaica and Panama.
Other archeologists are methodically sifting the sand and dirt of Bahamian and Caribbean islands for traces of the Taino people, who were doomed by their encounter with European ambitions, weapons and diseases and became virtually extinct within a generation or two. Excavations directed by Dr. Deagan in the last decade identified the most likely site of the lost encampment in Haiti, La Navidad, where Columbus left 39 men on the first expedition when the Santa Maria went aground and had to be scuttled.
Dr. David Hurst Thomas, an anthropologist at the American Museum of Natural History and explorer of early Spanish missions, said the branch of study known as historical archeology, which explores periods that are already documented through written records, "has come of age in the last decade" as an independent approach to investigating and interpreting the past.
 
Reconstructing a Way of Life

 
 Dr. Deagan, a leading archeologist of the early Spanish contact period, said: "There's very little archeologists can say about individuals in the past, but we can reconstruct the world of those people, their materials and the way they ordered things. At La Isabela, we're certainly getting insights into the material trappings of the first colonists."
Trying to piece together the past at La Isabela is no picnic. The village of El Castillo has no electricity or running water and lies at the end of an unpaved road 20 bumpy miles from the nearest town with any amenities. At the thatch huts of its 40 or so families, the chickens, dogs and pigs have the run of things.
Dr. Deagan's stoical graduate students who work the dig sleep in a small concrete dormitory. Off to the side, on the way to the outhouse, are the guest quarters, a shed lodgers share with rats, land crabs and whatever else it is that scratches and scurries in the dark outside the mosquito net. Archeologists endure these hardships because the prize of La Isabela is an understanding of a pivotal moment in history when, scholars say, Columbus changed from successful mariner and discoverer to grasping imperialist and inept colonial administrator.
On his second voyage, when he arrived at La Navidad in November 1493 to rescue the men he had left behind, Columbus found their stockade burned to the ground and the Spaniards dead. He then sailed 70 miles east along the north coast of Hispaniola to the wooded promontory he chose as the place for his first permanent colony.
It was apparently a poor choice. The high ground overlooking the Atlantic must have appealed to him as a defensible position. But there was no fresh water readily available. The reef waters made a poor anchorage.
 
Shortages and Rebellion
 There was trouble from the start, from widespread illness, food shortages, dissension and rebellion against Columbus and also from the surrounding Tainos, who were provoked to hostility. The gold the newcomers coveted was scarce. In 1498, two years after Columbus returned to Spain to defend his management of the enterprise, La Isabela was abandoned and a new capital established across the island at Santo Domingo.
The derelict capital crumbled and later became the refuge of buccaneers and smugglers. In this century, nothing remained but a few exposed stones in a pasture.
In a misguided attempt in the early 1960's to restore La Isabela, the Dominican Government bulldozed the site, pushing much of the topsoil and uncounted artifacts into the sea. Less than a decade ago, Dominican archeologists began a more careful exploration and exhumed the skeletons of several dozen Spanish settlers. With the support of the Dominican Government, Dr. Cruxenta, a 79-year-old emeritus professor at the National University of Francisco de Miranda in Coro, Venezuela, came to live at the site in 1987 and undertook its most thoroughgoing study.
 
A New Theory
 While digging at La Isabela, Dr. Cruxent cast his eyes on the plain a mile away where the Rio Isabela empties into the ocean. There, at an area called Los Coles, he discovered signs that there was more to the colony than historians had imagined. He found Spanish artifacts and other evidence he interprets as marks where posts were planted for settlers' huts. He also dug up the bricks and other remains of a large kiln.
Standing at the kiln, Dr. Cruxent explained his theory that this, not the site on the promontory, was where the Spaniards first came ashore and built their colony. They lived at Los Coles, he said, while constructing the fortified settlement known as La Isabela, and most of the people probably continued to live here, with the other place serving as administrative center, fortress and residence for Columbus and the leaders.
"This place," Dr. Cruxent said of Los Coles, "had everything the settlers needed -- fresh water, good anchorage, a fertile plain for farming, clay for pottery. Columbus was not a foolish man, and logic says they came here first."
Other archeologists say it is too early to judge Dr. Cruxent's thesis. The Florida researchers, mapping the area last year, found evidence that the 200 settlers' huts mentioned in historical documents could have stood adjacent to the main La Isabela compound, across the road in the village of El Castillo. In excavations this fall, supported by the National Endowment for the Humanities, the Florida team has uncovered post marks and shards suggesting that at least some of the settlers did live close to the fortress.
Dr. Deagan said the large amount of material found at this new site indicates that more people lived there than at Los Coles. But she conceded that archeologists must now consider the possibility that three discrete settlements made up La Isabela: the fortress, the El Castillo site and also Los Coles, which was "ancillary in some capacity." Perhaps, she said, it was the colony's farming and manufacturing center, and its source of water.
 There is little doubt, however, about the physical layout of the main settlement. The houses were built around a central plaza. To the north was the largest building, the storehouse or barracks that measured some 120 feet long and 13 feet wide. Below the plaza was the church, its few remaining stones shaded now by a gnarled guayacan tree. Beyond are the cemetery, foundation outlines of some small houses and, at the far end, the Columbus house.
Like the other structures, the Columbus house had a foundation of thick limestone, side walls of packed dirt plastered on the inside with lime and a roof of timbers overlaid with dun-colored tiles. The front wall was solid blocks of limestone, and a stone lintel rests on the spot of the front door. The house was 13 feet wide and at least 20 feet long.
Dr. Cruxent found that an outer stone wall enclosed the house and yard, an example of archeology reinforcing historical accounts. Columbus must have felt the need of protection aginst his restive comrades within the fortress as much as against the Tainos without.
One afternoon, Dr. Deagan and two other archeologists, Dr. Elizabeth Reitz of the University of Georgia and Dr. Margaret Scarry of the University of Kentucky, sat at a rough-hewn table under a thatch-roof pavilion. This is the project's laboratory. And they were examining shards Dr. Cruxent had collected at the Columbus house.
 
Columbus's Chamber Pot?
 Most of the pieces were a pale salmon color. They were in the Moorish style of 15th-century Spain and had been shaped on a wheel. Most were coarse and unglazed, indicating they were made in the colony's own kilns. The fragments were recognizable as plates, bowls and a globular vessel. "Despite the bulldozer, there's a tremendous amount of stuff still here," Dr. Deagan said.
She fingered the grooved rim to what had been a large pot. "Moorish traits," she said. "Probably a chamber pot."
"You mean, that could be Columbus's chamber pot?" someone asked.
"We'll never get any closer to Columbus than this," someone else said.
They all laughed, but not at Christopher Columbus. They laughed at themselves, at their eagerness for proximity to the man who changed history.

HEADLINE: Rebel Economists Add Ecological Cost To Price of Progress

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL

 BODY:
COULD biologists, ecologists and other natural scientists have more to say about the economics of the environment than economists themselves?
Researchers calling themselves ecological economists are challenging traditional economics on its own turf, accusing economists of mismeasuring development, underestimating the intangible costs of pollution and ignoring society's responsibilities to future generations. 
 While many economists dismiss the ideas of their fellow scientists and critics as both unoriginal and loosely reasoned, these notions are finding a respectful audience in bastions of the establishment, particularly those that deal with the third world. The rebels' ideas have special application for countries that depend heavily on their natural resources.
The United States Agency for International Development and the conservative Pew Charitable Trusts underwrote a conference on ecological economics last May at the World Bank, which drew some 370 participants. And the Jessie Noyes Foundation in New York is supporting one of the ecological economists' favorite projects, an effort by the United Nations to incorporate environmental factors in the basic accounts used to measure national output.
 If the rebels succeed in the goal set by Robert Costanza, a biologist at the University of Maryland and a founder of the five-year-old International Society of Ecological Economists, they will persuade governments to give the "sustainability" of natural "life support systems" priority over conventionally measured economic growth. That could mean radical changes in policy, sharply reducing efforts to improve living standards through investments in land-intensive agriculture and resource extraction.
But succeed or fail, they will almost certainly force economists to face up to the issue that traditional analysis tends to undervalue environmental resources. And they may kindle public debate on some very uncomfortable questions, everything from the need for population control to the compensation owed by the present generation to those who will inherit radioactive reactor wastes in the year 3000.
Economic science long ago filed "environment" under N for natural resources. Breathable air, mountain scenery and edible marine life were categorized as inputs of production or goods for consumption, like machine tools or wheat or copper ore. The right decision on how much to use, explains Allen Kneese, an economist at Resources for the Future in Washington, depends on the "opportunity cost," what society would lose later by consuming the resource now.
Sophisticated resource economists -- among them, Mr. Kneese -- are sensitive to the practical limitations of the framework. Markets alone cannot be depended upon to weigh, say, the cost of damaging a marsh that serves as a breeding ground for birds.
But "conceptually, such values are not hard to incorporate," says Robert Repetto, an economist at the World Resources Institute in Washington. A determined economist could tote up the losses associated with filling in the wetland and compare them to the benefits of the shopping mall that would replace it. All too often, though, argues Dan Dudek, an economist at the Environmental Defense Fund in New York, "economists tend to ignore such intangible benefits, effectively setting their value at zero."
 
Accounting for Resources
 Another limitation is the economists' dependence on "discount rates" to account for the dimension of time in measurements of value. Discounting works fine for showing, say, that a ton of steel delivered today is worth more than the same ton delivered a year from now; the difference is an extra year's worth of use from the products made from the steel. When economies are working efficiently, the discount rate is equal to the interest rate; a year's delay in receiving that ton of steel would make it worth about 8 percent less.
But the choice of discount rates becomes increasingly problematic in the long time frame of environmental resources. A town council might decide it is worth sacrificing a grove of 300-year-old spruce to save $100,000 on road construction. But what of future town residents, whose pleasure in seeing very large, very old trees will not be considered?
Mr. Repetto notes that decisions affecting many generations can be even stickier. Carbon dioxide emissions may eventually force people to live indoors, protected from perpetual desert summer by air-conditioning. But what point would there be, asks Mr. Repetto, in discounting the loss of free access to the outdoors to future generations who have never known, and thus may never miss, the pleasure?
 
Calculating Depreciation
 Mr. Repetto and his colleagues at the World Resources Institute have been widely applauded for an attempt to correct a common distortion introduced by conventional economics practices: the failure to include natural resource depletion in national income accounts.
When economists measure a nation's net output, they subtract capital depreciation, the loss of machines, vehicles and buildings in the ongoing production process. But they rarely account for gains or losses of less tangible forms of capital, like human skills. Equally important, they neglect changes in reserves of natural resources, which are typically large (and negative) in poor countries.
The Repetto team used the example of Indonesia to show how important this omission can be in gauging national development. Losses from soil erosion, they calculated, reduce the net value of crop production by about 40 percent. And net losses of forest resources actually exceed timber harvests. Moreover, from 1980 to 1984 depletion of oil fields reduced the value of Indonesia's reserves base by about $10 billion annually, roughly 15 percent of national income.
In industrialized countries, where total output is much larger relative to natural resource depletion, similar extensions of conventional accounting methods have been used to calculate the impact of environmental degradation. Japan, Germany and France all have ambitious projects under way. And the United Nations Statistical Office is working on a general framework for environment and natural resource accounting.
 
Seeking a New Framework
 But ecological economists are not ready to permit researchers like Mr. Repetto, who accept the basic tenets of modern economic theory, to be the profession's toughest critics. What economics needs, they argue, is a whole new framework, one that fits economic production into ecological systems, rather than the other way around.
To Herman Daly at the World Bank, David Pearce at the University of London and Paul Ehrlich at Stanford University, the key goal is the achievement of "sustainability" -- adjusting economic activity so it does not damage the natural systems that underpin all functioning economies. Too many economists, they argue, simply assume that the biosphere can roll with any economic punch and bounce back for more.
But sustainability is proving hard to define and even harder to measure. One approach, now popular among ecological economists, is to calculate energy balances on the assumption that energy supplies represent the ultimate constraint on human activity. Mr. Costanza, for example, measures the amount of energy absorbed by swampland in order to compute the swamp's value.
But economists have been down this road before. Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen, an eccentric Rumanian economist who is now retired, spent a career mulling the relationship between economics and the laws of thermodynamics. The amount of energy in the universe is constant, but the amount available for work in a closed (economic) system is limited and constantly in decline.
Energy is indeed critical to human activity, agrees William Nordhaus, an economist at Yale University. But only a tiny fraction of the fuel consumed is actually used to sustain life. Technology for efficient processing of inputs -- among them energy -- is thus the real, long-term constraint. And ecological economists are not any better at predicting how these limits will impinge than garden-variety economists.
Mr. Daly, an economist by training who long ago defected to the enemy, approaches sustainability from another angle. He constructed an "index of sustainable economic welfare," adjusting conventionally measured output for environmental damage, resource depletion, the availability of leisure, even the degree of income equality. The index suggests that Americans' sustainable welfare peaked more than a decade ago, that "economic development" as contrasted with economic growth has stopped.
Mr. Nordhaus and the Nobel Prize-winning economist James Tobin put together a less ambitious "measurement of economic welfare" in the early 1970's in response to Marxists, whose claims to be creating more liveable societies were then taken seriously. The two economists found that the output omitted from regular income accounts -- like the fruits of household labor -- roughly offset the unmeasured costs, like pollution. Thus growth in economic welfare (in advanced industrialized economies, anyway) roughly tracked the growth in conventionally measured output.
At the heart of the sustainability question, Mr. Nordhaus argues, is the issue of endowing future generations with at least as much productive capacity as this generation inherited. Current levels of economic activity may deplete valuable resources and degrade ecological systems in irreversible ways, he acknowledges. But this generation will also pass on precious new technologies, cures for fatal diseases, incredibly efficient means for processing information, for example. Who is to judge whether our resource-stripping, environmentally stressful ways will more than offset this technological bounty?
 
Technological Pessimists
 Robert Hahn, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute, offers another way of parsing the question. Traditional economics implicitly assumes that as a practical matter no wasting asset, whether it be oil or the atmospheric ozone layer, is absolutely critical to economic growth or human welfare. Ecological economists, by constrast, are technological pessimists: if nations are not careful, they will make the planet uninhabitable.
No one really knows who is right. And to Mr. Hahn, the appropriate response to uncertainty is not agnosticism. It makes sense, he argues, to err on the side of caution where the penalty in output forgone is not high. The ecological economists thus score most tellingly, Mr. Hahn suggests, in their criticism of traditional economists' live-and-let-live approach to population issues.
Traditional economics, with its focus on the welfare of individuals, has generally been indifferent to the overall scale of economic activity. But for any given living standard, it is virtually certain that more people mean more damage to ecological systems, whose carrying capacities are poorly understood. Thus, it may matter critically whether the world's population stabilizes at, say, 10 billion rather than 20 billion.
It is not yet clear whether the ecologists will establish a beachhead in the economists' carefully constructed intellectual empire. What they have already demonstrated, though, is that environmental economics is too important to be left by default to economists.
 
The Real Picture?
 
When resource depletion is accounted for, Indonesia's growth rate falls from a spectacular 7.1 percent annual rate to a more modest 4 percent.
(source: World Resources Institute)

HEADLINE: Freer World Trade Falls Victim to Its Own Success

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 26

 BODY:
In seven rounds of global trade talks over four decades, tariff rates have been slashed by more than 75 percent. As imports became so much cheaper, trade across frontiers surged, and workers the world over produced a staggering amount of wealth.
The problem for the eighth round of trade talks is precisely that success: World trade has outgrown the rules written for it. The round is scheduled to end with a meeting in Brussels next week, if a bitter dispute on farm subsidies can be resolved. 
 In general, all countries share the belief that opening markets is good. The trouble comes in translating this into specifics. The United States, with its strength in high-technology industries, in such services as finance and construction, and in agriculture, is seeking liberalization in these sectors as a way to increase exports and redress a gaping trade deficit.
The European Community, with five times more farmers than the United States, is striving to protect them, but shares Washington's hopes for cutting barriers in high technology.
Japan, the country that probably gains most from free trade, is also resisting opening markets in agriculture, while still making its consumers pay seven times the world price for rice.
Developing countries, which are being asked to open their markets in high technology and services, are pressing for greater access in textiles and food in the markets of the industrial countries.
The world's trade rules were written 43 years ago and codified as the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, known as GATT. They define what fair trade is and set rules for settling disputes. They impose penalties for unfair pricing, unfair subsidies and other trade violations and encourage member nations to promote even more international trade.
The alternative to GATT is bilateral bashing, in which countries square off against others that they believe are trading unfairly. In such cases, one government will curb the imports of certain goods, as the United States did in 1987, when it accused Japan of failing to comply with an agreement on fair trade in semiconductors and imposed $300 million in sanctions on a variety of Japanese electronics shipments.
Some of those restrictions have been lifted, but $165 million of Japanese products, including laptop computers and power hand tools, are still assessed a 100 percent retaliatory duty when imported into the United States.
 
 Retaliation Countered
 Such steps invite counter-retaliation, and as nation after nation responds to grievances by erecting barriers, the danger grows that trade will dry up.
The extent to which GATT rules have been overtaken becomes clear by a brief look at the communications sector. When the agreement was reached, business documents were sent across the ocean by ship or propeller aircraft; they now travel over telephone lines from computers and facsimile machines, arriving in seconds. In 1950, a 10-minute telephone call between the United States and Britain cost $209; it now costs as little as $9.90. Such changes have made it far easier to conduct business internationally.
 
 Not Covered by GATT Rules
 As more and more companies began to cross national borders, competition rose, economies of scale were realized and prices of many goods and services were driven down. These changes mean that more than a third of today's worldwide trade of $4 trillion is not covered by GATT rules, or not covered adequately.
No rule, for example, forbids the piracy of Paul Simon cassette tapes, which sell for up to $10 in the United States. Unauthorized copies are sold in Thailand for $1.25. Popular software is also unprotected, and the Lotus 1-2-3 spreadsheet, which sells in the United States for more than $300, can be had in Malaysia, in pirated form, for $2.
As a result, American entrepreneurs in entertainment, software, pharmaceuticals and scores of other industries lose more than $60 billion a year from the theft and counterfeiting of their ideas -- their "intellectual property" -- according to the tally of the International Trade Commission, a United States Government agency.
Nor do the rules cover insurance, law, accounting, telecommunications, banking, advertising, tourism and other services that have become increasingly important categories of international trade.
 
 Change in Types of Trade
 When GATT was created, almost all world trade was in manufactured goods and raw materials. Today, services provide a fifth of world trade. For the United States, services are especially important. They provide 70 percent of its total output and 90 percent of all new American jobs.
"Competitive industry demands competitive services," said Carla A. Hills, the United States trade representative. "You can't design world-class chips without engineering, manufacture them without financing or export them without insurance."
The lack of GATT rules means that there is no international forum to hear complaints when the Japanese markets for architecture, engineering and construction are shut to the United States, or when German and French telecommunications monopolies bar competition from the United States or Canada.
On the other side, the Europeans and Japanese complain that the United States flouts international rules when it acts unilaterally to eliminate unfair trading practices by others. GATT panels have found a number of American practices illegal, including certain types of export subsidies and fees that Congress has imposed.
 
 Washington's Big Stick
 To fill the vacuum in intellectual property and services, Washington has been using its own big stick -- Section 301 of a trade law originally written in 1974 and amended in 1988, which empowers the President to retaliate against any unfair trade practices abroad.
In recent years, Washington singled out India, Brazil, Thailand, South Korea and others for either unfairly closing their markets in services or pirating intellectual property.
Now, hoping to win international agreement on an expanded set of rules, the Bush Administration has suspended its unilateral trade offensive. But should negotiations fail in the current talks -- known as the Uruguay Round because they began in that nation -- Washington's offensive is likely to be renewed.
Another big gap in GATT is agriculture. GATT's past efforts to deal with farm trade have largely been unsuccessful, leaving a wide variety of import barriers and subsidies, including export subsidies, that make farm trade the most protected and distorted of any sector in the trade panoply.
 
 $250 Billion Farm Cost
 The Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development has found that it cost taxpayers and consumers in the world's 24 richest countries $250 billion last year to shelter their farmers from competition. That is five and a half times what these countries give in official aid to developing nations.
Although the United States protects dairy, sugar, peanut, cotton, grain and other sectors through subsidies and import quotas, Washington has offered to drop most of its protection if the European Community and other nations will go along.
The community -- with 10 million farmers, five times the number in the United States -- has balked. Jacques Delors, president of the community's Executive Commission, complained earlier this month at a meeting of European foreign ministers that American negotiators were treating Europeans like "plague carriers."
But Japan, which is reluctant to open up its totally protected rice market, has also balked. Because of Japan's barriers against rice imports, rice in Tokyo costs seven times what it does in the United States.
Although Japan has been a great beneficiary of trade liberalization, the rice issue has kept it from taking a leading role in the round. The Japanese reluctance has been a source of considerable disappointment to Bush Administration officials.
 
 Expanding Farm Exports
 As great as the gulf is on the issue of farm subsidies, agriculture remains the linchpin in the current talks. Washington believes that a sharp cut in subsidies could expand world agricultural exports by $100 billion a year. Congressional agricultural committees are unlikely to support a trade agreement without such a provision.
And other farm exporters like Australia, Argentina, Brazil, Uruguay, Thailand, Malaysia and Indonesia could refuse to make concessions on services and intellectual property that are needed for a package to come together.
Many developing countries are also concerned about what is known as the multifiber arrangement, a comprehensive set of import quotas that have governed trade in textiles since the early 1960's.
Three times in the last five years, Congress has voted for legislation that would make textile trade more restrictive. Supporters could never quite get enough support to overcome Presidential vetoes, but the effort still shows that selling any agreement to Congress that opens trade in this sector, to the detriment of American textile producers, will not be easy.
 
 Role of the Soviets and China
 At last count 107 countries were participating in the GATT talks. But the two most populous countries, China and the Soviet Union, are not. While they have been granted observer status, neither is yet a member, because they have not had the open markets that are required for GATT membership. Both nations have taken steps to open markets, and China might well have been accepted this year had it not been for its crackdown on the democracy movement last year, with the international condemnation that followed.
When the ministers gather in Brussels next week, a final agreement may prove elusive, given the bitter arguments, particularly over agriculture.
Some trade officials in the United States and elsewhere speculate that talks could continue in January.
But deadlines loom. Congress gave the Administration until March 1 to submit an agreement for ratification, after which Congress has 90 days for consultation. Sometime after next June 1 it would vote on the pact.
 
 Fast-Track Authority
 For these talks, Congress authorized what is known as fast-track negotiating authority, which requires that lawmakers approve or reject the entire package as submitted, with no amendments permitted.
Without such ground rules, it would be impossible for an accord already balanced among 107 nations to withstand the pressures for changes from special interests in Washington.
The deadline can be delayed only if the President certifies that progress justifies continuation of the negotiations and neither the House nor the Senate disapproves.

PERSON: FARNSWORTH, CLYDE H

HEADLINE: Youths Lacking Special Skills Find Jobs Leading Nowhere

SERIES: Scraping By: Last of three articles

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: OCALA, Fla.

 BODY:
A decade or two ago, young Americans fresh out of high school could find a job in construction or sales, in a factory or an office, and make a good career of it. They could wait for raises to come along, buy a car, buy a house and take a trip once in a while.
But now, young people without skills, and in many cases even with them, cannot count on good wages and steady work. Many are stuck in dead-end jobs and find that employers can play them like yo-yos, recruiting them in good times like the 80's, firing them in lean times like now.
 
Low-Wage Economy
 As a fast-growing city of 49,000 in one of America's boom states, Ocala represents what much of the nation has become, just as the old manufacturing hubs of New England and the Midwest represent what it has been. Ocala's multilaned highways are lined with discount stores and fast-food restaurants that cater to the middle-income retirees and the people who work in other discount stores and fast-food shops. 
 A city better known for its surrounding horse farms, Ocala has transformed itself into a low-wage, low-skill, "K Mart economy," as a local official calls it. Indeed, the biggest building under construction here is not a factory or an office building but a $20 million, 36-acre K Mart warehouse and distribution center -- the biggest building in Florida, some here say.
The newest, least experienced workers in Ocala, as in most other Southern communities, are paid the $3.80 hourly minimum wage, which works out to $7,904 a year for a 40-hour week. The Census Bureau reports that a person living alone last year needed an income of $6,311 to stay out of poverty and that a three-person family needed $9,885.
A recent study by the Economic Policy Institute, a liberal research organization in Washington, reported that from 1979 to 1988, the incomes of households whose principal earner was under 25 years old fell 19 percent in terms of the food, housing or clothing they could buy. Incomes of families whose top earners were 25 to 34 also fell, by 5 percent, while older families gained a little ground or held their own.
Sue Sowell, who is 27 and divorced and has an 8-year-old son and another child due in December, stopped by the state-run employment service here on a recent weekday. She had just quit a clerical job because of an irresolvable conflict with a supervisor and said she could not find other work because of her pregnancy.
She is engaged to a man who earns $7 an hour at a factory that converts vans to recreational vehicles. But for a while this year his work hours were cut to 28 hours a week, and many of his co-workers have been laid off. Even working full time, she said, "he can't support us."
A 19-year-old man who agreed to be interviewed only if his name was withheld, came to the Job Service office to help a girlfriend apply for work. He had quit school in the 10th grade and spent a few months in prison for a crime he declined to describe. "I was growing up fast," he explained.
At 6:30 most mornings, the young man lines up at the daily labor pool, where builders pick people to do menial jobs. "I get $31 a day and change," he said. He said he gives little thought to the future. "I just go from day to day."
 
The Causes
The Job Market, The Economy
 Even unmarried people who have avoided the traps of crime or drugs and have just themselves to support say their only hope is more schooling. Even then, they cannot be sure of landing jobs that are secure and pay much better than those they have left. More than the young of the past, the youths here are an itinerant lot, who hustle from state to state in search of work.
A year ago, Jerry Pulley, 24, fled depressed Huntington, W.Va., for Ocala. As a high school senior in Huntington, Mr. Pulley had become a certified electrician, the kind of skill that labor experts say the young must learn to make a good living. He completed a year at Marshall University in Huntington, all he said he could afford, and then went into the Marines for three years.
In Ocala, he found a job with an electrical contractor that paid $6 an hour, about half the going rate nationally. He said he often worked 10 to 20 hours a week of overtime, enough to pay the $350 monthly rent on his trailer and $150 in weekly expenses with a little left over. But Florida has seen its construction business fizzle because of overbuilding in the 1980's, and Mr. Pulley's job fizzled with it. The last hired, he was the first laid off.
Mr. Pulley then took a similar job 60 miles southeast of Ocala in Orlando, was laid off, took another job there and was laid off again. Out of money, he gave up the trailer and moved in with a friend. After a month out of work, he landed another $6-an-hour electrician's job.
Mr. Pulley, an earnest, robust young man, is disillusioned. "I'm definitely going to have to get another occupation to fall back on," he said. "I've been thinking about the community college, but I don't have the money to go."
He added angrily: "You know what we don't have anymore? It's when the employee is faithful, the employer is faithful. What we have now is, if they need you they keep you, if they don't, you go.
"You know what else? They really don't care if you've been in the service any more. And what really stinks about it around here is you have a trade and still get paid almost the same as a man who knows nothing."
 
 Differences for Men and Women
 The labor market has treated people like Mr. Pulley and Ms. Sowell quite differently in recent years. Women are paid less than men -- 69 percent as much, on average -- so it is that much harder for a woman to provide for a family than it is for a man. But during the last decade, the gap has narrowed, and many women have gained some ground while many men have lost.
Allowing for inflation, the Bureau of Labor Statistics says the average earnings of all men declined 5.3 percent from 1979 to 1988, while for women they rose 5.9 percent. Young college graduates, both men and women who have been working up to five years, realized gains of about 11 percent from 1979 to 1987, the Economic Policy Institute says. But among high school graduates, young men lost 18 percent, while young women lost 3.5 percent.
"Young working people, particularly men, particularly non-college-graduate men, have taken a terrible beating in the job market over the last 20 years," said Richard B. Freeman, a labor economist at Harvard University.
Gary Burtless, an economist at the Brookings Institution, a research organization in Washington, added, "I am not very sanguine about the current generation of men, unless they do a great deal to educate themselves and get a lot better skills."
Labor economists see little reason to expect much improvement over the next decade for the current crop of young, blue-collar workers and their semiskilled white-collar cousins who work in shops and offices. They say those entering the work force late in the 1990's might fare better. There are fewer of them than when the baby boom workers were pouring in, so the law of supply and demand says they should get more pay.
Some economists add that the ways that the Federal budget deficit, inflation, interest rates and the dollar conspired to drive down wages in the 1980's could change in the 1990's and raise all wages.
But they also say these developments are unlikely to influence more fundamental and seemingly permanent shifts in the workplace. Chief among these are the decline of well-paid manufacturing jobs and the spread of mostly low-paying service and retailing jobs.
"A kid used to be able to drop out of school and get a job with his old man at General Motors," said Arnold Packer, executive director of a new Labor Department commission studying ways of improving the education and preparation of America's workers. "Now the old man is lucky if he can keep his job, and his kid has to start somewhere else at the minimum wage."
 
 Failures of the Schools
 Labor experts cite several other reasons for the plight of the young: the failure of the schools to teach the well-paid skills of the modern workplace; Federal taxation and spending policies in the 1980's that increased the gap in income between the few with high incomes and everyone else; and American industry's efforts to fight foreign competition, often by keeping wages down, rather than by investing in more efficient factories.
As for the young themselves, some people here say that high school and college students are often shortsighted, self-indulgent and overconfident that things will work out.
"They get out there on their own," said Joseph W. Schroeder, manager of the state's Job Service office here. "They want a job, their own place and a telephone. And they find they're unskilled. They haven't been taught to work. In dealing with young people the problem that employers tell me about constantly is that they can't read or write very well. They can't fill out a job application."
Gordon Malmgren, business agent for the local carpenters' union, one of the few unions in Ocala, said, "They're a lot dumber and burned out, half of them." He said people who want to be carpenters "do not have the arithmetic they should," adding, "It was beat into me."
 
The History
The Lure Of Low Wages
 Hellbent-for-growth cities like Ocala magnify the squeeze on the young. The city's population zoomed to 49,000 last year, from 23,000 in 1970. Marion County, which surrounds Ocala, grew to 198,000 over the same period, from 69,000.
The population grew in part because economic development authorities here, as in many Sunbelt towns, encouraged businesses to move here and exploit the area's low wages and non-union climate.
Now Ocala finds itself awash in low-wage businesses, mostly outposts of businesses based somewhere else, that cater to low-income workers and the 21 percent of the local population that is 65 and older.
A quarter of the labor force works in retailing, many part time, at K Mart and Wal-Mart discount stores, Winn-Dixie, Publix and Kash 'N Karry supermarkets, J. C. Penney, Sears and Montgomery Ward department stores, every known fast-food chain and at hundreds of small businesses offering bank accounts auto repairs and hair styling. Most of the workers earn $4 to $6 an hour.
At the same time, the city is largely barren of the entrepreneurial-minded executives and professionals who could use their money and expertise to create home-grown businesses and the foundations for community wealth and diversity.
The result i the "K Mart economy," as City Councilman Mike Finn calls it. "We've had a lot of growth," Mr. Finn said. "But it was just quantity, not quality. Everybody's selling to each other. Our growth was purely explosive. There was no substance to it."
In promoting the area's low wages, Ocala also lured a strain of what people here call "soft tech" manufacturing. Since no high-wage manufacturers nearby offer competition, companies attracted workers by offering just slightly better benefits and wages than the retailers. The companies pay most workers $5 to $8 an hour here, well below the national average of more than $10.
Companies like Martin Marietta Corporation of Bethesda, Md., have kept their engineering and high-paid, high-skilled work elsewhere and sent the semiskilled, hand assembly work to Ocala. Usually they teach the workers a single task, often at little cost to the company. Before hiring workers, Martin Marietta requires that they take 80 hours of instruction for the assembly jobs, at no pay.
"Factories here teach them one skill, and that's all they teach them," said H. C. Jensen, an economic consultant in Ocala. "The thing we do not have here are technically skilled, trained employees like San Jose, Calif."
When demand for the product a company assembles here falls, so can the factory, shattering its employees' sense of job security.
In recent months, Martin Marietta has laid off about 400 of its 1,600 workers. "For our area, Martin Marietta was the job to shop for," a state job counselor said. "You could feel you had a job for life. That's gone."
The best-paid group here are the 600 Federal employees, including postal and office workers, who earn an average of $24,000, or more than $11 an hour. But the Government, facing its own budget problems, is doing little hiring now.
 
The Effects
High Hopes, But Little Else
 On a typical day last month, the Job Service office posted 35 offers for 101 workers. The best offer, for a librarian, paid $9.59 an hour or $20,000 a year, but it required a master's degree and went unfilled for weeks.
The great majority of the jobs paid much less. Among them were 26 that paid the minimum wage of $3.80, and 11 of those were part time. The average for all the jobs was $5.04.
One job seeker was Charles Cox. Mr. Cox and his wife, Teri, left high school in the 11th grade, have been married nine years and have two children, ages 7 and 4. They had considered themselves fairly well off. "We just went to Disney," Mrs. Cox said. "We pay our bills. We do what everybody else does."
She earns $4 an hour working 35 hours a week as a nursery school teacher. Mr. Cox earned $6.40 an hour operating heavy equipment at a limestone mine until he was laid off in October. He has no other skill.
"We may have to relocate him to Miami," Mrs. Cox said. There he could work for his father, who owns a small business operating construction equipment.
Stacey Smith, 19, also visited the Job Service. She has decided that her high school diploma is not enough and that she needs more schooling. She is single and has no children. A bookkeeper and sales assistant for a homebuilder, she earns $225 a week, or about $5.60 an hour.
"I'm making it," Ms. Smith said, "but I don't have any money to splurge. There isn't anywhere to go with this job. You have to have college degrees and experience. I plan to go back to college. I want to be a child psychologist."
Chris Griffin, 23, was another job seeker. He said he had been lured to Florida from New York State with the promise of sun, fun and job opportunities. His mother had moved earlier to Ocala, so he moved in with her. He got a $5.72-an-hour job as a stock clerk at Martin Marietta, but was laid off in October.
"It was a nowhere job anyway," Mr. Griffin said. After a futile search for work that would pay at least $4.25 an hour, he enrolled in the local Central Florida Community College. He is taking a two-year course in police work, studying fingerprinting and other crime laboratory skills, which he expects to lead to a better-paying job with an urban police department.
For 26 weeks, Mr. Griffin said, he will receive unemployment benefits of $150 a week, and the Federal Government, through the Job Training Partnership Act, is paying for his books and his courses for a year. After his benefits and the grant run out, he said he will try to work part time.
The college prepares about 5,000 of its 10,000 students to transfer to universities, although only about half of the 5,000 do, and the rest for jobs that require skills: accounting, hotel management, computer services and a variety of medical specialities.
The president of the college, William Campion, said the average age of the job-training students is 29 and of women studying nursing 34. "They're here to increase their wage-earning skills," he said. "They're tired of being in that vast army of retail clerks, compounded by the fact that their creditors are pounding at the door."
The younger these workers, the greater their expectations of high incomes and a better life. Ms. Sowell, for example, said she fully expects to own a home. "I just can't say when," she said.
But those who are just a few years older are more apprehensive. Gary Colon, 33, brought his wife, 10-year-old daughter and 2-year-old son to town in their home, a trailer, to look for work. Mr. Colon graduated from high school in Queens, and like Mr. Pulley he spent a year in college and later became an electrician. He, too, was laid off after working for a year in Orlando, where he earned $8.50 an hour. And that was after being laid off after three years in Texas.
 
 Don't Want to Be Laid Off'
 "Maybe I'm doing the wrong things," Mr. Colon said. "I'm not really looking for a higher wage, not that I don't want it. I just don't want to be laid off next month and then the next month. I want security for my family and health benefits."
He contrasted his experience with that of his father, who stopped school in the fourth grade but retired with a good pension after a long career as a laborer with an electrical power utility in Puerto Rico. He said his mother never worked. "My father would have gotten mad if she ever even thought of it," he said. "But I want my wife to work, at least part time."
In some communities, workers find some compensation for low wages in low prices, but that has not been the experience of workers who moved here. Ocala's cost of living is about the same as the national average, while the wages are well below.
Ms. Sowell, an articulate woman, has been caught in Ocala's wage and price squeeze. She grew up in De Soto County, south of here, and finished high school there. "I was prepared to go to college," she said, "but I didn't have the money. I met a man, fell in love and decided to have a family instead."
After her boy, Wade, was born, she and her husband broke up, and she and another man moved to Grand Rapids, Mich., where they planned to marry. That relationship failed, too, but she said she was lucky and found a job in a blueprint supply shop there. She started at $6 an hour and in two years was earning $8. With that, she said she was easily able to pay a $361 monthly rent on a two-bedroom, two-bath townhouse and have a telephone, too.
"That was the best job I ever had in the whole world," she said, "and it was a great place to live. But I missed my family in Florida, so I came back." She doubts she could get back her old job in Michigan or any other there. Jobs in Michigan pay more, but they are disappearing. The Bureau of Labor Statistics reports that from October last year through October this year, Michigan lost 95,000 jobs, while Florida gained 190,000.
Here, Ms. Sowell and her fiance live in a mobile home, the best they could afford. "There's no heat, it's falling apart and we pay $75 a week," she said.
She said they cannot afford a telephone or insurance on their cars. And since leaving her job, she said she will have to postpone the final, $94.22 monthly payment on the 11-year-old Ford LTD, with a crumpled rear fender, that she bought a year ago.
A decade ago, about 20 million people worked in factories, which paid high wages, and 15 million worked in stores, which paid much less. Over the decade, the number of retail workers, whether fast-food cashiers or discount store clerks, has climbed to 20 million, surpassing the 19 million who now have factory jobs. At the same time, factory wages have risen but cannot buy as much now as they did in 1980.
 
The Needs
Teaching Skills That Pay More
 A widespread consensus now exists, within the Bush Administration and Congress, in industry and among educators, that vast changes are needed to halt the spread of low-wage, low-skill jobs.
A score of studies have concluded that school curriculums need overhauling to prepare people for high-quality, better-paid jobs, both for the benefit of the young and for the economy. They urge better basic education, better preparation in the requirements of the workplace, and they suggest that vocational schools become more geared to the skills required in the next decades.
"The bank teller who just took deposits has been eliminated by the money machine," Mr. Packer of the Labor Department said. "Now he has to advise me on three types of money market deposits and explain to me why I want this one rather than that one."
By luck or foresight, some people in Ocala have learned the skills that the academics say the economy needs. But their skills serve only to provide a modest income -- and then only if both spouses work.
James P. Egert, 29, and his wife, Dorothy, 26, moved here from New Orleans with their 2-year-old son last summer. "New Orleans has extremely poor schools and extreme racial problems," said Mr. Egert, who is white. "I don't want my son growing up in that. I'll drive a two-year-old car and make $5 an hour so my son can grow up in a town that's nice."
Mr. Egert said he finished high school in Michigan and learned a skilled, sought-after trade, lithography, on the job at a print shop.
As a lithographer for four years in New Orleans, Mr. Egert said his pay rose from $7.50 an hour to $10. After a three-month search in Ocala, he found a printing shop that would take him at only $7 an hour. Mrs. Egert took a pay cut, too. She is a medical secretary, an especially sought-after skill in Florida because of the health care needs of the large retired population. Because of the city's prevailing low-wage level, however, her job at the Ocala hospital pays $7.18 an hour, $2.20 less than she earned in New Orleans.
Mr. Egert said he is struck by the contrast between his own career and his father's. His father worked as a safety engineer at the Dow Chemical Company while his mother stayed home, and he took early retirement with a comfortable pension. The senior Egerts sold their home in Coleman, Mich. They moved to the Florida Keys, where they have established the kind of life that James Egert calls his ideal.
"They live on a sailboat," he said.

HEADLINE: In Manhattan, Vacant Stores Are a Sign of Hard Times

BYLINE: By RICHARD LEVINE

 BODY:
There are signs of economic distress all over Manhattan these days, and many of them say "Space Available" or "Store for Rent."
Vacant shops pock lower Fifth Avenue, parts of the Financial District and Broadway on the Upper West Side, where some merchants have gone in and out of business in a matter of months. Even on the burnished stretch of Madison Avenue from 57th Street to 96th Street that is frequented by a platinum-carded clientele, about 30 stores are vacant. 
 Huberth & Peters Inc., a New York Cityreal-estate company, found that 9.75 percent of the retail space in Manhattan was vacant in September, up nearly half a percentage point since June. Michael W. Hirschfeld, the company's managing director, expects the rate to be above 10 percent by year's end. The last time that happened, he said, was in the 1970's.
 
Drop in Rents
 On the Upper East Side and the Upper West Side, the rate has already exceeded 10 percent. And in parts of the Financial District, the rate is 20 percent.
Huberth & Peters also found that it now takes an average of almost six months tolease an empty store in Manhattan, twice as long as it did a few years ago.
Another New York real-estate company specializing in store leasing, Garrick-Aug Associates, reported that at midyear 7.2 million square feet of retail space was available in Manhattan. Although this is partly the result of new development, Charles M. Aug, the company's president, said there are now 2 million or 3 million more square feet available than there would be if times were prosperous.
This surplus comes despite a drop of about 20 percent in the average asking price for retail-store rents in Manhattan, from more than $65 a square foot in 1988,according to the company. Mr. Aug said in some areas, including Madison Avenue, where prices had soared as high as $350 a square foot, rents had dropped by as much as 40 percent.
"Manhattan has been the leader in escalation," he said, "and it is the leader in deflation."
At 87th and Broadway, the hand-painted sign of Lillian's Dress Shop still hangs above the storefront even though the store closed a few months ago. Avital D'Lugoff, who shopped there for years, said she remembered the owner, an aspiring singer. "He knew my husband was in show business and he'd always sing opera when I came in," she recalled. "He had a beautiful voice."
She listed a string of neighborhood businesses that have closed recently, including a kosher butcher, a card shop and a "lovely little embroidery shop."
 
Some Areas Do Well
 "With so many stores disappearing," she said, "you have to walk forever, and still you're lucky to find what you need."
The vacancy problem looks somewhat worse than it is, real-estate consultants say, since some empty stores are actually awaiting renovation by new tenants or are in buildings earmarked for redevelopment.
And vacancies are still scarce in many parts of the city, particularly shopping strips in boroughs other than Manhattan, said David A. Smith, Commissioner of the New York City Office of Business Development.
Still, the increase in empty stores along some of the city's busiest streets only compounds the problems of an already troubled real-estate industry. And coming after a shakeout in which several large department stores closed, and just as the crucial holiday shopping season is beginning, the empty stores are silent testimony to a weakening economy and, in particular, sagging retail sales.
"Those vacant stores are telltale signs of serious problems," said Samuel M. Ehrenhalt, regional commissioner of the Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics. Consumers, he said, "are cutting back even when they don't have to."
 
Big Chains Move In
 New York would have even more vacant stores had not some national and foreign retailers, which had largely avoided the city, arrived. National chains now occupy 1.5 million square feet, Mr. Hirshfeld said, compared to less than 300,000 square feet 15 years ago.
Toys "R" Us has opened its first Manhattan store in Herald Square. A rival, Kiddie City, has new stores in Manhattan, too. HMV Records, a British company, has a new store on Broadway at 72d Street, a few blocks north of Tower Records, which is itself opening a book store. HMV has also opened a large store on Lexington Avenue at 86th Street.
Lechters, the kitchen-goods chain, the Bombay Company, which sells reproductions of English antiques, and Merry-Go-Round, featuring trendy clothing, have joined the Gap, Banana Republic and other national retailers in Manhattan.
With other stores in more prosperous parts of the country, the chains can afford to wait for the local economy to bounce back. And in the current real-estate market, they are able to negotiate good deals on Manhattan rents.
 
'Opportunities Out There'
 "There are opportunities out there for guys who want to take them," Mr. Aug said.
Indeed, last week Barneys New York said it planned to open a huge store in an office building on Madison Avenue and 60th Street, a venture that would draw more shoppers -- and doubtless more merchants -- to at least that portion of the avenue. Executives indicated they were not immediately concerned about the current retail slump and said were pursuing long-term goals.
But many small "mom and pop" stores struggling with diminishing profits will have a hard time affording a long-term strategy. And while rents have dropped from the levels of a few years ago, when some merchants were clamoring for commercial rent control, they are often geometrically higher than they were when when many businesses signed their current leases.
In the days when prices were $8 to $10 a square foot on Amsterdam Avenue, retailers could afford to make a mistake, said C. Bradley Mendelson, managing director of the Edward S. Gordon Company, a New York-based real-estate company. "At 50 bucks a foot, it's an expensive lesson," he said.
Even if entrepreneurs are willing to take the risk, banks are more hesistant to finance new businesses, and although landlords are anxious for tenants, they also face the prospect of getting even less for their property next year if a new tenant leaves. That has made some of them more selective, real estate consultants say.
Taxes are another growing problem for some city merchants. "In the last 10 years, my real-estate taxes have increased from $300 to $1,800 a month," said the owner of a Broadway bookstore, who attributed some of the vacancies to a rise in taxes that has been steeper for commercial property than residences.
 
More Homogeneity
 The stresses on Manhattan retailing may to lead to a restructuring in which local, one-of-a-kind merchants continue to be replaced by branches of chains, creating a more homogeneous marketplace that reflects national trends at the expense of neighborhood sensibilities and needs.
On Broadway at 89th Street, the impact vacant stores can have on a neighborhood is becoming apparent. "Flowers are often an impulse buy," said Randy Hirschorn, an employee at Harry's Florist. "We get a lot of business from people who are shopping for other things. With all these stores closing, it's going to get tougher."
The Huberth & Peters survey found that the market for retail space in midtown Manhattan was still strong, with a vacancy rate of just 4.65 percent. Near the intersecton of Fifth Avenue and 57th Street, one of the most desirable locations in the world, Galleries Lafayette, the French department store chain, recently acquired the lease of Bonwit Teller, and Bergdorf Goodman opened a men's store -- although only steps away is a store for rent at 9 West 57th Street.
But on the Upper East Side and the Upper West Side, the survey found vacancy rates of 12.6 percent and 14 percent, respectively. And in the Financial District, the rate was 20 percent -- "the kind of market, " Mr. Hirschfeld said, "that could take years to recover from."
Mr. Mendelson, who said he did not believe that more stores were becoming vacant but rather that empty stores were staying on the market longer, was skeptical about the prospects of several new shopping areas staked out in the boom of the 80's, including West Broadway in Soho and lower Fifth Avenue.
Although retailers have been opening stores near Union Square Park and West 14th Street is relatively stable, East 14th Street has sections where there are almost more empty stores than occupied ones.
"Going east on 14th, you really have some large gaps and holes in retail businesses," said Robert W. Walsh, executive director of the 14th Street-Union Square Local Developmnt Corporation. "Some people are talking about major drop-offs in business -- 40 to 50 percent. They're hurting. They're suffering."

HEADLINE: Police Rebut Daily News On Violence

BYLINE: By DAVID E. PITT

 BODY:
Senior New York City police officials yesterday rebutted accusations by The Daily News that the newspaper's nine striking unions were engaged in a criminal conspiracy to disrupt circulation through violence and intimidation.
'To date, our investigations show that the acts of of violence and other types of criminal behavior are the acts of individuals and are not part of a coordinated effort," said the Chief of Department, Robert J. Johnston, testifying at a New York State legislative hearing on strike-related violence. 
 Chief Johnston, who appeared at the hearing on behalf of Police Commissioner Lee P. Brown, also questioned allegations made yesterday by The News's publisher, James Hoge, that there had been 700 serious incidents of "harassment, assault and damges to persons and property" since the bitter strike began on Oct. 25.
 
229 Serious Incidents
 The chief said the Police Department knew of only 229 serious incidents, including 107 arrests, 75 of which were of strikers.
The testimony by Chief Johnston -- who was accompanied by Chief of Detectives Joseph R. Borelli, Chief of Inspectional Services Daniel F. Sullivan and Chief of Patrol David W. Scott -- came a short time after Mr. Hoge had angrily reiterated his accusations of a violent conspiracy by union leaders.
The paper's publisher was the lead-off witness at the daylong hearing in Manhattan on a legislation proposed by Assemblyman Frank J. Barbaro, Democrat of Brooklyn, that would outlaw the use of professional strike-breakers and replacement workers.
Although Mr. Hoge had given a series of interviews recently on local television news programs, his stormy 90-minute appearance yesterday was the first time he had been subjected to what amounted to a virtual cross-examination on his company's conduct during the strike and in the 10 months of fruitless contract talks that preceded it. Seated at long witness table in the elegant, burgundy-draped hearing room of the City Bar Association's hearing room on West 44th Street, Mr. Hoge was peppered with sharp and sometimes hostile questions from the legislators and their aides.
George E. McDonald, head of the Allied Printing Trades Council, who testified later on behalf on the striking unions, asserted that yesterday was the first time that he or any of the union leaders had been in the same room with Mr. Hoge in two years.
 
Free Enterprise
 Mr. Hoge, in an impassioned statement, said union-led violence and threats were nothing less than a direct attack on the American principle of free enterprise.
"Today it is The Daily News," he said, rapping the witness table with his knuckles. "Tomorrow it could be any other newspaper or magazine engaged in a labor dispute, or any delicatessen and supermarket, or any drugstore. What is involved here is a choice between the rule of law and the rule of force."
Mr. Hoge, promising he would produce documented proof of a conspiracy, told the panel of state legislators -- including Assemblyman Barbaro and Senator James L. Lack, Republican of Suffolk County -- that such a wave of assaults and harassment "could not have been as swiftly mounted as it was and could not be as sustained as it is without centralized planning and production."
But Chief Borelli told the hearing that in all of the cases of strike-related criminality the police had investigated, "each one seems to have involved someone getting upset and doing what they did."
"We don't see a conspiracy, at least not just now," he said in response to questions from legislators.
 
$4 Million in Overtime
 Jeremy Travis, the criminal-justice coordinator for Mayor David N. Dinkins, added that in the 32 days since the strike began, the Police Department had spent $6.7 million on strike-related duty, of which $4 million was in overtime alone.
Some of the legislators at the hearing, like Assemblyman Barbaro, who had expressed public support for the strikers at a union-sponsored breakfast meeting earlier this month, suggested that the root of the violence might have been in The News's decision to hire permanent replacements for the strikers.
But Mr. Hoge, saying the 71-year-old newspaper's 1990 losses would rise as much as $35 million, to $200 million, as a result of the strike, strongly defended his company's legal right to hire permanent replacements.
He also denounced Mr. Barbaro's bill as "a dangerous and unworthy capitulation to criminality" and told reporters after the hearing that he resented what seemed to him to have been an accusatory line of questioning by the legislators.
 
'Entirely Legal'
 "Hiring replacement workers is an entirely legal act, as well as an economically necessary act if this country's industries are to remain efficient and competitive," Mr. Hoge said. "To say that The Daily News or any employer somehow causes unlawful acts of violence by engaging in purely lawful acts is turn on its head our whole sense of right and wrong, of justice and injustice."
At one point, Assemblyman Barbaro asked Chief Johnston whether there was any truth to union accusations that The News's chief security consultant, Richard J. Koehler -- a former City Correction Commissioner and senior police official -- had "personally contacted police field commanders and asked them to limit pickets from exercising lawful strike activity."
Chief Johnston said he was aware only of letters Mr. Koehler had sent to the department complaining of strike-related criminality. But he promised to check with borough and precinct-level commanders to see whether there was evidence of such contacts.
 
Security Assistance
 The chief added that last Jan. 25 -- three months before the unions' contracts expired and nine months before the strike erupted -- Mr. Hoge, Mr. Koehler and other management officials had met with high-ranking police officials, saying they expected a strike and would need security assistance.
The chief said the the department arranged for its intelligence division and other departments to establish a liaison with The News's own security staff.
At another point in the hearing, Assemblyman Barbaro questioned Mr. Hoge closely about The News's hiring of at least four out-of-state security agencies during the strike, which the unions have accused of providing provocateurs. Mr. Hoge said their role had been completely legitimate and promised to supply evidence of this.
 
'Duped' by The News
 The panel heard testimony on this issue from Jim McCully of Clarksville, Tenn., who said he had been "duped" by The Daily News into accepting a security job in which his main duty was to act as "bait" for strikers, then videotape any evidence. A similar account was given by Al Kaminsky, an independent trucker.
During the hearing, which which lasted from 10 A.M. to nearly 5 P.M., two other witnesses -- Thomas F. Hartnett, the State Labor Commissioner, and Eric J. Schmertz, Mayor Dinkins's Commissioner of Labor Relations, sharply criticized The News's refusal to accept repeated offers of mediation by government panels.
Mr. Hoge, asked about this earlier, said The News was determined to continue its use of the services of a Federal mediator in some of the continuing contract talks with the union.
Pressed by Mr. Barbaro over why he would not accept offers of help from Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, Mayor Dinkins or even John Cardinal O'Connor, head of the New York Roman Catholic Archdiocese, Mr. Hoge said he was reluctant to engage the services of any mediator with "a political agenda."
"The Cardinal has a political agenda?" Mr. Barbaro asked. "I take umbrage at that as a Catholic. Please don't tell me that my Cardinal has political aims."
"I'm not," Mr. Hoge replied.

HEADLINE: Dow Edges Up by 5.94 Points, to 2533.17

BYLINE: By DANIEL F. CUFF

 BODY:
The stock market, following the bond market's lead, reversed its early losses around noon yesterday and closed higher.
The Dow Jones industrial average, which had lost as much as 30 points, crossed into the plus column in the last half-hour of trading, ending at 2,533.17, up 5.94 points. 
 Higher oil prices and heightened Middle East tensions weighed on stocks in the morning. "Bonds generally ignored the higher crude prices," said Dennis E. Jarrett, market analyst at Kidder, Peabody, "and the equity and bond markets then moved in lock step."
The recovery, in light volume, was helped as oil prices retreated later in the day. The oil price on the New York Mercantile Exchange for January delivery ended up $1.05 after having climbed more than $2 earlier in the day as the United States pressed for a United Nations resolution setting a deadline for Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait.
"The bond and stock markets were quite happy to see oil come back down," said Peter J. Canelo, an investment strategist at Bear, Stearns & Company. "But it was still higher than when it started the day. There's not really anything you can point to as being a decisive move by any means."
Financial and growth stocks showed strength in the late move upward. "The market is acting as if the interest rate environement will continue to improve," Mr. Canelo said, "and it's not going to worry too much about oil."
On the Big Board, advancing issues totaled 785, almost matched by decliners, at 728. Trading was light at 131.5 million shares, compared with Friday's extremely light volume of 63.4 million shares. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks gained 1.41, to 316.51.
Among active stocks in the financial sector, Citicorp gained 3/4, to 13 1/8; American Express added 1/2, to 20 1/4, and Federal National Mortgage, or Fannie Mae, gained 1/2, to 30 1/8. Wells Fargo gained 2 1/4, to 54 1/4, and Security Pacific tacked on 1 1/8, to 22 3/8.
MCA was the volume leader, slipping 1/4, to 65 1/8, after the announcement of its merger agreement with Matsushita. American depository shares of the Japanese company gained 2 1/4, to 128 3/4.
 
Stocks Fall in Tokyo
TOKYO, Tuesday, Nov. 27 (REUTERS) -- Japanese stock prices closed lower today. The Nikkei index of 225 issues fell 139.35 points, or 0.59 percent, to 23,623.51. About 250 million shares were traded. On Monday, the Nikkei gained 362.58 points.
