HEADLINE: Talking Business with Ammerman of Mattel;
Optimistic View Of Toy Industry

BYLINE: By Richard W. Stevenson

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
John W. Ammerman has been chairman and chief executive of Mattel Inc. for nearly four years after serving seven years as head of the company's international operations. He is given credit by analysts with turning around the company's once-flagging fortunes, largely by reinvigorating sales of Mattel's existing products, including the Barbie doll, one of the most successful toy lines of all time. 
 Despite a generally weak retail environment and lackluster results at other toy companies, Mattel's performance this year has been strong, and the company posted record earnings for the third quarter. Mr. Ammerman, who is 58 years old, was interviewed yesterday at the company's headquarters in Hawthorne, Calif.
Q. From the perspective of a toy maker, how is this Christmas retail season shaping up, and what does it tell you about the economy?
A. From Mattel's perspective, this is going to be a very good Christmas. You have to understand that 50 percent of our business or more is outside the United States, and of that total of international business the vast majority is in Europe, where the economy is strong. In the United States, we're also going to have a good Christmas because we have the right products and sound marketing. And as we look at history, toys have done well in periods when the economy is not as strong as you'd like it to be.
Q. Have you noticed any changes in the types of toys being purchased this year? Is there evidence of belt-tightening as parents shop for their children?
A. It's a little early to tell. About half of all toys are sold in the fourth quarter. We'll have to wait until the Christmas season is over to evaluate whether higher-priced or lower-priced toys did comparatively better. One would anticipate that lower-priced toys would do somewhat better in these economic times in the United States.
Q. What is the general outlook for growth in the toy industry?
A. The future of the industry is good. Clearly, if one looks at the amount of money that is spent on leisure-time activities in the United States, it's growing at a rapid rate, so if we can produce products that appeal to children and parents, the outlook is positive. Second, there is a mini baby boom developing, and that bodes well for the 1990's.
It's difficult to look at a particular year and say whether the industry is healthy or not. The levels of inventory that are available in retail stores play a part. If the trade is careful about asset management, they will hold inventories down. Then the results for manufacturers may not be so good, and I think we're seeing that in 1990. Q. Do you see an industry consolidation?
A. Consolidation is clearly occurring on the retail side. Maybe it will occur on the manufacturing side. It's hard to forecast.
On the retail side, consolidation means we have to be more aggressive, and one way we've done that is we have key account management teams that work with major retailers to help them meet their objectives, whether those objectives are inventory turnover or cash flow or return on investment. We have found that our business has prospered with those accounts.
As far as acquisitions go, the issue that we are interested in is core products, core products being those that will endure year after year. Those are the ones we'd be interested in. We're not interested in a company whose volume might be strong today, but that might see its volume disappear a couple of years into the future.
Q. A lot of toy companies invest most of their time and money in trying to develop huge new mega-hit toys, the next Cabbage Patch dolls. You've avoided that strategy. Why?
A. Our basic thrust is our core products -- Barbie, Hot Wheels, our Disney line, large dolls. In the third quarter, our core products accounted for 78 percent of our volume. That gives us the stability to withstand seasonal or cyclical economic trends that might affect others in the industry.
That's not to say we wouldn't be happy to find ourselves with something that does well in a short period of time. In just two years, we're up to a volume of $150 million this year on Disney toys. Incremental volume on Barbie has grown $270 million in three years, to $700 million. That's the kind o mega-hit we're interested in.

HEADLINE: Soviets Seek Grain Credits

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 26

 BODY:
Three senior Soviet officials met United States Government representatives today in Washington to lobby for loans to enable Moscow to buy much-needed American grain and meat, Government and trade officials said today.
The Soviet officials also briefed the Bush Administration on the acute food shortage in the Soviet Union and discussed the outlook for future food purchases, they added.
But Agriculture Secretary Clayton K. Yeutter dealt the Soviet mission a setback when he told reporters that Moscow must liberalize its emigration laws to be considered for such loans. He said United States money for food-aid programs handled by his department was running low and added that no official Soviet request had been made for emergency aid.
The visiting Soviet officials are the chairman of the state commission on food procurement, L .M. Timofhishin; the deputy minister for foreign trade and economic relations, Yuri Chumakov, and the head of the key Soviet grain-importing agency, Exportkhleb, Oleg Klimov.

THE DEAL FOR MCA
MCA agreed to be acquired by Matsushita. The deal, valued at $6.13 billion in cash, will be the largest purchase ever of an American company by a Japanese company. [Page D1.]

[D6.] The MCA deal is likely to change Hollywood, and it almost certainly will not be the last transaction to do so. [D7.]
MCA has not strayed far from its core business of movies and television. [D7.] An MCA subsidiary, G. P. Putnam's Sons, has seen its revenues surge eightfold since it was acquired in 1975. [D7.]
Only a few small investment firms will be dividing up $30 million to $100 million in fees from the MCA acquisition. [D7.]
Just what is a television station worth in the New York market? That question will soon be answered when WWOR-TV is spun off by MCA. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D8.]

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 3; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 2027 words

HEADLINE: THE DEAL FOR MCA;
$6.13 Billion MCA Sale to Japanese

BYLINE: By GERALDINE FABRIKANT

 BODY:
MCA Inc., one of the nation's largest entertainment companies, agreed yesterday to be acquired by the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company. The deal, valued at $6.13 billion plus stock in a television station, will be the largest purchase ever of an American company by a Japanese company.
The purchase price is $66 a share in cash and shares in an MCA subsidiary that would own WWOR-TV, the New Jersey-based television station owned by MCA. Analysts yesterday valued the deal at about $69 a share, lower than the $75 or more a share that MCA's chairman, Lew R. Wasserman, had been led to believe he would receive when preliminary talks began in September, one person involved in the talks said. 
 MCA stock fell 25 cents yesterday, closing at $65.125, below the offer price, in part because the deal will not close until early 1991.
 
Prospects Deteriorated
 But MCA's board, meeting Sunday, was apparently resigned to the fact that the economy -- and the cmpany's prospects -- had deteriorated since then, and that no rival bidder had emerged. The 77-year-old Mr. Wasserman said in an interview yesterday, "I feel satisfied or I wouldn't have recommended the deal to the board."
Some experts on corporate acquisitions said the Matsushita-MCA deal may well be representative of deals in the early 1990's. They see more American companies being sold to foreigners, particularly the Japanese, for lack of an American bidder. And they expect prices well below what those companies might have sold for in the 1980's, in the view of Arthur Fleischer, the chairman of Fried Frank Harris Shriver & Jacobson, which represented a large MCA shareholder, David Geffen, in the transaction.
MCA, with 1989 sales of $3.38 billion, owns Universal Studios, Universal Pictures, MCA Records, theme parks and the G. P. Putnam's Sons publishing house, as well as a stake in the USA cable network. In addition to the divestiture of WWOR, the company's Yosemite National Park and Curry Company will be sold to an American buyer within a year under terms of the agreement.
Matsushita (pronounced maht-SOOSH-tah) had revenues of $37.75 billion in the year ended March 31 and is best known for its consumer-electronics products sold under the Panasonic, Quasar and Technics names. But it controls an empire of 87 companies in Japan and many abroad, and its interests include computers, industrial equipment and semiconductors. Like its competitor, the Sony Corporation, which acquired Columbia Pictures and CBS Records, Matsushita is following the strategy that a manufacturer of "hardware" like high-definition television sets will not be competitive unless it also controls "software," like movies and recorded music, to play on that equipment.
 
Strength to Compete
 MCA's president, Sidney J. Sheinberg, said yesterday that the company needed such a deal because "size denotes resources -- as you expand, you need more and more to compete."
Critics of the sale feared growing Japanese cultural influence through motion pictures and television programs and loss of American control of an important industry, which is an export powerhouse for the United States. J. Richard Iverson, president of the American Electronics Association, said foreign investors were "picking up everything from womb to tomb in the communications field." He added, "If you control the production of material, the display of material and the manufacturing of equipment, you have a significant advantage in the future information age."
Supporters argued that the sale would bring additional financial resources to MCA, and viewed the acquisition as a sign of America's economic health and an increasingly integrated global economy.
Prof. Henry R. Nau, associate dean of the Elliott School of International Affairs at George Washington University, said: "If it makes sense as a business deal, it's something we should accept. Japanese and American markets are increasingly integrated, and we shouldn't assume that any acquisition is contrary to our interests."
The MCA-Matsushita deal capped days of tense negotiations during which talks collapsed over a $2-a-share difference in price and then resumed again on Thanksgiving afternoon when the offer was increased by the $2. After a 13 1/2-hour meeting on Sunday, the MCA board unanimously approved the offer. Price was not an issue at the meeting, one person involved in the talks said. The deal was announced yesterday once the final details were in place but it ran into additional tensions on Friday night and Sunday night.
 
Took on Momentum
 Neverthless it took on a momentum of its own.
It seemed unlikely that MCA would attract another buyer at a comparable price over the next several years at least, particularly from an American bidder.
Felix Rohatyn, the partner at Lazard Freres & Company, the investment banking firm, told the MCA board during the Sunday meeting that only a handful of companies in the world would be able to write a $6 billion check for MCA.
Mr. Wasserman and Mr. Sheinberg first met with the Matsushita representatives Masahiko Hirata, a specialist in finance, and Keiya Toyonago, a senior managing director, in Los Angeles last month.
Before beginning the talks in New York that began last week, Michel Ovitz, the head the Creative Artists Agency, a talent agency, who brought the companies together, had indicated to Mr. Wasserman that Matsushita would be willing to pay about $75 a share, said two people who were involved in the negotiations. Mr. Ovitz did not return phone calls yesterday. As a result, when the meetings began last Monday and Matsushita offered $60 in cash, Mr. Wasserman said he would not even discuss the offer and made no conteroffer, several people involved in the talks said. One person said some MCA executives wonder whether Mr. Ovitz really did get the $75 figure from Matsushita.
 
Better Bid Sought
 By late Tuesday Matsushita had improved its offer to $64 in cash. Mr. Wasserman, one executive involved in the deal said, asked for a better bid.
By Wednesday, Matsushita informed MCA that $64 in cash was the last and best bid. "We told them we would schedule a board meeting and not recommend the deal," said one person close to MCA. The MCA board was scheduled for a telephone meeting about the price on Thursday.
That evening Mr. Wasserman had dinner with Robert Strauss, a former chairman of the Democratic National Committee and a former United States trade representative, who acted as a go-between. The MCA chairman is said to have indicated that if the Japanese raised their bid by $2 a share, he would recommend it to his board.
About 6 o'clock Thanksgiving morning, Mr. Strauss called Herbert A. Allen, the investment banker from Allen & Company who was advising Matsushita, to suggest that MCA might accept a $66 cash offer. Mr. Ovitz had gone back to Los Angeles, but was involved by telephone.
 
Matsushita Persuaded
 Sometime Thursday morning, the pair persuaded Matsushita to raise its price to $66 in cash. One person involved in the talks said Mr. Ovitz had argued that this was a make-or-break issue and that they had to give a little. When they made the offer to Mr. Wasserman that afternoon, he indicated that he would recommend the deal to the board.
But the end was hardly in sight. Mr. Sheinberg, who was staying at his appartment at Trump Tower with his wife, Lorraine, canceled plans to go to the home of the producer-director Steven Spielberg, in East Hampton, L.I., for Thanksgiving dinner, instead dining at the Four Seasons restaurant in Manhattan.
Throughout the weekend teams of lawyers struggled with the contracts and a number of other issues that made talks extremely tense at several points. But at least two people involved in the talks said that after the price had been settled, they basically expected the deal to close.
 
Important Elements
 One person involved in the talks said one element that was crucial to MCA was that the deal be as airtight as possible so that in the 30 or 40 days until it was completed, Matsushita could not back out. Matsushita, for its part, wanted to assure itself that major MCA shareholders like David Geffen, who owns 10 million shares of the company's 93 million shares, could not tender to another party.
Matsushita sought lockup agreements from such shareholders so that they would agree to tender their shares to Matsushita so long as Matsushita's bid remained outstanding.
Additionally the Japanese company was concerned about signing long-term deals with top management. One person said Matsushita was very worried about what happened at Sony. After Sony acquired CBS Records, friction with its management led to the departure of CBS Records' chairman, Walter Yetnikoff. Sony has found it necessary to put in a new management team and take a much more active role in the running of the company. "Matsushita did not want that to happen," this person said.
Mr. Wasserman made a special arrangement for the sale of his stock. He owns roughly five million shares of MCA stock. But instead of selling for cash, he will receive preferred stock of a wholly owned subsidiary of Matsushita that has been organized for the acquisition. The structure of his preferred stock agreement also became an extremely tense issue.
 
Some Disappointment
 When the board met Sunday, "there was some disappointment about the price," according to one board member who declined to be named. Mr. Rohatyn, in a presentation to the board, argued that if one used the yardstick of other deals, like Time Inc.'s merger with Warner Communications or Sony's purchase of Columbia, this offer was in the best interest of the shareholders.
But some points still remained unresolved as late as yesterday morning.
As part of its efforts to secure the transaction, Matsushita received an option to acquire 16.9 million shares at $71. The effect of the option is to increase the purchase price should a rival bidder emerge. Under the agreement, Matsushita would receive $125 million should MCA accept a higher offer from another party.
Despite the fact that the deal is complete, MCA was still extremely sensitive to possible criticism that it was selling out to the Japanese, who might attempt to make creative decisions. Mr. Sheinberg said: "We impressed upon our clients the fact that our businesses would continue to be run in the fashion they had been run. The idea that the Japanese interfere in making movies and writing books is borderline silly."
 Neverthless, one person close to MCA acknowledged that the thought of Japanese owning two American movie companies is disturbing. And the cultural difference surfaced even at Sunday's board meeting, albeit as a joking matter. Matsushita has a 250-year business plan, one board member said. "Lew told them he has his own 500-year business plan."
The reaction to the deal was varied and strong. Alfred Sikes, chairman of the Federal Communications Commission, said yesterday that the MCA deal would probably improve the chances that the agency would eliminate regulations that prohibit American television networks from buying Hollywood studios.
Current regulations prohibit the television networks from owning an interest in the shows they buy from Hollywood. They also prohibit the networks from syndicating television programs both in the United States and abroad. Representative John D. Dingell, chairman of the Energy and Commerce Committee, has announced hearings on regulations, which prevented the sale of MCA to General Electric, which owns NBC.
If the rules are eased to permit such mergers, industry executives think the Walt Disney Company would seriously consider an offer for one of the networks, probably CBS. Paramount Communications has long been rumored to have some interest in a merger with Capital Cities/ABC. NBC, which is owned by the General Electric Company, has also shown some interest in being able to acquire or merge with a studio.
Federal regulations also prohibit foreign entities from owning American television stations, thus requiring the spinoff of WWOR.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 28, 1990, Tuesday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day yesterday about the sale of MCA Inc. to the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company misidentified an MCA company that is to be sold off within a year. It is the Yosemite Park and Curry Company.

HEADLINE: THE DEAL FOR MCA;
Dedication to Entertainment Has Been Profitable at MCA

BYLINE: By GERALDINE FABRIKANT

 BODY:
No Hollywood studio has adhered as closely as MCA Inc. to the theme that "there is no business like show business."
MCA, the parent of Universal Pictures, has always been based in Los Angeles and has not strayed far from its core business of movies and television. For decades it has been run by Lew R. Wasserman, its chairman, and Sidney J. Sheinberg, its president. The two pride themselves on their conservative and businesslike approach to entertainment, even though they live in the heart of an industry that does not always follow those precepts.
At MCA, television and movie productions accounted for about half of the company's $3.38 billion in revenue and half of its $191.8 million in profits last year. 
 MCA's devotion to entertainment has served it well. Universal has built up a library of thousands of movies and has specialized in producing hourlong television programs.
 
Movie Business Has Improved
 Although its film operations ran into problems several years ago, performance has picked up this year under a new chairman of the motion picture group, Thomas P. Pollock.
Now Universal will soon bring out "Kindergarten Cop" with Arnold Schwarzenegger for the holidays. The studio will also release "Havana," a new film by Sidney Pollack, who directed "Out of Africa," that stars Robert Redford.
Earlier this year the studio released both "Bird on a Wire" and "Back to the Future III," both of which did respectably if not superbly at the box-office. The later was produced by Steven Spielberg. Universal has a special arrangement with the popular director who has produced numerous films for the studio.
 
Movies Thrive
 MCA does not break out profits from films separately, but its revenues from its movie business have more than doubled, to $466 million since 1987.
Universal's television production business has run into tough times, as have other television production studios. The problems in television have particularly hurt Universal, since the company specializes in one-hour series. Over the last decade, television stations have preferred to buy rerun rights to half-hour comedies rather than rights to hourlong shows.
Universal Television has been bolstering its comedy production and now has two series, "Major Dad" and "Coach," on the air. But it has also been able to get some value out of its hour-length shows through a circuitous route. Unable to sell reruns to television stations at acceptable prices, it has sold them to the USA Network, an advertiser-supported cable service in which MCA has an interest. That has helped the ratings of USA Network, according to Richard Simon, a media analyst at Goldman, Sachs & Company.
MCA's record business remains relatively small, compared with giants like CBS and Warner Brothers. Now headed by Al Teller, a former top executive at CBS, the label has strength in rhythm and blues and country music and can now have a stronger presence in the pop business with the acquisition of Geffen Records last April for 10 million shares of MCA stock, which were then valued at $540 million. The company, headed by David Geffen, is strong in pop music.
 
Theme Park Troubles
 The theme park business is one area where MCA has arguably not paid enough attention. MCA stubbed its toe badly last summer when it opened its Universal Studios/Florida in Orlando, Fla., without all of the rides able to run.
Nevertheless, Gordon Crawford, a media analyst at Capital Research Markets, believes that the park will eventually run smoothly and the company will begin to expand in Japan and Britain. Last year the theme park and its studio tour in Hollywood generated $1.7 billion in revenues,
Mr. Crawford noted that the business was one with high profit margins, and added that every movie company was becoming aware of the need to merchandise its characters more effectively.
Beyond that, MCA controls the Putnam Publishing Group, which includes G. P. Putnam's Sons, the hardcover publishing house that publshes books by authors like Dick Francis, Tom Clancy and Art Buchwald.
 
Extensive Promotions
 Putnam's paperback arm consists of Berkeley and Jove, two aggressive publishers that do extensive promotion. During the last several years, they are known to have made money while the book-publishing industry was, in general, stagnant or struggling.
The entire MCA empire has been built under the leadership of Mr. Wasserman, who has taken a conservative approach to the economics of the entertainment business. MCA has never been willing to borrow heavily, as have competitors like Warner Brothers, nor has it been willing to expand very aggressively into other media businesses, as other studios have done.
Warner, for example, prospered from its investment in cable television, while MCA preferred to stay in entertainment software. On the other hand, MCA never suffered the losses of an Atari, the game company whose financial debacle hurt Warner so badly.
Essentially Mr. Wasserman has stuck to the businesses he knows best, and he has done so with a relish for his role of a Hollywood leader and the power that brings. Indeed, few in Hollywood had had any idea Mr. Wasserman saw fit to sell his company even as he advanced into his 70's.

HEADLINE: Oil Prices Rise $1.05, To $32.95

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
Crude oil prices rose yesterday for the third straight trading day. Traders and analysts attributed the gain to the belief that the United Nations Security Council might authorize the use of force against Iraq.
Oil for January delivery soared to $34.25 a barrel yesterday morning on the New York Mercantile Exchange, before settling somewhat to close at $32.95, up $1.05. The gain followed an increase of $2.27 a barrel Friday and a 72-cent rise on Wednesday. 
 "Basically the oil market is responding to the new fears of war, and since the Thanksgiving holiday, we have had some real tough talk from President Bush," said Peter I. Cardillo, the commodities research director at Jesup, Josephthal & Company, a New York brokerage.
 
Could Reach $36
 Mr. Cardillo said the price of crude could climb to $36 a barrel if, as predicted in news reports yesterday, the Security Council passes a resolution on Thursday sanctioning armed action against Iraq if it does not withdraw its forces from Kuwait by some time in January.
The price of crude slipped back in late trading on rumors that the United States might fail to obtain support from other Security Council members for a Jan. 1 deadline for an Iraqi pullout. But no final decision had been made by last evening.
Traders also cited reports that the Soviet Union had accused Iraq of preventing Soviet citizens from leaving the country and had threatened to take a tougher stance. But crude prices remain well below the record of $41.15 a barrel, reached during trading on Oct. 10.
Oil prices have tended to rise and fall with war fears for nearly four months because of traders' concern that a war in the Persian Gulf could destroy a significant portion of the world's oil production capacity.
 
Oil Products Rise
 Gasoline for December delivery rose 1.8 cents a gallon on the New York Merc to close at 84.20 cents, while heating oil for December delivery rose 3.72 cents a gallon to settle at 92.38 cents. The warm weather in North America this month is holding down the price of heating oil and also of crude oil, as refiners buy less oil to process, said Thomas P. Blakeslee of the Pegasus Econometric Group in Hoboken, N.J.
Only a fraction of the oil and refined products consumed in the United States is traded on the exchange. But futures prices are the benchmark for the cash price at which cargoes of crude and refined products actually change hands.
Prices for actual oil and refined product cargoes moved up in lock step with futures prices yesterday, and were unchanged in the late afternoon after the exchange's close of trading, said Andrew B. Shasha, an energy broker at Star Supply Inc. in Englewood, N.J.
 
Supplies Are Abundant
 Energy trading continues to reflect war fears rather than oil supply and demand, said Nauman Barakat, a senior energy trader at Merrill Lynch. Without the possibility of war, the price of oil would be no higher than $25 a barrel, he said.
Indeed, the recent rise in oil prices has come despite abundant supplies. The Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries produced 22.3 million barrels a day in July, before Iraq's Aug. 2 invasion of Kuwait, and has been producing 22.8 million barrels in recent weeks, said Ken Miller, editorial director of the OPEC Listener, a petroleum analysis service.
The increase is partly seasonal, because worldwide demand for oil tends to rise during the Northern Hemisphere's winter. But current production levels indicate that the 3.6 million barrels that Kuwait and Iraq were exporting daily before a United Nations embargo was imposed on Aug. 6 have been more than replaced, Mr. Miller said.
Saudi Arabia has led the way, increasing production by about 2.4 million barrels a day. The official Saudi news agency yesterday reported an agreement to set up a joint venture in South Korea to refine and market 175,000 barrels a day of Saudi oil.
"It shows that despite sitting in the middle of all this, they're thinking of the post-crisis period," Mr. Miller said.
Traders probably would have pushed up prices more if recent headlines regarding the possible Security Council resolution had appeared two months ago, Mr. Blakeslee of Pegasus said. "They've heard so many rumors and reports that traders are waiting for it to be signed and sealed before taking action," he said. "It's the 'boy who cried wolf' reaction."

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Pricing a Station In Biggest Market

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
JUST what is a television station worth in the New York market? That question will soon be answered, as investors get a chance to buy the first "pure play" available. WWOR-TV is being spun off from MCA Inc. as part of its deal to be acquired by Matsushita.
The figures bandied about yesterday covered a wide spectrum, reflecting questions about how much prices have fallen and whether any companies would step forward and try to buy the station. 
 "My guess is that it is worth $400 million to $500 million," said Harold Vogel, an analyst at Merrill Lynch, who noted that MCA paid $387 million for WWOR in 1987. "The two key factors are that, one, they did improve the ratings, so that is a positive," but he added that the second factor was negative.
"There is a full-blown recession in the Northeast," he said, "and that diminishes the value."
The plan, made necessary by Federal regulations that bar foreign companies from controlling American television stations, is to spin off WWOR to MCA stockholders. The ratio will be one new WWOR share for each five MCA shares held.
Mr. Vogel's estimate works out to a range of $4.30 to $5.40 for each MCA share from the spinoff, in addition to the $66 in cash that Matsushita agreed to pay in the deal announced yesterday. But the stock market appeared not to be convinced of that value. MCA shares fell 25 cents, to $65.125, below even the cash portion of the offer. Part of the discount reflects the fact that the acquisition is not expected to be completed until early next year.
One money manager, who asked not to be identified, said he thought that the station's value was about $4.25 per MCA share, but that he expected the new stock to trade for much less -- $2.50 to $3.
Mario Gabelli, a money manager whose Gabelli & Company owns three million MCA shares, said he thought the station was worth about $300 million, but that the trading value was well below that, about $2 per MCA share, or $10 per WWOR share.
Figures on WWOR operations are sparse, making it difficult to place a value on the station. But that situation should change by early next week, when financial details of the deal are filed with the Securities and Exchange Commission.
MCA said yesterday that the operating cash flow of the station, which is based in Secaucus, N.J., and serves the New York market on Channel 9, was $31 million last year. Since there will be about 18.6 million shares outstanding, that works out to cash flow of about $1.67 per new share, or 33 cents per MCA share. Some analysts speculate that the station has annual revenues of about $150 million.
Based on the cash-flow figure provided by MCA, a value of $4.30 would imply a cash-flow multiple of 15 times, very high in the current market. To justify that range, investors would probably have to assume either that cash flow was likely to rise, or be willing to pay a substantial premium for a station in the nation's largest television market.
The other reason to hope for a high price would be a possible future acquisition of the station. "Anindependent single station doesn't make any economic sense, long-term," Mr. Gabelli said.
People involved in the MCA-Matsushita talks said that after their discussions became known more than one expression of interest was received from possible buyers of the station. But those expressions were not pursued, in part because of concerns that selling the station could delay the deal.
Potential buyers could include the Walt Disney Company, Paramount Communications and Chris-Craft Industries, all of which own at least one independent television station -- a station not affiliated with a network -- but none in New York. Disney and Paramount spokesmen declined to comment; Chris-Craft officials did not return telephone calls.
In the current market for financing, it is unlikely that a much smaller company seeking to expand would be able to get financing for the acquisition, investment bankers say.
Two companies that own independent television stations, Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation and the Tribune Company of Chicago, are ruled out because they already own New York stations. Mr. Murdoch became an American citizen to allow his company to own stations in the United States.
Speculation about a possible takeover of WWOR could keep the shares trading higher than they otherwise would. Many companies that own independent television stations trade at less than 10 times operating cash flow.
One variable that has not been disclosed is what takeover defenses, if any, will be built into the company when it goes public. Another question is how much debt the company will be carrying. Harold Haas, the treasurer of MCA, said he understood that the station would have no long-term debt, but might have some borrowings for working capital. He said the cash-flow figures did not include any interest payments.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Rebound Strongly

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Treasury bond prices rose in a strong turnaround yesterday as dealers shrugged off worries about heightened tensions in the Middle East and rising oil prices and bought securities.
Much of the early decline in prices took place during European trading, when reports first appeared about a United Nations resolution authorizing the use of force unless Iraqi troops withdraw from Kuwait by sometime early next year. Those reports helped to push up oil prices. 
 But the selling stopped soon after trading began in the United States, and even though oil prices remained strong a recovery was under way by early afternoon.
 
'No Longer Intimidated'
 "What we saw was a demonstration of the bond market's resiliency," said William V. Sullivan, Jr., a senior vice president at Dean Witter Reyolds. "Developments like a sharp jump in energy prices are now being interpreted as a force that almost assuredly will push us into recession. The bond market is no longer intimidated by rising energy prices."
Reports on comments by Robert Parry, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, may also have prompted some buying, market participants said. Speaking in Scottsdale, Ariz., to a business group, he was reported as saying that warding off a recession was now the Federal Reserve's most important priority, but he added that the Fed must not lose its fight against inflation.
The Fed is concerned about the risk of an economic downturn, he said, but suggested that the central bank was not about to ease monetary policy in an aggressive manner. "We've got to be careful not to overreact to today's weak economic numbers," he said.
 
Stronger '91 Growth Seen
 In his speech, Mr. Parry projected that economic growth for all of 1990 would amount to a sluggish five-tenths of 1 percent, but that output would be somewhat stronger in 1991, primarily because of continued export growth.
Whatever the cause, the turnaround in bond prices was striking. In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered at a price of 103 18/32 late yesterday, up 7/32, to yield 8.42 percent, down from 8.43 percent late Friday.
Early in yesterday's session the benchmark issue was down by as much as five-eighths of a point.
Among notes, the 8 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 101 19/32, up 3/32, to yield 8.26 percent.
In advance of today's auction of new two-year Treasury notes, the securities were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 7.48 percent. And the five-year notes to be sold on Wednesday were offered in when-issued trading at a price to yield 7.90 percent.
 
Rates on New Bills Down
 Interest rates on new three-month and six-month Treasury bills fell at the weekly auction yesterday.
Three-month bills were sold at an average discount rate of 7.02 percent, down from 7.08 percent a week ago. It is the lowest rate on a three-month bill since early August 1988.
Bids on six-month bills produced an average discount rate of 6.96 percent, compared with 7.05 percent at last week's auction. The rate on the new six-month bill is the lowest since the auction of July 5, 1988.
 
Notes by BP America
 Activity in the investment-grade corporate bond market was highlighted by a number of new debt issues, including a $300 million offering of seven-year guaranteed notes by BP America Inc. The 8 7/8 percent notes, which mature Dec. 1, 1997, were priced at 99.56 to yield 8.961 percent, 80 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 8 3/4 percent seven-year notes at the time of pricing.
The securities, which are guaranteed by the British Petroleum Company, BP America's parent, are noncallable for life.
Morgan Stanley & Company acted as lead manager for the transaction. The issue is rated AA-3 by Moody's Investors Service and AA by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
 
Issue From Merrill Lynch
 In addition to the BP offering, Merrill Lynch & Company offered $125 million of two-year, seven-month notes. The 8.90 percent securities, which mature June 1, 1992, were priced at 100, or par, 1.38 percentage points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 7 3/4 percent two-year notes at the time of pricing.
Merrill Lynch acted as lead manager for the underwriting. The securities are rated A-2 by Moody's and A by Standard & Poor's.
Activity in the secondary market for investment-grade corporate bonds was light, market participants said, but despite the limited activity prices of most issues rose by a quarter of a point to half a point.
In the secondary market for tax-exempt municipal bonds, traders said that prices of the most widely quoted issues were unchanged on the day.
Following are the results of yesterday's auction of three-month and six-month Treasury bills:

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar and Gold Advance As Mideast Concerns Grow

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar advanced moderately against most leading currencies yesterday, as traders reacted to growing concerns about the crisis in the Middle East.
Gold prices moved moderately higher, gaining $2.40, to close at $386.10 an ounce on the Commodity Exchange in New York. The Republic National Bank later quoted a bid of $386.15 an ounce, up $2.40. 
 Analysts said foreign-exchange traders were focusing on the Bush Administration's attempts to win support for a proposed United Nations Security Council resolution authorizing military force to drive Iraqi troops out of Kuwait.
Investors tend to view the dollar as a haven during times of political uncertainty overseas.
 
Benefits for the Mark
 Robert Ryan, corporate foreign exchange manager for the Bank of New York Company, said other currencies -- in particular the German mark -- were also beneficiaries of fears about war. The mark declined against the dollar, but gained against other currencies.
The dollar closed at 1.4870 marks in New York, up from 1.4860 on Friday. The American currency rose as far as 1.4948 marks in New York but then fell back, leaving traders skeptical about the dollar's chances for holding its gains.
Meanwhile, the yen suffered as war speculation raised questions about the Japanese economy. Japan is heavily dependent on the Middle East for its energy needs, importing 99 percent of the oil it uses. The dollar jumped to 129.05 yen in New York from late Friday's 127.10.
The dollar turned in a mixed performance against the British pound. Sterling rose to $1.96895 in New York from late Friday's $1.9668.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Friday's rates, included: 1.2620 Swiss francs, up from 1.2600; 5.0130 French francs, up from 5.0115; 1,116.125 Italian lire, up from 1,115.50, and 1.16075 Canadian dollars, up from 1.16015.
Silver prices rose, with the metal gaining 2.3 cents to close at $4.149 an ounce on the Comex in New York.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Midway Delays A Sale to USAir

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Nov. 26

 BODY:
Midway Airlines Inc. said today that it had agreed to delay the closing on the sale of its Philadelphia operations to USAir Group Inc. to give the Justice Department more time to review the deal.
Midway, based in Chicago, announced in October that it would sell its costly Philadelphia operations to USAir, the nation's seventh-biggest carrier, for $64.5 million.
The deal was expected to face tough antitrust scrutiny because it would give USAir, already the largest operator in Philadelphia, authority for routes to Toronto and Montreal, more landing slots, 11 gates and other facilities.
A Midway spokeswoman said the Justice Department "just wished further time to look it over and asked us not to close before mid-December."
Like their bigger rivals, Midway and USAir have been hurt by high oil prices because of the Mideast crisis and a weakening domestic economy. This year the industry is expected to have its worst financial performance since deregulation in 1978.
Midway said in September that it would cut its staff by 500 people, or 10 percent. USAir, which is based in Arlington, Va., has also had to scale back its ambitious growth plans and has announced the layoffs of hundreds of employees.

HEADLINE: Careers;
Repair Jobs Are Going High Tech

BYLINE: By Elizabeth M. Fowler

 BODY:
AS computers, robots and other high-technology equipment play a bigger part in daily life, jobs in high-technology services are becoming one of the fastest-growing employment areas, according to the Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics.
Stock market analysts might also want to take a look at companies in the field because of the many changes that are expected. 
 Messages for both job seekers and for analysts are in a new study, "Future Trends II -- Perspectives on the International High-Technology Services Industry in the 1990's." It was prepared by Andersen Consulting, a unit of the accounting firm Arthur Andersen & Company, for the Association for Services Management International, which is based in Fort Myers, Fla.
In an interview in New York last week, George Keller, the association's executive vice president, said he was concerned about finding enough technicians. His organization represents 6,000 high-tech service executives and professionsls who manage about 500,000 technicians at companies like Hewlett-Packard, Digital Equipment and I.B.M. The need for these technicians is expected to increase by 50 percent by 1995.
"By the middle of the 1990's, high-tech services will become truly international." Mr. Keller said. He had recently returned from a trip to South Africa, where, he pointed out, managers will face difficulties providing high-tech training to people whose backgrounds and education vary widely. He also said technical services would be greatly needed in Eastern Europe.
Computers have become commonplace in handling airline and train reservations and now handle emergencies such as dispatching police and fire units. Robotics is used in many industries, including manufacturing, printing, and utilities. High-tech services not only involve repairing the hardware but also include maintaining and updating software, consulting and training.
The Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics says the computer service technician field is one of the 20 fastest-growing occupations, along with computer systems analyst and operator, office machine repairer, electronic data processing operator and mechanical engineering technician.
High-tech services appear to be replacing the simpler repair jobs of the past because it is often more efficient to replace some electronic equipment than to repair it. Also, more sophisticated technicians are now needed to keep telecommunications systems and other electronic equipment working.
The Andersen Consulting study predicted that traditional service delivery, a man or women with a bag of tools doing a specific repair job, would be replaced by remote diagnostics. Also, more problem solving and project management will be required of the technicians as service organizations try to focus on training customers to handle equipment and on anticipating customers' needs.
These were among the other findings of the study:
*The Pacific Basin will be the fastest-growing area in terms of electronics revenue, increasing about 16 percent yearly. In Europe, revenue will grow 13 percent; North America will gain 8 percent.
*Some companies are better positioned than others in terms of technical service. The study said the International Business Machines Corporation was well positioned internationally, but the American Telephone and Telegraph Company, while strong in North America and Europe, was weaker in the Pacific Basin. It judged Wang Laboratories to be poorly positioned in all three markets.
*Customers will focus on service performance more than on the instant profitability of the equipment. "Service is one area in which American companies are not only competitive but also extremely well positioned to take advantage of opportunities around the world," Mr. Keller commented.
Electronics manufacturers, understanding that repeat sales will be tied to customer satisfaction, will have to increase and change their training of technical personnel and the managers in charge of the technicians.

HEADLINE: Poland Votes Its Impatience

 BODY:
The first-round results of Poland's presidential election may advance political pluralism. They also reflect explosive social discontent. The winner of next month's runoff will face mounting public impatience for a better life. That's reason enough for America and Western Europe to accelerate existing programs to help Poles and other East Europeans make the transition from Communism. Otherwise, last year's democratic euphoria could become next year's angry disillusionment.
Impatient with the failure of Prime Minister Tadeusz Mazowiecki's policies to bring rapid prosperity, four out of five voters rejected his presidential candidacy. He resigned as Prime Minister yesterday. Solidarity's chairman, Lech Walesa, and Stanislaw Tyminski, a once-obscure businessman, face a runoff next month. 
 Mr. Walesa's campaign stressed his claim that he understood his fellow Poles better than anyone else. Mr. Tyminski portrayed himself as the incarnation of free enterprise. It's therefore difficult to predict their policies. But whatever else they finally stand for, both are friendly toward the U.S. It would insult Poland's hard-won democracy for Washington to play favorites. America's real interest lies in easing the economic anxieties highlighted by the first-round results.
Poles unrealistically but understandably expected that the end of Communism would instantly bring living standards up to West European levels. After decades of misdirected state investment, the most anyone should have expected is a long period of adjustment.
Bravely, the Mazowiecki Government rushed forward with radical reforms, squeezing household budgets and throwing thousands out of work. Now it has paid the price extracted when democratic governments ask for sacrifice without showing convincing results.
Only Poles can make the reforms succeed. But outsiders can help promote that success. What's needed most is debt relief, easy access to international markets and programs to encourage private investment. Western governments have made a start on each, but these efforts now need to be pushed forward.
Germany is preoccupied with reunification, and Washington with the Persian Gulf; Eastern Europe no longer draws the most urgent attention of the world. Yet the cost of more generous relief is trivial compared with that, say, of the superfluous anti-Soviet weapons systems Congress left in this year's budget.
And the stakes are large. The U.S. fought two World Wars and a cold war in part to assure Eastern Europe a democratic future. Poland's democracy has now shown itself a living reality.

HEADLINE: How to Let Arab States Work It Out With Iraq

 BODY:
To the Editor:
What has been peculiar about the debate on President Bush's decision to send additional troops to the Persian Gulf to provide an "offensive capability" (front page, Nov. 9) is that it has been (1) an argument over means rather than goals, and (2) little attention has been given to the effectiveness of other means. 
 For example, former Secretary of State Henry Kissinger and former Under Secretary George Ball on the Nov. 13 "MacNeil-Lehrer Newshour," both assumed that the goal should be to force Iraq out of Kuwait, and devoted their arguments to the means for accomplishing that goal.
But only a brief glance at how the situation arose shows how unrealistic such a goal is. A few days before the Iraqi attack on Kuwait, Saddam Hussein called in the United States Ambassador to Iraq, April C. Glaspie. He repeated his complaints that Kuwait had violated the agreements of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries on oil supplies and prices, and that Kuwait had stolen Iraqi oil by tapping deposits on the Iraqi side of the border. Also clearly on his mind were the boundaries of Kuwait, fixed by Britain in 1923 in such a way as to deny Iraq a port on the gulf.
Ms. Glaspie, acting on instructions, replied that the United States had "no opinion" on Arab versus Arab conflicts like Iraq's border dispute with Kuwait. Ms. Glaspie later conceded that President Hussein undoubtedly understood this to be a green light for settling his differences with Kuwait by force.
If President Hussein now withdraws under the threat of military action or the actuality of sanctions, it will mean the end of his rule and most likely the end of his life, as well.
On the question of means, Mr. Kissinger approved of President Bush's decision to deploy more troops because it would increase the pressure on Saddam Hussein. He then went on to say that an attack might still be necessary -- and soon. Mr. Ball argued that the pressure should be economic sanctions, and they should be given time, even a year or more.
If the attack that Mr. Kissinger has in mind is by air alone, the experience in Korea and Vietnam suggests that it probably would not work. If the attack includes a land invasion against an enemy as battle-hardened as the Iraqis, dug in to defensive positions and equipped with chemical and biological weapons, American casualties would be horrendous.
As for sanctions, boycotts are not like wartime blockades. With Jordan, Iran and Yemen taking Iraq's side, a boycott is likely to leak.
In either case, in a few months the United States may well find itself standing alone except for Israel, and possibly Britain.
The alternative is to declare that our purpose was to prevent an invasion of Saudi Arabia and that since that purpose has been accomplished, we are turning responsibility over to the Arabs.
The result is most likely to be a negotiated settlement that gives Iraq a slice of Kuwait that includes a port. If the Arabs fail to rise to the crisis, Iraq may well take Saudi Arabia and the emirates. Saddam Hussein would then control 21.5 percent of global oil production. Oil prices would rise. But the United States would finally be forced to take the long-overdue steps to make itself independent of Middle East oil.
More important, Egypt and the other Arab states would be thoroughly alarmed and more likely to take responsibility for containing further Iraqi expansion.
 
ROGER HILSMAN
New York, Nov. 14, 1990
 
The writer, a Columbia University professor, held intelligence and State Department appointments in the Kennedy Administration.

HEADLINE: A Peruvian Is Laying Out Another Path

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LIMA, Peru

 BODY:
Hernando de Soto was irritated when a journalist credited him with secret authorship of Peru's new anti-drug policy.
"Not one initiative," Mr. de Soto, a Swiss-trained economist, corrected the journalist. "Seven new initiatives." 
 One year ago, Peru's brightest lights flocked around the presidential candidacy of the novelist Mario Vargas Llosa, but Mr. de Soto hung back.
When Alberto Fujimori, a virtual unknown, unexpectedly headed toward victory, he looked around for uncommitted talent. There was Mr. de Soto, a polished 49-year-old son of a diplomat with a stable of experts, a battery of word processors and an international reputation for innovative critiques of Latin American capitalism.
"Fuji was elected on June 10, and on June 13 he called me," Mr. de Soto recalled.
"I take more heat than the Prime Minister," Mr. de Soto continued, referring to Juan Carlos Hurtado Miller.
Indeed, Mr. Hurtado has complained that he is overshadowed by Mr. de Soto, whose title at the presidential palace is "personal adviser."
 
'Crony Capitalism'
 Mr. de Soto had already gained fame from Washington to Zurich for a book, "The Other Path," printed by Harper & Row. In his writing, he compared Latin America's "crony capitalism" of state-imposed cartels and bureaucracy to the aristocratic mercantilism that prevailed in 18th-century Europe and America.
First, he said, Peru's bureaucracy creates legal obstacle courses that serve to keep striving outsiders in an underground, informal economy. Then Mr. de Soto called for slashing red tape to create "popular capitalism."
"The real polarization which racks Peru is the division between those who have access to legal institutions and those who have to work on the edge or outside of them," he said this month at a symposium in Chile on Latin American development.
Addressing the right, Mr. de Soto argued, "The poor should be motors for their own growth." Turning to the left, he argued, "Capitalism is not incompatible with the eradication of poverty."
Today, Mr. de Soto suddenly has political power along with his Freedom and Democracy Institute, a research organization that gets A.I.D. money from the United States Government's and from European foundations.
 
Help for the Excluded
 The seven initiatives from his institute include plans to provide land titles to virtually all of Peruvian property holders; to deregulate Peru's economy; to institute a hearings system to open up closed rule-making procedures; to make the judicial system faster and less class-biased, and to create a free-market agricultural environment that will encourage farmers to try alternatives to coca leaves, the raw material for cocaine.
The policies grow out of a central tenet of "The Other Path" -- that Peru's economic and judicial system must stop excluding people and start opening up or else it will continue to drive poor Peruvians into the ranks of the Maoist Shining Path rebellion.
"More than 75 percent of our prison population -- that means 14,000 inmates -- are detained without having been sentenced," Mr. Fujimori said in September as he announced a policy to thin the population of Peru's overcrowded jails. Under a new pardon policy, most prisoners who have not been tried within nine months of arrest will be set free.
"If you steal a chicken, you can stay in jail for three years, waiting for trial," Mr. de Soto said. "But no middle-class person ever goes to jail for misappropriation of funds."
In October, Mr. de Soto's words were again heard in the President's mouth when Mr. Fujimori pledged to clear away bureaucracy facing coca growers who seek alternative crops, saying, "If farmers want to export directly, avoiding export oligopolies, they will confront the hard reality that the customs bureaucracy first requires them to follow 36 administrative steps in seven public and private offices."
 
'What's That Going to Do?'
 Some Peruvians say Mr. de Soto is filling a vacuum that arose when a dark-horse candidate took office without a program or a team.
"De Soto is making a reputation for himself as a ventriloquist," said Gustavo Gorriti, a journalist here. "Giving titles to peasants, what's that going to do? Now you are going to have coca farmers with land titles."
Mr. de Soto has played an important role in escorting Peru's new Government down the corridors of power. In June,he orchestrated a meeting in New York between Mr. Fujimori and the heads of Peru's multilateral creditor agencies. The meeting helped produce an orthodox economic austerity attack on Peru's hyperinflation.
In a move arranged by Mr. de Soto, Mr. Fujimori announced this month that Ann B. Wrobleski, a former Assistant Secretary of State for International Narcotics Matters, is to help Peru design an anti-drug program that will persuade the United States Congress that Peru is limiting coca leaf cultivation.
And because of a legal quirk, Mr. de Soto's role as overseas salesman for Peru has become increasingly essential to the Fujimori Government. In Peru, Congress must vote to approve each presidential trip outside the country. Lacking a congressional majority, Mr. Fujimori has been unable to travel overseas since taking office on July 28.

HEADLINE: Kiley Resigns After 7 Years as Chairman of the M.T.A.

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS

 BODY:
Robert R. Kiley, the chairman of the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, who oversaw a vast program to rebuild New York City's subways, bus lines and commuter railroads, resigned yesterday after seven years with the agency.
Mr. Kiley, whose resignation was not unexpected, is credited with reversing the deterioration of the nation's largest mass transit system, especially the New York subways, which had been plagued with delays, derailments, graffiti and track fires. Mr. Kiley successfully lobbied the Legislature for an $8.4 billion capital program to overhaul the tracks, switches, signals and stations and to buy new subway cars and buses. But Mr. Kiley leaves with his rebuilding program only half finished and new money in doubt; with service cuts or fare increases virtually inevitable; with ridership levels stagnant or dropping, and with crime and fare-beating at all-time highs. [Page B7.] 
 Mr. Kiley, 55 years old, will become president and chief executive of the Fischbach Corporation, an electrical engineering firm based in New York. He declined to say how much he would be paid, but said it would be considerably more than his current salary of $150,000 a year.
Daniel Scannell, who is the first vice chairman of the M.T.A. board, will assume the responsibilities of chairman until Mr. Kiley's successor is named.
The transportation authority is a state agency that oversees management of thesubways, city and suburban buses, commuter rail lines, seven bridges and two tunnels. The New York City Transit Authority, the Long Island Rail Road, the Metro-North Commuter Railroad and the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority all come under the jurisdiction of the M.T.A. chairman.
Transportation authority officials said candidates under consideration for the post included Peter Stangl, president of the Metro-North Commuter Railroad; David L. Gunn, former president of the New York Transit Authority; Franklin E. White, the State Transportation Commissioner, and Fabian Palomino, head of the Jacob Javits Center.
The M.T.A. chairman is appointed by the Governor and requires State Senate confirmation. The officials said they expectedGov. Mario M. Cuomo to appoint someone familiar with New York transit issues, but they did not rule out the possibility of an outsider.
During his tenure, Mr. Kiley hired top managers like Mr. Gunn, who virtually eliminated subway graffiti by putting squads of car washers at the end of each line and who substantially reduced track fires by having trash swept up more often.
Government and transit officials praised Mr. Kiley's achievements at the M.T.A. and said that he would not be easily replaced.
"Bob Kiley's management skill and tenacious resolve in confronting complex problems have improved accountability of the M.T.A. and made subway, bus and com muter rail service safer and more reliable," Governor Cuomo said in a statement. "New York has a vastly improved mass transit system and I regret his departure."
Mr. Kiley's leaves the transportation authority with a legacy of long-term planning and vision that will save the New York metropolitan region millions of dollars in future costs, said Richard T. Anderson, president of the Regional Planning Association, a private research group. Mr. Anderson cited the M.T.A.'s strong administrative and planning staff and the agency's plans to install cutting-edge technology like an automatic fare-collection system.
Mr. Kiley, who once headed Boston's transit system and was an unsuccessful candidate for mayor there, became M.T.A. chairman on Nov. 16, 1983, when Governor Cuomo appointed him to fill the unexpired term of the former chairman, Richard Ravitch, who stepped down to return to private business.
The Governor reappointed Mr. Kiley to a new six-year term in 1987, which expires in June 1993. His departure was widely expected since April, when he announced that he did plan to finish his second term.
 
'A Great Job'
 "I'm not tired or frustrated," Mr. Kiley said at a meeting with reporters yesterday. "It's been a great job, but one that I never intended to spend this long in. It's just time to move on." He said that he informed Mr. Cuomo of his decision to resign last Wednesday.
Perhaps the biggest challenge facing the new M.T.A. chairman will be obtaining Federal and state financing to continue the overhaul of the subway and rail system. With New York State's current budget crisis and limited Federal funds available for local transit systems, the M.T.A. may be hard pressed to find money to continue the capital improvement plans.
Mr. Kiley has said that the agency needs about $15 billion starting in 1992 to continue improving transit service and to pay for the first major expansions of the region's subway and commuter rail systems since the early 1970's.
Mr. Kiley's departure comes as the M.T.A. and its various subsidiary agencies are facing swelling deficits because of a decline in state subsidies. The New York City Transit Authority, the Long Island Rail Road and the Metro-North Commuter Railroad are planning or considering fare increases and severe cutbacks in service to help offset the deficits.
 
Difficulties for Successor
 The Straphangers Campaign, an advocacy group for New York subway riders, said that if New York City is to have a safe, efficient and affordable transit system, Governor Cuomo must appoint a "transit evangelist" to serve as the next agency chairman.
"This is a crucial time in the history of the city's transit system, and we need someone who works hard to win money for the subways, to beat fare hikes, and get more riders," said Joseph Rappaport, the advocacy group's coordinator. "What we don't need is a crony of the Governor who can't make the case for transit or gives in to budget cuts."
Mr. Kiley said yesterday that his successor would have difficulty convincing the Legislature to approve a new capital plan because of the weak economy, but that the transportation authority has made "a good case for getting the money, because we have spent it well in the past."
He said the major disappointment of his tenure at the M.T.A. is the declining ridership on the subways and some commuter rail lines in the past year.
In 1986, Mr. Kiley pledged to increase subway ridership by 15 percent by 1991. But ridership has risen only about 6.5 percent since 1986 and is currently declining. "It's very disappointing to see fewer and fewer people use the system that you are trying so hard to rebuild, but I believe most of the decline is due to a weak economy and loss of jobs," Mr. Kiley said.

HEADLINE: The G(reedy) O(ld) P(arty)

BYLINE: By Benjamin J. Stein; Benjamin J. Stein, an economist, wrote speeches for Presidents Richard M. Nixon and Gerald R. Ford

DATELINE: MALIBU, Calif.

 BODY:
William Bennett is the first Republican National Committee chairman in memory, maybe the first ever, who is an intellectual. His stock in trade is not cronyism, not the glad hand, certainly not the politician's ready smile. Rather, his credentials are training in history, logic and philosophy, along with fearlessness about new ideas and debating them. It was these credentials that first put him on the map. 
 He will need them all, because the Republican Party is in an identity crisis. The question is: Does the party stand for anything except the wind of the moment and cradle-to-grave coddling of the rich?
Sadly, the days when the G.O.P. stood for responsible government and protection of public finances are long gone. As recently as my childhood, Dwight D. Eisenhower wanted to reduce the deficit and put the Government on a pay as you go basis. Ike did not want to burden posterity with more debt and either more taxes or more inflation to pay it off.
But Ronald Reagan openly and cheerfully said he would tax all future generations far more heavily than the 1980's taxpayer and impose upon them the burden of paying off his largesse to the electorate. This was, by any known standard, the exact opposite of responsibility, and yet it became Republican gospel.
Richard Nixon came into office with a raft of innovations to help the poor: greatly expanded welfare, education incentives for the less well-off, large-scale job training and retraining, set-asides of all kinds for minorities. Many, if not most, did not work. But at least there was an effort at social responsibility, at least a try at reducing poverty and suffering. This, too, was in the days when Republicans thought they had to stand for something, and behaved responsibly.
The Reagan-Bush era has been different. Its attitude toward anyone not rich or middle class has been: "Hell with you, Jack. We're all right -- and you wouldn't vote for me anyway."
The G.O.P. once stood for protecting the small businessman, the small investor, the small saver. A sound currency, a solid expectation that property rights would be protected were integral parts of what Republicanism was supposed to be about.
In the 1980's, the national Republican Party came to stand for protecting those who stole from the small businessman, who looted the small saver, who cleaned out the tills of Main Street businesses. Through deregulation of finance, nonenforcement of securities laws, basically turning over white collar criminal law enforcement to the white collar criminals, the G.O.P. gave the back of its hand to the victims of every manner of financial looting, and opened its palms wide to the looters.
Even in Ronald Reagan's early years, the Republicans stood for a strong national defense. Today, the U.S. cannot confidently confront a third world nation of about 15 million people -- after 10 years of Republican government. So much for a strong defense as a G.O.P. commandment.
Law and order was a G.O.P. strength. Not today. In the era of budget cuts mandated by irrational tax cuts, there is no Federal money to help states with their police budgets, not enough money for the F.B.I., not enough for the I.R.S., not enough for adequate protection on the streets and in the suites.
The music has been avoided at the polls because the Democrats, with rare genius, have put up such personally unappealing candidates for the White House, and probably still will. But winning elections is not the same as governing, and holding office is not the same as having decent principles.
This is all a heartache for a third- generation Republican like little me. For William Bennett, it's a far greater test. He must think about what the nation truly needs and what the G.O.P. must become to meet those needs. Or, he can go to lavish parties and dispense patronage as the chairman of a party that used to mean something but has come to mean wine and roses to its friends, and a hard, cold pavement to those outside the elephant's tent.

HEADLINE: New World for Junior Achievement

BYLINE: By SUZANNE DALEY

 BODY:
Having settled on the product their company would sell -- a singing mug -- the teen-agers in the applied economics class at Prospect Heights High School in Brooklyn turned to the question of how much it should cost.
"They decided on $6," said John Piccione, a New York Telephone executive who is the class's Junior Achievement consultant. "They said that was just one dollar more than a vial of crack. That seemed reasonable." 
 For many, the name Junior Achievement evokes an image of middle-class, goody-two-shoes teen-agers assembling their own products and starting small companies after school, often in the hope of embellishing their college applications.
But in the last decade, Junior Achievement has taken its volunteer adult executives and marched into the classroom. And it has increasingly reached out to the poorest urban schools, whose students are most at risk of dropping out of society. Last year, 1.2 million children participated in Junior Achievement programs in 14,000 schools around the nation, up from 1,000 in 1980.
 
Reshaping of Program
 To do this, the 70-year-old nonprofit organization has drsharply reshaped its program. It is still dedicated to teaching young people about the world of business, but it can start as early as elementary school and and its programs are much more varied.
For instance, its newest effort, being tried this year in 18 cities, including Chicago, Los Angeles and New York, is aimed at second graders, who are taught about differences between mass and individual production by coloring pictures of doughnuts.
At first, each child is responsible for coloring a whole doughnut. Later, the children are arranged in an assembly line, where one child might be in charge of coloring only the jelly while another does the white icing.
Junior Achievement's original after-school programs still exist, with more than 75,000 participants around the country. In Detroit, for instance, teen-agers working with Michael Judge, a product manager with Michigan Bell Telephone, are constructing bookshelves this semester and selling them for $9. The parts cost $3.
But the program has also been adapted to the classroom. In many high schools, students now participate as part of a social studies or economics class. The manufacturing aspects have largely been eliminated from the school version because they are too time-consuming. But teen-agers still sell stock to create their company, choose a product, elect officers and eventually close down the company, taking their profits home.
 
Encounters Are Revealing
 In inner-city schools, the encounters between executives and students are often testimony to how little contact the students have had with the legitimate business world.
Equating the price of mugs with that of crack is one particularly telling example. But there are others. When Maria Alvarez, a Chase Manhattan Bank executive, gave dividend checks to a group of Manhattan high school students last year, most had no idea what to with checks of any kind.
"I must have gotten calls from every student," Ms. Alvarez said. "One of them called me to say the bank teller had asked for I.D., and I had to explain what could be used as I.D."
This lack of knowledge about the business world does not dampen the students' enthusiasm or creativity, many executives said.
In one of Mr. Piccione's classes, the students recently came up with the idea of selling first aid kits to the owner of a local used-car lot, persuading him to put the kits in the glove compartments of his cars as bonus gifts to customers. The class sold several dozen that way.
First aid kits have prompted other interesting selling techniques. Douglas E. Shallau, president of Junior Achievement of New York Inc., said one Manhattan high school student had sold more than 150 kits in just a few weeks.
When asked how, the student said she had stopped at a local health clinic, got free condoms and put one in each kit. Then, she stood in front of school selling the kits to male students.
"It wasn't exactly what we had in mind," said Mr. Shallau, "but we had to admit that she had absorbed a lot of the things we were teaching, like 'know your market.' "
The thrust into the schools began in the mid-1970's when Junior Achievement, which depended on executives willing to spend at least two evening hours a week with the younsters, began having trouble recruiting volunteers.
"The demographics were changing," Mr. Shallau said. "With more women working, nobody had extra time for evening volunteer work."
So, the organization turned its focus on schools, creating programs for the classroom and urging companies to donate their employees' time. Only the elementary school programs lack volunteer executives helping to teach the courses.
Junior Achievement costs the schools nothing. The organization is financed by corporations, foundations, special fund-raising events and individual gifts. Its budget was $60 million last year. More than 40,000 executives donated their time.
 
Effectiveness of Programs
 How effective are the programs?
The organization makes no claims to single-handedly keeping children in school. But its programs include many of the components considered most effective in reducing dropout rates: role models, activities with tangible results and lessons easily related to life.
One survey, conducted by an independent consultant hired by the Kellogg Corporation, found that in several hundred junior high schools, attendance increased 20 percent on days when the Junior Achievement executives were scheduled to visit.
Students in the program often say the executives are more interesting than their teachers and can speak with more authority about the real world.
"She knows a lot more about this stuff," said Noel Alpert, a senior in Ms. Alvarez's class this year at Manhattan's Park West High School. "Most of the time what we get is whatever is in the plan book. It's the routine. But here she's giving us hands-on stuff. This is someone who is out there every day. A teacher can't do that."
Mr. Piccione, of the mug-selling project, recently went to his class at Prospect Heights, a school that routinely screens its students for weapons, and found a dejected sales force.
"I'm not selling Hondas," said one student, referring to advertisements for the car company that suggest Hondas sell themselves.
Mr. Piccione started a lesson on sales pitches, like "the puppy dog" approach (If you don't buy this I'm going to fail my class, get fired, look stupid . . . ) and the "building an agreement staircase" approach (It's a great mug, isn't it? Kids would really like it, right? I'm sure you want one, right?).
At one point, when the class's attention appeared to be waning, Mr. Piccione changed the subject briefly.
A salesman in his company, he said, could make more than $600 a week. The room was silent, the awe obvious.
