HEADLINE: Nairobi Journal;
On KTN, Mr. Big Man Is Often Not in the Picture

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NAIROBI, Kenya, Nov. 27

 BODY:
For television viewers in sub-Saharan Africa, the diet of nightly news is predictable: the head of state, often referred to in common parlance as the Big Man, is always featured as subject of the first, often endless and always adoring news item. No matter how trivial the occasion, "Mr. Big Man (fill in leader's name) said today" is the common opening.
In Kenya, this has changed in the last eight months as a commercial television station established by the British publisher Robert Maxwell and, surprisingly, the ruling political party in this one-party nation, offers competition to the Government-run television operation. 
 Sometimes President Daniel arap Moi does not appear at all in the newscast of the new station. Sometimes it is a fleeting shot as he leaves or returns to the country. Sometimes he leads the news.
"When it is worth it," said the news editor, Herman Igambi, a veteran of the Government broadcasting station who intuits what will offend State House and what will not. "I explained to State House it doesn't mean we don't like the President. We like him. But I told them it was boring." So far, he said, there have been no complaints.
 
Some Press Rivalry
 The new television station, Kenya Television Network, which reaches about 40,000 viewers in Nairobi, has gone beyond changing its priorities in coverage of the news. In a nation where editors of the three daily newspapers constantly look over their shoulders and Government censorship is common, KTN, as it is known, has engendered some press rivalry.
Mutegi Njau, the city editor of The Nation, the boldest of the three papers, was angry the other day when he noticed that The Kenya Times, the newspaper allied with the new television station, had a report he didn't.
His paper had been called the night before and instructed by President Moi's office not to run an article about a government Minister's marital problems. The Nation agreed. The next morning, Mr. Njau discovered that KTN had broadcast the report and that because television had broken the item The Kenya Times felt no compunction in printing it -- on the front page.
Several weeks ago, KTN did a straight report on the visit of three United States Senators who criticized Kenya's human rights record. In contrast, the Government station ran a report of a saccharine meeting between the Senators and the Finance Minister, George Saitoti, which made no mention of human rights. KTN reported on riots in Nairobi in July, although rather gingerly; the Government station, Kenya Broadcasting Corporation, blanked them out.
"KTN is probably the freest station in sub-Sahara Africa," said Gary Strieker, the reporter for Africa for Cable News Network, who has lived and worked on the continent for 21 years. "Everything else is government-dominated. I can't think of any other place that has a comparable degree of autonomy from the government."
 
Just One Camera
 The network offers not only a sharper newscast, replete with up-to- the-minute graphics and a talented reporter, Kathleen Openda, but also has the rights to CNN's newscasts. Mr. Strieker says KTN is the only station in sub-Saharan Africa to consistently program CNN news at scheduled hours.
The station's headquarters are on the 20th floor of Nyayo House, the red brick main government building in Nairobi, the same place as the security branch of the police, immigration and a few other sensitive government agencies. There, six reporters and four editors, a handful of technicians and cameramen -- with one camera -- produce the nightly news show.
Ms. Openda, 27 years old, has quickly become a star. With a rare camera presence and an agreeably forthright voice -- "a relic of the colonial era," she jokes of the accent developed at a missionary school -- she usually works with the one camera.
On a recent day, she jumped into a station wagon with her crew, much like a television crew in any big Western city, and raced to the airport for mandatory coverage of Mr. Moi's departure for a two-day trip south.
That night, KTN ran a squib of the Moi footage well down in the news; KBC led its news for several minutes with Mr. Moi's departure and arrival in Swaziland -- the night that Margaret Thatcher resigned and the longest-serving member in the Kenyan Cabinet was declared bankrupt and booted out of the government.
 
A Former Teacher
 After the airport, Ms. Openda decided to cover Government moves to clear a slum, one of several such efforts in the last month that have chased squatters from their shanties, leaving thousands of families homeless. Ms. Openda reported on a recent razing at Muoroto, while KBC did not, and she and Mr. Igambi were disappointed that they had missed several other shanty clearances in the week. Their tip proved unfounded, so by noon, Ms. Openda was fretting: she was traveling with the station's only camera and had nothing.
But by late afternoon, she had visited three ministers' offices, one of which came up with what she thought was a reasonable report about a distastrous aphid infection in Kenya's hardwood forests.
Ms. Openda was a high-school literature teacher who read the sports news at KBC part time when she learned of the new television station. She had no journalistic training or experience. "Journalism is similar to teaching," she said. "It's about explaining."
But even so, there is a limit to how much explaining Ms. Openda and her fellow reporters can do at KTN. The station's report on rioting in Nairobi in early July, which started days after the imprisonment of three advocates of a multiparty system, emphasized the economic frustrations of the demonstrators but failed to show the politically motivated hand gestures -- a v-for-victory sign -- shown by many members of the crowd.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Kenya Television Network, Nairobi's commercial station, has changed the style of television news in sub-Saharan Africa. Kathleen Openda, a reporter for the station, broadcast from a street corner in the capital. (Jane Perlez/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
George Bush's Secret Weapon

BYLINE: By Peter Passell

 BODY:
WAS it Willie Horton, or the L-word, or the "read my lips" ploy that sunk Michael Dukakis's bid for the Presidency in 1988? None of the above, argues Ray C. Fair, an economist at Yale University who moonlights as a political prognosticator. Mr. Fair, who has just updated his remarkably accurate statistical model for explaining Presidential election results to include the Bush-Dukakis contest, suggests that nothing much can change the predisposition of voters to elect Republicans (especially incumbent Republicans) except parlous economic times.
That is bad news for Democrats, who may be inclined to think they have George Bush on the political defensive. For unless the economy is in absolutely rotten shape, Mr. Fair's equation implies that Mr. Bush will win in a walk. 
 The Fair model of voting behavior, first published in the May 1978 issue of the Review of Economics and Statistics, offers a breathtakingly simple view of Presidential elections. Republican candidates begin each contest at a substantial advantage, Mr. Fair surmises, as do incumbent Presidents. But beyond that, the only factors that seem to have influenced the vote split in the last 19 Presidential contests have been the rate of economic growth during the election year and the rate of inflation over the previous two years. A high growth rate favors the incumbent, of course, while a high rate of inflation favors the challenger.
The election game presented by Mr. Fair is so easy that anyone with a pencil can play. In the latest version of the formula, derived from what statisticians call regression analysis, a Democrat starts with a presumptive claim to 48 percent ofthe two-party vote. If he is an incumbent, add five percentage points; if he is running against an incumbent Republican, subtract five.
Now calculate the incumbent's economic bonus -- or penalty for presiding over hard times. Figure the economy's growth rate in the six months preceding the election. Then deduct for inflation by multiplying the inflation rate by .3 and subtracting from the growth rate. If, for example, inflation has been running at 10 percent, subtract 3.
Add this subtotal to the Democratic candidate's score if the Democrat is an incumbent and subtract it if a Republican is an incumbent. If neither candidate is running from the White House, scrap the calculation: By Mr. Fair's analysis, economic conditions seem to matter only when voters have an individual to credit or blame.
Simple, not to say, simple-minded? Perhaps, but econometricians live or die by the numbers, and in this case the numbers are lively indeed.
The statistical model explains 89 percent of the variation in the vote split over the last 19 Presidential elections. And the average error is just threepercentage points; pollsters sampling thousands of voters a few days before elections rarely do better. For the last six elections, the Fair equation has missed the mark by a stunningly small 1.1 percent of the vote.
The analysis confirms the conventional wisdom that Republicans have assembled a winning combination of interests and issues over the decades: 52 out of every 100 voters lean toward Republican candidates. Some four voters in 100, however, are ready to give the benefit of the doubt to an incumbent President from either party; once a Democrat is in office, he has the edge. If the economy is in deep enough trouble, though, enough voters are apparently prepared to switch parties to overwhelm the combined effect of incumbency and the Republican bias.
The level of income or employment does not seem to affect votes; what does is the rate at which the economy is growing or shrinking. And that will likely make the tough job of beating George Bush even tougher.
As both a Republican and an incumbent, Mr. Bush begins the race with a 56-44 advantage. A recession in 1991 would not hurt him, as long as the economy bottoms out early in 1992. In fact, the Fair equation suggests that it might help him: the economy generally grows most rapidly as it is coming out of a recession.
If, as is entirely plausible, the economy is growing at a 5 percent rate in mid-1992 and inflation is running at 6 percent, Mr. Bush's predicted margin would be 59-41. Only an economy in a free fall -- say, a combination of 7 percent inflation and a -5 percent growth rate would allow a Democrat to squeeze by the incumbent.
Stranger events have happened: In 1980 President Carter was humbled by 9 percent inflation and a -6 percent growth rate. But unless economic lightening strikes again, the Fair equation suggests that it will take at least six more years to dislodge the Republicans from the White House.

HEADLINE: EVOLUTION IN EUROPE;
YUGOSLAVIA SEEN BREAKING UP SOON

BYLINE: By DAVID BINDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 27

 BODY:
United States intelligence is predicting that federated Yugoslavia will break apart, most probably in the next 18 months, and that civil war in that multinational Balkan country is highly likely.
The predictions, included in a long National Intelligence Estimate produced under the auspices of the Central Intelligence Agency, are unusually firm and sharp for such a document, a senior Government official said. 
 The intelligence estimate runs counter to views of Yugoslavia's future in the State Department, although some specialists in that department also subscribe to the premise.
The intelligence prediction received backing in Yugoslavia on Nov. 15 when Prime Minister Ante Markovic declared: "The acts of the highest state organs of Slovenia, Serbia and, partly, Croatia inevitably lead to a straining of political relations in Yugoslavia and directly threaten the country's survival."
 
Nationalism Is Growing
 The Prime Minister continued, "The situation is characterized by growing nationalism and separatism and an alarming worsening of ethnic relations, all of which is expressed in violence, a drastic threat to public order, peace and citizens' safety,"
According to United States officials who have read the intelligence document, its two basic findings are that "the Yugoslav experiment has failed, that the country will break up" and that "this is likely to be accompanied by ethnic violence and unrest which could lead to civil war."
"It did not predict absolutely that there would be a civil war, but said it was highly likely," one official noted. He said he was "startled by its stark terms."
Yugoslavia, a federation of six republics formed under Tito's victorious Communist partisan army at the end of World War II, has been plagued by increasing internal tensions since the death of Tito in 1980. The stresses in the country of 23 million have been worsened by political rivalry between Serbia, the largest of the six republics, and Croatia and Slovenia, the most prosperous; and by Serbia's repression of its Albanian minority of nearly two million in the region called Kosovo.
 
Democratic Elections Held
 In the spring, Slovenia and Croatia held democratic elections that ended Communist Party rule, while Serbia -- also facing elections -- retains a Communist government under its president, Slobodan Milosevic.
The authors of the intelligence estimate, and virtually every other American official concerned, blame Mr. Milosevic as the principal instigator of Yugoslavia's troubles, both for initiating the latest repression of the Kosovo Albanians and for stirring Serbian nationalist passions.
After an auspicious start with an economic reform program last winter, Prime Minister Markovic has seen his federal authority wither in the face of mounting disputes among the various republics. Slovenia and Croatia have threatened to secede from the federation and Mr. Milosevic has lately pursued a go-it-alone policy for Serbia.
The United States intelligence estimate on Yugoslavia's future is set in this context.
 
Army Termed Fraying
 "I think you can almost write the death certificate now," a Government official who specializes in Yugoslav affairs said recently. He pointed to moves by Belgrade authorities to impose customs duties on goods entering Serbia from other Yugoslav republics and to withhold Serbia's payments to the Federal Government. He also said the Yugoslav People's Army "is beginning to fray."
"I could see a formal breakup by mid-1991," this official said.
Another American Government specialist took a more cautious view of Yugoslavia's future, saying, "I am not willing to write them totally off" at this moment.
The C.I.A.'s pessimism is shared by some State Department officials, including Deputy Secretary Lawrence S. Eagleburger, who served two tours in Yugoslavia, most recently as ambassador, and who is widely respected in the country as a friend.
It is also shared to a degree by American scholars specializing for many years in Yugoslav politics and history -- among them Steven L. Burg of Brandeis University, Charles Jelavich of the University of Indiana and Denison I. Rusinow of the University of Pittsburgh.
Late last month, the House and Senate passed an amendment to the Foreign Operations Appropriation law that bars any United States loans or credits for Yugoslavia unless the assistance is directed to a republic "which has held free and fair elections and which is not engaged in systematic abuse of human rights."
The legislation was fueled mainly by members of Congress who desire to penalize Serbia for its repression of the Kosovo Albanians.

HEADLINE: Reshaping a Real Estate Dynasty

BYLINE: By RICHARD D. HYLTON

 BODY:
The Reichmann family of Canada built a fortune by attacking while others were in retreat. They bought office towers in New York at distressed prices in the mid-1970's when the city was on the brink of bankruptcy and developers were running for cover. In the early 1980's, in the midst of a deep real estate slump, they risked billions of dollars building the vast World Financial Center in downtown Manhattan. Both bets paid off handsomely.
Now, the Reichmanns are preparing to make more acquisitions and are reshaping their closely held empire, Olympia & York Developments Ltd., which includes the World Financial Center in New York, the Canary Wharf project in London and energy, newsprint and financial-services operations, all worth perhaps $30 billion.
 
 Goal for Next Century
 The goal is to enter the next century firmly anchored in real estate in the world's three biggest financial centers -- and on a scale few developers could easily match. 
 "New York, London and Tokyo are the three financial and business centers for the world," said Paul Reichmann, one of three brothers who control the Toronto-based empire. "These big centers are necessary for the world economies and they will rebound stronger than their problems."
While the Canary Wharf project and the World Financial Center give the company roots in London and New York, Tokyo has proven more elusive: A proposed $30 billion project with Japanese partners in Tokyo, a first for the Reichmanns, has been abandoned.
To position the company for expansion in the 1990's, the Reichmann brothers -- Paul, 60 years old; Albert, 61, and Ralph, 59 -- are making changes that will provide more financial security and make operations less interdependent and able to function without a Reichmann at the helm.
The family is moving away from bank financing for future investment projects and toward long-term sources of money like pension funds and insurance companies. The Reichmanns believe these sources will be more stable than banks and not subject to shareholder pressure during hard times. The brothers have also embarked on a plan to eliminate all long-term debt from the books of their non-real estate properties.
And because the 14 Reichmann children, now mostly in their late teens and early 20's, are too young to demonstrate an interest in running the business, the brothers are preparing the company to proceed even if there is not a Reichmann in charge. In January, they hired John Zuccotti, the former deputy mayor of New York City, to head the American real estate subsidiary. He is the first outsider to head one of the family's wholly owned companies.
 
 Hints of Trouble?
 They are also seeking to sell a 20 percent stake in the United States subsidiary to a major American financial institution. News of that plan this fall touched off speculation that the Reichmann empire might be buckling in the slumping real estate market -- or, at the least, that its $8 billion Canary Wharf project was in trouble.
But in an interview in his New York office on Park Avenue at 45th Street, the usually reclusive Paul Reichmann dismissed as "children who don't know what they are talking about" those who speculated that the company was pressed for cash.
Indeed, the Reichmanns say worries about their liquidity are exaggerated. They say the Olympia & York Canary Wharf Company, the London subsidiary, has won a vote of confidence from lenders: A consortium of 6 European and 4 American banks has completed the first part of a deal to provide 500 million (about $980 million) of interim financing for the construction project, Europe's largest. In addition, the Reichmanns are reviewing several bank proposals to provide up to 600 million (about $1.15 billion) in long-term financing for the main tower of the project.
The company says the two financings are in accordance with its original timetable for pulling some equity out of the first phase of the development before moving forward. Edward Minskoff, a New York real estate executive who worked for the Reichmanns for 20 years, said that they have typically planned such things -- as well as the sale of the 20 percent stake in the United States subsidiary -- long in advance.
"Timing-wise, it just happened to come when there is perceived weakness in the marketplace, but it doesn't signal weakness in the company," Mr. Minskoff said.
 
 A Substantial Cushion
 The Reichmanns' non-real estate holdings provide a substantial financial cushion. Last year, the non-real estate companies -- including Gulf Canada Resources, Abitibi-Price and GW Utilities -- paid several hundred million dollars in profits to their parent, according to a financier who works closely with the company. Gulf Canada, the giant oil and gas company controlled by the family, has seen its cash flow increase by about $1.5 million a day since the crisis in the Persian Gulf sent oil prices climbing. Much of the cash generated by the non-real estate interests has been paying for Canary Wharf.
Still, there are problems with some Reichmann-controlled companies that are in cyclical industries and that carry large amounts of debt. Last week, for example, commercial paper issued by Abitibi-Price Inc., the money-losing forestry and paper products giant that is 82 percent owned by the family, was downgraded by Toronto's Dominion Bond Rating Service because the rating agency deemed the company's 40 percent debt-to-equity ratio too high, given the downturn in its markets, and saw its ability to service its debt as weak.
 
 30 Years of Growth
 The Reichmann empire has been three decades in the making. The family, Hungarian Jews who fled Austria on the eve of World War II, arrived in Canada in the 1950's. By 1956, Ralph Reichmann had started a tile and floor-covering business, the Olympia Floor and Tile Company, and was soon joined by his parents and brothers. In 1969, Albert and Paul formed York Developments to build warehouses and shortly thereafter, Olympia & York Developments Ltd. was formed.
By 1974, the family had begun First Canadian Place, a huge office complex in Toronto that has proved to be one of the family's great successes. By 1977, the brothers had plunked down $320 million for New York office buildings.
Today, Paul, the brother regarded as the financial mind behind the business, oversees the real estate and financing sides while Albert is chairman of the board of the holding company. Ralph still runs the tile and floor covering business he started in 1956.
The strong real estate base the family built in the 1970's gave the Reichmanns an advantage over other developers in financing growth. Most developers get banks to provide construction financing, usually at higher interest rates than those paid by top-tier corporate borrowers because of the higher risks of these projects, which serve as the collateral.
In contrast, Olympia & York often has enough money to finance the construction phase of its projects. When it does borrow, it gets lower rates than most developers because it guarantees the loan itself, rather than putting up the building as collateral.
Like most developers, the company pays off the original construction loan once the projects are completed with a lower-cost, long-term mortgage from a pension fund or insurance company.
 
 Spewing Out Cash
 During the 1980's, property values appreciated rapidly and the company was spewing out cash, sending the Reichmanns looking for buying opportunities and ways to protect against a downturn.
Like scores of American companies in that leverage-loving decade, Olympia & York took on debt, in addition to spending its own money, to buy major holdings in Abitibi-Price, Gulf Canada Resources and Interhome Energy, and to acquire large minority interests in a host of public companies.
But now, with the economy weakening, real estate values falling and companies struggling to raise cash, the Reichmanns are rethinking their position. They are joining the trend among corporations to cut their debt. "I believe that debt-free companies will have the greatest opportunities in the 1990's," Mr. Reichmann said.
 
 Limits on Debt Levels
 In the Reichmanns' real estate portfolio, debt levels are limited to about 60 percent of the appraised value of the assets, according to Mr. Reichmann. Most real estate companies carry much more debt on their books. The company said debt levels were lower in its non-real estate operations.
Some of the dividends the Reichmanns earn from their investments in public companies are being used to pay down the long-term debt in these companies. In some cases, assets are being sold as part of the strategic focusing on real estate and oil. For example, GW Utilities has agreed to sell its Consumers' Gas Company to British Gas P.L.C. for $915 million (Canadian). GW plans to use the proceeds to pay off about $600 million (Canadian) in long-term debt and to pay a special $10 dividend to shareholders, mainly Reichmann family members. The Reichmanns, who own 89 percent of GW, eventually intend to take that company private.
In addition, Olympia & York is looking to combine some of its oil interests. Eventually, it plans to merge Gulf Canada Resources, in which it owns a 74 percent share, with Home Oil, a unit of GW, and to sell a large pipeline company, Interprovincial Pipeline.
Olympia & York is also looking to raise tens of millions of dollars by refinancing its 10 percent interest in Allied Lyons P.L.C., the British supermarket company.
 
 More Stable Financing
 The Reichmanns are also starting to favor pension funds and insurance companies as lenders, which the brothers regard as more stable sources of capital than banks, which have become cautious real estate lenders and are subject to pressures from regulators and shareholders.
"It is a strategic concept," Mr. Reichmann said. "It costs about one-third of 1 percent more than the kinds of rates we get from foreign banks, but we decided that we would be better off with institutions without certain pressures, and that the time is now to move to a different segment of the financial markets."
The company has already begun to refinance many of its 50 or so office towers in its portfolio in the United States and Canada by bringing in deep-pocketed partners like the major pension funds and some insurance companies.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Pound Dominates Trading As Dollar and Gold Decline

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The British pound, helped by the election of John Major as the country's next Prime Minister, dominated the foreign exchange markets yesterday. The dollar fell against the pound and most other major foreign currencies.
Gold prices slipped lower. 
 The pound surged after Mr. Major, who is currently Chancellor of the Exchequer, which is the British equivalent of the United States Treasury, was assured of becoming the next British Prime Minister.
Mr. Major, who had the support of departing Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, fell just short of winning election as head of the Conservative Party in a vote yesterday. But his challengers withdrew from the race and conceded defeat, effectively giving Mr. Major the nation's top job.
The pound rose to $1.9775 in New York, from $1.96895 late Monday. In London trading before Mr. Major's election was assured, the pound rose to $1.9750, from $1.9655 late Monday.
Foreign exchange traders had hoped Mr. Major would win because they were familiar with his policies and expected little change if he succeeded Mrs. Thatcher.
Mike Faust, a currency market annalyst with Money Market Services in Chicago, said sterling's fortune might be short-lived. He said there was speculation among traders that the British Government would lower base lending rates to 13.5 percent from their current 14 percent.
 
 Higher Interest Rates
 Currencies are supported by higher interest rates, so a drop in British rates would make that country's investments less attractive and could send the pound lower.
Mr. Faust said the dollar fell against other currencies amid the belief that the big industrial nations are satisfied with the dollar's low levels.
The dollar fell to 128.35 yen in New York, from 129.05 yen late Monday. In Tokyo earlier, the dollar rose to 128.95 yen from 127.95 yen at Monday's close, and in London, the United States currency fell to 128.30 yen.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Monday's prices, included: 1.48025 German marks, down from 1.4860; 1.2595 Swiss francs, down from 1.2620; 4.9950 French francs, down from 5.0130; 1,111.25 Italian lire, down from 1,116.125, and 1.16405 Canadian dollars, up from 1.16075.
Gold edged down 90 cents on the Commodity Exchange in New York, closing at $385.20 a troy ounce. The Republic National Bank of New York later quoted a bid of $385.00, down $1.15.
Gold fell earlier in London to a late bid of $384.75 a troy ounce, from $387.00 late Monday. In Zurich, gold fell to a closing $384.00 an ounce, from $386.50 late Monday.
In Hong Kong, gold fell $1.16 an ounce, to $385.19.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Security Prices Ease

BYLINE: BY KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities eased slightly and interest rates inched up a bit in sluggish trading yesterday, as dealers concentrated on an auction of $12.3 billion worth of new two-year Treasury notes.
Demand for the new notes was fairly strong, market participants said, and bids for the securities produced an average yield of 7.49 percent, down from an average yield of 7.84 percent at the last two-year note sale on Oct. 30. It is the lowest yield on a two-year note since the auction held in late March, 1988. 
 With the yield on the new notes squarely on top of the 7.5 percent level that is widely seen as the Federal Reserve Board's target for the overnight Federal funds rate, analysts said the auction results showed that traders sense the Federal Reserve Board will be easing monetary policy again fairly soon. Unless short-term rates move lower, dealers will not realize a profit on the new securities.
"The two-year came right on top of the funds rate, but by March or April I think rates will be significantly lower, and people are going to kick themselves for not having bought this issue," said Joe Liro, a senior vice president and money market economist at S.G. Warburg & Company.
 
 Treasury Securities Market
 The Fed entered the Treasury securities market to add reserves to the banking system yesterday. The move was something of a surprise, but analysts described the action as an attempt to push the funds rate down from levels the Fed viewed as unacceptably high. Funds were trading at around 7 3/4 percent when the Fed added reserves.
"They wanted to make it clear that funds are at 7 1/2 percent, not 7 5/8 percent or 7 3/4 percent," one Government securities trader said. "This was just a rate protest, not a new policy signal."
Away from the two-year auction, economists said a report that showed that auto sales, at an annualized rate of 7 million units, remained fairly strong for the 10 days ended Nov. 21 was a mild disappointment to credit market participants.
"We don't know how much of this represents sales to fleets, but auto sales are holding up very well, given the sharp drop in consumer confidence and the other signs of recession we have seen," said Michael J. Moran, chief economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc.
Mr. Moran said auto sales of six million units or less were levels that were normally associated with an economic downturn.
Early yesterday, the Conference Board reported that its widely watched index of consumer confidence declined to a reading of 61.5 in November from 62.6 in October. The November decline was far more modest than the steep drops recorded in the first two months following Iraq's invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2. The report had little impact on prices in the credit markets.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered at a price of 103 14/32, down 3/32, to yield 8.43 percent, up from 8.42 percent late Monday.
 
 10-Year Note Issues
 Among note issues, the 8 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 101 17/ 32, down 1/32, to yield 8.27 percent.
In advance of today's auction of new five-year Treasury notes, the securities were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 7.91 percent, up from 7.90 percent late Monday.
Short-term Treasury bills inched higher.
By late in the day, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.02 percent, up one basis point, or hundredth of a percentage point. Six-month bill rates rose by a similar amount, to a late offered rate of 6.96 percent.
A number of new issues were priced in the tax-exempt municipal bond market yesterday, including the negotiated sale of $168.89 million worth of revenue bonds for the City of Boston.
The bonds, which will be used for the construction of a new Boston City Hospital, are insured by the Federal Housing Administration.
The securities, which range in maturity from 1996 to 2021, were reoffered to investors at yields from 6.65 percent in 1996 to 7.70 percent in 2021.
Investment bankers at Merrill Lynch & Company, which acted as lead manager for the transaction, said this represented the first time the F.H.A. had offered insurance on a hospital issue. The bankers added that without the insurance yields the securities would have been at least a full percentage point higher.
The securities are rated AA by both Moody's Investors Service and the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
The State of Wisconsin offered $120 million worth of general obligation bonds at competitive bid.
A bid from a group led by First Chicago won the securities.
The serial bonds, which range in maturity from 1992 to 2011, were reoffered with yields from 5.50 percent to a maximum rate of 6.90 percent in 2011.
The issue is rated AA by Moody's and Standard & Poor's.
In the secondary market for municipal securities, traders said that prices of most widely quoted issues were unchanged to up about 1/8 point.
Following are the results of yesterday's auction of two-year Treasury notes:

HEADLINE: About New York;
Global Company Recalls Its Roots In Old Brooklyn

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN

 BODY:
Last August, a rock flew through a window in the office of Thomas J. Kline Jr.
Mr. Kline, the manager of Pfizer Inc.'s Brooklyn plant, where Williamsburg meets Bedford-Stuyvesant on Flushing Avenue, called his security officers. They told him that a group of youths had thrown the stones and then had run to a ramshackle apartment building. Mr. Kline asked that the police be called but wanted no arrests. He expressed hope he might meet the perpetrators. 
 Soon a young man identifying himself as B. B. and another named Angel appeared. Both apologized. Mr. Kline invited them and a dozen others from the group on a plant tour; gave them T-shirts as their clothes were wet from playing in a hydrant, and invited them to order whatever they wanted in the cafeteria.
The same teen-agers -- minus Angel whose brother needs him as a drug lookout -- have been coming back for weekly computer instruction and free meals. The other day, they huddled around computers, each supervised by a Pfizer expert. A favorite program allowed a user to design a city.
"We felt this would be especially interesting to kids who are urban," Janet L. Scherer, systems manager for the 600-employee plant.
"We have become quite enchanted with these young people," Mr. Kline said. "All of us in this neighborhood are in it together."
 At a time when companies and people are fleeing New York, Pfizer is a company that stayed -- and stayed in a ragged, crime-ridden neighborhood that many would term one of New York's worst. At a time when manufac turing is dying here, Pfizer has found both social and economic reasons to maintain and modernize. Pfizer is spearheading the redevelopment of its shabby, shaky neighborhood by instigating a public-private partnership to create 500 new jobs and 400 housing units. The jobs would be created by companies that buy unused space from Pfizer at bargain rates. Pfizer is working with a number of public and private groups on the low- and medium-income housing in both existing and new space.
Carl B. Weisbrod, president of the city's Public Development Corporation, part of the project, calls Pfizer a "terrific' example: "You really have to take destiny into your own hands and shape your own environment. That's exactly what they're doing."
Pfizer has woven itself into the fabric of the neighborhood. Well over half the employees volunteer to do community work, much of it on their own time. Engineers repair appliances in homes for the elderly. Others built a stage for a rock concert sponsored by a community organization. Schools have been adopted, a subway station rebuilt, hundreds of students tutored, Halloween contests judged and financial contributions given to groups as varied as a Hassidic health clinic and a Hispanic day-care center.
"We can't solve all the problems," Mr. Kline said. "But we can make a difference at our doorstep."
Pfizer is a proud Brooklyn institution. It was founded in 1849 by two German immigrants, perhaps attracted by the 57 breweries. It pioneered mass production of penicillin and by the end of World War II made half of the world's supply. A former president, John L. Smith, owned the Brooklyn Dodgers with Walter O'Malley and Branch Rickey, until he died in 1950.
But loyalty has limits. In 1961, Pfizer moved its headquarters to Manhattan. By the 1970's, the company was a multinational force, one thinking hard about leaving a deteriorating Brooklyn altogether. But in 1983, it decided to keep the plant, though on a smaller scale. Chemical operations went elsewhere. What remained was making medicine into pills, capsules and the like.
Economic factors prompted Pfizer to stay. One was that a new pill-making plant would have cost $125 million, forcing the company to raise product costs. Another was reduced utility rates in Brooklyn. A third was a trained work force -- average age 50 years, average experience 25. "We have some of the best tablet operators in the world," Mr. Kline said.
But people are yielding to machines, raising productivity. Even good-natured participation in contests to name robots doesn't disguise the imminent job loss. The hope for employment is that efficiencies will persuade bosses to assign the plant more products.
The redevelopment scheme will help. But in two years of recruitment, Pfizer has found only one company to move into space it is offering at one-third the going price. That was a printer, whose main customer is Pfizer. It has 82 employees, 11 hired since moving. Nine are from Brooklyn, six from the local zip code.
The future? "We think we have a potentially fabulous neighbor for them," said the city's Mr. Weisbrod, declining to elaborate.

HEADLINE: Macy's Loss Said to Reach $66 Million

BYLINE: By ISADORE BARMASH

 BODY:
R. H. Macy & Company has told creditors and credit agencies that it had a loss of $66 million in its latest quarter -- a bigger loss than creditors expected and double its $33.1 million loss in the quarter last year, according to a number of creditors and credit agencies.
A Macy spokesman yesterday said the company would not comment on the reports. Macy is expected to report the quarterly loss to the Securities and Exchange Commission in several weeks. 
 The loss in the retailer's first fiscal quarter, ended Oct. 31, would follow a full-year loss of $215.3 million and would be much higher than the $45 million deficit expected by some creditors. Some creditors noted that the period was not nearly as critical a quarter as the current one, which includes the Christmas selling season and January clearances.
The report of the latest loss, however, heightens the general concern in the apparel field that Macy, with more than $5 billion in debt from its 1986 leveraged buyout and from its 1988 acquisition of the Bullock's and the I. Magnin retail chains in California, faces increasing financial pressures while the general retail climate remains cloudy. The Christmas season, during which retailers traditionally register up to 30 percent of sales and as much as 50 percent of profits, is seen as a difficult to predict this year, particularly in the Middle Atlantic States, where Macy has many of its more than 100 stores and where the economy has been especially weak.
Retailing observers attributed the bigger loss to erratic sales during the quarter, with a particular slump in October in stores more than a year old.
The $66 million loss was reported by Macy executives in a periodic progress report it gives to creditors, credit agencies and suppliers, according to creditors. It was not mentioned in a letter sent a week ago by a Macy financial executive to creditors and suppliers that reported other results, like an improved gross margin on sales for the latest quarter over the comparable 1989 quarter and lower inventories through mid-November.
In the letter, Macy said it intended to maintain ample availability of funds under a $475 million revolving bank loan. But in the week ended Nov. 16, Macy said, it borrowed $374 million against that revolving loan. From that point on, Macy said, its borrowing needs were expected to decline.
Although suppliers said they were keeping a close watch on the Macy situation, the largest ones were maintaining a business-as-usual attitude. "We're shipping Macy, and they are paying their bills on time," said Jerome Chazen, chairman and chief executive of Liz Claiborne Inc.

HEADLINE: 3d Fiber-Optic System Installed in Manhattan

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
A Chicago company has completed lower Manhattan's third fiber-optic telephone system, part of a national binge of construction of urban systems that bypass the local telephone companies.
The new high-capacity system is intended to provide large corporations with cheap, reliable, high-quality connections to the nearest call-switching centers of long-distance companies and the New York Telephone Company. 
 The system will give New York Telephone more competition for the profitable business of carrying large volumes of calls and data communications among a few skyscrapers in a small area. To the extent that the new system is successful in draining away business traffic, it could also force New York Telephone to seek higher rates for residential customers.
Residential customers have been subsidized by business customers, who tend to be cheaper to serve because their offices are highly concentrated and therefore require the installation of fewer miles of cable.
 
 Pacts to Serve 27 Buildings
 Metropolitan Fiber Systems Inc., a privately held Chicago company, announced yesterday that it had completed construction of its high-capacity system south of Canal Street, and had signed agreements to serve 27 office buildings, including 11 financial services companies. Royce J. Holland, the president and chief executive of Metropolitan Fiber, said the system had been tested and would start carrying calls within a week.
New York Telephone and the Teleport Communications Group, a company similar to Metropolitan Fiber, already have fiber-optic systems in Manhattan. Metropolitan Fiber has also built such systems in nine other American cities in the last four years, and plans to expand its New York system next year to include most of Manhattan plus parts of the Bronx, Brooklyn and Queens.
Among the initial users of Metropolitan Fiber's system is Bear, Stearns & Company. Jeff Marshall, managing director for communications, said the large New York brokerage firm already used New York Telephone, Teleport and its own building-to-building microwave service, but would now begin giving traffic to Metropolitan Fiber as well.
Many local companies have sought alternative cables to local and long-distance switching centers to protect against equipment failures in any one network and to increase price competition. Bear, Stearns has been paying 40 percent more in New York than in other American cities for the standard corporate package of 24 digitally switched local lines, Mr. Marshall said.
Lower prices for telephone service are needed to keep businesses in New York and make them competitive in world markets, said Ronald K. Shelp, the president and chief executive of the New York City Partnership, a 10-year-old coalition of business, government and labor leaders that seeks to improve the city.
 
 Cost of Subsidies
 But part of the higher price of business service in New York reflects the cost of subsidizing rural and other residential service. Private fiber-optic systems undermine this system of subsidies, said Eli M. Noam, a Columbia University economist and former New York State Public Service Commissioner.
"It's a classic cream-skimming situation, where the cream is the above-cost pricing of the short-haul business district," he said.
Mr. Holland disagreed, contending that many of New York Telephone's business services are now provided at cost. Metropolitan Fiber has told the Public Service Commission, which is reviewing New York Telephone's request for an $832 million rate increase, that the phone company may have used revenues from small businesses and residential customers to subsidize construction of its own fiber-optic systems.
New York Telephone denies this.

HEADLINE: Vehicle Sales Up 12.6% in Midmonth

BYLINE: By PAUL C. JUDGE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Nov. 27

 BODY:
Propelled by rebates, sales of vehicles made in the United States rose by 12.6 percent in mid-November, compared with sluggish demand in the period last year.
Several analysts said they were at a loss to explain why vehicle sales continued at a fairly steady pace despite a weakening economy. 
 Hefty rebates, averaging $1,400 to $1,500 on many domestic models, "help explain some of the increase in sales over last year," said David Healy, an analyst at Barclays de Zoete Wedd Ltd. in New York. Nevertheless, he said, "It's as if car buyers are oblivious to the fact that we're in a recession."
 
 The Fleet Factor
 Ronald A. Glantz, an analyst with Dean Witter Securities Inc. in San Francisco, said he was "flabbergasted" by the figures.
"Fleets have largely finished taking deliveries of 1991 models, so they are not much of a factor" in the latest results, he said.
But other analysts said fleets were the primary factor in the latest sales gains posted by the Big Three.
"Fleets are still a huge part of the equation," said Joseph S. Phillippi of Shearson Lehman Brothers Inc. in New York.
Joel Pitcoff, a market analyst for the Ford Motor Company, said, "This is a time of the year when fleet sales represent a higher proportion of overall sales."
The 10 auto makers that manufacture in the United States sold 291,287 vehicles, compared with 255,492 a year ago.
Car sales surged by 16.7 percent, to 182,749. Seasonally adjusted, cars sold at an annual rate of 7.1 million, compared with an annual rate of 6.1 million in the period last year. So far this year, cars have sold at an annual rate of 7 million.
Sales of domestic light trucks rose by 9.8 percent, to 108,538, reversing several weeks of sales declines that auto makers and dealers had attributed to the lower fuel economy of pickup trucks and mini-vans compared with many car models.
The Japanese auto makers with plants in the United States increased light-vehicle sales by 27.5 percent in mid-November, while those of the Big Three increased by 13.4 percent. Japanese "transplants" held 10.6 percent of the American market in the middle of the month, up from 9.5 percent in last year's period.
 
 G.M.'s Rise Nearly 17%
 General Motors sold 77,538 cars in mid-November, up by 16.9 percent from last year. G.M. reported improving sales of several of its small cars, including the Geo Metro and Storm, Chevrolet Cavalier and Pontiac Sunbird.
Some larger models also sold well, like Oldsmobile's Cutlass Supreme and Buick's intermediate Regal, and the full-size Park Avenue. G.M.'s light-truck sales rose by 10.6 percent, to 44,180 units, mainly from increased sales of the company's plastic-body APV vans.
Ford's car sales rose 17.6 percent during the period, to 49,364 cars, led by the subcompact Escort. Strong sales gains were also posted by the full-size Crown Victoria and intermediate Taurus sedans -- two models popular with fleets. Ford sold 37,648 light trucks, a gain of 14.6 percent, with sales gains posted by its Explorer four-wheel-drive vehicle and Aerostar van.
The Chrysler Corporation's sales of pickups, Jeeps and mini-vans were flat, however, and the company continued to lose market share in that critical segment. Chrysler sold 22,865 light trucks, a gain of less than 1 percent. Chrysler's car sales increased 10.8 percent, to 22,362 cars.
 
 'Transplants' Fare Well
 The Honda Motor Company posted a gain of 5.2 percent in sales of cars made at its plant in Marysville, Ohio. The Toyota Motor Corporation, which confirmed plans today to expand its plant in Georgetown, Ky., reported sales of 9,025 cars in mid-November, an increase of 53.4 percent. The Nissan Motor Company sold 3,399 Sentra cars, up by 45.9 percent, and 2,709 compact pickup trucks, a decline of 18.6 percent. The vehicles are made at Nissan's plant in Smyrna, Tenn.
Among the remaining transplant manufacturers, the Mazda Motor Corporation sold 1,541 cars, an increase of 57.6 percent, and the Mitsubishi Motors Corporation sold 1,383 automobiles, a rise of 39.7 percent.
Subaru of America, which began selling domestically produced cars in the American market last summer, sold 431 autos in mid-November, and Isuzu Motors Ltd., Subaru's partner in a plant in Lafayette, Ind., sold 1,136 light trucks.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 29, 1990, Thursday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day yesterday about vehicle sales in the United States in mid-November misstated the overall increase. Sales of domestically made cars and light trucks from Nov. 11 to 20 rose 14.7 percent, not 12.6 percent, over last year's.

HEADLINE: Trade Deficit Much Wider in 3d Quarter

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 27

 BODY:
The United States trade deficit, aggravated by rising oil prices and a drop in farm exports, swelled to $29.75 billion from July through September, the worst quarterly imbalance in a year, the Commerce Department said today.
The third-quarter gap between imports and exports widened by a sharp 28.9 percent from a second-quarter deficit of $23.1 billion. Imports climbed 5 percent, to an all-time high of $125.91 billion, while exports slipped six-tenths of 1 percent, to $96.16 billion.
Economists predicted that the deterioration from the second quarter, which had posted the lowest deficit in more than six years, would continue in the final three months of 1990 as rising oil prices further increased America's import bill. Analysts said the slumping United States economy should lower American demand for non-oil imports next year and thus help to narrow the trade deficit.
The July-September imbalance was the largest quarterly deficit since a $29.8 billion deficit in the corresponding period last year. The deficit had been narrowing since that time as an export boom helped sales of American products.
Today's report on merchandise trade, calculated on a balance-of-payments basis, confirmed trends already noted in the Commerce Department's monthly merchandise trade reports. The quarterly report, however, excludes military sales by the United States Government and makes other minor adjustments to the monthly figures.

HEADLINE: Washington Talk;
Bush and the Economy: Any Cure for the Jitters?

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 27

 BODY:
To Presidents, the economy hardly ever looks good. Recession seems to be looming or, if not, inflation is threatening, or the markets are down, or productivity is lagging, or something else is wrong that the voters might hold them responsible for. By the middle of their term, the economist Herbert Stein has observed, Presidents come "face to face with the dreadful prospect" that unless they change course, they might enter their re-election campaign "with an economy in misery."
"This is a condition that frequently afflicts administrations in their second or third year and sets them to searching, open-mindedly or desperately, for new solutions," Mr. Stein, who headed Richard M. Nixon's Council of Economic Advisers, wrote in his book, "Presidential Economics." 
 Perhaps it was these midterm jitters that led President Bush this year to abandon his stance against tax increases. But if so, it may have been a passing impulse. Although the state of the economy nowadays appears more dismal with each new release of data, there is no indication as Mr. Bush winds up his second year in office that his Administration has much in the way of new economic plans.
In their public statements this fall, top officials have tried to soothe anxiety rather than offer policy prescriptions. Instead of using the harsh word "recession," they have talked about "tough times," "slowdown" and "downturn" (President Bush), "lull" (Michael J. Boskin, chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers) and "the downphase of the cycle" (Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady).
Richard G. Darman, the budget director, warned against "neo-neo-ism," which he described as always looking for a "bold new program," and "Hubble-ism," which he defined as launching an idea at full scale before it was properly tested. Instead, Mr. Darman advocated "empirically based analysis." In other words, look before you leap -- hardly a wonder drug for a sick economy.
Before Thanksgiving, Mr. Bush met several times with bankers and business executives. Perhaps afraid to acknowledge that the President had no trump cards in his economic hand, the White House kept the meetings off the official schedule, would not describe what was discussed and would not even give a list of participants.
At the Treasury Department, the center of economic policy-making in past Administrations, attention is focused not so much on the overall economy as on legislation the Administration plans to propose next year to revise Depression-era banking regulations. The changes could eventually improve economic activity by making more loans available. But the measure will take a year to get through Congress, and many more years will pass before its effects are fully felt in the credit markets.
For better or worse, other recent Presidents at the midpoint of their first terms took steps to show the country that they were trying to turn the economy around. At the end of 1962, John F. Kennedy proposed the biggest tax cut in American history up to that time. In 1971, Mr. Nixon imposed wage and price controls. In 1982 and 1983, Ronald Reagan retreated substantially from the "supply-side" philosophy of his first year in the White House and supported a large tax increase and legislation to create new jobs.
But President Bush has much less freedom than his predecessors. Saddled with a $300 billion deficit, he can hardly propose tax cuts or spending increases. New budget rules make it difficult if not impossible for Mr. Bush to expand unemployment benefits, a step taken by all other recent Presidents who faced rising joblessness. And Congress has taken away what used to be one of Presidents' strongest levers, the ability to give the economy a quick jolt by shifting Government spending from one part of a fiscal year to another.
So Plan A, said an Administration official working on economic matters, is to "wait and hope" -- hope that the gulf crisis ends peacefully and oil prices come down, hope that the unemployment rate next year rises no higher than the 6.5 percent now being forecast, hope that the Federal Reserve Board will lower interest rates by at least another percentage point and, above all, hope that a recovery is in full force by 1992.
And Plan B? "Right now," the official said, "there isn't one."

HEADLINE: Chemical Plants Buy Up Neighbors for Safety Zone

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BATON ROUGE, La., Nov. 21

 BODY:
Prodded by lawsuits over pollution and damage claims from a number of explosions, several of the nation's largest oil and chemical companies are spending millions of dollars to create safety zones by buying up the homes around their plants.
Although some homeowners are eagerly grabbing the offers of up to $200,000 for a house, the efforts are coming under growing criticism from environmentalists who say the companies are dismantling communities rather than than dealing with the root problems of safety and pollution. 
 A quarter of the nation's chemicals and a sizable portion of its transportation fuels are processed in more than 75 large industrial plants along the 85-mile stretch of the Mississippi River between here and New Orleans. Some of the plants are a tangle of pipes and tanks covering more than a square mile.
 
An Assortment of Concerns
 Stirred by new medical findings and rising concerns about industrial accidents and environmental issues, Louisiana's river communities are aggressively pressing lawsuits and damage claims that are costing the companies tens of millions of dollars.
"There are some things you just should not mix, including houses near these industrial facilities," said Dr. Paul Templet, the state's top environmental official. While he generally supports the idea of the buyout programs, he said the issue has not yet received enough scrutiny from state officials.
Some of the relocation programs are new, and others are continuations of programs that have been under way for years but are attracting new attention because of accidents or lawsuits.
 
A Variety of Programs
 The Dow Chemical Company began a buyout program across the Mississippi River from here last year that could cost $10 million. Five miles south of the Dow plant, the Georgia Gulf Corporation completed a program last year to move 50 families away from its vinyl chloride plant, turning a community founded by freed slaves into a six-acre grove of oaks and pecan trees.
The Exxon Corporation has spent $4 million on a similar progam here in Baton Rouge, and the Shell Oil Company is in the middle of another one down the river. Separate explosions killed two workers last year at the Exxon plant and seven workers in 1988 at the Shell plant, but the companies each say that the purchases of nearby homes began earlier and are not related to the accidents.
Another program is beginning in Ponca City, Okla., where Conoco settled a lawsuit over pollution last July and is preparing to spend $18 million to buy 377 homes along the eastern boundary of its refinery there.
 
Reducing Risk, on Both Sides
 The property purchases are defended by company executives as a sensible approach to moving people out of harm's way. A consultant who helps design the programs said that in addition to reducing the companies' legal risk, it is also good citizenship.
Guy S. Barone, a spokesman for Dow's plant at Plaquemine, La., across the river from Baton Rouge, said: "We have a large manufacturing plant and we have operated it safely. At the same time there is a risk of an acute accident and we want to protect people living adjacent to the fence lines."
Michael J. Lythcott is a consultant for Moran, Stahl & Boyer, the New York relocation company that helped design the program in Ponca City for Conoco and Dow's effort in Morrisonville, a small community adjacent to the plant in Plaquemine. He said that relocation was a new trend in the chemical and oil industries and that such programs were being planned in California, Louisiana, Virginia and West Virginia, though he declined to identify the companies involved.
"From a business sense, it is economically sound," he said. "There are potential toxic torts and if there is an accident it has an impact on worldwide sales. It makes sense in putting a program together instead of waiting for an accident, and lives to be lost." He acknowledged that one problem with relocation is that companies cannot "compensate people for emotional attachment to land or their homes."
 
Criticism by Environmentalists
 Environmental leaders have criticized Dow's program and several others. "Companies are reducing their problems by moving people instead of reducing accidents and pollution," said Mary Lee Orr, the executive director of the Louisiana Environment Action Network, a coalition of environmental groups based in Baton Rouge.
Property owners are also divided by the buyout projects. Most who sold their homes did not have much in the first place and view the offers as the break they needed to move into new and larger homes. But there is also anguish in some communities as neighbors who have lived together for decades are separated.
Nowhere is this more true than in Plaquemine, along the western banks of the Mississippi River just south of here, where Dow is breaking up Morrisonville, a community of 87 white and black property owners, and 110 homes, that was founded after the Civil War by freed slaves.
Morrisonville is situated on 200 acres along the eastern boundary of the 1,800-acre plant, Dow's third largest. The site is the second for Morrisonville. In 1931 it was moved a mile and a half west after the Army Corps of Engineers refused to continue maintaining the levees that protected the town, then 50 years old, from the Mississippi River.
In May 1989, Dow began a novel program to pay landowners $20,000 an acre, and homeowners at least $50,000, and up to $200,000, to get control of the ground for a safety zone.
Dow also offered a minimum of $10,000 to resettle families who rented homes in the town, and it built a subdivision nearly four miles down river where some of Morrisonville's families could move into new brick homes and establish another community.
Most of Morrisonville's families accepted Dow's offer for their property, but only 12 chose to move to the new subdivision, Morrisonville Estates. Doretha Thompson, 78 years old, said she received $80,000 for her worn wooden house no bigger than a sharecropper cabin, and her move to the subdivision is just weeks away.
 
'We Didn't Hold Together'
 "We didn't hold together like I thought we might," she said while helping friends clean mustard greens just picked from the garden. "Now some are down the river, some across, some in town. White and black are scattering."
Hilda King, 54, Ms. Thompson's neighbor and a lifelong Morrisonville resident, also sold her house to Dow and moved to Morrisonville Estates last month. "I got away from all those chemicals, all that noise," said Ms. King, showing a visitor around her new three-bedroom, one-and-a-half-bath home.
Perhaps 20 other Morrisonville families have remained in their homes, saying Dow's offer is not generous enough. "Dow doesn't pay for attachment to land, for the inheritance that is in this community," said Rosa L. Martin, who lives with her husband, Jack, in a modern brick house so close to the plant that hissing compressors and commands over the plant loudspeakers can be heard inside.
"This spot is worth more to me than anywhere," Mr. Martin, 63, a retired Air Force computer and electronics instructor, said of the community his father joined at the turn of the century."Dow doesn't understand that, but we're still talking."
An explosion on May 5, 1988, at the Shell refinery and chemical complex in Norco, La., just north of New Orleans, killed seven workers and has cost the company at least $40 million in damage claims, with more than 20,000 claims still to be settled.
Shell is buying property from homeowners near the plant, but Norman Altstedter, a company spokesman, said the purchases have been going on for 20 years. "It has been a continuing effort to be a good neighbor," said Mr. Altstedter, "and has not been in response to any industrial accidents or the explosion."
 
$4 Million to Move 110 Homes
 Exxon has spent $4 million to buy and remove 110 homes and businesses in the neighborhoods along the southern and eastern boundaries of its enormous refinery and chemical complex along the river here.
Some of the homes were damaged last Christmas Eve by an explosion at the refinery that killed two workers, injured seven and generated 8,000 damage claims.
Walter J. Eldredge, an Exxon executive, said the company wants to establish a greenbelt around the plant by buying homes and converting them to empty lots. He said the project was started before the accident and was intended to beautify the area.
"I don't believe that," said Curtis Brown Jr., 18, who lives with his parents, a brother and a sister in the Standard Heights neighborhood south of the refinery. "They're scared of another tank blowing up or gas coming over here. When that tank blew up I thought an airplane had fallen on the house. People were running down the street holding their heads."
Mr. Brown said his father declined an offer of $18,000 for the house. "You can't buy a house with kind of money," he said.
Across the Mississippi from Baton Rouge and five miles south of the Dow plant, Georgia Gulf completed its program last year to move 50 families from Reveilletown, another black community formed by freed slaves after the Civil War.
The six-acre parcel that was Reveilletown (pronounced re-VEAL-town) is now a grove of oaks and pecan trees spreading over clipped grass.
 
Exits Began After '87 Suit
 Edward A. Schmitt, the general manager, said Georgia Gulf began buying Reveilletown property in the mid-1970's, only a few years after the chemical plant was built in 1969. "We didn't need the six acres for expansion, but it serves as buffer zone if there was a major release," he said.
A lawsuit over contamination and health complaints in 1987 by 13 Reveilletown property owners was settled a year later and hastened the town's end. The owners who sued moved away after receiving cash. Twenty other families then agreed to sell to Georgia Gulf for $1.2 million. Twelve families moved two miles north to a subdivision near Morrisonville Estates that has come to be called New Reveilletown.
"We have to stop these relocations," said Les Ann Kirkland, a leader of AWARE, a local environmental group. "They're going to slip into normalcy. We can't keep putting price tags on people's history. Instead of removing the people, remove the risk."
Allen Craig, 77, and his wife Mary, 69, are the last Reveilletown residents. They were left behind in a legal dispute with the company handling relocations, but they are hoping to go, too. "I want to leave because everybody else went," said Mrs. Craig.

HEADLINE: After Decades of Rises, Rents Sag in Much of New York City

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
For the first time since the Great Depression, apartment rents are dropping in much of New York City.
Real-estate professionals say rents have dropped at least 5 percent to 10 percent -- and in some cases as much as 20 percent and more -- in a variety of city neighborhoods during the last three years. 
 Rents on luxury apartments have dipped occasionally over the last half-century, but this is the first time that the number of apartments available for rent has begun to outstrip demand even in working-class and middle-class neighborhoods, said real-estate brokers, landlords and other housing specialists.
Ever since World War II, when the city's housing shortage began, a tight rental market and rising rents have been as central to New York's fabric as traffic, noise and glitter. The decline in rents has produced fundamental changes in many of the unwritten rules and rituals that have made finding a decent apartment a distinctive New York ordeal.
 
Haggling Is Possible
 No longer do prospective tenants routinely find it necessary to scour obituaries to discover vacant apartments, or bribe doormen, superintendents or managing agents to gain first rights to a vacancy.
Nor do landlords automatically increase rents to the maximum allowed under the rent-stabilization law, as they have for decades. On renewing a lease, landlords and tenants now often negotiate a rent increase. Landlords also say that when an apartment becomes vacant, not only must they often forgo the rent increase that is allowed by law, but they also cannot attract a new tenant without offering a rent below the previous level.
To people accustomed to the traditional New York housing market, in which landlords held most of the leverage, the new world of renting can be startling.
Thomas Lynch, an investment banker, is moving from Boston back to New York this week after spending seven years away. In the city he remembers, a good apartment was so hard to find that "people actually paid off doormen to find out who had died in the building," Mr. Lynch said.
When he began looking for an apartment two months ago, he said, he discovered immediately that things had changed. The first broker he visited took him into a small room, in which three of the four walls were covered with small filing cards with information on apartments that were available immediately. Because he was looking for an apartment seven or eight weeks before he intended to move in, the broker suggested that he postpone his search.
A few weeks later Mr. Lynch looked at apartments for several days. "I found it very easy to find a lot of apartments," he said.
He selected a small one-bedroom apartment with a private garden in a building on East 64th Street, off Fifth Avenue. He will pay $1,450 a month. The owner first put it on the market at $2,500 a month, Mr. Lynch said.
 
An Apartment Survey
 Apartments in Mr. Lynch's new neighborhood and on the Upper West Side have carried rents averaging about 10 percent lower than those for similar apartments in 1987, a survey by the Feathered Nest, a Manhattan real-estate brokerage, found.
Two-bedroom apartments in good Bronx neighborhoods like Pelham Parkway and along parts of the Grand Concourse, which had rented for $650 a month two years ago, are now renting for $600 to $615 a month, said Michael Laub, a partner in a real-estate company that owns and manages 70 buildings in Washington Heights and the Bronx.
Some Manhattan developers are so hard-pressed for tenants that they have begun to pay the real-estate broker's fee, ranging from one month's rent to 15 percent of the total first-year rent. New York renters have traditionally paid the broker's fee.
The decline in rents does not mean that New York apartments are now a bargain, at least compared with most American cities. It also does not auger much relief for low-income New Yorkers, for whom the shortage of decent, inexpensive housing has long been most acute. Rents in poor neighborhoods have remained stable, housing specialists said.
But the overall decline does reflect a significant softening of the rental market, many owners and brokers say. The last time any New Yorkers experienced rent decreases was in the early 1970's, but then it was limited only to the most expensive apartments, real-estate professionals said.
If sustained, the changes in the rental market might lead to revisions in New York's system of rent regulations. By law, rent control and rent stabilization remain in effect as long as the city's overall vacancy rate is below 5 percent. If it goes above that figure, the regulations are lifted.
No one knows how much the overall vacancy rate has changed in the last three years, or, despite the decline in rents, whether it has changed at all. In the most recent city survey, released in 1988, the rate was 2.46 percent. The next survey will be next year, city housing officials said.
Some landlords, who have long sought a phasing out of rent control and rent stabilization, have begun citing the decline in rents to bolster their argument. They contend that some owners, squeezed by higher costs and falling rents, will be forced to abandon their buildings.
"The market is proving to be an excellent regulator," said Richard Esposito, a partner in Prana Investments Inc., which owns and manages 18 buildings in Harlem.
Tenant groups have long contended that any relaxation of the rent regulations would most harm the poor and elderly, who already are paying disproportionately large shares of their income for housing. Tenants are certain to oppose any changes.
 
Rent Stabilization
 Rent stabilization covers 840,000 apartments, generally in buildings constructed between 1947 and 1974 and those built after 1974 with city tax abatements. Tenants in 140,000 more apartments are covered by separate rent-control rules if they have lived for at least 19 years in buildings constructed before 1947.
Of course, not every apartment is cheaper than it was a year or two ago. New York is a huge city, with many sub-markets. Some developers contended that rents had remained stable in fashionable Manhattan neighborhoods, and they questioned whether the Feathered Nest survey measured actual rents or just initial asking prices. Nancy Packes, the president of the Feathered Nest, said her report surveyed actual rents.
Louis Winnick, a housing economist who has closely studied the Sunset Park section of Brooklyn, said rents had not risen or fell in that neighborhood. But in the adjacent Park Slope section, rents have declined 5 to 10 percent in the last few months, said Bryan P. Gay, a real-estate broker.
"It's no longer just a phenomenon at the top end of the market, but has spread itself through the entire system," said John J. Gilbert 3d, president of the Rent Stabilization Association, the largest owners' group.
Richard LeFrak, president of the Lefrak Organization, a leading real-estate concern, said the reasons for the decline were clear: "The laws of supply and demand are at work. It's not that complicated."
The faltering economy has diminished people's willingness to pay high rents at the upper end, where the retrenchment on Wall Street has sharply altered spending habits, and at the middle of the market, in working-class and middle-class housing.
At the same time the weak sales market in cooperative and condominium apartments has stimulated interest in rentals. Many affluent New Yorkers who might ordinarily buy an apartment have decided to rent temporarily because they think sale prices will continue to drop.
 
Renting Instead of Buying
 But a substantial increase in supply offsets any increase in demand, real-estate professionals said. Some of the new rentals are the result of the building boom of the 1980's. Some new buildings were constructed to be rentals, but many were built as co-ops or condos. Some developers, unable to sell their new apartments, have begun renting them instead.
In addition, thousands of individual co-op and condo apartments are being offered as rentals by their owners, often after owners failed to sell them. Many speculators who bought several condo apartments during the roaring 80's are also being forced to rent them in the retrenched 90's.
The rental oversupply is most pronounced at the high end. Two-bedroom apartments in rent-stabilized buildings without doormen on Manhattan's Upper West Side are renting for an average of $1,750 a month, the Feathered Nest found, while similar apartments were renting for an average of $2,150 a month last year.
One-bedroom apartments in newer, unregulated buildings with doormen on the Upper East Side are renting for an average of $1,850 a month this year, according to the survey, while similar apartments rented for about $2,000 a month last year.
Many rent-stabilized building owners in Queens, the Bronx and Upper Manhattan said that when apartments became vacant, they often had to lower rents to find tenants. Under this year's rent-stabilization guidelines, they are entitled to raise the rent on vacant apartments by nearly 10 percent for a one-year lease.
While a boon to consumers, the market shift could hurt some landlords. Rents are declining just as owners are being hit with large cost increases on everything from property taxes to water and sewer bills to heating oil. Many landlords predicted that the financial squeeze would force some owners to cut services and ultimately abandon their buildings.
"This certainly doesn't spell out a positive future for rental housing in New York City," said Mr. Gilbert of the Rent Stabilization Association.
