HEADLINE: Pay Abuses in Border Garment Shops

BYLINE: By LISA BELKIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: EL PASO, Nov. 21

 BODY:
On the last Friday in May, Amelia Lambarena reported, as usual, to her job at Diana's Fashions workshop here. But on that day she did not sew a single stitch. The doors were locked, all the machinery had been removed in the night, and there was no sign of the owner, who owed her $700 in back pay.
Through an efficient grapevine she learned that the entire operation had been transported down the block and given a new name: El Paso International Apparel. So she and other stranded employees walked together to the new location, where they confronted the owner of the old shop. He denied that he owned this new operation and said he would call the police if they did not leave. They went home without their money. 
 In El Paso, one of the largest garment-producing cities in the country, contractors who close shops without paying, then reopen under other names, are increasingly common, according to several Government officials and advocates for these workers. Even more common are shops that do not close, but simply refuse to pay their employees for months at a time.
At one point last month the United States Department of Labor estimated that 337 employees were owed $87,330 by 18 El Paso garment industry employers.
It is a symptom, officials say, of the problems of an industry that is becoming steadily less profitable and of a group of workers, mostly poor immigrant Hispanic women who are in the process of applying for legal residency and who are too frightened of deportation to complain.
"The problem is nationwide," said Susan Cowell, vice president of the International Ladies' Garment Worker's Union, "particularly in areas where there are a lot of immigrants, like Los Angeles, Miami, New York and San Francisco."
 
'It's the Isolation'
 But the practice of garment industry employers who do not pay their employees appears to be particularly entrenched in El Paso.
"It's the isolation," explained Jeff Hermanson, organizing director for the union, which has taken an interest in the problems of workers applying for amnesty. "El Paso is a long way from the centers of power. It's a lawless atmosphere, and some of the employers have an attitude of lawlessness."
Mr. Hermanson, based in New York City, was echoing a description that is often used by those here who complain that legal protections against such abuses often are not enforced.
Cutting and sewing employs more than 15,000 of El Paso's 500,000 people, according to the Texas Employment Commission. It is estimated that 120 shops operate at any given time, completing contracts ordered by large manufacturers with familiar labels.
These thousands of people and hundreds of small factories are all but invisible to the unknowing observer, however. Most of the shops employ 50 to 150 people each and are hidden behind the unmarked doors of warehouses and houses that look abandoned.
Small shops like these are particularly vulnerable to economic hard times. "As contracts dry up, a lot of these little manufacturers start going under," said Ray Brito, assistant director of the Department of Labor's Wage and Hour Division office here. He said the problem had worsened in the last year.
 
'I'll Be O.K.'
 "But they refuse to face reality," he said. "They're like players at the craps table in Las Vegas, trying to hang on, thinking, 'If I just get one more contract, I'll be O.K.' "
As a result, Mr. Brito said, "they promise money they know they can't pay. And the workers stay and take it."
Many workers fear their employers will sabotage their applications for legal residency status if they complain, he and other officials said. Other workers keep quiet in hopes that they will be paid if they do not make waves.
In recent weeks, some have begun to fight back. A group called La Mujer Obrera, Spanish for "the working woman," has been tracking factories and owners since May. The group, made up mostly of factory workers, has picketed several and provided assistance to employees who are owed money.
 
Stronger Measures
 This month the employees of a factory called Sonia's held a daylong work stoppage, which ended after La Mujer Obrera negotiated a loan from a local religious group to pay wages owed to workers. The workers also obtained a written agreement, drawn up by a lawyer, in which the owner promises they will be allowed to sell his property and keep the proceeds if future wages are not paid.
The Federal Government has also become involved. A team from the Department of Labor visited 38 factories this summer and found wage violations at nearly half. Mr. Brito said the owners of those factories face fines if the problems are not corrected. Of the factories' 337 workers who were unpaid as of early fall, Mr. Brito estimates that 87 have received a total of $40,000, about half the wages owed, since then.
But employers, employees, government officials and advocates for the workers all agree that only a few factory owners are deliberately abusing their employees. Others, they say, are caught in a changing industry and are managing those changes badly.
Until 10 years ago, the garment factories of El Paso provided stable, well-paid work. They were owned by the nation's largest clothing manufacturers, including Farah and Levi Strauss. Then foreign competition increased and manufacturers found it more economical to close their plants and contract for much of their piecework.
The result was a patchwork of smaller contractors that pay minimum wage without providing any medical benefits and that set production quotas for workers. Never particularly stable, their businesses' futures have become even more questionable as threats of a recession have sharply curtailed contracts from larger manufacturers.
At TNT Manufacturing here, the owner, Rogelio Tinajero, described the last two months as being "like a roller coaster -- up and down." He said that until this summer his two-year-old company was regularly receiving orders for 1,000 garments, but that in recent months the orders had been for only several hundred pieces.
In an interview he said that he was afraid he would not be able to meet his next payroll and that he might have to close the factory. Soon after, the company laid off 38 workers and reduced the hours of the 12 who remained.
 
Caught by Change
 The changes in the industry come at a time when other changes are taking place in the workers' lives. Most factory employees are Hispanic; a majority entered the United States illegally and are in the process of applying for amnesty under the Immigration Reform and Control Act, according to Cecelia Rodriguez, head of La Mujer Obrera.
One qualification for amnesty is that workers must show proof of steady employment, and this may be in the form of pay stubs or a letter from an employer. Because of this, many employees continue working for employers who do not pay them on time, if at all, fearing that the employer will rescind certification that he has already signed.
Which is why Prisiliano Valdez, an employee of Diana's who was owed wages when it closed, continued to work for the man he says is the owner, Hector Gutierrez, at El Paso International Apparel.
Mr. Valdez said in an interview here that the day before Diana's closed, he was handed a slip of paper with the address of El Paso International and told to report there the following day. "Only certain people got them," he said. "I guess the ones who wouldn't complain."
 
'He Is the Boss'
 When he arrived at the new location, he said, he recognized the sewing machines with which he stitched the thick edges of pockets on denim jeans and the same shorts on which he had been working. He said that Mr. Gutierrez was at the new location and that he "acted like he is the boss."
Like Mrs. Lambarena, he said, he was owed money from the old factory. Despite that, he said, he continued to work for the new factory because "I wanted to have a job and I had hopes that he would pay me." Five weeks later, he said, he found another job and left El Paso International. He still had not been paid for his work at Diana's.
At El Paso International a man who said he was Mr. Gutierrez said he did not own the company, but merely managed it. He said he could not say who the owner was. When asked further questions, he said he did not have time to talk "because I'm working." He said that he would not be able to talk later in the day and that he could not receive calls at work because "there is no phone." The pay telephone on the wall behind him, he said, does not receive incoming calls, and he said he did not have a telephone at home.
Several weeks later, in response to a complaint from La Mujer Obrera, some of the machines at El Paso International were impounded by the sheriff and auctioned for $1,500, which will be divided among the workers who are owed back wages by Diana's.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
DEMOCRATS PRESS BUSH TO PUT OFF MILITARY ACTION

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 27

 BODY:
With the United Nations Security Council due to approve a resolution later this week authorizing the use of military force against Iraq any time after Jan. 15, Senate Democrats urged the Bush Administration today to delay taking military action and give economic sanctions more time to work.
As the Senate Armed Services Committee opened hearings on the Persian Gulf crisis, Senator Sam Nunn, the chairman of the panel, once again questioned the need for rushing into combat with Iraq. Senator Nunn's caution was generally echoed by the nine other Democrats at the hearing. 
 Some conservative Republicans expressed concern that the hearing was playing into the hands of President Saddam Hussein of Iraq by suggesting that the United States lacked the will to use military force and disclosing that there were divisions in the United States.
 
'Lighting Our Own Fuse'
 "Time is working on our side," Senator Nunn said, suggesting that economic sanctions would be effective in prompting President Hussein to withdraw Iraqi troops from Kuwait.
"We are lighting our own fuse," the Georgia Democrat complained to reporters after the hearing, saying that the Administration had limited its options by building up a large military force in the gulf and shelving plans for troop rotations.
In Baghdad today, Foreign Minister Tariq Aziz expressed defiance in the face of efforts to make Baghdad's forces pull out of Kuwait. Mr. Aziz dismissed the importance of the proposed United Nations resolution that would authorize the use of force to oust Iraq from Kuwait, saying that his country would "never succumb to pressure." [Page A14.]
 
A List of Questions
 The Congressional hearings, which grew out of concern that President Bush would go to war without consulting Congress, represent an effort by Mr. Nunn and his Senate colleagues to influence American policy even as the Bush Administration continues to threaten Iraq with a winter war unless Iraqi forces leave Kuwait.
Senator Nunn, whose views on military issues carry great weight in the Senate, began the hearing by reading a list of questions that he said should guide the national debate over possible offensive action against Iraq.
"The question is not whether military action is justified; it is," Mr. Nunn said. "The question is whether military action is wise at this time and in our own national interest."
"Is it in our vital interest to liberate Kuwait through military action by a largely American military force?" he asked. "Will economic sanctions force Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait?"
"How long can we sustain over 400,000 American troops in Saudi Arabia," Mr. Nunn continued.
"Should our military objectives extend beyond the restoration of Kuwaiti sovereignty to the elimination of key aspects of Iraq's offensive military capability, particularly their ongoing nuclear efforts?"
"How durable is the multinational coalition?" he also asked.
Representative Lee H. Hamilton of Indiana, the second-ranking Democrat on the House Foreign Affairs Committee, said separately that President Bush, through an envoy, should initiate talks with President Hussein.
The talks, he said at a breakfast meeting with reporters, should not be for negotiations but rather to explain United States intentions in the Middle East, which Mr. Hamilton said might not be clear to the Iraqi President.
 
Schlesinger Testifies
 The first witness today, James R. Schlesinger, Defense Secretary in the Ford and Nixon Administrations, told the Committee that economic sanctions would be successful in forcing Iraqi troops to leave Kuwait if only the Administration would give them time to work.
Mr. Schlesinger, who also served as Director of Central Intelligence, said that an early "official estimate," which he suggested committee members would hear more about in a closed-door intelligence briefing later in the day, held that it would take a year for the sanctions to take effect. "It now appears to be working more rapidly than anticipated," Mr. Schlesinger said.
"In three months time, civilian production in Iraq is estimated to have declined by some 40 percent," he said. "Oil exports are essentially nil and export earning have dropped correspondingly. The hoard of hard currency, necessary to sustain smuggling, is dwindling away. The economic pressure can only grow worse."
But Mr. Schlesinger asserted that the effectiveness of economic sanctions would be diminished if President Hussein believed that the underlying American goal was not just to liberate Kuwait but also to eliminate Iraq's military ability or remove Mr. Hussein from power.
 
'Sound Advice,' Nunn Says
 He also asserted that an American military victory over Iraq "may result in an enmity directed at the United States for an extended period, not only by Iraq and its present supporters, but ultimately among the publics of some of the nations now allied to us."
Senator Nunn said in an interview later that Mr. Schlesinger had provided "sound advice" that paralleled his own counsel to the Administration.
"The real cost is lives," said Senator Richard C. Shelby, an Alabama Democrat, indicating agreement with the thrust of Mr. Schlesinger's presentation.
Senator Carl Levin, Democrat of Michigan, said of economic sanctions in a statement, "Instead of eliminating troop rotations and adding huge numbers of American forces, the President should be displaying American staying power for the long haul."
 
Glenn Expresses Concern
 Senator John Glenn, the Ohio Democrat, also expressed concern that President Bush would order an attack "before the embargo has had time to really bite and to be effective."
Senator Nunn said he agreed with the Administration's decision to seek a United Nations resolution authorizing force to build pressure on Iraq.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Carrier's Pilots Confidently Face War

BYLINE: By JAMES LeMOYNE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ABOARD THE U.S.S. MIDWAY, off Saudi Arabia, Nov. 26

 BODY:
The F-18 Hornet fighters catapulted off the Midway's deck in a scream of engines and a shimmering pall of jet exhaust.
Flight crews ran to and fro on this 45-year-old carrier, lining up fighters for a takeoff assisted by a power catapult that hurtled the planes off the deck so fast that they accelerated from zero to 150 miles per hour in two seconds. 
 At the last instant, the fighters shot into space, where, like supersonic knuckleballs, they briefly wobbled and floated before suddenly roaring up into the clear winter skies of Arabia.
The operation gave an impression of enormous raw power harnessed to hurl warplanes into the sky from the squat platform of the Navy's smallest and oldest aircraft carrier. During the Vietnam war, her planes flew tens of thousands of sorties against Hanoi when she operated in the South China Sea.
 
'Land on a Postage Stamp'
 The sleek fighters returned as furiously as they departed, slamming down at nearly full power on just 190 feet of deck, considerably less than on longer, wider and newer carriers. A hook trailing the aircraft snags a lateral deck cable, jerking them to a shuddering stop and often leaving the pilot staring over the edge of the deck into the sea far below.
"You fly through the eye of a needle and land on a postage stamp," said Lieut. Dave Leppelmeier as he described landing on a pitching runway.
The Midway, which was commissioned in September 1945 and survived Congressional proposals to scrap it a few years ago, is part of what will soon be the largest deployment of American warships since World War II, according to Rear Adm. Daniel P. March, commander of the Carrier Group Five Strike Force of the Seventh Fleet.
There are three United States naval battle groups, each led by an aircraft carrier, already in the region. They will be joined by three more battle groups under orders from President Bush.
 
More Ships Than Used in Vietnam
 In addition to a carrier, a battle group includes computerized missile-bearing destroyers and frigates, among other ships. Admiral March estimated that there would be well over 60 American warships in the theater within the next month, more ships than were deployed at any one time during the Vietnam War, he said.
The ships from the United States appear to have sealed these seas to Iraqi shipping, senior officers said.
Capt. J. W. Parker, commander of Destroyer Squadron 15, said that since Nov. 1 the Midway-led flotilla of eight ships has been intercepting about 100 boats per day by radio to check their nationality, cargo and destination.
He said no Iraqi-flagged ship nor Iraqi-bound ship had tried to enter these seas, which are now restricted under the United Nations embargo.
 
Ship Destinations Monitored
 "I think Iraq has probably come to the conclusion it can't get in this way," Captain Parker told a group of reporters visiting the Midway today.
Captain Parker did not rule out that ships bound for Iran could unload material to be shipped overland to Iraq. But officers said such traffic would appear to be minimal. They added that every passing ship is watched to be sure that it does not try to unload in either Iraq or Iraqi-occupied Kuwait.
His account would appear to indicate that the maritime embargo against Iraq, while slow to affect the Iraqi economy, is as effective as Navy ships can make it.
 
Iran Cooperating With U.S. Ships
 And officers aboard the Midway said Iran appeared to be making an effort to cooperate with Navy forces in the Persian Gulf and the Gulf of Oman.
In what seemed to be a strong sign of neutrality in the surging military deployment, Iranian surveillance aircraft and helicopters have taken special care to make radio contact, identify themselves and then maintain distant positions requested by American commanders as the Midway recently steamed through the narrow Strait of Hormuz, the officers say.
Capt. James M. Burin, commander of the carrier's fighter air wing, said Iraqi planes and radar made no attempt to make contact with American fighters deployed in the exercise, indicating a continuing Iraqi policy of caution.
The Midway carries a crew of 4,300 and some 60 aircraft, including approximately 30 of the Navy's newest F-18 jet fighters. It is guarded 24 hours a day by jet fighters, special radar and electronic-jamming planes, air surveillance aircraft and armed helicopters, as well as by accompanying destroyers armed with missiles and radar systems that can pick up an oncoming aircraft hundreds of miles away.
The pilots of the Midway's F-18 fighter squad said they are on full alert and combat ready. They trade fighter radio call signs such as "Lefty," "Big Wave Dave" and "Bones" to one another as they whip past the Midway's viewing bridge, called "Vultures Row" by gawking sailors who sometimes watch the aerial show.
 
Iraq Pilots Get High Marks
 "Things are changing so fast right now," said Lieut. Comdr. Jeff Ashby, one of the Midway's F-18 pilots. "We don't know from day to day how they will go." .
Others appeared to step over the line separating confidence from cockiness.
"I just refuse to believe that a third-world person who rides a camel can be as smart as I am," declared Lieutenant Leppelmeier, an F-18 pilot, referring to Iraqi pilots.
Saudi pilots who have studied the Iraqi airforce speak with greater prudence. They give Iraqi pilots high marks for having combat experience and for being skilled with laser-guided weapons and jamming devices.
Almost all crew members interviewed said they hoped Iraq would pull out of Kuwait and avoid a war, although all said they were ready to fight if the time for fighting comes. A heady few, like Lieutenant Leppelmeier, said they were looking forward to battle.
"Sure," the F-18 pilot replied when asked if he wanted to fight. "I'm 30, can't keep a girlfriend and can't save a dime."

HEADLINE: Shop? Many Say, Only if I Must

BYLINE: By TRISH HALL

 BODY:
"SHOP till you drop" may have been the mind-set for the 1980's. But these days, a new phenomenon is emerging: the shopping dropout.
With the crucial holiday selling period under way, retailers are blaming the sagging economy for forecasts that range from cautious to bleak. But interviews with scores of market researchers and shoppers suggest that growing numbers of people have become averse to shopping even if they have the money to buy. They cite lack of time, dissatisfaction with service and merchandise, and changed priorities. 
 "In the 1970's, shopping was entertainment," said Laura Petrucci, a partner in the management consulting group at the Marketing Corporation of America in Westport, Conn. "In the 1980's, it was the quest for trendy merchandise. In the 1990's, it's a mission: find it, buy it and get out."
These purposeful buyers, unlike the shoppers who foraged for fun in years past, may be less open to the seductive displays and promotions that retailers rely on to bring about impulse purchases. Several surveys in the last year show mounting disaffection for shopping at the same time that some of the nation's most prestigious retailing chains have been struggling to survive the effects of debt-financed changes in ownership.
Now, with the industry facing difficult times, consultants like Ms. Petrucci say stores must become more sensitive to the barriers that make shopping a chore: cumbersome security devices attached to clothes, too few clerks, limits on the number of items that can be taken into dressing rooms, inconvenient hours and confusing or inefficient store design.
Some shoppers agree. "In a department store, the process of trying something on and paying for it takes an unbelievable amount of time," said Dea Breitman, a publicity agent in Los Angeles. She prefers shopping in discount stores, not only because of the prices but because she finds the merchandise is displayed better.
Retailing chains continue to expand selling space and plan new stores on the assumption that when the economy improves, shoppers will return in droves. "But we think attitudes have changed sufficiently so that's not going to happen," said Barbara Caplan, a vice president at Yankelovich Clancy Shulman, market researchers in Westport. "The emphasis now is more on life than style."
In an annual survey of 2,500 adults, Yankelovich found this year that even among people who said they liked to shop for clothes, the percentage who found the experience frustrating and time-consuming rose to 66 percent, from 60 percent in 1989. The respondents were interviewed in person for the survey, which had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus two percentage points.
Women's attitudes toward clothes shopping have big consequences for retailers because their apparel purchases dwarf those made by men. But in the Yankelovich survey, changed feelings were evident.
The number who said they liked to shop for clothes dropped to 44 percent this year from 47 percent last year. Although that is not a major change, Ms. Caplan said it is striking considering that 55 percent would rather shop for food. Ms. Caplan said she believes that is because consumers are very likely to find what they want, and fast, in a grocery store.
Other surveys show similar attitudes, and not just about clothes shopping. In a November poll of 1,255 adults for Business Week by Louis Harris & Associates, nearly half of the respondents said they spend less time shopping than they did five years ago. Only 6 percent described shopping as their favorite leisure activity, while 63 percent said it was drudgery, or worse. The survey had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points.
The attempt to limit the time spent shopping was especially evident among the 25 women whose incomes average $120,000 and who share their thoughts on shopping each year with the Marketing Corporation of America. Fifteen of them buy Paul Mitchell shampoo, Ms. Petrucci said, not because they are so attached to it but because it is sold at their hair salons.
While market researchers consult with a variety of retail clients, many chains do not avail themselves of these insights. Representatives for Macy's, Bloomingdale's, Bergdorf Goodman, Saks Fifth Avenue, Nordstrom and Neiman Marcus said their stores commission little or no research on consumer attitudes. They said they kept in touch through the daily give and take on the sales floor.
"The relationship between the customer and the sales associate is a very personal one," said Jan Roberts, a spokeswoman for Neiman Marcus, which is based in Dallas. Beyond that, computerized inventory systems also tell store executives what merchandise is selling well.
Many of these stores recognize that an unserved customer may never come back. Their chief responses have been a return to commissions to motivate salespeople and a new emphasis on the personal shopper, a salesperson who will select items for a busy customer or simply help navigate the store.
Saks Fifth Avenue, for instance, has increased the staff of its One on One personal-shopping service in the last year, from 10 to 18. "When you have nine floors of a store, sometimes it is very frustrating," said Nancy Mickelson, manager of the service at the Saks in Manhattan.
But the market researchers say retailers need to look at the way people's lives and levels of tolerance have changed.
"We recently did a focus group in a mall, and one of the major complaints was that the tags were hidden inside the clothes," said Judith K. Langer, who has her own market research company in New York. But, she added, the stores' managers insisted that was necessary because the merchandise would look too messy with tags hanging out.
Small irritants like that loom large for harried consumers. "We're finding that people want to conserve time to spend with their families," said Jeffrey St. Clair, who manages consulting projects for Retail Planning Associates, in Columbus, Ohio. "I think that's putting pressure on stores."
It is certainly putting pressure on shoppers like Barbara Wankoff, who holds a job and has a young child, a combination that leaves her little spare time. Interviewed this month at the new Nordstrom in Paramus, N.J., a clothing store she praised for its unobtrusive but efficient service, she said: "My life is more hectic than it used to be. I have to go in and out."
Lives like hers, says the Direct Marketing Association in New York, have helped fuel a 60 percent increase since 1983 in the number of people who shop by mail or phone. Retailers have learned from the success of the catalogue merchants, and they are increasingly offering mail-order shopping themselves, said Chet Dalzell, the association's spokesman.
Some retailers reject the notion that people are shopping less because they have less time for it. "We always have time for the things we like and we don't have time for the things we don't," said Ralph Holt, a divisional vice president of marketing for Lazarus, a department store chain based in Cincinnati. He believes that changes in attitudes about the importance of clothing are a more likely cause of sluggish sales.
But the conviction that people want to help themselves, and fast, was crucial in the design of the year-old Abraham & Straus store in Manhattan, where executives say sales have exceeded expectations. Signs are clear and simple, the layout is open, and the store is relatively small. "As you ride the escalator, you can see where everything is," said John Hoeller, vice president and general manager of the store.
Target, a discount chain with a wide variety of merchandise that has stores in much of the country, used the responses from 80,000 customer-comment cards received each year to design its new store in Apple Valley, Minn., a suburb of St. Paul, to allow for efficient self-service, similar to that provided in a supermarket. It has wide aisles, clearly displayed signs, plenty of cashiers and easy return policies. Items are displayed the way people buy rather than the way a store's buyers buy.
"We put shampoo and hair dryers and combs together," said Gail Dorn, a spokeswoman for the company. "We put the shoelaces with the shoes," rather than in the toiletries or notions section, which is common.
Traditional department stores often make self-service difficult, perhaps because luxury stores believe that their customers prefer cosseting to convenience and because financially strapped stores must make do with curtailed staffs while being vigilant about costly inventory.
Myrna M. Egeth was recently shopping at Macy's in Manhattan, looking through clothes by Mary Ann Restivo. But because all the clothes are locked up with hoops and chains, requiring sales assistance, "I would be unlikely to ask to try something on," said Ms. Egeth, who lives in Manhattan.
A survey by Management Horizons, a retail-consulting company in Dublin, Ohio, indicates that Ms. Egeth's feeling is typical. Seventy percent of 500 shoppers surveyed said they would not ask to try on locked-up clothes. "It is a device that deters not only shoplifters, but shoppers," said Sharyn Brooks, vice president for consumer research.
But some shoppers remain unfazed by such obstacles. Fran Omero, who works in Manhattan and attends college part time, shops at Macy's on her lunch hour.
"I'm here every day," she said. "You picked a sick person. I think it's an outlet. It's better than drugs or alcohol or cheating on my husband."
But Ms. Omero, a recreational shopper that many retailers would consider the norm, is no longer typical, if she ever was. Market researchers say women are becoming more like men in their shopping habits, dispassionate about the experience and unwilling to tolerate inconvenience.
Ian Grant, a telecommunications engineer in New York who not only shops there but often in Philadelphia while visiting family on weekends, may be much closer to the average shopper today. "If they're not quick, I'll just leave the stuff on the counter and walk away," he said.
Speed is only part of the service problem. Shoppers, regardless of their income, are also looking for value, retailing analysts say, and cascading markdowns have made them cynical about pricing.
They are frustrated, too, when they cannot find what they want. That may happen more frequently now with an older population, said Walter K. Levy, a consultant in New York who specializes in retailing. "Customers have a greater sense of who they are," he said, "and they are less inclined to buy into the trend of the moment."
But that does not mean that people are not eager to buy. "There is a mismatch between customer need and what the stores are providing," Mr. Levy said. "There is a lot of latent demand out there that is not being satisfied."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Part of the ground floor of Saks Fifth Avenue in New York, which has increased its staff of personal shoppers. (Dith Pran/The New York Times) (pg. C8)

SECTION: Section A; Page 21; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 737 words

HEADLINE: IN THE NATION;
The Horse Before The Cart

BYLINE: By Tom Wicker

 BODY:
Here are three reports from the American Dream:
* Young people "fresh out of high school" are having a hard time finding "good wages and steady work," a New York Times survey discovered. Household incomes where the principal earner is under 25 fell by 19 percent in the decade ending in 1988. 
 * Ulster County, New York, officials have proposed an 82 percent property tax increase for 1991, on top of a 42 percent increase in 1990. Taxpayers, predictably, are up in arms.
* The Times reported recently that numerous working people in New York City, like many in Moscow, are having to share apartments or houses, being unable to find or afford their own.
Similarly grim examples are common everywhere, and you don't need three guesses to find the common denominator among them: a stumbling United States economy that even George Bush admits is "sluggish."
That's a mild word for an engine performing at far less than capacity, in which unemployment nearing 6 percent has come to be regarded as normal. Not too many years ago, 4 percent employment was the national goal; 3 percent was sometimes achieved, and many wage employees earned high incomes. Now the overall economy not only is producing too few jobs; the new service-dominated economy is providing too few good jobs at substantial wages.
One consequence is that too little revenue is being raised to finance governments confronted with strong, in some cases rising, demands for public services. A second consequence is that insufficient savings for the future are being accumulated -- and the cost of a good education system should be counted as a savings for the future.
Attempting to remedy this situation by reducing the Federal budget deficit puts the cart before the horse, despite claims from both parties and from President and Congress. What is needed, instead, is a bold program to expand aggregate demand -- to increase the purchasing power of the American people. That means jobs, putting people back to work.
To hear recent Presidents and Congresses tell it, the only economic enemies to be feared are inflation and budget deficits. Little is said about unemployment and underemployment and the millions of Americans too discouraged to seek a job. Yet these are the chief causes of shrinking demand and the consequent "sluggish" economy.
A program to expand employment and increase demand would get the horse out front where it belongs, pulling the cart along; because in the long run it would also reduce the Federal deficit, probably more than the recent "deal" or any other in prospect. That result would not be immediate, but neither will negotiated deficit reduction; and anyway, the American insistence on quick fixes and overnight results is one of the nation's most prevalent weaknesses.
As employment and demand increase, a stimulated economy will begin to produce greater revenues -- not only for the Federal Government but for starved states and cities. That will make possible expanded public investment in improved education -- perhaps the single greatest need for the American future -- and in such other necessities as redeemed and expanded infrastructure (highways, bridges, sewage and water systems, etc.).
Investment in such necessities -- they are more than merely desirable -- will also be useful beyond its effect on revenues. Expanded job opportunities for young people, for example, may have some good effect on crime rates. Better health care, particularly for children (in a country where an eighth of the population has no medical insurance and millions get no or inadequate treatment) will mean a healthier work force, just as better education means a more capable work force; and both will contribute to greater productivity and renewed American competitiveness.
Apologists for the recent budget deal claim that it should cause increased private investment because of a reduction in interest rates expected to follow reduced Federal borrowing. But there's been little evidence since the budget deal that the Federal Reserve can or will reduce rates sufficiently to produce the promised effect; and there's no proof, anyway, that lower rates, if realized, will cause business to invest in a sluggish economy.
What will cause increased private investment, as numerous economic studies show, is strong and growing demand. That can only come from high employment, good wages, a productive people.

HEADLINE: New 'S' and Old 'U' in U.S.S.R.

 BODY:
If Mikhail Gorbachev has his way, the post-Socialist Soviet Union will remain the U.S.S.R. By making it the Union of Sovereign Soviet Republics, he hopes to keep it from degenerating into disunion. Depending on what he means by the word sovereign, it's an outcome Americans can welcome. As the Bush Administration recognizes, the U.S. is better off helping a pluralist Soviet Union than encouraging many separate Soviet states.
Mr. Gorbachev's strategy for preventing a breakup makes sense: to negotiate a new treaty of union that devolves some powers from Moscow to the republics. A vote on his draft was blocked in the Russian parliament yesterday, pending fuller debate. But a treaty along these lines could reduce centrifugal forces in the republics. That would allay concern in Washington and elsewhere that the Soviet Union is about to come apart. 
 Full sovereignty is the rallying cry of independence movements throughout the Soviet Union. Understandably, it is the devout goal of the three Baltic republics, which never chose to be associated with Moscow. But elsewhere, leaders say what they really want is a bigger share of the power Mr. Gorbachev wants to keep. He says he's prepared to take power from the ministries in Moscow and hand it over to them.
Soviet republics already enjoy far more of the trappings of sovereignty than do America's states. Many have foreign ministers of their own, and Byelorussia and the Ukraine even have their own seats in the U.N. Despite these appearances, the Soviet Union is a unitary state, much more centralized than, say, the U.S.
Under Mr. Gorbachev's predecessors, the Politburo decided everything from the price of bread to who was party secretary in Sverdlovsk. Under these circumstances, devolution of power makes sense. But there have to be limits. If all the republics represented in the new Federation Council must approve changes in economic policy, tiny Turkmenistan could veto what mighty Russia wants.
And empowerment of the republics will raise fears among minorities who look to Moscow to protect them against domineering neighbors, like the Gagauz in Moldavia and the Armenians in Nagorno-Karabakh.
For Mr. Gorbachev to devolve control is to leave many decisions to the market. But transforming a command economy to a market economy leaves officials with lots of tough decisions to make -- and no stars to steer by.
Boris Yeltsin and other radicals appeal to nationalist sentiment by seeking greater authority from Moscow. They may be pushing themselves into a dilemma. If they accept the powers that Mr. Gorbachev may be prepared to concede, they'll saddle themselves with new, potentially unpopular responsibilities. To refuse his offer would undercut their own demands for greater sovereignty.
In time, a better balance of authority can be struck; what's missing so far is a structure that devolves actual decision-making. The Soviet Union can endure and prosper only if it pushes many decisions to the market and delegates others down to the republics, localities and the people. The strength of Mr. Gorbachev's union depends on how sincerely he means to share sovereignty.

HEADLINE: Mrs. Thatcher's Lengthened Shadow

 BODY:
Margaret Thatcher may be finished. But the selection of John Major to succeed her as Prime Minister suggests that Thatcherism is not. Mr. Major was her chosen heir. Of humble origins, he epitomizes her spirit of enterprise and individualism. And he was judged the most reliable custodian of her social and economic policies.
Mr. Major won by a surprisingly large margin over two opponents, Foreign Secretary Douglas Hurd and former Defense Secretary Michael Heseltine, whose challenge to Mrs. Thatcher last week impelled her to resign. Mr. Heseltine, who had been favored, suffered from the perception that he was impatient to rework parts of the Thatcher legacy. In particular he favors some state intervention to help British industry, anathema to both Mrs. Thatcher and Mr. Major. 
 Mr. Major is not, however, a Thatcher clone. He has neither her ideological rigidity nor her abrasive manner. And he is certainly shrewd enough to know that Mrs. Thatcher's sudden fall -- and his own meteoric rise -- owes much to her habit of confusing disagreement with disloyalty. He became Foreign Secretary when Mrs. Thatcher fired Sir Geoffrey Howe because he challenged her on Europe. And he became Chancellor of the Exchequer when Mrs. Thatcher brutally undercut Nigel Lawson on the same issue.
Furthermore, on the two issues that brought Mrs. Thatcher down -- her resistance to Britain's integration into the European Community and her regressive poll tax -- Mr. Major has indicated a willingness to listen to competing views.
But for the most part, Americans can expect little change in Britain's policies. Mr. Major's belief in free markets matches Mrs. Thatcher's. He is equally warm in his admiration for the U.S. Indeed, he strongly supported the Cabinet's decision Sunday to add 14,000 more British troops to the forces deployed against Saddam Hussein.
Britain's longest-lasting Prime Minister in this century has now been replaced by its youngest. Mr. Major's path won't be easy. Britain suffers from both recession and rising prices. And he must heal his divided party in time for the general election scheduled for mid-1992. But his chances of doing so are surely greater than Mrs. Thatcher's would have been. So are his chances of preserving her legacy.

HEADLINE: We Can't Afford More Delay on Hudson River Waterfront Park

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The defeat of New York State's environmental bond (news story, Nov. 8) eliminated the source the state planned for its contribution to the Hudson River waterfront park. Parkland in lower and midtown Manhattan amounts to about 4 percent of the city's park space per resident, a token share.
To delay construction of the waterfront park will simply add more dollars to the hundreds of millions already lost to inflation because the state had no plan or highway alternative for the Westway trade-in.
The state and city must apply for the maximum waiver as a scenic easement. This would eliminate the need for Albany and City Hall to take $40 million each from their budgets to repurchase the Westway property. Other fund sources to explore:
* Federal highway funding is available for scenic enchancement of the waivered areas, which could help pay for necessary landscaping.
* A continuous bikeway and walkway need not be an appendage to the highway to qualify for highway funds. Where feasible, they could be separated from the noise and pollution of the highway and incorporated in the scenic landscaping.
* Millions of dollars that the state has been realizing from leases in the Westway corridor should be used for repairs along the waterfront.
* Additional funds are available to restructure piers as staging areas for highway construction
These financing options should be considered in planning the park, and work should be started now to rehabilitate the piers. In this way, losses due to inflation can be held at a minimum, and we can at last make the Hudson River waterfront park a reality.
 
BILL HINE
New York, Nov. 12, 1990
 
The writer is a member of Save the Piers.

HEADLINE: Earning Success

 BODY:
To The Living Section:
Having experienced late in life the trauma of losing what had been a comfortable, if not luxurious, life style, I can have some sympathy for those yuppies who are learning that life is earnest, life is real ["Young, Successful but Between Jobs," Nov. 7].
The difference between my generation and theirs is that most of mine earned the success they achieved. Many started with jobs paying $85 a month or $10 a week with lunch. We had to do without many of the things we wanted so we could have the things we needed.
We made a lot of sacrifices so those that followed us could have choices we never had. I suppose it does require that both husband and wife work if they are to maintain a standard of living that includes BMW's and Porsches, $90 jeans, $135 sneakers, $1,000 camcorders, skiing in Aspen and surfing in Hawaii before the age of 40.
Too much too soon has often been the downfall of many people in every generation, but never before did so many have so much to lose. The "Thirtysomething" crowd will just have to learn the economic facts of life the hard way.
 
PAUL S. WEBER
Kennebunk, Me.

HEADLINE: Soviet Union on Its Way to a Market Economy;
Help Is Needed

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"After the Cold War: Frozen Chaos?" (editorial, Nov. 13) is long overdue and more timely every day.
Aid to the Soviet Union is something we must consider in the event that the Soviet distribution system breaks down this winter and that hunger or even famine grips certain areas of the country. This contingency planning is needed even if most reports from observers indicate that the trouble rests with a breakdown in distribution, rather than with basic food shortages.
A deep and dangerous crisis looms just over the horizon in the largest country on earth, and the cause of it cannot delay our humanitarian, as well as our self-interested response. We Americans have a stake in preventing extreme suffering and the resulting social chaos in the Soviet Union.
But, in the long run, providing emergency assistance will not help rectify the deficiencies of the Soviet system -- deficiencies that are also problems in Poland, Hungary, Romania, Bulgaria and Czechoslovakia. The solution lies in establishing effective distribution systems in these countries, introducing modern techniques of marketing and, most important, helping them structure a legal framework that encourages development of a free market and a regulatory framework to prevent hoarding, gouging and black market activities -- the rule of law.
I am proud to be devoting a good deal of effort to the work of the Institute of East-West Business Dynamics at New York University. The institute represents a coordinated attempt by the countries of Central and Eastern Europe and the American private and public sectors to develop a crash program to train experts in marketing and distribution, as well as to provide training for newly elected parliamentarians.
Ours is a United Nations-generated approach to stabilize the economic and legal systems of formerly centrally planned economies. It is nonideological and has strong bipartisan American support.
In addition to planning for emergencies, we must also help the new democracies in Europe to develop the wherewithal themselves to make a successful transition to market economies.
 
ANGIER BIDDLE DUKE
Chmn., Exec. Committee, Institute of East-West Business Dynamics
New York University
New York, Nov. 16, 1990

HEADLINE: We Can't Afford More Delay on Hudson River Waterfront Park;
'Why I Voted "No" '

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Why I voted "no" for the environmental bond: During the Depression in the 1930's, New York City had a great idea. It put a 2 percent sales tax on luxury items to help the unemployed. The tax was to be removed after the Depression. Instead of its being removed, New York State also put a sales tax on those items.
During the same Depression another great idea was born, Social Security, to provide security for our aged by putting a small tax on income. Now the Social Security tax is no longer small, and the money collected is used to balance the budget -- never the original intention!
Somewhere down the line gasoline was taxed to maintain roads and bridges. Why weren't the road and bridges maintained?
A 25-cent toll was placed on bridges and tunnels to cross the Hudson and East Rivers, to be removed when the bonds were paid up. The bonds were never paid up. The bridge tolls have been increased to $2.50 to cross from one borough to another. The money is used for purposes other than maintaining the bridges and tunnels.
I voted "no" on the environmental bond because I believe the money will not be used to clean up the environment and the bonds will not be repaid.
To restore credibility, taxes on consumers must be reduced; money must be spent as intended and bonds paid up to reduce government expenditure.
 
ALEXANDER DENEGA
Bronx, Nov. 12, 1990

SECTION: Section A; Page 3; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1480 words

HEADLINE: Kohl's Campaign Foe: Standard-Bearer Who Leaves Party and People Cold

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BONN, Nov. 27

 BODY:
The campaign posters show Oskar Lafontaine, the Social Democratic standard-bearer in the German election, with a gentle smile and a white shirt. The slogan is "The New Way."
But the man who rises to the throaty cheers is the vintage Lafontaine, with a dark-purple shirt and a razor-sharp repertoire of irony, indignation, polemics and sparkling intelligence. 
 Chancellor Helmut Kohl has insisted that unification will not raise taxes, Mr. Lafontaine declared, starting a favorite assault. "How can anyone lie so miserably?" he said, stripping off his jacket as if to underscore his identification with the indignant burgher.
"It's just like in America," he continued. "There, the President said before the election, 'no taxes, read my lips.' " Mr. Lafontaine delivered that line in English, and then dropped his voice for the clincher. "Of course, Kohl would never say that." Mr. Kohl speaks no foreign language, and the student-dominated crowd responds with glee.
 
Returns More Combative
 After about an hour, Mr. Lafontaine exited to rowdy cheers from a standing audience. But outside, a longtime Social Democrat shook his head, sighed and said, "Very entertaining, but no chancellor."
That, in brief, is the consensus of most Germans as they near their first election as a free and united nation in 58 years. Public opinion polls are unanimous that Mr. Kohl, the "unification chancellor," and his center-right coalition will win big.
In April, when a deranged woman stabbed Mr. Lafontaine in the neck with a kitchen knife and nearly killed him, some of those who watch politics here speculated that he would return subdued and cautious.
He did return with a brutal scar, 12 permanent bodyguards and, he admitted in a recent interview, a fear at the start of every public appearance. He no longer mixes with the public or signs autographs after an appearance.
But he returned if anything more combative, challenging Mr. Kohl on everything from monetary union to the date of political union. Der Spiegel magazine wrote that he has become even less compromising with his party.
 
Looked 'Death in the Eye'
 "He touches with his hand the scars at his neck and says, they just have to take me the way I am," the magazine quoted him as saying.
Introducing Mr. Lafontaine in Bonn, Horst Ehmke, a longtime Social Democrat and a local candidate, referred obliquely to the assassination attempt.
"He's not an old man, but he has had to look death in the eye," he declares to applause.
Yet for all his brilliance on stage, wrote Hans Ulrich Kempski of the Suddeutsche Zeitung, Mr. Lafontaine "does not mobilize feelings, because the fire that speaks out of him shines brightly, but does not warm."
 
Criticism From Schmidt
 Privately, the Social Democrats agree. But when Helmut Schmidt, the former Chancellor, said publicly to a Dutch interviewer that Mr. Lafontaine would not win and did not deserve to, the party exploded in anger, insisting that the comments were not authorized and barring the Dutch newspaper, Algemeen Dagblad, from Mr. Lafontaine's campaign train.
The problem for the party was probably less what Mr. Schmidt said about Mr. Lafontaine than the public confirmation he offered that the grandees of the Social Democratic Party are unhappy with their standard-bearer and are clearly keeping their distance.
In any case, what Mr. Schmidt said in public was considerably gentler than what he has been saying in private about Mr. Lafontaine since the early 1980's, when the maverick from the Saarland was a leader of the campaign against new NATO missiles and Mr. Schmidt was the Chancellor who agreed to accept them.
In a celebrated comment, Mr. Lafontaine said Mr. Schmidt's sense of duty and steadfastness were "secondary virtues" also suitable for "running a concentration camp."
 
Miscalculation on Unity
 It was that sort of cheek that brought Mr. Lafontaine to the prominence he now has, and that seems poised to undo him. He was chosen as standard-bearer in December, when the Social Democrats believed that unity was far off and that the times demanded a fresh generation.
Intelligent, 47 years old, quick, fast-moving, twice married and now living with a career woman, Mr. Lafontaine was supposed to offer a contrast to the staid, old-fashioned Mr. Kohl at a time of dynamic movement.
But German unity undermined all the plans. The Social Democrats were slow to recognize the imminence of unification, and Mr. Lafontaine miscalculated the inherent patriotism of the moment by publicly speaking out against East German refugees and then trying to block economic union, almost causing a split within his own party.
On the night of German Unity, Oct. 2, as the German flag rose over the Reichstag and the leaders assembled there began singing the national anthem, Mr. Lafontaine stood in stony silence.
 
Little Grist for Campaign
 All the while, Mr. Kohl unerringly moved with the public will, setting a schedule for unity that no one had believed was possible, ushering it through domestic and international resistance and emerging as the German unifier.
At the same time, the collapse of Communism led to a broad crisis throughout the political left, leaving the Social Democrats trying to conceal the fact that they still call each other comrade and used to proudly hold what they called dialogues with the fallen governments of Eastern Europe.
All that has left Mr. Lafontaine with pitifully little to campaign on. He had hoped in the summer that the economic hardships of unity would set in earlier, but that has yet to show political effect. He had hoped to trip up Mr. Kohl on taxes, but then Mr. Kohl admitted that taxes would rise -- though he insisted that unity was not the reason. And after opposing unity, Mr. Lafontaine has been compelled to skirt the theme of fatherland.
His basic diet is a potpourri of Social Democratic standbys -- restructuring the economy, penalizing polluters, abortion, child care, and banning nuclear power. But at a time when the challenge of unifying Germany and salvaging Eastern Europe stands so high, such issues do not have the same immediacy.
 
Kohl's Patriotic Message
 The current issue of Der Spiegel had Mr. Lafontaine's image on its cover as a red-nosed acrobat balancing atop the Brandenburg Gate over the words, "The Lonesome Candidate."
The latest polls by the Allensbach Insitute show the Christian Democrats favored by 42.9 percent of the voters and their coalition partners, the Free Democrats, by 10.8 percent, for a majority in the new parliament of 600 members. The Social Democrats, by contrast, stand at 33.4 percent.
Mr. Kohl also ranks far above Mr. Lafontaine in personal popularity -- 2.1 to 1.1 on a scale of negative 5 to 5.
The relative certainty of Mr. Kohl's victory, and a nation somewhat fatigued by a year of innumerable "historic moments" and emotional highs, has moderated the campaign and the interest in it.
 
Tumultuous Politics Expected
 For the first time since he came to office in 1982, Mr. Kohl has enjoyed the luxury of campaigning as national leader and statesman. He radiates confidence at his rallies -- he is the man who brought unity, and his message is unabashedly patriotic and triumphant.
He is almost haughty in his references to the opposition Social Democrats -- he refers dismissively to Mr. Lafontaine as "that man from Saarbrucken," and he makes a point of referring to the opposition only as the socialists to taint them with the discredited ideology of the east.
For many Social Democrats, the issue now is less what will happen Sunday than whether they have a chance at the next general election in four years.
A decade ago, one of the party's leaders, the late Herbert Wehner, predicted that if the Social Democrats went into opposition they would stay there for 20 years. The strength of Mr. Kohl and the disarray on the left has left many Social Democrats fearful that that prophesy might come true.
But the huge travails that lie ahead for the Germans and for Europe are certain to be reflected in tumultuous politics. Some Social Democrats have argued that the next four years are best weathered in opposition, and that with time the Protestant Prussians and Saxons of eastern Germany will return to their traditional place on the political left.
Recent polls show that the gap between the Social Democrats and the Christian Democrats is already beginning to narrow. In only week, Mr. Kohl's party dropped 1.7 points, while the Social Democrats nudged up 1.4 points.
The more immediate question is whether the Social Democrats will stick with Mr. Lafontaine. He has said he will return to the Saarland state, of which he is premier. But some of his followers, including Gerhard Schroder, premier of Lower Saxony, have declared him "the symbol of the Social Democrats for the 90's."

HEADLINE: EVOLUTION IN EUROPE;
Bulgarian Communist Stalwart Says He'd Do It All Differently

BYLINE: By CHUCK SUDETIC, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SOFIA, Bulgaria, Nov. 27

 BODY:
Todor Zhivkov, Bulgaria's deposed Communist dictator, long regarded as the East bloc leader most subservient to Moscow, looked back on his long political career during an interview here on Monday and said it was all a big mistake.
"If I had to do it over again, I would not even be a Communist, and if Lenin were alive today he would say the same thing," said Mr. Zhivkov as he sat in his granddaughter's luxurious villa on a hillside above Sofia, where he remains under house arrest. 
 Mr. Zhivkov, 79 years old, ruled Bulgaria for 35 years before being forced to resign just over a year ago. He said he began to doubt the Communist system in the mid-1950's and realized after the rise of Mikhail S. Gorbachev in the Soviet Union that Communism's underpinnings were faulty.
"I have been a soldier, I have been a Communist, it is my deep belief and conviction that I served my people and my country," Mr. Zhivkov said. "But I must now admit that we started from the wrong basis, from the wrong premise. The foundation of socialism was wrong."
 
Prefers Pure Marxism
 "I believe that at its very conception, the idea of socialism was stillborn," Mr. Zhivkov said. "We should have gone back to the original Marxist sources and had socialism develop within a highly developed capitalist world."
Mr. Zhivkov laughed at a statement by his one-time East German counterpart, Erich Honecker, that Communism would rise again. "This is utter nonsense," he said grinning.
"Now perhaps we have to cling to the United States," Mr. Zhivkov said. "If I were ruling today, I would go and decisively strike a deal with the United States. I say this not just for pragmatic reasons but out of a deep conviction. In fact, Bulgaria should do this as soon as possible."
But pragmatism was apparent, both in what Mr. Zhivkov said and in his decision to grant an interview. Bulgaria is broke, its former Soviet patron is preoccupied with its own difficulties, and Mr. Zhivkov is facing corruption and embezzlement charges.
 
The 'Vandals' in Power
 Openly bitter and afraid of imprisonment, he appealed to his old foes, the Western democracies, for support against the "vandals" in Bulgaria's present Government, who he says are trying to make him the scapegoat for all the nation's ills. The governing party in Bulgaria now calls itself the Socialist Party, and is a reformed version of the old Communists. The head of state is an anti-Communist.
"I am not legally responsible," Mr. Zhivkov said of his time in power. "I made political errors, but I have not committed a crime against my people or the world."
"I appeal to the West to check to see whether Todor Zhivkov or his family have even a single dollar abroad," he said. "I am facing corruption charges, but in fact I have only my clothes and the money I had in my pockets when I was arrested."
He said he survives on a small monthly pension.
Despite his reputation here as an old-style, hard-line Communist leader in the mold of Leonid I. Brezhnev who thwarted reform, Mr. Zhivkov said that in the 1980's he surrounded himself with pro-Western advisers and was energetically trying to turn the country toward the West.
 
Quotes Gorbachev
 As evidence, he showed what he said was a transcript of a 1987 meeting he had with Mr. Gorbachev. In the transcript, Mr. Gorbachev upbraids Mr. Zhivkov for picking advisers who "think of making Bulgaria a mini-West Germany or mini-Japan."
"Such orientations concern us," the Soviet leader reportedly said. "You should distance yourself from these people because all answers and solutions to the tasks we are facing should be sought in socialism and in its dynamic development, not elsewhere."
Mr. Zhivkov said Bulgaria had no choice but to cast its fate with the Soviet Union and its system after World War II, and that this is the prime reason for the country's present woes.
"We were surrendered to Stalin's camp," he said. "Bulgaria would certainly be far ahead of where it is now if it had been Western-oriented."
 
'Just Rhetoric'
 Such comments contrasted sharply with virtually all the public pronouncements he made while in power. He was reminded of a typical passage made 20 years ago in which he said that the Soviet Union and Bulgaria "act as a single body, breathing with the same lungs, and nourished by the same bloodstream."
"That was just an image, just rhetoric," Mr. Zhivkov said, waving his hand. "Those were different times."
Mr. Zhivkov said he knew of the existence of labor camps and of political killings that were carried out in Bulgaria and elsewhere in Eastern Europe even before 1956, when Nikita S. Khrushchev denounced some of the crimes of the Stalinist period. The former Bulgarian leader said, however, that he and his associates could do nothing to stop the terror.
"We were aware that we were not in a position to get out of the orbit we were circling in," Mr. Zhivkov said. "It was impossible for us to break apart from the system, and the reason for that was not the personality of Todor Zhivkov."
"Churchill, Roosevelt and Stalin divided the world into two zones," he argued. "Bulgaria fell into the Soviet political division headed by Stalin and the Soviet Union. Even in 1956, it was impossible to make a historic leap. That would have resulted in events like those in Hungary in 1956 and Czechoslovakia in 1968."
 
Denounces '68 Attack
 Labeling the Warsaw pact invasion of Czechoslovakia an outright case of foreign occupation by the Soviet Union, Mr. Zhivkov said that he and Bulgaria had no choice but to go along because the Kremlin had Bulgaria's economy in a stranglehold.
"From today's point of view, I've no doubt that it was an occupation and that nothing can justify it," the former Communist Party leader said. "But at that time we were allies and we had to participate. I could do nothing by myself. I was not exactly ordered to participate, but I could not refuse. It had nothing to do with personal courage. The fate of our country was at stake. The Soviet Union could have cut us off economically, and we would not have survived for more than a month."
Mr. Zhivkov rejected allegations that he and Bulgaria were in any way involved in the attempted assassination of Pope John Paul II in St. Peter's Square in May 1981. Mehmet Ali Agca, who was arrested for the attack, had told his interrogators of his links to Bulgaria's secret police.
"Can you imagine a government doing such a thing?" he said. "Can you imagine me going to Brezhnev and asking him to do this? These are all imaginary things -- just like all those stories about the Pope."
 
Denies Any Bulgarian Connection
 "Ask your intelligence services," he said, charging that Western intelligence organizations had spread false information about a Bulgarian connection with the shooting of the Pope. "They know more about this than I do."
Mr. Zhivkov said that soon after taking power he resigned himself to improving Bulgaria's economic lot, and he expressed pride in the country's progress under his leadership, lamenting its current problems.
"The nation is on the verge of self-destruction, national suicide," Mr. Zhivkov said. "It doesn't matter anymore whether the system is called socialism or capitalism."

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Chinese Official Leaves in Doubt How He Will Vote on Iraq at U.N.

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BEIJING, Nov. 28

 BODY:
China's Foreign Minister, Qian Qichen, suggested today that he would not vote in favor of a United Nations Security Council resolution authorizing the use of force against Iraq.
His comments were ambiguous. As a permanent member of the Security Council, China could veto the resolution, although most diplomats believe this is unlikely. An abstention by China would diminish the international support that President Bush would like to have before moving to drive Iraqi troops from Kuwait. 
 It is possible that Mr. Qian's comments were intended to increase his bargaining power in negotiations with the Secretary of State, James A. Baker 3d, before the vote. In exchange for supporting the resolution, China would like the United States to drop sanctions and its criticism of Chinese human rights violations.
Mr. Qian tried today to dispel any notion that China had promised the United States that it would vote for a resolution endorsing the use of force if Iraqi troops do not leave Kuwait by Jan. 15. He said, "Such reports are groundless."
 
'You Will Know After I Vote'
 In a brief meeting today with reporters at the airport before flying to New York for the Security Council session, Mr. Qian refused to specify whether China would veto the United Nations resolution or abstain. Asked whether he meant that China would not vote for the resolution, he replied, "I think so."
 When pressed on whether China would veto the resolution, he replied, "I didn't say so." Asked if China would abstain, Mr. Qian said, "You will know after I vote."
The United States will have one final chance to lobby Mr. Qian before the vote, as Secretary of State Baker is expected to meet Mr. Qian after his arrival in New York and before the Security Council session, expected on Thursday.
On Tuesday, Bush Administration officials said that Mr. Qian would make an official visit to Washington at the invitation of Mr. Baker. The official visit would symbolize an end to the 18-month-long chill in official relations since the military crackdown in Beijing in June 1989. But an Administration spokesman said Mr. Qian's visit would not constitute an "exchange" and would therefore not contravene the Administration's ban on exchanges with senior Chinese officials.
 
Regained Some Legitimacy
 Mr. Qian, however, said he saw no difference between contacts and exchanges, and said that his visit would be a "fairly important" event in American-Chinese relations.
A deputy trade minister, Gu Yongjiang, is also scheduled to visit the United States, on Dec. 10-14, at the invitation of J. Michael Farren, Under Secretary of Commerce for international trade.
China has gradually regained some of its legitimacy in the world after many Western countries scaled down ties in response to the crackdown. But China would welcome a return to normal relations with the United States because it would help restore lending and commercial ties as China's economy is in its worst slump in more than a dozen years.
The World Bank has resumed lending to China, but not at the levels that preceded the crackdown. Although Iraq is an ally, China has viewed the Gulf crisis with a bit of relief because attention has been diverted away from China's human rights issues.
 
Editors Charged With Sedition
 Because of its position on the Security Council, China has been able to assume a significant role in United Nations actions toward Baghdad, and Mr. Qian recently concluded a few weeks of hectic diplomatic activity, visiting several Middle East capitals.
While China's recent role may help boost its profile in the world, there are signs that China is continuing a tough line against dissidents jailed after the crackdown in June last year. Two men, Wang Juntao and Chen Ziming, were charged last week with counter-revolutionary sedition and trying to overthrow the Government.
Relatives of the two men, who are well known for their peaceful efforts to promote democracy and economic change in China, were notified of the charges. But they were not told when the trial would be held. Hundreds or thousands of other people are still in Chinese prisons or labor camps for their role in the democracy movement.
So far, China has voted in favor of the United Nations resolutions against Iraq. Initially it emphasized its opposition to the use of force, but in recent weeks there has been a change of emphasis, so that officials now say that so long as there is a glimmer of hope, peace should be pursued. Mr. Qian echoed this position again today.
Diplomats have said that China could be positioning itself so that it could eventually announce that there is no longer even a glimmer of hope for a peaceful resolution and that thus it is obliged to endorse a use of force against Baghdad.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Saudi King, Hardening His Stand, Dismisses Any Dealing With Iraq

BYLINE: By JAMES LeMOYNE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: RIYADH, Saudi Arabia, Nov. 28

 BODY:
On the eve of a United Nations vote on military action against Iraq, the King of Saudi Arabia appears to be taking his toughest stand yet against the Iraqi leader, Saddam Hussein, warning that if he does not withdraw from Kuwait, it will "be a tragedy for Iraq."
Both senior Saudi officials and Western diplomats here expanded on the King's statement by warning that war was near. In what appeared to be a hardened stand, they declared that if fighting began, a major objective would be not only the freeing of Kuwait but also the destruction of Iraq's chemical, nuclear and biological weapons capacity. 
 King Fahd made his most extensive and outspoken public statement on the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait in a Monday night meeting with Saudi newspaper editors that was reproduced in the local press today. Diplomats here say many of his comments reflect statements that he has previously made in private, and the significance is that he has now made them in public.
Diplomats and Saudi officials said the King's statement was a clear message to the Saudi people to prepare for war and a final warning to Iraq that with the expected favorable United Nations vote, war was imminent.
 
Only One Solution
 Appearing to lay to rest any possible doubt about Saudi Arabia's commitment to support an attack on Iraq, King Fahd declared that "no other solution will be accepted" except total and unconditional Iraqi withdrawal from Kuwait.
The Saudi ruler dismissed Iraqi appeals for negotiations to determine the conditions for possible future withdrawal from Kuwait.
"Negotiations on what?" he asked. "To compensate the aggressor? I don't think anybody will even consider it."
Other Saudi officials followed up on King Fahd's statements in interviews, saying that even if Iraq withdrew, Saudi Arabia would seek United Nations sanctions to force Iraq to pay compensation for the destruction of Kuwait and to strip Iraq of chemical, biological and nuclear weapons.
"The only way to make this area stable is to get rid of his missiles, chemical weapons and nuclear capability -- and reduce the armed forces," said an influential Saudi official referring to President Hussein. "Iraq cannot be a military state."
 
Virtually Certain Targets
 A Western diplomat who asked not to be identified further, said the United States would almost certainly destroy Iraqi chemical, biological and nuclear weapons installations if war began. The diplomat said it would be foolish "to put the most frightful weapons of the enemy off bounds" during an attack on Iraqi forces.
"No one in this region wants to live next to somebody who's putting the kind of money and effort this guy is in these weapons," the Western diplomat said.
The statements by the King and the other Saudi officials appear to dispel questions raised last month when the Saudi Defense Minister, Prince Sultan al-Faisal, made comments that were interpreted as perhaps softening the Saudi stand against Iraq.
In a society attuned to nuance and highly attentive to statements made by its ruler, King Fahd's unusually harsh and frank public statements denouncing Iraq appear likely to have a significant impact on the debate that continues in private here about the wisdom of going to war.
 
Some Doubt Is Expressed
 In the privacy of their offices and homes, some prominent Saudis continue to express concern about the political, social and economic cost of war with Iraq and a large American military presence here.
As if in answer to such doubters, the normally soft-spoken King threw down the gauntlet, accusing Mr. Hussein of personal betrayal and detailing his fears that Iraq would have invaded Saudi Arabia if the United States and other countries had not intervened.
The King said he had asked Iraqi and Kuwaiti officials to meet to discuss their differences, but that "a few hours later I was astonished to learn that the Iraqi forces had invaded Kuwait.
"Then I realized that after Kuwait, it would be the turn of the eastern part of the kingdom," King Fahd said at his meeting with editors, referring to Saudi Arabia's border region next to Kuwait.
"We never thought of an Arab state invading another Arab state," he said in apparent bewilderment and anger. He added that the day after the invasion, Mr. Hussein "called me and sent his deputy to tell me that he had annexed Kuwait."
 
'A Blessing From God'
 Realizing the Saudi Army was no match for the Iraqis, King Fahd said he asked for outside support. He told the newspaper editors that the military backing he received from "major countries" was "a blessing from God."
"I believe that all Saudi citizens consider what we did was a wise and logical action," he said. "Most of the big powers in the world responded to our call in no time."
His statement appeared to be an explicit signal to religious fundamentalists and other critics of the arrival of American and other non-Muslim troops here that the monarchy firmly backed the foreign military presence and was not interested in hearing more criticism on this or any other sensitive issue at a time of national crisis.
"He is saying, 'cool it guys, there are bigger things at stake here,' " said a Western diplomat who termed the King's speech a major statement of Saudi policy.
In interviews over the last two days, a number of Saudi officials echoed the King's hard line, expressing concern that Mr. Hussein might in the end withdraw from Kuwait and preserve both his army and his weapons of mass destruction.
 
U.S. Resolve Questioned
 They emphasize that Saudi Arabia no longer feels it can live with such an army and arsenal on its doorstep.
But several officials said they feared the United States would in the end not have the will to fight what might prove to be a difficult, politically unpopular war.
Their comments appear to reflect a worry that, having taken a hard stand against Iraq, Saudi Arabia is now extremely vulnerable and needs American backing more than ever.
"We're paranoid about it," an influential Saudi official said. "We're afraid you'll pull out."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Saudi tanks patrolling the northeastern desert, where Egyptian, Kuwaiti and Syrian units are also deployed. (Agence France-Presse)
