HEADLINE: New York Offers Plan to Reduce Haulers' Rates

BYLINE: By ALLAN R. GOLD

 BODY:
In a move that could cut the cost of doing business in New York, the city proposed yesterday that the rate that private garbage haulers charge commercial customers be reduced.
The city's Consumer Affairs Department said that cutting the top rate for picking up garbage -- now the highest in the nation -- by 7.5 percent would save the city's 250,000 businesses millions of dollars. They now pay private haulers more than a $1 billion a year. 
 The city has regulated the haulers since 1957 but has never lowered their rates. Yesterday, the haulers said that they would fight the proposal.
Private garbage pickup is a big cost of doing business in the city, particularly for smaller concerns, and such costs are ultimately passed on to consumers. Although the proposed 7.5 percent decrease may not result in less expensive consumer services, it could lead businesses to delay price increases and give them more breathing room in a weakening economy.
 
Thousands in Savings
 The Consumer Affairs Department estimated that Macy's at Herald Square, for example, could shave more than $35,000 a year from its annual garbage collection bill of more than $500,000. Con Ed spends $2.1 million, and could save more than $140,000, the department said.
A deli or grocery now spends about $15,000 a year, according to the Korean-American Small Business Service Center, and these costs could decline by $1,000 a store.
The Consumer Affairs Commissioner, Mark Green, said yesterday that the rate-cutting proposal is based on an analysis of financial information supplied by haulers showing that their costs had fallen in recent years. The proposal would decrease the maximum rate to $13.60 a cubic yard of garbage, from $14.70.
"We cannot accept an excessive rate that taxes businesses and consumers," Mr. Green said yesterday at a news conference.
Representatives of several business organizations welcomed the proposal, but the private hauling industry vowed to fight it.
 
A Hearing, Then a New Rate
 "We cannot operate at that rate," said Salvatore Spinelli, a lawyer for the Council of Trade Waste Associations, a group representing many private haulers. He said the department had improperly calculated haulers' costs.
Mr. Green said he expected the rate to take effect within 60 to 90 days. The rate can be formally adopted after a public hearing is held, and one is scheduled for Jan. 9. It would then go into effect 30 days later. Mr. Spinelli said the haulers plan a court challenge if the reduced rate is adopted.
The announcement came at a difficult time for the private garbage-hauling companies. They are facing the possibility of a strike next week by members of Local 813 of the International Brotherhood of Teamsters, which represents 2,100 private sanitation workers in the city. The two sides met yesterday, and a strike vote has been scheduled for Sunday.
Private haulers pick up about 40 percent of the city's garbage, or some 10,000 tons a day. The Sanitation Department collects the rest from residences and institutions.
Mr. Green characterized the city's private hauling businesses as a cartel that limits entrance of new companies into the field and keeps prices artificially high. Law-enforcement officials go further. They say the industry is controlled by organized crime, but representatives of the industry vehemently deny that.
Mr. Green said a lower rate was justified because haulers' cost of dumping garbage at landfills had declined and because of technological improvements that enable trucks to compress garbage more effectively, making each load more profitable. Mr. Spinelli, of the trade waste association, said the city had overstated both changes.
Private haulers last received an increase in the fall of 1988, when the rate rose to $14.70 a cubic yard from $10.66. They were given that raise because the city greatly increased the cost of dumping at its Fresh Kills Landfill in Staten Island.
Instead of paying the higher dumping charge, private haulers began shipping garbage by truck to less expensive out-of-state landfills. Last year, the city tried to readjust the rate, Mr. Green said, but the haulers refused to supply the necessary financial information. The department has since received most of the information it needed to determine a new rate.
"The carters have a right to a fair rate," said Marilyn Dahl, director of government affairs for the New York Chamber of Commerce and Industry, representing about 1,500 businesses. But she added that "anything that reduces the cost of doing business in New York City is something to be welcomed."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Up After Comments By Greenspan About Rates

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar rose yesterday on comments by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, that suggested United States interest rates would remain at their current levels.
Gold prices were narrowly mixed. 
 The dollar advanced after Mr. Greenspan voiced concerns to the House Banking Committee about the dollar's recent weakness.
But Mr. Greenspan also spoke to one of the market's big concerns when he suggested that no further cuts in interest rate were likely soon.
The dollar, like all currencies, is supported by higher interest rates. When yields are up in this country, American investments become more attractive to foreigners, who then buy dollars to purchase the securities.
The dollar has fallen recently amid prospects that United States rates would come down because of the weakening economy. The dollar has also dropped as overseas rates moved higher and made foreign currencies more valuable.
The pound, meanwhile, fell yesterday as traders feared that British interest rates would soon decline under the new Government of John Major. Sterling advanced on Tuesday after Mr. Major's selection as head of the Conservative Party and the next Prime Minister, but concerns about interest rates and some profit taking turned the pound around yesterday.
Sterling dropped to $1.9695 in New York, from $1.9775 late on Tuesday. In London earlier, the pound fell to $1.9723, from $1.9750 late on Tuesday.
The dollar jumped to 130.10 Japanese yen in New York from late Tuesday's 128.35. In Tokyo overnight, the dollar fell to 128.90 yen, from 128.95 yen on Tuesday, and in London, it rose to 129.02 yen.
Richard Levine, an analyst at Merrill Lynch & Company, said concerns had been voiced in the market about the Japanese economy, which he said could experience the same kind of debt problems as the United States.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with those of late on Tuesday, included: 1.4860 German marks, up from 1.48025; 1.2700 Swiss francs, up from 1.12595; 5.0140 French francs, up from 4.9950; 1,118.10 Italian lire, up from 1,111.25, and 1.16445 Canadian dollars, up from 1.16405.
On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold finished unchanged, closing at $385.20 an ounce. The Republic National Bank of New York later quoted a bid of $384.70 an ounce, down 30 cents.
In London, gold rose to a late bid price of $385.50 an ounce, up from $384.75 late on Tuesday. In Zurich, it rose to a closing bid of $384.50, up from $384.00 bid late on Tuesday.
Earlier, in Hong Kong, gold rose $2.04 an ounce to close at a bid $387.23.
On the Comex, silver rose 2.4 cents an ounce, closing at $4.124. But the metal fell earlier in London, to a late bid price of $4.10, from $4.12 late on Tuesday.

HEADLINE: Orders Rose For Durables Last Month

BYLINE: REUTERS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
A surge in demand for commercial aircraft helped push durable goods orders up a sharp 3.6 percent in October, the best gain in five months, the Commerce Department said today.
The jump in orders last month was far above Wall Street economists' expectations of a modest rise of five-tenths of 1 percent from September levels. But excluding the transportation component, total orders in October fell four-tenths of 1 percent, after rising one-tenth of 1 percent in September. 
 "There isn't any sign in these numbers of an upturn," said Cynthia Latta, an economist with DRI/McGraw Hill Inc. in Lexington, Mass., "It's perfectly consistent with the economy being in a recession."
But Russ Sheldon, an economist with the Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh, said the durables report, even after the heavy sales of aircraft were removed, offered hope that any recession would be short-lived and mild.
"The underlying trend looks too strong to be consistent with all this news of recession that we've had," he said. "It's the brightest ray of hope we've had among all these dismal indicators."
 
Crucial Part of Economy
 Durable goods, which are products intended to last three years or more, are a crucial part of the manufacturing sector of the economy, and orders for these relatively expensive items are closely watched as an indicator of the economy's health.
The overall October increase to $129.4 billion followed a revised 1.6 percent drop in orders during September and was the largest monthly gain since an increase of 4.1 percent in May.
Still, many private economists contend the economy has slipped into recession in the final three months of 1990, and they said they were not overly impressed by the report.
"Aircraft account for the entire increase," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist at Merrill Lynch Government Securities. "We are moving down and not up."
Analysts noted before the report was published that the Boeing Company reported it had received orders for 107 new aircraft last month worth about $10.7 billion.
Marilyn Schaja, an economist at the Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Securities Corporation who sees a weak economy, said, "The numbers do not change the scenario at all.
 
"Surge in Transportation
 Over all, orders for all types of transportation goods rose 14.8 percent in October, after falling by 6.1 percent in September.
Orders for nonmilitary capital goods, an indication of business investment in new equipment, rose 8.3 percent last month, after a gain of 6.7 percent in September.
Military orders fell 10 percent in October, after a 6.1 percent decline in September.
Excluding military items, orders for durable goods rose 4.5 percent in October, after a 1.3 percent drop in September.
The department revised its estimate for September orders to show a 1.6 percent decrease instead of the 1.4 percent drop reported on Oct. 31.
Shipments of durable goods rose by 1.8 percent in October, in contrast to September's 3.3 percent drop. Unfilled orders, an indicator of future activity, were up six-tenths of 1 percent last month, after rising one-tenth of 1 percent in September.
Among specifc sectors, electrical machinery orders rose 1.9 percent in October, after a 6.9 percent decline in September, and orders for nonelectrical machinery fell 1.5 percent, after gaining 5.5 percent in September.
Primary metals orders were up 4.9 percent in October, after declining nine-tenths of 1 percent in September.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
As Folksy Replaces Trendy, Wigwag Magazine Thrives

BYLINE: By Kim Foltz

 BODY:
TRENDY, caustic magazines like Spy and Details that chronicle and criticize the lives of powerful, or often merely glamorous, people were the rage in the late 1980's -- but now it is hip to be square.
Media analysts say one of the few successful new magazines this year is Wigwag, a folksy publication that takes a serious look at politics, issues and life in America. 
 Although the overall advertising environment for magazines is dismal, Wigwag picked up 202 pages of advertising for its first full year of publishing, which ended in September. What is more, Wigwag is attracting some ads from the kind of advertisers, like car companies, that usually avoid little magazines.
That is a small achievement compared with the thousands of ad pages that some large magazines publish each year, but analysts say Wigwag's showing is significant for a new publication with a circulation below 100,000.
The magazine's second year is also off to a good start. Samuel Schulman, the magazine's publisher, said that during the fourth quarter of this year the magazine published 90 pages of ads, a 22 percent increase from a year earlier.
"Wigwag is one of the bright spots in the magazine industry," said David Verklin, the media director at Hal Riney & Partners, which is running ads for the new Saturn car in the magazine. "I have been very impressed. It is far more inviting than most of the new magazines I see."
Wigwag, which is published 10 times a year, is attracting advertisers like Grand Marnier and Absolut. Such advertisers used to flock to magazines like 7 Days, which was the darling of New York's hip downtown social set until it closed in April.
"Wigwag is the magazine for this decade, which is more serious," said Claude Fromm, the media director at TBWA, the New York agency that creates advertising for Absolut and Grand Marnier. "We like to be in the magazines that are hot, that reach the consumers who are leading the way."
Jack Decker, the president of Decker, Decker & Freas, a magazine consulting firm, said that "even though it's a tough marketplace, Wigwag is making it because they found the right niche."
What Wigwag offers is a magazine that appeals to young, well-educated readers. Mr. Schulman said 82 percent of Wigwag's 85,000 readers were college graduates. The median age of its readers is 39 years old and the median household income is $53,000, he said.
Loosely modeled after The New Yorker, where some of its founders once worked, the magazine publishes articles about politics, the arts and science, as well as humor and fiction. Wigwag's most distinctive -- and popular -- feature is derived from its name, which means to signal someone home. The "Letters From Home" section features funny and sometimes poignant real-life tales from people living in big and small towns across the country.
More serious magazines are generally gaining in popularity, analysts said. In the last year, publications like The Atlantic and The Economist have seen their circulation and ad pages increase significantly.
"Advertisers aren't interested as much now in reaching the glitzy and greedy crowd," Mr. Fromm said. "They want to target a more limited and influential quality audience."
Wigwag has The New Yorker to thank, in part, for its success. Some analysts said many readers disenchanted with the changes at The New Yorker might have jumped over to Wigwag, enabling the new magazine to increase its circulation quickly without relying on expensive direct-mail subscription campaigns.
The next hurdle for Wigwag is amassing a circulation of several hundred thousand readers so it can attract a wide range of large national advertisers.
"The big question now is, will the magazine find the resources to help it grow substantially?" Mr. Verklin said. "In the long run, the key to survival will be pumping up the circulation."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The current issue of Wigwag magazine, which achieved success in its first year of publication. (Wigwag)

HEADLINE: To Many Britons, Major Is Unknown

BYLINE: By STEVEN PROKESCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
If Government leaders around the world are not too sure who John Major, Britain's new Prime Minister, is, they shouldn't feel too bad. A lot of people in Lambeth, the working-class borough in south London where he grew up and began his political career, aren't too sure either.
"I didn't have a clue who he was until last week," when he stepped into the spotlight as a contender to succeed Margaret Thatcher," said Tony Kelly, a 33-year-old construction worker. 
 Throughout Britain, people were still in shock today, trying to adjust to the fact that after 11 1/2 years in power, Mrs. Thatcher was gone. Whether they loved or hated her, virtually everyone in Britain knew where the warrior against socialism stood on things.
 
'Better That He's Younger'
 But despite the many interviews with Mr. Major that have appeared since last Thursday, when he entered the race to succeed her, many Britons confess that they don't know much about the low-key, quiet-spoken man who has served as Chancellor of the Exchequer for 13 months and who has been in the Cabinet for 3 1/2 years.
"I did want him to get it, but I don't know why" said Jackie Jeavons, 16, as she waited for a train at Victoria Station in central London. Could it be that he is 47, while the other contenders were in their 50's and 60's? "It's much better that he's younger," she agreed.
At The Atlantic, a pub in the poor Brixton section of Lambeth that is frequented by blacks, Clint Davis and his friends were struggling to size up their new Prime Minister. "He's a mystery man," said Mr. Davis, a 29-year-old electrician who works for British Aerospace P.L.C. "I hadn't heard of him until he was named Chancellor."
He is hardly the only one. In August 1988, when Market and Opinion Research International Ltd. showed a photo of Mr. Major to 1,891 people, only 2 percent could identify him.
 
Heralded in the Press
 It really has only been in the last week, when newspapers and television programs heralded him not only as a leading candidate to succeed Mrs. Thatcher but also her choice, that the public began to take him seriously as a potential leader, according to opinion polls.
"He wasn't considered a real contender among the general public," said Simon Braunholtz, a senior research executive at Market and Opinion Research. "He doesn't have a strong identity."
To Americans, who probably know a lot more than they want to know about their Presidents by the time they get elected, it might seem strange that such a relative nobody could suddenly be the leader of the British people.
But it was not the people who chose him: it was the 372 Conservative Members of Parliament. And they ignored what most opinion polls taken in the last week indicated: that Michael Heseltine, a former Defense Secretary who has campaigned to succeed Mrs. Thatcher for nearly five years, would have the best chance of enabling the Tories to win a fourth-consecutive general election.
 
A Strange Result
 One could also argue that Mr. Major was not the overwhelming choice of the Tory M.P.'s: he received 185 votes on Tuesday, or 2 less than a majority, and only won after the two other contenders -- Mr. Heseltine and Foreign Secretary Douglas Hurd, withdrew and threw their support to Mr. Major.
Even to many Britons, who have had centuries to get used to the fact that they have no direct say in choosing their leader, it is strange that a mystery man is suddenly in No. 10 Downing Street.
While Mrs. Thatcher was a relative nobody when she ousted Edward Heath as Tory leader in 1975, she then served as opposition leader in Parliament for more than four years and thus was well-known by the time she became Prime Minister in 1979.
"Major's not the people's choice," Marcus Samuels, a 63-year-old semi-retired local government worker, said as he nursed a drink at The Atlantic in Brixton.
 
Complaints of Britons
 Up the street at the Prince Albert, a pub mainly frequented by whites, Alison Bumstead, a 25-year-old single mother and Labor Party supporter, agreed: "The country should decide who is Prime Minister. It shouldn't be just the Conservative M.P.'s."
After some 30 years of not voting, Colin Harris, a marriage registrar, sees Mr. Major's ascension as only another reason not to start doing so now. "It's not true democracy at work," he said.
One thing he and many Britons resent is the fact that those running to represent a constituency in Parliament don't have to live in it. And in the case of the country's other major party, Labor, local party members can have their choice disregarded by the central party organization.
Another sore point for Britons is that unlike in many other countries with parliamentary systems, the number of seats that a party holds in Britain's House of Commons may have little to do with its share of the vote. In the last general election in 1987, the Conservatives won 57.7 percent of the seats with only 42.2 percent of the vote.
 
How Major Is Judged
 Mr. Major, who left school at 16 to work as clerk and laborer and who was unemployed for a while, says his ambition is to turn Britain into a truly "classless" society. But since he has been vague on detail so far and is widely seen as Mrs. Thatcher's creation, it is hardly surprising that most are judging him on the basis of what they thought of her.
In Brixton, where most vote Labor, that means people are not embracing him as the hometown boy. "He's Margaret Thatcher's lap dog. He won't last very long," said John P. McCaffrey, a retired truck driver.
At Victoria Station, it was easier to find more people willing to give him a chance.
"We expect him to carry on," said Kenneth Northall, stevedore from Seaford, Sussex. A Tory supporter, he said he was "very pleased" with the choice, although "I would have expected him to be Prime Minister five years from now" given that he "hasn't had a lot of prominent positions as such."

HEADLINE: Shifts in Deposit Insurance Weighed

BYLINE: By STEPHEN LABATON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
The Bush Administration is exploring a wide array of ways to alter the Federal program that insures bank deposits, including basing the premiums paid by banks to the insurance program upon their financial health, Treasury officials and senior regulators said today.
The Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation's Bank Insurance Fund, which is at its lowest level since its creation 56 years ago, is under considerable financial strain. Some experts have predicted that the onset of a severe recession could make it insolvent, leading to a taxpayer bailout of the industry. 
 To avert such a situation, Government officials are preparing a report on deposit insurance that is expected to be completed next month. Officials emphasized that no consensus had emerged on many elements of the plan, which would likely encompass several major changes in the present payment system for banks and the insurance coverage for customers.
Nonetheless, certain key provisions are becoming apparent, and more may be made public on Friday, when Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady speaks at a meeting of members of the Securities Industry Association in Boca Raton, Fla.
The Treasury Department has already signaled its decision not to lower the $100,000 limit on Federal insurance coverage of deposits, but officials have said that the department would probably propose limits on the number of accounts that can be insured.
The debate over deposit insurance within the inner circle of the Bush Administration is a small but significant aspect of a much wider plan under consideration. In their report, Treasury officials expect to use the difficulties of the Federal deposit insurance system as a basis for overhauling banking laws that go back to the Depression.
The report is expected to provide a framework for top lawmakers in the House and Senate who have said they will begin hearings early next year aimed at fashioning legislation.
Among the measures expected to be proposed is a substantial revision of the Glass-Steagall Act, which restricts the ability of banks to move into other businesses, notably securities underwriting. While there is strong agreement among most senior Administration officials and regulatory heads that Glass-Steagall should be modified, important differences remain over how extensive and quickly revisions should be made.
 
Adjusting Premiums
 One proposal strongly under consideration, known as risk-based premiums, would give the F.D.I.C. the ability to tie the premiums paid by banks to the level of their capital and income. This year, institutions pay a flat rate of 12 cents on each $100 of deposits, and in 1991 the rate will increase to 19.5 cents.
L. William Seidman, chairman of the F.D.I.C., said that researchers at the agency had begun working with industry analysts to come up with a formula for risk-based premiums and that the Treasury officials had been receptive to an idea under which banks with lower capital and income would pay higher premiums.
Under another proposal, which was disclosed today in an article in The Wall Street Journal, banks would be required to purchase preferred stock from the F.D.I.C., a move that could generate between $14 billion and $28 billion depending on the size of the purchases. The Treasury is considering a requirement that banks must purchase between 50 cents and $1 worth of the stock for each $100 in deposits. The stock could be included in the banks' capital base.
A Treasury official who insisted on not being identified said that the department had already ruled out bolstering the bank insurance fund by borrowing money or using the interest from the $37 billion in bank reserves the Federal Reserve holds.

HEADLINE: G.N.P. Up By 1.7% in 3d Quarter

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
The Commerce Department reported today that the economy expanded at a 1.7 percent rate in the third quarter, a tenth of percentage point less than previously estimated. But private analysts said the figure masked the progressive economic contraction that started in the late summer.
The third quarter "started fast but died at the finish line," said Stuart G. Hoffman, chief economist for the Pittsburgh National Bank. A solid advance in national output in July, he said, appeared to be followed by a flat August and by rapid deterioration since then. 
 The National Association of Business Economists announced on Tuesday that three-quarters of its forecasters believed that a recession had already begun, with 34 percent of these pessimists saying it began during the third quarter.
 
'Meaningful Downturn'
 And in his most negative assessment to date, the Federal Reserve Board's chairman, Alan Greenspan, told Congress today that a "meaningful downturn" during October and November, if continued, would result in the first shrinkage in gross national product -- the total output of goods and services -- since 1986.
Two straight quarters of a shrinking G.N.P. constitute a recession, according to a popular definition.
The Commerce Department also reported today that orders for durable goods rose 3.6 percent in October, reversing a 1.6 percent September decline that had initially been estimated at 1.4 percent. Analysts noted, however, that much of the increase was in orders to Boeing Company and other companies for aircraft that will not be built for years. If transportation equipment is excluded, orders for durable goods fell by four-tenths of 1 percent last month. [Page D24.]
John B. Taylor, a member of the President's Council of Economic Advisers, called the durable orders results "fairly positive," but private analysts tended to regard them only as evidence that the economy was not in a free fall.
 
Slowdown in Spending
 Today's reports on the revised G.N.P. and orders for durable goods were consistent with assumptions that the economy was progressively weakening. Spending on personal consumption, for example, rose at a slower pace than previously thought -- a 3.2 percent annual rate instead of 3.6 -- and business inventories fell at a rate of 4.4 percent instead of 1.7 percent.
Many analysts had expected inventories to contribute to an upward revision in G.N.P., but instead this sector helped to pull it down. While the reasons for inventory shifts are never entirely clear -- a decline, for example, could result either from strong sales or from lower orders by pessimistic retailers -- a buildup had been expected to increase third-quarter production figures.
There was, however, a substantial advance in exports, with a revised $7.4 billion increase more than offsetting the combined revised decrease of $5.7 billion in personal consumption and inventories, the report showed.
Today's report also included the first aggregate data on the profits of American corporations, including private ones and farms, for the third quarter.
Profits from current production fell at an annual rate of $11.7 billion, or 3.8 percent, following a 3.3 percent rate of increase in the second quarter. Current-production cash flow -- the internal funds available for corporate investment -- fell $13 billion, or 3.2 percent, nearly twice the spring's quarter's advance.
Over all, corporate profits after taxes rose 6.7 percent in the third quarter, after falling six-tenths of a percentage point in the second quarter. And profits before taxes increased 5.4 percent in the third quarter, after rising eight-tenths of a percentage point in the previous quarter.
 
Pickup in Inflation Rate
 Today's G.N.P. report of a 1.7 percent rate of growth in the summer quarter, up from four-tenths of 1 percent in the spring, also showed that inflation was a bit faster than initially thought. The fixed-weight price index, which assumes that the composition of output remains unchanged, ran at a 4.2 percent pace instead of 4.1 percent, while the so-called deflator was 4 percent instead of 3.4 percent.
G.N.P. expressed in current dollars, instead of constant 1982 dollars, rose at a 5.8 percent pace in the summer instead of the 5.3 percent estimated last month. But the increase reflected inflation, not output.

HEADLINE: Banks Rush to Sell Assets by End of Year

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
Department stores are not alone in having end-of-the-year holiday sales.
Many of the nation's largest commercial banks, hurt by losses on real estate loans, are trying to sell big chunks of their businesses, like credit card operations, business loans and even branches. 
 Arranging sales before the end of the year would make the Dec. 31 financial statements of these banks look better and cast a more positive light on a disappointing year. But regardless of when the sales occur, they signal a major adjustment in the way bankers are viewing their business. After years of expanding into new businesses and markets far from home, many banks are retrenching to concentrate on fewer businesses where their expertise is greatest.
"Like many others, we are focusing our efforts in the things we do best," said David A. Spina, chief financial officer at the State Street Bank and Trust Company in Boston. State Street, a leader in custodial and record-keeping services for mutual funds, recently agreed to sell its $425 million credit card business to a unit of Household International Inc., the consumer finance company.
The idea of shrinking in size does not come easily to bankers, most of whom have spent their careers pushing for growth. But with an increase in bad real estate loans and the threat of mounting losses on other kinds of loans as the economy weakens, regulators are encouraging banks to strengthen their financial positions.
"The regulators' scrutiny of those loans is intense enough to make everybody want to reduce their holdings," said one banker who spoke on the condition he not be identified. "There is a strong desire among many banks to sell loans before year-end."
Selling loans and other assets reduces the size of a bank but increases its capital. Even if the sales do not produce a profit, a bank's financial condition appears to be stronger after assets are sold because its capital is spread across a smaller base.
One quick fix sought by many bankers is the sale of credit card businesses. These operations can often be sold quickly and at attractive prices because they are highly profitable.
By far the largest such attempt is by MNC Financial Inc. in Baltimore, which was hurt by real estate losses at its Maryland National Bank subsidiary. The company expects a profit of more than $1 billion from the sale of its card business, which earned more than $300 million last year and is the fourth largest among banks, with $6.8 billion of loans outstanding.
 
$10 Billion in Card Loans
 "There are more bank card businesses for sale now than were sold during the first nine months of the year," said Robert K. Hammer, a specialist in selling card businesses and chairman of an investment banking firm bearing his name in Newbury Park, Calif. He said that about $10 billion of credit card loans are now for sale, "and I expect several transactions will be announced before year-end."
At the Southeast Banking Corporation in Miami, executives hope that the sale of their $900 million credit card business will offset a $86.9 million loss in the third quarter and help replenish the bank's capital, which has been eroded by losses on real estate loans.
Home equity loans are another kind of consumer product that bankers are eager to sell. Yesterday, the Shawmut National Corporation in Hartford sold more than $500 million of securities backed by home equity loans. Shawmut is also seeking a buyer for its $450 million credit card business.
Other banks, unwilling to part with credit card businesses, are trying to sell loans they made to businesses. Buyers range from other banks to insurance companies and mutual funds. Among the most frequently offered loans are those made to highly indebted companies as part of the financing for leveraged buyouts.
 
Dismayed by Discounts
 But to the dismay of bankers, many loans for leveraged buyouts that are paying interest and are not now threatening to default can be sold only at discount prices. The discounts could result in losses for banks and cause regulators to question whether similar loans on banks' books are worth as much as shown.
Charles Kiley, a managing director at the Bankers Trust Company who handles loan selling and trading, said many bankers had been unwilling to accept the losses that would result if they cut prices sharply. "We are seeing more banks interested in selling loans," he said, "though that is not to say that there is more selling going on."
Peter M. Lupoff, a former banker who is now managing director at M. J. Whitman & Company, a New York firm that specializes in trading bank loans, thinks buyers are playing a waiting game. "There is a scant amount of cash available and buyers are clever enough to know that the closer they get to year-end, the better the prices are likely to become," he said.
 
Scrutiny of Insurers
 In addition, demand from insurance companies for loans has also dropped substantially because, like the banks, insurers are under greater scrutiny from their regulators. A new rating system being developed by the National Association of Insurance Commissioners, for example, may judge the credit quality of many bank loans so harshly that insurance compaies would be required to set aside more reserve funds for bad loans than they do now.
"In anticipation of the new ratings, a lot of insurance companies have become much more cautious about what they will buy," said Martha C. Briley, president of Prudential Power Funding Associates, a unit of the Prudential Insurance Company of America.
To make selling loans easier, bankers have begun listing their offerings on electronic video services, where they may be seen by potential buyers. McCarthy Crisanti & Maffei, a New York firm that rates corporate debt and operates an electronic news service for corporate and mortgage-backed securities, began a new service in June listing corporate loans offered for sale.
A unit of the Bankers Trust Corporation was the first to list its offerings with McCarthy Crisanti, and was quickly followed by Chase Manhattan, Barclays Bank and a few other foreign banks. Manufacturers Hanover and Chemical have signed up for the service and several other banks are studying the possiblity, said Lindley B. Richert, senior vice president at McCarthy Crisanti.
 
Seeking Quality Ratings
 In another effort to whet buyers' appetites for loans, bankers are talking with analysts at Moody's Investors Service Inc. and the Standard & Poor's Corporation about obtaining quality ratings on loans for the first time. This independent analysis of loans could make some buyers more comfortable.
For some banks, selling entire businesses is an alternative to the difficult process of finding buyers for individual business loans.
Marine Midland Banks Inc. in Buffalo recently agreed to sell to the Ford Motor Credit Company most of the Marine Midland Automotive Financial Corporation, a unit that finances car dealers and buyers.
The Chase Manhattan Corportion is seeking a buyer for its 15-branch consumer banking business in Spain. Anticipating tough competition from European banks when national barriers to trade and finance come down in 1992, Chase is also looking to sell its British credit card business, industry analysts said.
 
Buying Savings Branches
 Closer to home, Chase is taking advantage of the desire of some New York-area savings banks to sell their less profitable branches. In October, Chase announced an agreement to buy two branches with $330 million of deposits from the Dollar Dry Dock Bank of New York. It said it expects to acquire more branches from other savings banks before the end of the year.
The First Fidelity Bankcorporation, the largest banking company in New Jersey, announced last week that it would sell the Fidelcor Business Credit Corporation, a $500 million commercial finance subsidiary with nine offices, to the CIT Group. The CIT group was owned by the Manufacturers Hanover Corporation until last year, when 60 percent of the company was sold to the Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank of Japan.
Some of the largest sales are expected at Citicorp, whose $234 billion of assets make it the nation's largest banking company, at more than twice the size of the second-ranking BankAmerica Corporation. The bank has already indicated plans to sell $6 billion of loans and other assets in the final quarter of 1990. Thomas H. Hanley, a banking analyst at Salomon Brothers, estimated that by early 1992, as much as $25 billion, or nearly 10 percent of the company, may have been pared away.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Investors Seeking Safety in Foods

BYLINE: By H. J. Maidenberg

 BODY:
AS economists argue over whether the nation is in a recession or about to enter one, more investors are seeking safety. Regardless of the state of the economy, they reason, people still eat every day. As a result, the normally stodgy food sector of the market has again become attractive.
Thanks to abundant harvests of basic farm products and growing consumer acceptance of a variety of new products, many food manufacturers have been able to increase their dividends by double-digit percentages this year. 
 As a group, food stocks have outperformed market averages so far this year. As of yesterday's close of the New York Stock Exchange, the Dow Jones industrial average of 30 stocks was down 7.92 percent for the year to date, while the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks was off 10.03 percent.
By comparison, the index of 22 milling and baking company stocks compiled weekly by Milling & Baking News, Kansas City, Mo., the leading journal of the industry, was up 5.95 percent, to its highest level since late last July, before the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait sent oil prices soaring and most share prices tumbling.
Whether the food companies will be able to sustain or increase their profit margins in 1991 will largely depend on energy costs, which are a much larger industry expense than the cost of raw materials. Food industry analysts think they will.
"While low prices of grain, oilseeds and other basic food ingredients have taken some of the sting out of the doubling of energy costs, the industry is also benefiting greatly from volume-building new products and the increasing health consciousness of consumers," said Morton I. Sosland, publisher and chief editor of Milling & Baking News.
With prices of wheat, corn, oats and oilseeds hovering near seasonal lows, the cost of energy is the food industry's main problem. The reason is that much of the industry's raw materials and almost all the finished products are transported by road. The amount of energy used in processing food products is significant, but not nearly as much as in transportation.
Gone are the days when neighborhood bakeries were a community fixture. Since World War II, the food industry has become concentrated in relatively few manufacturing centers and thus is more vulnerable to rising transportation costs.
Offsetting the near doubling of oil prices in recent months, Mr. Sosland said, is the food industry's success with profitable products like frozen baked goods. At the same time, consumer concerns about health and fitness are increasing the use of enriched breads, pastas and cereals.
Enriched bread and hamburger buns usually provide more nutrition than the contents of the sandwiches, and health-conscious consumers are eating more protein-rich pasta than ever before. Also, ethnic foods like pizza and Mexican tortillas have become part of the average diet.
These developments have combined to make shares in food companies a leading defensive sector, said Nomi Ghez, food industry analyst at Goldman, Sachs & Company. "Normally, the food sector was at best an unglamorous group with slow growth," she said. "Today, the food sector is not only outperforming a weak stock market, but providing investors with double-digit increases in dividends that have averaged 14 percent thus far in 1991."
Among some examples Ms. Ghez cited was the 14.3 percent increase in the annual dividend by H. J. Heinz, to 96 cents a share; Hershey Food's 15.4 percent rise, to 90 cents; Quaker Oats' 11.4 percent gain, to $1.56. Also, General Mills raised its payout 16.4 percent, to $2.56, and Conagra, 16 percent, to 69 cents.
"Were it not for the near doubling of oil prices, food company share prices would be at their 52-week highs," she said.
William M. LeFevre, senior vice president and market strategist at Advest Inc., said the food manufacturers were also benefiting from a decline in exports of grain and oilseeds. "While the drop in exports has made it more economical for processors like Archer-Daniels-Midland to convert more corn into ethanol fuel blends, it has also reduced costs for poultry," he said. "So we will be seeing more fast-food chains featuring chicken in the near future."
Following are the stocks in the Milling & Baking News index: Anheuser-Busch, Archer-Daniels-Midland, Borden, Bridgford, Campbell Soup, Conagra, CPC International, Flowers Industries, General Mills, H. J. Heinz, Hershey, International Multifoods, Kellogg, Lance, Midwest Grain Products, Philip Morris, Quaker Oats, Ralston Purina, Sara Lee, Seaboard Flour, Tasty Baking and Vie de France.

HEADLINE: Catalogue Sales Lag

 BODY:
The pace of Christmas catalogue sales is even slower than that of retail sales in stores because catalogue companies are hampered by prices set many months ago, Rosalind Wells, chief economist of the National Retail Federation, said yesterday at a news briefing in New York.
Prices fixed six to nine months ago, when the catalogues were being produced, are higher than those now found in stores, where much discounting is taking place, she said. 
 Ms. Wells said that general merchandise, appliance and furniture sales in December should show a gain of 3 percent over December 1989. But because price inflation in those goods averages about 2 percent a year, the real increase in sales should only be about 1 percent, she said.
She also said she expected real personal expenditures to increase 1.1 percent during the current economic slowdown. That would be only one-tenth of a percentage point less than in 1989 and is higher than in the recessions of 1974 and 1980, when consumer spending dropped. She did not say how long a recession might last.
Separately, the Conference Board said Americans would spend about $37 billion on Christmas gifts this year, or about 4 percent less than last year. This would come to $325 per family compared with $335 in 1989.

HEADLINE: Cuomo to Forgo Inaugural Ball, Citing Expense

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Dismissing a ceremony as expensive and a celebration as inappropriate, Mario M. Cuomo said today that his inauguration to a third term as New York's governor would be a strictly private affair.
In contrast to his 1987 festivities here -- attended by almost 5,000 people and highlighted by a 19-gun salute, a Beethoven symphony and an "I Love New York" medley -- the Governor's inaugural on Jan. 1 will probably be held in the study of the Executive Mansion and attended only by family members, the Governor said. 
 For Mr. Cuomo, whose penchant for introspection is legendary, a day spent quietly in his chambers or with his diaries might not be any less enjoyable than one spent popping champagne corks with masses of admirers.
Still, the move is unusual -- several officials here said they could not recall a Governor forgoing the inaugural ceremony -- and it will deprive the Capitol of a colorful ritual that has served to brighten up the Albany winter once every four years.
In a brief statement, Mr. Cuomo said that a formal inaugural, estimated to cost between $60,000 and $70,000 in state money, would be unbecoming "at a time of national economic crisis." He recently proposed cutting $1 billion from the state budget and laying off up to 10,000 state workers, and he said that eschewing the ceremony was part of his effort to share in the "inconvenience, sacrifice, even pain" of the state's fiscal turmoil.
"I hope the people of New York will understand that my purpose is to express our gratitude," he added, "and to reaffirm our commitment to the idea of family -- in all things -- pleasant and difficult."
Lieut. Gov. Stan Lundine, Attorney General Robert Abrams and State Comptroller Edward V. Regan, all re-elected earlier this month, said through aides today that they, too, would not hold a public celebration for their inaugurals.
Mr. Cuomo will give no inaugural speech and may not have any public schedule on New Year's Day, he said in an interview tonight.
Asked whether he would miss the ceremony, the Govenor said: "Yes. But I was not brought up to demean the value of $70,000. And it's difficult for me to believe that any of us in our family could fully enjoy ourselves knowing that we had called for the layoffs of thousands of public employees."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Mercedes-Benz To Raise Output

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Mercedes-Benz A.G. said it wanted to increase production of commercial vehicles next year. The company, a unit of the German industrial giant Daimler-Benz A.G. said it wanted to step up worldwide production by 3.7 percent, to 270,000 units, in 1991.
Helmut Werner, a member of the company's management board, also announced a "wide-ranging, ambitious" cost-cutting program that was aimed at saving the company 1.8 billion marks, or about $1.2 billion, by the mid-1990's. Overseas sales of Mercedes vehicles, mainly in Brazil, South Africa and the United States, have begun to feel the effects of the worldwide economic downturn.

HEADLINE: Graduate Schools Fill With Foreigners

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA

 BODY:
Long considered a safe harbor for scholars from abroad, American universities are being flooded by waves of foreign graduate students who are changing the face of higher education and exposing in many ways the nation's declining ability to produce its own researchers, scientists and academicians.
Over the last two decades, the number of Americans earning advanced degrees has declined, leaving empty seats that have been filled by foreigners. The percentage of doctoral degrees earned by noncitizens has swelled from 15 percent in 1972 to 26 percent last year. 
 The smaller number of deAmericans in graduate school can be partly attributed to a decreasing college-age population, with the end of the baby boom. But educators are concerned that fewer and fewer college-age students are interested in or capable of handling graduate studies.
Foreign graduate students are found in every field but especially in technology and the sciences, where well-equipped American research laboratories are the envy of the world. Foreigners now earn half or more of the degrees in those fields and practically dominate in some places, like the New Jersey Institute of Technology, where 734 of 887 full-time students are from Asia, or the civil engineering department at the University of Texas at Austin, where 205 of 355 graduate students come from other countries.
 
'A Know-Nothing Country'
 The growing presence of foreign scholars may be proof of the quality of American universities, and in many cases may enhance that quality. But some educators see problems. They say the noncitizens are receiving financial aid from the universities that could go to hard-pressed American graduate students. They also worry that the United States is heading for trouble because many of these students will return home, taking their training with them. That will benefit their countries, but deprive American industry of scientists and American universities of professors.
The biggest concern is that the United States is jeopardizing its future by not producing enough of its own academic high achievers.
"We face the serious problem of becoming a know-nothing country," said Neville R. Kallenbach, chairman of the chemistry department at New York University, where only one of 15 first-year graduate students is American. "That's what deep down really bothers me."
Already, universities have come to rely on international students to keep graduate programs filled, and by all accounts their numbers will continue to grow. At some schools foreign applications outnumber domestic ones, and in some departments more than 80 percent of first-year students come from overseas. Foreign scholars run laboratories, lead research efforts and teach ever more undergraduate courses.
 
Xenophobia Rears Its Head
 "Our university research system is dependent on the supply of foreign graduate students," said Manfred J. Cziesla, director of the office of international affairs at the University of Illinois at Chicago. "Without them now it would collapse."
For the most part, concerns about the surge of foreign students have been based on academic issues, not xenophobia, but some of those fears have surfaced. In 1987 the Massachusetts Legislature approved, then quickly repealed, a law requiring foreign students to pay much higher tuition than other students. Elsewhere, attempts have been made to bar foreign students from sections of research libraries containing closely held information.
But the more pressing concern, most experts say, is not that there are so many foreign students enrolled in graduate programs but that there are so few Americans.
Since the early 1970's, the number of doctorates granted has remained about the same but the number of Americans receiving them has dropped. Educators believe American students are driven away by the long years and near poverty that go with graduate study. It is far easier, they say, to go directly into the work force with a bachelor's degree.
 
The Wages of Poor Schooling
 Recognition is growing that many American students cannot make the grade in the demanding graduate and postgraduate levels because they have not received adequate training and motivation, especially in the sciences, from kindergarten through college.
"Our graduate schools are extremely attractive internationally," said Peter D. Syverson, director of information services for the Council of Graduate Schools, a national organization. "We get terrific applications from abroad, but not the same level and quality of applications from American students."
In every field surveyed by the Council of Graduate Schools, the percentage of Americans earning advanced degrees declined over the last two decades.
The drop has been steepest in technology and the hard sciences, where the work is among the hardest and the salaries generally lower than in professional fields like medicine and law. A survey released this month by the American Mathematical Society showed that 57 percent of the 933 mathematics doctorates awarded last year were given to noncitizens.
Students from other countries have filled seats once held by Americans. For instance, foreigners at Columbia University's Graduate School of Arts and Sciences have increased 47 percent in the last five years. At the University of Pennsylvania, nearly 43 percent of the graduate students in the college of earth and mineral sciences come from other nations. Half of the electrical engineering graduate students and almost 58 percent of the civil engineering students at the University of Texas are noncitizens.
 
Once Europe, Now Asia
 In 1986, foreign applications actually outnumbered domestic ones at Ohio State University, which has one of the largest graduate-school populations in the country. About 23 percent of its 10,493 graduate students are foreigners.
The new academic immigrants reflect the shifting economic and political world. Europeans once dominated but now students from Taiwan, Korea, India and the People's Republic of China outnumber all others. Most of the students are men but the number of women is rapidly increasing.
The chemistry department at Rutgers University, for instance, has around 160 students, about 100 of them noncitizens and most from the People's Republic of China.
"If this keeps up," said Dr. Martha A. Cotter, vice chairwoman of the department, "in another 15 years it's not going to be 'My teaching assistant has an accent,' but 'My professor has an accent.' "
The Chinese students wear jeans and carry backpacks like their American counterparts, but they tend to study and socialize together, apart from other students. Dr. Cotter is concerned that foreign graduate students around the country now seem to be drawing more criticism than earlier waves of foreign scholars.
 
An Element of Racism
 "I'm not sure if there isn't a little element of racism, in that people get more upset about lots of Chinese people than they do about lots of European people," Dr. Cotter said.
At the Courant Institute of Mathematical Sciences at New York University, 60 of 160 graduate students are noncitizens. Most receive about $20,000 a year in financial aid from the university, including payment for their work as teachers of undergraduates.
The foreign students at Courant are first-rate mathematicians. But, like graduate students at other universities, many are also first-time teachers with limited command of English, which can cause problems.
"Some come and a year later sound like Americans," said Jerome Berkowitz, deputy director of the institute. "There are others who come and 10 years later they are still incomprehensible."
Mr. Berkowitz said N.Y.U. helped graduate students learn teaching methods and master English skills. The students who have the most trouble being understood, he added, are sometimes restricted to grading papers or working in a lab.
 
Baffling Jokes and Strict Visas
 Many of the students have studied English in school for years before they arrive, and it is the culture, not the language, that confuses them.
"In Taiwan I was learning English from the ninth grade," said Ping Chiang Lyu, a 31-year-old Ph.D. candidate in chemistry at N.Y.U. who has taught several undergraduate classes. "But I still cannot pick up on the jokes."
Some institutions became so dependent on the stream of foreigners that admissions standards are bent to get them in. At the New Jersey Institute of Technology in Newark, 73 percent of full-time graduate students are noncitizens. The percentage was even higher when the former director of the graduate school recruited heavily from universities in India, including some whose students, it was later found, could not meet American standards.
Professors say the great bulk of foreign students are competent, even brilliant, scholars and researchers. But because they are in the United States on temporary student visas, most are forced by immigration laws to leave after they get their degrees.
 
Staying in America
 Increasingly, foreign students are trying to extend their stay. According to a survey of doctorate recipients by the National Science Foundation, 57 percent of foreign students who had made definite plans wanted to stay in the United States.
Of those who wanted to stay, 33 percent planned to extend their student visas by pursuing postdoctoral work. The remaining 24 percent said they were going to look for jobs either teaching or working in industry, which entails a two-year process to prove that there are no qualified Americans to take the position.
But eventually, most foreign students return home. Some see this as a way to improve the image of the United States abroad and to help less developed countries expand their economies.
"The growth of knowledge is a good in itself," said Hanna H. Gray, president of the University of Chicago, who is chairwoman of the Association of American Universities.
 
Shortage of Home-Grown Talent
 But others fear that by training and supporting so many scholars who will not work here, the United States is wasting valuable resources.
"We're very concerned about shortages of skilled people, particularly in the sciences and engineering, computer science and mathematics," said Harris N. Miller, coordinator for the Business Immigration Coalition, a group of multinational companies that has lobbied for revisions to immigration law.
Congress has approved some of those changes, including a measure to triple the quota for skilled professionals to 140,000 a year from 55,000. President Bush has said he would sign it.

HEADLINE: Aquatic Pest Turns Up Early in Hudson

BYLINE: By HAROLD FABER

 BODY:
The zebra mussel, an aquatic pest that fouls the intake pipes of power plants, has been found in the Hudson River for the first time, scientists who have been tracking its spread eastward from the Great Lakes said yesterday.
The mussels, which had not previously been seen east of the central section of the Erie Canal, were found in the Hudson on a piece of wood in Haverstraw Bay off Croton Point in Westchester County. They were identified yesterday by experts at the New York Zebra Mussel Information Clearinghouse at the State University of New York at Brockport. 
 "It is the first confirmed North American sighting of the mussel outside of the Great Lakes drainage basin," said Charles F. O'Neill Jr., project supervisor of the Brockport center.
The zebra mussel, named for its dark brown stripes on a light tannish shell, has produced environmental problems elsewhere. Reproducing prolifically, it clogs the intake pipes of power plants and devours microscopic plants at the base of the food chain for freshwater fish.
"It should be worrisome for anyonewho has a pipe in the river, any muncipality or any utility," said Charles I. Keene, director of natural history at the Museum of the Hudson Highlands in Cornwall. "The mussels will plug up the pipes."
A native of Europe, the mussel was first found in the United States in 1988 in Lake St. Clair, east of Detroit, presumably deposited there by oceangoing ships using the Great Lakes. Without any known natural enemies to attack it, the mussels reproduce quickly: females produce as many as 40,000 eggs a season. The mussels often cluster around water-intake pipes, which provide a moving source of food.
 
Sooner Than Expected
 From Lake St. Clair, they have spread rapidly as far as Duluth, Minn., on the western end of Lake Superior, along the western edges of Lake Huron, along the shores of Lakes Erie and Ontario and into the St. Lawrence River.
The United States Fish and Wildlife Service has estimated that the expanding population might add as much as $500 million a year in expenses for public utilities, water companies and factories and loss of business in sports fishing, marinas and motels along the inland waterways of the nation.
Though biologists had predicted that the mussel would sooner or later infect the Hudson River -- perhaps in a year or two -- its presence in the river so soon surprised them, Mr. O'Neill said. The discovery came as a sidelight to a collection for a scientific display on biological filtering at the Museum of the Hudson Highlands. On Oct. 1, a small party led by Christopher Letts, an educator for the Hudson River Foundation, went into the river at Haverstraw Bay to collect marine specimens. They gathered grass shrimp, sticklebacks, barnacles and a piece of wood covered with various marine growths. The stick was carried back to the museum and placed in an aquarium.
Last week, Mr. Keene examined it. "I was taken completely by surprise," he said. "I noticed something peculiar on the stick, some mussels. I took a couple off, and looked at them under a magnifying glass but they didn't look like any native mussels I had ever seen."
 
'A Lot of Questions'
 Mr. Keene said they did not look like zebra mussels either, because they were smaller -- about a quarter of an inch in diameter -- and lighter than he thought they would be. He sent them to Brockport for identification.
"We have a lot of questions about how they got there," Mr. O'Neill said, noting that the zebra mussel, a fresh-water organism, is not usually found in salt water like the lower Hudson River.
To combat the zebra mussel, utilities have flushed pipes with treated water, cleaned intake screens by hand and used chlorine and other chemicals.None of the methods has proved to be economical or nonpolluting.
Four major power plants are close to the site where the mussels were found: the Lovett and Bowline plants of Orange and Rockland Utilities, just across the river, and Indian Point 2, operated by Con Edison, and Indian Point 3, operated by the New York Power Authority, a few miles to the north.
 
'March Across the State'
 "We haven't found any zebra mussels yet," said Jim Lois, a spokesman for Orange and Rockland.
Cliff Spieler, a vice president of the Power Authority, said samples were taken yesterday but no visible signs of the mussels were found.
"We were fully aware of their march across the state," said John Holsapple, manager of water quality programs for the New York Power Pool, which represents all the state power plants. "We knew they were coming, but it was a bit of a surprise to see them so soon."
None of the experts had any explanation of how the zebra mussels arrived at Croton without any signs of them appearing east of their last sighting, in the Erie Canal near Rochester.
Mr. O'Neill also said it was too early to say if the zebra mussels have formed a major colony at Croton.
He offered three possibilities for how the mussels could have been transported from the Great Lakes: on a European ship discharging ballast water in the Hudson, on a pleasure boat from the Great Lakes transferred to the Croton River or the Hudson or, more unlikely, on a piece of wood floating down through the Erie Canal, the Mohawk River and the Hudson.
"The river is certainly going to be the site of intense scrutiny by scientists now," Mr. O'Neill said.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
2 Ex-Military Chiefs Urge Bush to Delay Gulf War

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Two former Chairmen of the Joint Chiefs of Staff urged the Bush Administration today to postpone military action against Iraq and to give economic sanctions a year or more to work.
"If in fact the sanctions will work in 12 to 18 months instead of six months, the trade-off of avoiding war with its attendant sacrifices and uncertainties would, in my view, be worth it," said Adm. William J. Crowe, who appeared with Gen. David Jones. 
 "It's curious," Admiral Crowe continued, "that some expect our military to train soldiers to stand up to hostile fire but doubt its ability to train them to occupy ground and wait patiently."
The testimony by the two retired officers before the Senate Armed Services Committee was significant because it reflects a strong current of opinion within the active military and because the two men remain influential with Congress. The hearings took place as the United Nations Security Council prepared to meet Thursday to vote on a resolution authorizing the use of force if Iraq does not pull out of Kuwait.
In recent weeks, Gen. H. Norman Schwarzkopf, commander of American forces in the Persian Gulf, and Gen. Alfred Gray, the Marine Corps Commandant, have urged that the United States avoid a rush toward war and give economic sanctions more time to work.
 
Kissinger Opposes Delay
 The remarks by Admiral Crowe and General Jones were warmly received by the committee's Democratic members, who have called for restraint by the Administration.
But in his appearance before the panel, former Secretary of State Henry A. Kissinger argued that the United States could not afford to wait a year for the sanctions to work because the prolonged presence of American forces in Saudi Arabia would destabilize the country and other moderate Arab states.
"The issue in Arabia is not American staying power but the host country's domestic stability," Mr. Kissinger said.
"Conditions in the gulf are not even remotely comparable to Europe or Northeast Asia," Mr. Kissinger said. "There, American forces contributed to domestic stability. In Saudi Arabia they would threaten it."
The sharply conflicting assessments were a microcosm of the escalating debate over American policy in the Persian Gulf.
The Bush Administration's decision earlier this month to almost double the number of American troops in the Persian Gulf and to seek a United Nations Security Council resolution authorizing the use of force in an offensive against Iraq shattered the bipartisan Congressional support for the Administration's gulf policy that developed in August when President Bush announced that he was sending American troops to Saudi Arabia for defensive purposes.
The two former military chiefs, whose testimony came before Mr. Kissinger's, echoed the assessment of former Defense Secretary James Schlesinger, who testified on Tuesday, that the economic sanctions would be effective in persuading Iraqi troops to leave Kuwait if only the United States would give them time to work.
 
Anti-U.S. Sentiment
 In his testimony, Admiral Crowe, the top military chief in the in the later part of the Reagan Administration and the first year of the Bush Administration, also argued that an American strike against Iraq would inflame anti-American sentiment in the Arab world.
General Jones, the top military official during the Carter Administration and the early part of the Reagan Administration, said he also believed the embargo would work and expressed concern that the American military buildup would create pressure for premature action.
"As we build to 400,000 troops, however, the support, morale, training, cultural and readiness problems rise sharply the longer the troops remain in forward garrisons," General Jones said. "My main concern with this latest scheduled reinforcement isn't that we might choose to fight, but rather that the deployment might cause us to fight -- perhaps prematurely and perhaps unnecessarily."
Mr. Kissinger agreed that the growth in the size of the American force in Saudi Arabia would make it more difficult to extend their stay there. Mr. Kissinger argued that that merely strengthened the case for taking military action against Iraq early next year.
 
Muslim Month of Fasting
 If the economic sanctions have not forced Iraq to withdraw its troops from Kuwait by the spring, a situation that Mr. Kissinger suggested was very likely, the United States would face a decision of going to war against Iraq or risking contributing to the destabilization of Saudi Arabia.
"I do not believe we can maintain 400,000 troops much beyond the spring of next year," he said. "I am nervous about the period that begins with Ramadan."
"By the time it is evident that sanctions alone cannot succeed, a credible military option will probably no longer exist," said Mr. Kissinger, who remains an influential figure among White House officials.
General Jones sought to balance his call for patience by sending a clear warning to President Hussein.
"The country is unified in seeking that he does not succeed," the retired general said. "How badly he is hurt is his choice. If we take military action, we're not going to stop in Kuwait.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Maxwell House

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: JACKSONVILLE, Fla., Nov. 28

 BODY:
Despite a sluggish economy, Maxwell House plans to begin work on a $30 million expansion of its plant here by February, the plant's manager, James Reese, said. The expansion will allow the company to hire 40 new workers next summer, Mr. Reese said. Maxwell House now has about 360 employees. Maxwell House's parent company, General Foods U.S.A., has said it will keep the Jacksonville plant open and close its operation in Hoboken, N.J.

HEADLINE: GREENSPAN SAYS NATION'S ECONOMY IS IN A DOWNTURN

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Alan Greenspan, the Government's most influential economic policy maker, today became the first top official to say publicly that the nation's economy is actually shrinking.
Although others in the Government have acknowledged that the economy is growing little if at all, Mr. Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, told Congress today that economic activity had been contracting since last month, largely because of the crisis in the Persian Gulf. 
 Many private economists have reached similar conclusions, but none have access to the wealth of data that Mr. Greenspan has.
 
The Effects of a Downturn
 A shrinking economy, or drop in the gross national product, means less business activity, higher unemployment and a much larger Federal budget deficit, as tax revenues decrease and spending for unemployment compensation and welfare programs rise. If the situation got bad enough, the Federal Reserve would probably lower interest rates to give the economy a lift, but Mr. Greenspan would not say today whether that was his intention.
Testifying before the House Banking Committee, he said of the economy, "All indications are that a meaningful downturn in aggregate output occurred as we moved through October and into November."
Asked if that meant a recession had begun, Mr. Greenspan replied that it was too early to tell whether the slide would last long enough to amount to a recession. But he added, "What we're looking at is a gradual decline, which, if it persists, will give us a fourth-quarter G.N.P. which is negative."
 
A Year-End Decline
 That means he expects the gross national product, adjusted for inflation, to be less from October through December than it was from July through September.
The Commerce Department reported today that the economy grew at a rate of 1.7 percent in the third quarter, marginally less than initially estimated. Most of the growth came early in the period, before the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait on Aug. 2. [Page D1.]
The official definition of a recession is a decline in the gross national product for two consecutive quarters.
So far, Mr. Greenspan said, the weekly economic data he analyzes did not indicate that a serious recession was in the offing. "It's still a mixed case," he said, "but there's no question but that the economy, at least as of a week or so ago, was still easing."
Two weeks ago, President Bush said he was worried about the possibility of recession, but he and his advisers have given no clue of what policies they might adopt to turn the economy around. In any case, their options are limited, and they probably have less influence over the economy than Mr. Greenspan, whose authority is independent of the President's.
 
 Holding Sway Over Rates
 Mr. Greenspan is so influential because the Federal Reserve controls some interest rates and can affect others. As is his habit, he refused today to discuss his plans in that area.
Still, two observations he made before the Banking Committee could be read as an indication that he was prepared to drive rates somewhat lower, although not necessarily immediately. Mr. Greenspan said he was concerned about a continuing tightening of credit by banks, and he also said that the threat of inflation seemed to have moderated in recent weeks.
Since July, the Federal Reserve has allowed the Federal funds rate, a rate it controls directly, to fall from 8.25 percent to 7.5 percent, and many economists expect it to drop to 7 percent by early next year. The Federal funds rate is what banks charge for overnight loans, and changes in it can lead to changes in commercial and consumer interest rates.
 
 Bond Prices Unchanged
 Mr. Greenspan's comments were viewed as mildly positive for the credit markets early in the session, as participants anticipated lower rates eventually, but other factors also affected the market throughout the day and the yield on the benchmark 30-year bond ended unchanged.
When he is asked about interest rates, Mr. Greenspan tends to talk in riddles. Today, for example, Representative Jim Leach, an Iowa Republican, suggested that current economic conditions "militate rather forcibly" for lower rates.
Mr. Greenspan responded: "The evidence suggests that the maximum real economic growth occurs with a stable, noninflationary economic and financial environment. And if that were consistent with an easier monetary policy, then I would agree with you on the general proposition. I'm not sure it is. It may be in certain cases. It may not be in other cases."
Mr. Greenspan said that he paid close attention to surveys of public attitudes and that he knew the confidence of businesses and consumers was now very low. But he said, "It is clearly the case that the world out there when you look at the hard data is not in as bad shape as it feels."
 Economic activity in many parts of the country and many segments of the economy has been declining for months, but there are signs of strength. In parts of the Middle West, for instance, economic activity is still robust, Mr. Greenspan said.
Mr. Greenspan said the economic contraction was mainly the result of three factors: rising oil prices, "the enormous uncertainty about how and when the tensions in the Persian Gulf will be resolved" and the tightening of credit by banks. The interaction of these elements, he said, "is apparently creating a greater suppression of economic activity than the sum of the forces individually."
"A significant part, even a major part," of the economic decline "is Persian Gulf related," he said, and he suggested that the Federal Reserve could do little to counteract that factor.
 
 'The Transfer of Wealth'
 "We must not lose sight of the fact," he said, "that there is no policy initiative that can in the end prevent the transfer of wealth and cut in our standard of living that stems from higher prices for imported oil."
On the other hand, Mr. Greenspan said he had considerable "anecdotal evidence," though little hard data, that what he called the credit crunch had worsened since the summer, and he suggested that this was one force dragging down the economy that the Federal Reserve could respond to.
"We shall want to make certain that money and credit remain on appropriate growth tracks, with due attention to the credit situation," he told the lawmakers.
Although economists agree that credit is less available to many businesses than it ought to be in the current economic climate, they disagree on the main cause. Some say banks have become reluctant to lend either because Government regulations have become too strict or because the banks are uneasy in light of the savings and loan debacle. Others say the main problem is that interest rates are so high that businesses cannot afford to borrow.
In either case, a reduction in borrowing results in less investment and less economic activity generally.
In July, when the Federal Reserve began to lower rates after holding them steady for more than six months, Mr. Greenspan said the tightness of credit was the deciding factor.
Since then, he, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady and even President Bush have encouraged bankers to be more forthcoming with loans to creditworthy customers, but unless interest rates come down, their pleas may have been in vain.
 
 Bank Rule Under Study
 Mr. Greenspan also confirmed today a report in The Wall Street Journal this week that the Federal Reserve was studying whether to lower the percentage of deposits that banks are required to hold in reserve in currency or in interest-free deposits at the Federal Reserve.
A reduction in the reserve requirement, which has not changed in more than a decade, would increase bank profits and might encourage them to lend money more freely.
Mr. Greenspan said the matter had been under consideration since the summer. "We have not come to a conclusion," he said, "because there are lots of pluses and minuses, but we are looking at it."
In an aside, he also expressed concern to the committee today about the recent decline in the dollar's value on the currency markets. Partly in response to those comments and to his apparent inclination to not reduce interest rates further just yet, the dollar strengthened today against most major currencies.
 
 Some Economists' Views
 Mr. Greenspan's view that current data do not suggest a serious recession was seconded by private economists who testified after him at the Banking Committee hearing today.
Charles L. Schultze, who was chairman of President Jimmy Carter's Council of Economic Advisers and is now a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution, said he expected a recession but that it "should be noticeably less severe and the inflation consequences far less than those with which the economy was afflicted after the previous two oil shocks."
Alan Sinai, a prominent forecaster who is president of the Boston Company Economic Advisors, said he expected a moderate recession lasting until the middle of next year.
