HEADLINE: Argentina and Brazil Renounce Atomic Weapons

BYLINE: By SHIRLEY CHRISTIAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Moving to soften a long dispute with the United States just days ahead of a visit to this area by President Bush, the Presidents of Brazil and Argentina formally renounced the manufacture of nuclear weapons today at a meeting on their border.
Presidents Fernando Collor de Mello of Brazil and Carlos Saul Menem of Argentina promised that their nuclear potential, the most advanced in Latin America, would be used for "exclusively peaceful ends." 
Meeting on the Brazilian side of the Iguassu Falls, they signed an accord to open negotiations with the International Atomic Energy Agency to set up a system of international safeguards and inspections and promised to begin inspections of each other's nuclear installations within 45 days.
Neither President said anything about eventually signing the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty, which has always been viewed in the two countries as an assault on national sovereignty.
If they join the more than 130 countries that have signed the nonproliferation accord, Argentina and Brazil would be required to submit to international controls and regular inspections by the international agency.
Both emphasized the agreement's voluntary, bilateral nature, with Mr. Menem, in remarks after the signing, saying it was part of a "global treatment of the different aspects of integration that will permit us to preserve the equilibrium necessary for peace, regional security and democracy."
Jose Luiz Carvalho, president of Brazil's National Nuclear Energy Commission, said in a television interview today in Rio de Janeiro that "all nuclear materials -- enriched uranium, plutonium -- will be checked and counted down to the last gram."
The agreement was brought about not so much by the changing international climate as by improving relations between the two countries, each now in its second term of civilian government.
The nuclear race between the historically antagonistic neighbors began during their periods of military rule, but neither is known to have actually produced a nuclear weapon.
The accord comes when both countries are putting aside some of their traditional nationalism and looking to their Latin American neighbors, the United States and other rich countries to help improve their economic situations through trade and investment. The trip that Mr. Bush is to begin Sunday to five South American countries -- Brazil, Uruguay, Argentina, Chile and Venezuela -- is expected to focus on his promise to create a hemispheric free-trade zone.
Last month, the Collor Government announced that it had uncovered a 15-year-old military program to make a Brazilian atom bomb and had halted the program.
In November 1983, shortly before giving up power, the Argentine military Government announced that it had the technology to enrich uranium at its plant at Pilcaniyeu, but it is not believed to have ever done so to the level needed to make fuel for nuclear explosives. Argentine civilian governments have always said they had no interest in nuclear weapons.
Both countries use nuclear power. Argentina's two functioning nuclear power plants have been plagued by failures and shutdowns in recent years, and construction of a third has been virtually halted by economic problems.
The accord signed today, a result of contacts begun between the two governments five years ago, says the two countries are prepared to reach an agreement with the International Atomic Energy Agency, a United Nations body, on the basis of "mutual accountability and control."
It further says that once that pact is completed they will proceed to abide by the Treaty of Tlatelolco, under which most other Latin American nations have committed themselves to keeping the area free of atomic weapons.
Argentina and Brazil signed the 1967 agreement, but neither nation has abided by it, citing a clause that the treaty does not formally go into effect until all the countries in the region have signed. Cuba is the sole holdout.
The United States and some European countries have long pressed Argentina and Brazil to subscribe to either the Tlatelolco Treaty or the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty.
But the two South American countries complained that the treaties seemed intended to prevent developing countries from enjoying the peaceful technological advances that industrialized countries have obtained as a side effect of nuclear weapons development.
Both Mr. Menem and Mr. Collor seemed to have that point in mind still with the last phrase in their agreement, which says that before abiding by the Tlatelolco Treaty they will make efforts toward "the updating and perfecting of its text."
 
MOVE WELCOMED IN U.S.

WASHINGTON, Nov. 28 (Special to The New York Times) -- The White House and State Department today had no official comment on the announcement by Brazil and Argentina, but an Administration official called it "something we have always promoted."
"We're very happy to see it happen," said the official, speaking on condition that he not be identified.
Private organizations that oppose the proliferation of nuclear weapons and materials also praised the two nations' action. The president of the Washington-based Nuclear Control Institute, Paul L. Leventhal, called the pact "an important breakthrough for global efforts to halt the further spread of nuclear weapons and for averting a nuclear arms race in Latin America in particular."
"This still remains an agreement in principle, however, and Argentina and Brazil should act promptly to ratify the Tlateloco Treaty" and carry out other terms of the accord, he said.

HEADLINE: Reservations On Upswing At Eastern

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS

 BODY:
Martin R. Shugrue, the trustee of Eastern Airlines, buoyantly told a news conference yesterday morning that bookings at Eastern reservation centers were 15 percent higher than had been expected, now that the carrier had beaten back the pressure to liquidate and the bankruptcy judge had given it $120 million with no strings attached.
Independent travel agents confirmed in interviews that they were booking more passengers on Eastern. 
 Peter Sontag, the chairman and chief executive of US Travel Systems, a major agency in the East, said that customers who had been uncertain whether Eastern would remain in business over the holidays were reassured by the judge's order.
"We've seen an increase in bookings today," he said. "Whether this moment of joy will last through spring is questionable."
 
'Eastern Is Getting Stronger'
 "Eastern still has a long way to go in proving to us that there is going to be consistent performance," Mr. Sontag added.
Steve Heydt, senior vice president of marketing for Liberty Travel, a large travel agency that is major user of Eastern, also said that the decision of Judge Burton R. Lifland had led to an increase in reservations. He said the ariline's bookings were already improving, thanks to its offer of an unrestricted $79 one-way fare between New York and Florida.
"Eastern is getting stronger," Mr. Heydt added.
Mr. Shugrue, relishing his victory yesterday morning in the news conference at the Holiday Inn Crown Plaza in midtown Manhattan, said that an unusually high number of calls were being taken at Eastern reservation centers, and that about three-quarters of the callers were booking flights compared with the usual half.
While some analysts publicly said Eastern might have a chance to reach its goal of breaking even by March, privately they were pessimistic, because of forces beyond Mr. Shugrue's control, like the Persian Gulf crisis and the weakening economy.
 
Walking a Fine Line
 The threat of war with Iraq has more than doubled the price of aviation fuel since last August, although prices have come down recently. Meanwhile, major carriers have seen their bookings drop, and have begun a series of promotional fares to attract more business.
Consequently, Mr. Shugrue will have to walk a fine line in the next few months, doing everything he can to turn the airline around while also taking into account the interests of the unsecured creditors and telling them and Judge Lifland if he believes that a turnaround has become impossible.
Yesterday morning Mr. Shugrue said he had been called by the heads of two trade associations of travel agents assuring him that the agents would book passengers on Eastern. That would address a major problem for Eastern; many travel agents have urged customers to book on other airlines because of fears that Eastern could shut down and leave them stranded.
Mr. Shugrue, in trying to keep the line going, will seek to sell off more of Eastern's assets to build up the airline's cash position and will continue to search for another carrier that may want to buy Eastern.
While major carriers like United, American, Delta and Northwest have said that they were interested in pieces of Eastern -- notably, the international routes and gates -- none has yet made an offer for the entire airline.

HEADLINE: EVOLUTION IN EUROPE;
U.S. Seems Baffled by Soviets' Disarray

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
With the Soviet Union under increasing domestic stress, Bush Administration officials say they are baffled about how to respond and increasingly worried that President Mikhail S. Gorbachev will have to use military force to maintain control.
Administration officials say that while they are considering an emergency shipment of medical supplies to Moscow, to help the Soviets deal with shortages caused by faulty production, they do not believe that there is anything more they could do at this time that would really help Mr. Gorbachev or stabilize events inside his country. 
 This is partly because officials in Washington say they believe that despite some alarming reports to the contrary, the Soviet Union will not experience any famine this winter.
 
A Distribution Problem
 The food shortages there, American officials insist, are not a production problem but a distribution problem. American officials say that they, unlike the Germans, are unwilling to simply give emergency food assistance on the assumption that anything would be helpful, because they are convinced it would be subjected to the same waste, hoarding and black-marketeering as Soviet food production.
These officials say they desperately hope that Mr. Gorbachev will remain in power because they see him as a cooperative partner for everything from arms control agreements to dealing with Iraq at the United Nations. Yet they say that investing in his economy before he has instituted a serious free-market reform program would be throwing away money.
While the Bush Administration has begun -- ever so slightly -- to hedge its bets on Mr. Gorbachev by opening more lines of communication with radical reformers and leaders in the 15 republics, it is reluctant to take this very far for fear of further eroding Mr. Gorbachev's position.
"We have considerable stakes in the foreign policies of Gorbachev," a senior official ssid. "We can, will and must do business with the central Government and Gorbachev, because our relationship with them has paved the way for the world to change, and no one should forget that. Beyond Gorbachev lies the unknown."
 
'Increasing Concern'
 "We don't count Gorbachev out by any means, but we see his options narrowing," the official said. "I would say there is increasing concern here that he will soon find himself boxed in and will have to resort to coercive force to hold things together."
Any crackdown by the Soviet leader could seriously complicate the Administration's relations with Moscow, precisely at the moment when its cooperation is most needed for dealing with Iraq. Fears of such a situation were heightened on Tuesday when the Soviet Defense Minister, Marshal Dimitri T. Yazov, said that his armed forces had been instructed to use force to defend military installations, monuments and servicemen against threats in rebellious republics.
The Administration's hesitation to adopt more far-reaching responses to the fragmentation of the Soviet Union also appears to be a result of the fact that President Bush, Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and other senior officials are so distracted by the Persian Gulf crisis that they have been unable to focus on the magnitude of events now unfolding in Moscow.
Officials say events in the Soviet Union are unfolding in such a confusing and contradictory manner that producing some overarching strategy to deal with them all would be impossible.
 
Famine Discounted
 On the most immediate question of economic assistance, a senior Administration official said, "All of our evidence says that there is no famine in the Soviet Union."
The official added: "There are ample food stocks, but the problems are hoarding, black-marketeering and a breakdown in the distribution system. If we were to give food, we would run into the same problems that the indigenous supply is. You can't just parachute in food. People say we supported an economic stabilization fund for Poland and not for the Soviets, but the Poles had a comprehensive economic reform plan. You knew exactly what you were feeding into. Here, there is no such thing. It is a black box."
Asked why the Administration could not follow the German example, the official replied: "People are looking into a black box and maybe some are more willing to run the risk of working in there than we are. Remember, the Russians haven't even asked us for anything yet. It is abundantly clear that we would respond if there were a real emergency, but there is no food emergency."
That approach to the Soviet question appears to be widely supported in Congress.
 
Food and Warheads
 "I would be a little more forthcoming on the food and humanitarian side, but beyond that I don't have any real bones to pick with the Administration's policy," said Representative Lee H. Hamilton, Democrat of Indiana and chairman of the House Foreign Affairs subcommittee on Europe and the Middle East. "We have to make our decisions on aid on the basis of whether it will be effective, not to score political points."
Always in the background when the question of Soviet disintegration is being discussed is the question of what might happen to the Soviet Union's 12,000 nuclear warheads, dispersed in virtually every Soviet republic.
 
Importance of Russia
 "One can't discount anything," a senior Administration policy maker said. "That said, the sort of nightmare of the crazed nationalist general threatening nuclear holocaust for his cause is probably a misplaced fear. We assume, and again nobody can be absolutely certain, that there are enough dual and overlapping controls in the system that you would have to be dealing with a very big conspiracy in order to get control of these weapons from the center."
The official concluded that the Administration's "working assumption" is that as long as there is a central authority in the Russian republic -- that is, as long as it doesn't fragment along with the Soviet Union as a whole -- there is little danger of a breakdown in the systems for securing, releasing and launching Soviet nuclear weapons.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY Alan Greenspan said the nation's economy is shrinking, making him the first top Government official to speak of a contraction. He told Congress economic activity has been contracting since last month, largely because of the crisis in the Persian Gulf. [Page A1.]
 The economy expanded at a 1.7 percent rate in the third quarter, a tenth of percentage point less than previously estimated, the Commerce Department said. But private analysts said the figure masked the progressive contraction of the economy that started in the late summer. [D1.]
 Durable goods orders rose a sharp 3.6 percent in October, the best gain in five months. But without a surge in demand for commercial aircraft, orders fell four-tenths of 1 percent, after rising one-tenth of 1 percent in September. [D1.]
 Ways to strengthen the bank deposit insurance program are being sought by the Administration. [D8.]
 
COMPANIES
Scores of small, independent clothiers are closing or thinking seriously about it because of burdensome loans, fearsome competition and faint-hearted consumers. A wave of store closings will come with the New Year, experts say. [D1.]
 Many big commercial banks are trying to sell chunks of their businesses. Arranging sales before the end of the year would make financial statements look better and cast a more positive light on a disappointing year. [D1.]
 Manufacturers Hanover Trust agreed to buy 13 branches and about $1.6 billion of deposits from Goldome F.S.B. [D1.]

HEADLINE: AFTER SCARE, SUIT BY APPLE FARMERS

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: YAKIMA, Wash., Nov. 28

 BODY:
A group of apple growers filed a lawsuit today against the CBS and its news program "60 Minutes" and an environmental group, seeking compensation for declining sales during a scare over the growth chemical Alar.
The industry has estimated that growers lost more than $100 million following the news program's report on Feb. 26, 1989, about a study, "Intolerable Risk: Pesticides In Our Children's Food," by the Natural Resources Defense Council, the environmental group named in the suit. 
 The report concluded that Alar, the trade name for the chemical daminozide, posed a particular risk of cancer in children, who eat and drink larger amounts of apples and apple products for their body weight than adults do. Alar was used to make the crop ripen uniformly on the tree so that the apples could be harvested all at once. It also helped the fruit retain its firmness and color.
 
 Voluntarily Removed
 The report was disputed at the time, but sales and wholesale prices of apples plummeted. Some school districts temporarily pulled apples from lunchrooms. Signs appeared in grocery stores advertising apples free of the chemical.
Alar was voluntarily removed from the market in October 1989 by its manufacturer, the Uniroyal Chemical Company. The Environmental Protection Agency banned it from all food uses in May.
The reaction was felt most acutely in Washington, where more than 60 percent of the apples that are sold in the nation's supermarkets are grown.
The lawsuit, filed in Superior Court by a group of 11 Washington apple growers, seeks to represent the estimated 4,700 growers of red apples in the state. It seeks unspecified damages running to millions of dollars.
The lawsuit contends that growers suffered economic losses as a direct result of "false, misleading, scientifically unreliable statements of the defendants."
 
 'Fundamental Issues' Seen
 Burt Chestnut, an apple grower from Wenatchee who is a plaintiff in the suit, said: "The gain is for agriculture in general nationwide, that we stop this misleading information by the media and scare tactics by environmental groups. We want the media to be responsible."
Steven Berzon, a lawyer representing the Natural Resources Defense Council, said the lawsuit could have a chilling effect on public activism.
"Beyond Alar, this suit threatened by a small group of Washington growers raises fundamental issues of free speech and the right of citizens to petition the government on important questions of public policy," Mr. Berzon said.
A spokesman for "60 Minutes," Roy Brunett, said: "We believe the broadcast highlighted an important public health matter. It was well researched and well documented."

HEADLINE: Dinosaur Displays Closing for Renovation

BYLINE: By MALCOLM W. BROWNE

 BODY:
The American Museum of Natural History, which houses the largest collection of fossils in the world, has begun closing down its immensely popular dinosaur exhibitions as it initiates a five-year renovation project, the institution's most ambitious such remodeling since it was founded in 1869.
Displays as familiar to generations of children as the Empire State Building will be swept away, to be replaced by old bones in dramatic new guises. 
 The Hall of Early Dinosaurs has already been partly closed, and in February, it will close completely for two years. The destruction of a famous blue-and-white chalk mural depicting various Jurassic-period dinosaurs was under way yesterday. The Hall of Late Dinosaurs (which contains Tyrannosaurus rex, the museum's superstar) will remain open until February 1992, but will then close for nearly three years.
At a news conference yesterday, museum officials said the long-overdue modernization would make the museum's dinosaur displays the most impressive and informative in the world. But some schoolchildren touring the museum were dismayed to learn that some familiar Mesozoic friends were temporarily off limits.
 
Classification at Issue
 A more formidable critic is Dr. Edwin H. Colbert, the museum's own former curator of vertebrate paleontology, who in 1953 directed the remodeling of the dinosaur halls that are now being replaced. Dr. Colbert and other paleontologists' main complaint is that the museum will no longer group dinosaur fossils chronologically, but will instead group them according to shared physiological characteristics, even if they did not live at the same time or in the same place.
Thus, for example, the horned dinosaur triceratops, which was an ornithischian (bird-hipped) dinosaur, will no longer be displayed next to its contemporary arch enemy, Tyrannosaurus rex, which was a saurischian (lizard-hipped) dinosaur.
George D. Langdon Jr., the museum's president, acknowledged that some museumgoers wished the dinosaur exhibits would never change, while others approved of the museum's plans. "The fact is, we must change to maintain our position as the great institution we are," he said. "New York's Metropolitan Museum of Art carried out a modernization program that was eminently successful, and we must do the same."
Mr. Langdon said contributors had already pledged $20 million for the project, and that New York State and City administrators had been supportive. Despite the straitened economic climate, he said, the museum is confident it can raise the rest of the needed money.
The refurbishing and modernization of the museum's fossil exhibits will produce some spectacular new displays, including a 55-foot-high cast of a dinosaur fossil that will tower over visitors as they enter the cathedral-like Roosevelt Rotunda from Central Park. The fossil replica will be displayed rearing on its hind legs to protect its fossilized baby from a fossil allosaur. The cast is being prepared in Toronto from bones of the real fossil supplied by the museum.
 
It's Huge, but Is It Real?
 The barosaur replica will be by far the largest dinosaur ever displayed anywhere in the world. But Dr. Colbert expressed disapproval. "I think that when a kid looks at a dinosaur skeleton, he or she wants to know that it's the real thing, not a cast," he said. "If you have to replace some missing bones with casts, that's fine, but something of the real animal should be there."
Dr. Michael J. Novacek, a paleontologist and the dean of science at the museum, replied that it would have been impossible to mount so large and heavy a fossil in such a dramatic pose. The comparative lightness of cast bones, however, made the construction possible. Several real bones from the huge beast will be displayed elsewhere in the museum, he said.
"The major organizational change," Dr. Novacek said, "is that a visitor will be able to walk around the entire floor of fossils, moving logically from one evolutionary phase to the next. In the new sequence, a visitor will start in an orientation center and then move through the hall of primitive vertebrates, the hall of saurischian dinosaurs, the hall of ornithischian dinosaurs, the hall of primitive mammals, and the hall of late mammals. The entire span of vertebrate evolution will be encompassed by the fossils."
The renovation will include new artwork and models, dramatic lighting, computer animation, interactive computer explanations, and possibly some dinosaur robots. The displays will also summarize some major scientific debates, including the debate as to what caused the extinction of the dinosaurs.
 
Correcting Some Errors
 The project will also give the museum's experts time to correct some errors dating from the 19th century. The most widely publicized of these errors is in the museum's brontosaur (or as some paleontologists prefer to call it, apatosaur), the 150-foot behemoth that has dominated the Hall of Early Dinosaurs since the hall opened on May 21, 1953. Pictures of the Nine-Mile Quarry Brontosaur, named for the quarry from which it was excavated in 1898, have illustrated countless publications, including a 1954 cover of Colliers magazine. But in the 1970's paleontologists realized that the skull mounted on the fossil was actually that of a camarasaurus -- a completely different animal. The museum has never replaced the skull, but since last year it has displayed an acknowledgement and explanation of the creature's wrongheadedness.
Dr. Colbert, who, with Henry Fairfield Osborn, was one of the main architects of the museum's world-famous dinosaur exhibitions, believes remodeling and renovation of the museum are essential. "A museum has to change or it withers," he said. But he objects strongly to part of the scientific philosophy underlying the museum's planned modernization, a system known as "cladistics."
The traditional system for ranking and relating different types of organisms places each type on an evolutionary tree, which suggests not only kinship by evolutionary sequence. Cladistics, on the other hand, compares the "characters" (distinctive physiological characteristics or antecedents) of one organism with another, and adds up the shared characteristics, without specifying the lines of descent. The more matching characteristicsthat are found between two organisms, the closer is their presumed kinship.
The British Museum (Natural History) was embroiled for a decade in a bitter debate between supporters of cladistics and their tradition-minded opponents, and Dr. Colbert said the debate had affected the Museum of Natural History as well. In that debate, the cladists overwhelmed their opponents and the museum has been organized on cladistic principles.
"Cladistics has resulted in serious errors of classification," he said. "A kitten may have been born in an oven, but you can't assume, as the cladists might, that the kitten is therefore a biscuit."

HEADLINE: When the Need to Help Won't Take Early Retirement

BYLINE: By PATRICIA LEIGH BROWN

DATELINE: ASHEVILLE, N.C.

 BODY:
GEORGE KREIDLER, 71 years old, drives a Chevy Cavalier with "A Touch of Class" on his license plate. "You have to have verve," he said.
A former linebacker for the Green Bay Packers with the shoulders to prove it, Mr. Kreidler retired six years ago after 31 years supervising the construction of oil refineries and chemical and nuclear-power plants for the Bechtel Corporation. 
 At age 65, after living all over the country and raising "five girls, one boy and a dog," he settled in Asheville. He now plays a lot of golf and bridge, but his daily routine also includes supervising house construction for the nonprofit Habitat for Humanity, singing with a group of troubadours composed of "16 men and one lovely lady," and taking part in a program at the North Carolina Center for Creative Retirement, in which he is the mentor to a young university athlete.
Founded in 1987, the center, which is on the campus of the University of North Carolina in the gentle furrows of the Blue Ridge mountains, represents the "institutionalizing of an informal trend," said its director, Ronald J. Manheimer. In contrast to a more hedonistic, leisure-oriented view of retirement, the center, which is part of the university, is combining volunteerism and community service with the national movement toward retirement-age learning.
"We're looking at new roles older people are playing and new norms of what it means to be an older person," said the 47-year-old Dr. Manheimer, a philosophy professor by training whose unglamorous campus office is down the hall from the wildlife parasitology laboratory.
Many who have flocked to Asheville represent a new breed of active, independent retiree, for whom a need to help society at large is as important as personal enrichment. " 'Creative retirement' is obviously a catchy phrase," said 61-year-old Charles E. Lilien, a retired foreign service officer and former managing director of First Boston International, now active with the center.
But he added, "As a concept it's useful, because through the center we have an institutional home, an entity that serves as an armature, like a sculptor uses."
While some of its concepts might not be new, the center's comprehensive approach has attracted the attention of many educators and gerontologists. Participants, who number around 2,000 a year and find out about the center mostly by word of mouth, take part in activities that include a College for Seniors; programs that provide mentors for young people; a "leadership for seniors" program that enlists retirees to tutor elementary and high school students; off-campus reading discussion groups, and an annual Senior Wellness Day offering everything from health screening to mall-walking.
Participants share a commitment to social responsibility and the idea that older students "can also be leaders," Dr. Manheimer said. Most of the current crop moved to the region before the center's founding. Although many had audited courses and had volunteered for civic causes, "finding the paths to these roles isn't always easy," he said.
Mr. Kreidler, for example, said the opportunity to counsel a young college athlete about his academic performance and career direction "just tickled me."
"If I've helped some young fellow get associated in life," he said, "I've made my life more full."
"We knew about beer and women," he added, referring to his own college days. "There were many times if someone had squared me up I would have gotten more out of college."
The center's existence reflects broad social and demographic trends, said Harry Moody, the deputy director of the Brookdale Center on Aging at Hunter College in New York. "The notion of creative retirement is bubbling up from the grass roots," he said. "Many options held out to older people view life as a big playpen, but the unfortunate truth is they don't necessarily inspire a purpose in life."
Cathy Ventura-Merkel, a senior education specialist for the American Association of Retired Persons, said older people are increasingly interested in "sharing their wisdom, not retiring from life."
There are more retirees nowadays, and they are better educated: the older population is healthier, the number of people taking early retirement is rising, and their median educational level is going up -- to 12.1 years in 1988, as against 8.7 years in 1970. In 1989, people reaching age 65 could expect to live an average of 17 years longer. The numbers will reach an apex between the years 2010 and 2030, when members of the baby-boom generation turn 65, Ms. Ventura-Merkel said.
Until fairly recently in history, retirement was considered a "brief period of life that only a fraction of the population would experience," said Ken Dychtwald, the chairman and chief executive of Agewave Inc., a market research and consulting concern in Emeryville, Calif. But now, older Americans can expect to spend 20 to 25 percent of their lives retired.
Among the first generation of long-lived retirees, this period was symbolized by leisure, by verdant freshly mowed communities that suggested, Mr. Dychtwald said, "recreationland instead of Disneyland." But values are being reappraised, he said: "The new vision of retirement intermingles work, play, learning and service."
That intermingling was on display in Asheville during a cookie and decaffeinated-coffee break in a bioethics class at the College for Seniors in Owen Hall, when the students -- among them an auto dealer, a nurse, a chemist, an English teacher, three physicists, two doctors, an insurance executive and two clergymen, all retired -- began a noisy debate over whether there should be a market in human body parts.
College classes are taught and attended by the retirees, who pay a fee of $80 a semester. The College for Seniors is one of the center's approaches to what Dr. Manheimer called "imaginative pathfinding." There are more than 130 such institutes for retirement-age students across the country.
The morning also saw 19-year-old Paige McKenzie -- a Phil Collins fan and physics major -- huddled over a spectrometer with her 66-year-old mentor, Edward Schurtz, who has a predilection for baggy sweatshirts, bridge games and classical music.
Miss McKenzie was not sure at first what to make of her older tutor, a retired Du Pont chemical engineer with a Ph.D. degree. And he was not sure what to make of her, since "when I went to school you didn't find young women majoring in physics."
But this "intergenerational research team," as the school calls it, has a dual mission. Mr. Schurtz is getting back into scientific research, while Miss McKenzie gets the benefit of his years at Du Pont and the message, he said, that "she's not alone."
The center operates on a yearly budget of $300,000 to $400,000, about a third of it from the state and the rest from grants and membership fees. Currently it is wrestling with several problems, like how to keep the retirees involved in the program as they age and lose some mobility. There is also the question of how to reach out to the region's rural, less-affluent and less-educated population. This year the center sponsored a conference about how similar programs might be set up elsewhere.
The center's founding was an acknowledgement of the significant economic and social contributions of the region's relatively privileged retired "amenity migrants," who have long been drawn to western North Carolina's climate, mountains and cultural milieu. The new immigrants are well educated and affluent, spending an average of $16,791 a year in routine purchases and doing volunteer work for an average of 7.4 hours a week, according to a recent study by the center financed by the Appalachian Regional Commission, an economic-development agency.
The volunteerism is not solely altruistic. Evelyn Smith, 64, taught kindergarten and nursery school in private schools in Montgomery County in Maryland for 20 years. She said that when she was approached by center volunteers about tutoring at a public school in Asheville, William Randolph Elementary, she was prompted to do it "because I'm concerned about inequality in our schools."
But there were other motivations. "It makes me feel youthful," she said of helping 7-year-old Kita Banner with her reading. "I'm not doing it to be goody two-shoes."
"Teaching is something I know I can do," she added. "It's nice to be able to do the thing you're good at."
Long on life experience, center participants like Mrs. Smith seem attuned to what makes their generation special. When asked his qualifications for tutoring grade-school students, Marcus D. Beck, who taught school for 21 years after working as a locomotive engineer, listed his job experience on the application. Then he added a P.S.:
"I have my act together."

CORRECTION-DATE: December 4, 1990, Thursday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An article in The Home Section on Thursday about the North Carolina Center for Creative Retirement referred incorrectly to a participant, George Kreidler. He was a blocking back for the Green Bay Packers in the 1940 exhibition season, and later played with the team's farm club. He was not a linebacker.

HEADLINE: Architects Show How to Set a Grand Table

BYLINE: By SUZANNE SLESIN

 BODY:
NINE years ago Nan Swid and Addie Powell went into business "to design functional, mass-produced objects for the home by well-known architects," in Mrs. Powell's words. Now, after proving that a pitcher designed by Richard Meier can be a suitable vehicle for Kool-Aid, the two women are about to introduce the Architects' Collection, a limited-edition series of household items that happen to cost $5,000 to $35,000. 
 The collection goes on display Tuesday at the SwidPowell showroom in Manhattan (213 East 49th Street) and the partners are a bit nervous about their latest endeavor. "After all," Mrs. Powell asked, "who does these things in a recession?"
They do.
And to top it all off, on Saturday they are celebrating the publication of "SwidPowell: Objects by Architects" ( Rizzoli , $35), a kind of family album of the first steps of their progeny, at the Rizzoli Bookstore on West 57th Street in Manhattan. Several of their famous architect friends will join them to autograph the new volumes.
All the goings-on recall the days when the partners were starting out on their adventure of bringing architects' designs to every dining-room table. Until 1982, both worked at Knoll International, the modern-furniture company. Mrs. Swid, whose husband, Stephen, was Knoll's co-chairman from 1978 to 1986, was a design director in product development and Mrs. Powell a vice president in sales.
"At the time," Mrs. Swid said, "it seemed that all design was coming out of Europe and Japan. We felt this generation of architects could do it, too."
The fact that some of the top architects of the day also happened to be good friends made it all the more congenial. The fledgling partners gathered a group that included Joseph Paul d'Urso, Stanley Tigerman, Charles Gwathmey and Robert Siegel, Robert A. M. Stern and Mr. Meier in a private room at the Four Seasons, a favorite watering hole.
"Philip Johnson was there as everyone's leader," Mrs. Swid said. "After lunch, everyone went back to their drawing boards."
No design fees were to be paid to the architects; the architects would get royalties from the sale of their products. In what seemed less time than it takes to get most architectural jobs out to bid, "we found we were in a real business," Mrs. Swid said. "It's called tabletop."
After the initial collection appeared in 1984, including 18 dinner plates that retailed for $50 to $80 each, it was on to gift wares: a $125 cookie jar in the shape of a little building by Mr. Tigerman; a Meier key ring and money clip ($60 each) and a baby collection. They have sold more than 25,000 five-piece place settings ($190 each) of Gwathmey Siegel's black-and-white Tuxedo dinnerware and introduced children's versions -- pink for girls, blue for boys.
Now, instead of marrying architects' visions to mass-production methods, the partners have given the designers an opportunity to do whatever they want.
"Think of it as fashion design, when the designer comes out with the extreme and fun wedding gown," Mrs. Swid said. "We asked the architects to parallel that. We gave them total creative license.
"Usually we give the architects strict guidelines," Mrs. Swid went on, "specific price points and what kinds of products they should be thinking about."
The resulting collection was made by young silversmiths and jewelers out of a king's ransom of precious materials: silver, mother-of-pearl, lapis lazuli, ebony and onyx. This time nobody told Mr. Tigerman to design a fluted sterling silver tureen with wing-shaped handles and feet made of rose quartz spheres (limited to 20 at $30,000 each).
Nor was Frank Gehry discouraged about the high price of lead crystal goblets in the shape of fish, balanced on asymmetrical bases (250 sets of six glasses are planned at $5,000 a set). "One of the most difficult," said Marc Hacker vice president of design and development at SwidPowell. "The base is cast glass, the bowl hand blown. The problem was how to marry the two."
Nor was Robert Venturi's centerpiece a piece of cake. The sterling silver tripod holding a porcelain sphere adorned with Matisse-like decorations ($20,000 each, edition of 20) was "extraordinarily difficult," Mr. Hacker said. "It looks pretty simple, but it was like trying to send someone to Mars."
Peter Eisenman's 10-candle candelabrum -- an intricate interlocking structure of L-shaped black polished chrome elements (an edition of five at $15,000 apiece) -- must have been an engineering feat, while George Sowden's sterling-silver and black-marble bowl (an edition of 20, at $12,000 each) offers a deceptively simple variation on the paper doily.
While most of the pieces reflect postmodernism, the candelabrum by David Palterer ( 25 at $7,500 each) seems to hark back to a more fanciful era. It is described in the SwidPowell catalogue as "a fluted column of Medici marble resting on a gold-plated disc supporting a gilded bronze casting of a Mount Sinai twig."
Then there is Mr. Stern's contribution, described in the catalogue as: "A sterling silver ice bucket with eight columns supporting a circular beam with triglyph motifs. The lid has a radiating tile pattern edged with a sea scroll and is capped with an urn supporting a gold flame finial. The columns sit on a stepped base and surround a drum of etched cobalt blue glass."
Or, if you care, it looks just like the Choragic Monument of Lysicrates in Athens. Only 20 are to be made, at $20,000 each.
"We've already gotten calls from Japan," Mrs. Powell said. "But the most fun will be the first sale."
Mrs. Swid is already looking ahead to the day when her company completely takes over its small building in the group of town houses known as Amster Yard. "That's when we'll be the House of SwidPowell," she said. "Dior will have nothing on us."

Two hundred and fifty sets of Frank Gehry's fish-shaped goblet ($5,000 for a set of six) are planned. (Fred R. Conrad/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Changes Don't Improve College Entrance Test

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The revisions to the Scholastic Aptitude Test that go into effect in spring 1994 (front page, Nov. 1) further discredit the College Board as a responsible guardian for the S.A.T. The S.A.T. under the College Board has always measured, and will continue to measure, not a person's aptitude for success in college, but an individual's ability to perform well on a poorly designed test. 
 The essay section of the revised test, for example, will be part of the verbal section graded on a 200-point to 800-point scale. I am curious as to how the Educational Testing Service plans to assign an intrinsic value to an essay written in 20 minutes by a teen-ager in a highly stressful situation. I am skeptical of the company's claims that it can effectively categorize the writing skills of prospective college students, many of whom might find it difficult to write something profound on a subject in which they have no interest.
The credibility the College Board seeks in assigning David Gardner, president of the University of California, to head the panel that reviewed the changes vanishes when one considers that the State of California threatened to stop using the S.A.T. if the proposed essay section was mandatory, rather than optional.
Another strange tactic to improve the S.A.T. can be found in the revised math section. If one of the College Board's goals is to make the test less culturally and economically biased, why allow calculators? Will the Educational Testing Service supply calculators to children who cannot afford one or to children who have not been coached to bring one?
The S.A.T. is obsolete. A test that has made references to such concepts as royalties and dividends in the verbal section is not culturally unbiased. The best way for colleges and universities to evaluate their applicants is through transcripts, recommendations and achievement tests for specific areas of knowledge, such as foreign languages, history, physics.
Although many colleges profess to downplay S.A.T. scores in their admissions, a great number of students are accepted into highly competitive schools because of S.A.T. scores, and many other students are rejected because of S.A.T. scores.
The only group that stands to benefit from S.A.T. revisions are the coaching services that prey on fears of parents that secondary schools will not prepare their children for the S.A.T.
 
DANIEL I. SHRAGER
Philadelphia, Nov. 9, 1990

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: Keeping Commodities Exchanges in New York

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The decision of the commodities exchanges to remain in New York City (news article, Nov. 7) and expand their work forces demonstrates the importance of city-state action when employers are reconsidering their commitments.
This victory for New York also proves that public incentive programs count when our local economy's competitiveness is tested. Mayor David N. Dinkins and the Urban Development Corporation chairman, Vincent Tese, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's representative, and Deputy Mayor Sally B. Hernandez-Pinero led a team that designed an incentive package attractive enough to keep the exchanges from accepting a somewhat larger offer from Jersey City, but prudent enough to make New York a big net gainer in jobs and taxes.
The state-city coalition saved us an industry that contributes $1.3 billion a year in salaries, goods and services to our community; employs 11,700 men and women, and generates $164 million in revenue annually for City Hall and Albany. The $138 million in benefits to the exchanges provided by our municipal and state governments is a sound investment that will be repaid to the public treasury many times over.
No less significant, Mayor Dinkins, Mr. Tese, Ms. Hernandez-Pinero and their colleagues have reinforced the city's standing as the country's financial capital. For all this, they deserve every New Yorker's congratulations and gratitude.
 
STEVEN SPINOLA
President
Real Estate Board of New York
New York, Nov. 12, 1990

HEADLINE: Governador Valadares Journal;
You Might Say, This Is the Town Uncle Sam Built

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: GOVERNADOR VALADARES, Brazil

 BODY:
In the vast, arid interior of Minas Gerais State rises a tidy, thriving city that residents call "Brazil's best American town."
Lacking any visible source of income other than ranching, Governador Valadares is sprouting high-rise apartment buildings. The tallest, with an Art Deco motif, is called the Empire State Building. Around the central square, teen-age girls shop for outfits at Benetton. Boys sit on park benches, reading Mad magazine in Portuguese. 
 When money exchange shops started to outnumber tractor dealerships, locals knew a fundamental change had taken place. "Almost all the families here now have a relative in the United States," said Ilca Quirino dos Santos, surveying her neighborhood from her veranda.
The sound of hammering emerged from the shell of a building next door. A two-story house for her son, Carlos Alberto, was being built with weekly remittances from his job washing dishes in a Boston restaurant.
 
Unbroken Flow of Emigrants
 The lure of working illegally in the United States, traditionally associated with Mexico and the Caribbean, has reached down to Brazil, with 153 million people South America's most populous nation.
Disillusioned after a decade of economic stagnation, inflation and politicians' unfulfilled promises, Brazilians are migrating north in a flow unbroken by the inauguration this year of Fernando Collor de Mello, a President who promises a "new Brazil."
Although no reliable figures are available, hundreds of thousands of Brazilians are thought to be working in the United States. Historically, those most prone to emigration are residents of this city of 250,000 people.
The United States connection dates from World War II, when American planes took off regularly from here carrying mica, then an essential material for making radios. In return, the American Government helped to combat malaria and to build the town's water and sewage system. After the war, American businessmen started coming here as the city became a trading center for semiprecious stones.
America "was always something palpable," said Lana Mara de Castro Siman, a regional historian.
 
A Nest Egg Is the Aim
 In the 1960's, unemployment spread after loggers cut down all the tropical forest around here. Young men started making the 10-hour bus trip to Rio de Janeiro, the first step in the journey to the United States.
"The Brazilian is not an emigrant -- he is generally earning a nest egg for life in Brazil," said Jose Victor Bicalho, a psychiatrist here.
Fascinated by the exodus, Mr. Bicalho traveled in 1989 to Framingham, Mass., where an estimated 6,000 Brazilians work. Supporting himself by washing dishes in a steak house and pressing clothes in a dry cleaner's, he studied the emigrants.
The profile that emerged was of a single man under 35 years of age with a high-school education or less. Virtually all of the group surveyed said they planned to return to Brazil within three years.
Eighty percent said they shared an apartment with five or more people to save money and 92 percent said they spent less than $300 a month on rent, food and transport. Mr. Bicalho found that Brazilians most commonly worked as dishwashers, busboys, hotel maids, gardeners, factory workers and supermarket checkout clerks. But with the United States enjoying a per capita income 10 times that of Brazil, the last in Boston are the first in Governador Valadares.
Livio Rodrigues Gomes, a cook's helper in Framingham, is the owner of Livio's bar here, on the second floor of the city bus station. "It is very hard in Brazil to earn any real money," Mr. Gomes said shortly after returning from a two-year stint in a Framingham steakhouse.
With his savings, he put a new floor in his house here, bought a $6,000 station wagon, spent $3,000 on a medical operation for one daughter and paid for a $3,000 wedding for another. Over all, the estimated 10,000 Valadarenses living in the United States are believed to send home more than $20 million a year.
 
The Money Goes for Cows
 The dos Santos household uses a classic hedge of rural Brazil against inflation -- all surplus remittances are invested in cows.
"He earns $800 a week and sends home $400 a week," Mrs. dos Santos said, leafing through an album containing photos of Carlos Alberto in New England. Before going there last year, he earned $25 a week helping his father drive a milk truck here.
Claudia dos Santos, his 22-year-old sister, exclaimed, "I would give anything to go the United States."
But Miss dos Santos, like many residents here, has run into a brick wall at the United States Consulate in Rio de Janeiro. Consular officials who issue visas for this part of Brazil say that the overall refusal rate has averaged 19 percent over the last three years. But travel agents here, where several agencies have closed in recent months, charge that the consulate routinely denies most visa applications from residents of "Brazil's American town."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Governador Valadares, above, has been nicknamed Brazil's American town because of the many residents who go to the United States. Livio Rodrigues Gomes, left, was a cook's helper in Framingham, Mass., where he earned a better income than as the owner of a bar back home. (Photographs by James Brooke/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: PAKISTANI OFFERS ATOM ISSUE TALKS

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ISLAMABAD, Pakistan, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif said today that he was ready to open talks with Washington on Pakistan's nuclear program, the central issue in what officials here call Pakistan's most serious crisis in relations with the United States in more than three decades.
But the new Prime Minister added that his Government, elected on a platform of greater economic self-reliance for Pakistan, is prepared to live with the consequences if negotiations, scheduled to begin next Thursday, fail. 
 Mr. Sharif took office on Nov. 6, a month after all aid to Pakistan was suspended by the United States Congress because of developments in Islamabad's nuclear program.
He said in an interview today that "there are certain conditions that we cannot accept."
"We've got to live honorably," he said. "Our self-respect is very dear to us." He did not elaborate.
 
Advances in Arms Program
 American officials say advances in Pakistan's nuclear weapons program in early 1990, apparently in response to fears of a possible war with India over Kashmir, were clearly unacceptable under American laws governing aid to Islamabad.
Because the Bush Administration could no longer certify that Pakistan was not building a nuclear weapon or that American aid was dissuading Pakistan from building one, Congress cut off aid to Islamabad, which had been running at $500 million a year.
All major parties in Pakistan have at one time or another backed a nuclear arms program. Public opinion polls here support Islamabad's belief that it has the right to develop a nuclear deterrent so long as India, which detonated a device in 1974, has one.
Many Pakistanis are sharply critical of Congressionally mandated certification process that they say single out Pakistan not only on the nuclear issue but also on narcotics control and the general progress of democracy.
They believe that because the civil war in neighboring Afghanistan is slowly grinding toward resolution, the United States no longer needs Pakistan as a conduit for assistance to the Afghan rebels.
Mr. Sharif's Islamic Democratic Alliance made "American interference" a potent issue in its campaign for the Oct. 24 national elections, accusing the former Prime Minister, Benazir Bhutto, of compromising national sovereignty because of her close ties to members of the American Congress.
 
Peaceful Uses Promised
 On his first full day as Prime Minister, Mr. Sharif said he would step up the country's nuclear energy program, but he stressed that the effort would be devoted exclusively to peaceful ends and that he was "against the destructive use of atomic power."
But today, speaking in his official residence, he said it would be a mistake to "mix up" the aid issue and his calls for self-reliance, which are prompted by Pakistan's long-term economic failures.
"There should be some day in our history when we learn to stand on our own," said Mr. Sharif, an industrialist and the first Prime Minister not to come from the landed aristocracy of Sind Province. His father was a self-made man.
Mr. Sharif added that since the nationalization of industries and banks in the 1970's, Pakistan has been on an economic slide that must be arrested from within its own resources.
"It has come to the point where we have been borrowing money for nondevelopment expenditures," he said. "Can any nation run like this? It is suicide."
 
Koreans Once Came for Advice
 He said that in the 1950's, South Koreans were coming to Pakistan for development advice.
"Now look at South Korea," he said, implying a contrast with Pakistan, one of Asia's poorest nations.
Mr. Sharif says that he has assigned committees to draw up plans for the privatization of industries and banks. He wants to attract investment and build up economic sectors that earn hard currency, including tourism. He wants to make Pakistanis start paying income taxes.
In the interview, he said he was framing legislation to deal with rising violence and the spread of narcotics trafficking and drug abuse. Pakistan has 1.5 million heroin addicts in a population of 114 million.
His new Minister for Narcotics Control is Raja Chandra Singh, a Hindu with no links to powerful Pakistani Muslim families, some of which are suspected of being involved in the drug trade, along with Afghan exiles.

HEADLINE: Consumers Said to Retrench As October Spending Stalls

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Consumer spending, the driving force behind eight years of economic expansion, did not budge in October, the Government said today. Analysts viewed the report as further evidence of a recession.
"The consumer has retrenched," said Gilbert Benz, an economist with the Swiss Bank Corporation in New York. "It clearly supports those who say we're in a recession." 
 John Silvia, an economist with Kemper Financial Services in Chicago, said, "It looks like consumer spending will be negative in the fourth quarter," and the gross national product will decline in both the current quarter and the first three months of next year.
Consumer spending, which accounts for two-thirds of the nation's economic activity, was unchanged from September at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $3.73 trillion, the Commerce Department said. Personal incomes rose just one-tenth of 1 percent, to a $4.71 trillion rate.
The marginal increase in incomes failed to match the six-tenths of 1 percent increase in the Labor Department's Consumer Price Index in October. It was also the smallest gain since incomes were unchanged in August 1989. Spending had posted gains each month since last May, when it was unchanged.
 
 Incomes After Taxes
 Disposable incomes, or incomes after taxes, also inched ahead one-tenth of 1 percent, down from a four-tenths of 1 percent gain the previous month. It was the smallest gain since after-tax incomes fell one-tenth of 1 percent last April.
The spending and income figures were not adjusted for inflation. When adjusted, spending actually fell seven-tenths of 1 percent after a two-tenths of 1 percent increase in September. Disposable income dropped five-tenths of 1 percent, even more than the four-tenths of 1 percent declines in the previous two months.
"Consumer spending can survive a lot of things -- temporary shocks, consumer confidence, oil surprises -- but three months in a row of negative disposable income suggests that people are going to have to cut back even more," Mr. Silvia said.
Indeed, Alan Greenspan, Federal Reserve chairman, noted on Wednesday that shrinking employment and the number of hours worked were causing personal income to decline while rising energy prices "are squeezing many household budgets."
"This drop in real purchasing power, along with plunging consumer sentiment, does not bode well for the near-term trends in consumer demand," he told the House Banking Committee.
It was the first look at consumer spending in the final quarter of the year. The department on Wednesday revised personal consumption in the third quarter down from a 3.6 percent annual rate in its initial report to 3.2 percent, suggesting spending was slowing toward the end of that period.
"There was weakness across the board" in October, Mr. Benz said.
A key component of the income category -- wages and salaries -- fell $9.3 billion after gaining $16.7 billion the previous month. It was the first decline since November 1989, when it dropped $7.6 billion.
Mr. Silvia said the major prop in the one-tenth of 1 percent gain in overall personal income was transfer payments like unemployment insurance and cost-of-living adjustments in the food stamp program -- both further evidence of a weak economy.
Americans' savings rate -- savings as a percent of disposable income -- edged up to 3.8 percent, from 3.6 percent in September. The September rate was the lowest since a 3.5 percent rate in January 1988 and an indication that consumers are having to dip into their savings to pay for their purchases.
Americans' savings rate for all of 1989 was 4.6 percent.

HEADLINE: Murdoch Hopes for Pact To Defer $5 Billion Debt

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The News Corporation hopes to reach an agreement with bankers by early January on a new financing agreement that would defer repayment on about $5 billion in debt for the next three years, an executive close to the company said yesterday.
The Australian-based company, headed by Rupert Murdoch, has amassed a large debt by aggressively expanding internationally into newspapers, book publishing, movie and television production, television stations and magazines. 
 But unexpectedly slow asset sales, economic weakness in Britain and Australia and a sluggish advertising market in the United States have made it tough to keep up with debt payments.
 
 Meetings With Bankers
 Mr. Murdoch has met over the last few weeks with groups of bankers in Australia, Europe and the United States to outline a proposal to consolidate some $6.5 billion in bank loans otherwise due by mid-1993.
An executive close to Mr. Murdoch, who insisted on anonymity, said yesterday that Mr. Murdoch had made the proposal because principal payments were coming due that the company would have trouble paying.
Under terms of the proposed package, only $1.4 billion in bank loans would have to be repaid by December 1993, the executive said. The company would then hope to renegotiate the remainder by early 1994, he said.
In exchange for permitting the debt deferral, the bankers would get higher rates of interest and other fees. The company has also told the bankers it plans to sell about $1 billion in assets over the next two years.
 
 $600 Million Bridge Loan
 As part of the refinancing proposal, the News Corporation would get a $600 million bridge loan that would have to be repaid in mid-1992. The bridge loan would give the company the ability to make interest payments, meet business expenses and allow extra time to arrange asset sales. "We hope to have it finalized by the end of December or early January," the executive said.
Under its existing loan agreement, the News Corporation would have had to pay back $2.4 billion in both bank loans and public debt by next June, another $1.4 billion by June 1992 and another $3 billion by June 1993.
The new agreement would require no repayments by next June, $1.2 billion including the bridge loan by June 1992 and another $800 million by June 1993, with the chance for delaying the final payment until December 1993.

HEADLINE: Clash of Free Trade and National Interest

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Free trade is wonderful, most nations agree -- except for those instances where it impinges on some powerful national interest.
The Japanese bar rice imports, for instance, to protect the politically influential rice farmers. The United States politely insisted that the Japanese voluntarily limit the number of cars they ship to the American market. The Europeans erect walls against Japanese cars, American semiconductors and American pork and beef. 
 Now, as ministers from more than 100 governments gather in Brussels next week for trade talks, such exceptions are seen to be strangling the rule of free trade.
And in recent years, some of the 105 nations that belong to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, the world body that oversees trade, have increasingly been using safeguards, escape clauses and anti-dumping measures in GATT rules to create barriers to free trade.
Negotiators at the talks next week -- known as the Uruguay Round because the talks began in Uruguay four years ago -- will try to write rules that set clear, objective procedures to prevent countries from building such barriers.
The backbone of GATT, which was drafted in 1947, is unconditional most-favored-nation treatment. That means that any reduction of tariffs or other trade concession made by one government to another is automatically extended on a nondiscriminatory basis to all governments belonging to GATT.
In earlier rounds of negotiations during the post World War II era, GATT nations have managed to reduce tariffs by 75 percent, greatly increasing world trade. But the surge of imports has brought joblessness in some areas and other domestic problems. The protectionist exceptions to the rules were intended to provide nations with a means to address such problems -- in the short term.
One such step that has become widely used is anti-dumping measures -- duties that a government imposes when it finds that an imported product is sold for less abroad than in the nation where it is produced. This is regarded as unfair pricing, a way of building market share in foreign markets.
The biggest cases in the United States have involved steel from Europe and textiles from the Far East. In Europe, dumping charges have been levied chiefly against Japanese consumer electronics.
 
 'Protectionist Measure of Choice'
 The U.S. Council for International Business, the American affiliate of the International Chamber of Commerce, has found that in the last decade, the United States, the European Community, Canada and Australia have brought 800 dumping cases, more than half of which have led to the imposition of penalty duties.
"There is no question in my view," said Robert J. Morris, the council's senior vice president, "that anti-dumping actions have become the protectionist measure of choice."
As part of the process of forging a single European market by 1992, the European Community wants to apply restraints selectively against imports of Japanese cars and other consumer products.
But the United States and many third-world nations argue that such selective restraints open the door for collusion.
On the issue of dumping, however, the United States and the European Community are on the same side. Under pressure from domestic industries, they have submitted proposals that would make it easier to win dumping penalties.
Japan and other Asian exporters and most third-world countries, oppose such steps.
 
 Used Against West
 But some Asian nations, including South Korea, are pressing their own anti-dumping actions against Western companies.
Earlier this year, E. I. du Pont de Nemours & Company and the Hoechst Celanese Corporation complained to the International Trade Commission, a Federal agency that investigates trade petitions, that South Korean chemical companies were dumping polyethylene terephthalate, a resin used to manufacture soda bottles, at prices below "fair value" in the United States market.
Now the South Korean Government has begun an investigation of Du Pont and Hoechst Celanese to determine whether they are selling below "fair value" the chemical substance polyacetal, used to make plastic goods, in the South Korean market. The South Koreans are also investigating a Japanese company, Asahi Chemicals, on the same grounds.

HEADLINE: U.S. Backs World Bank Environment Unit

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHABECOFF, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Industrial and developing countries agreed in Paris this week to establish a new fund within the World Bank devoted to protecting the global environment.
The United States, which had resisted the formation of such a fund, has now conditionally agreed, Bush Administration and bank officials said today. 
 Under the agreement, a three-year pilot program would provide $1 billion to $1.5 billion for projects like preserving tropical forests and biological diversity, protecting the marine environment and promoting the efficient use of energy to slow global warming.
Supporters of the fund said it would help build environmental concerns into the development process in the poorer countries of Africa, Latin America and Asia. Development in many if not most of these countries now emphasizes rapid industrialization, economic growth and export expansion without regard to the effect on ecological systems.
The United Nations Environmental Program and the United Nations Development Program will help oversee the new fund, a World Bank statement said today.
The United States, which had hedged on supporting the establishment of such a "green window" inside the World Bank, agreed in Paris to back it, said Leandro Coronel, a bank spokesman.
 
Bank's Record Criticized
 An Administration official who insisted on anonymity said the United States' support of the fund was a conditional compromise.
One condition, he said, was that any contributions to environmental projects beyond what the United States is now contributing to the World Bank would be made through the United States Agency for International Development rather than through the bank.
Another condition was that loans to developing countries for environmental purposes could be forgiven only if the benefits of the loan were global in scope as opposed to benefiting only the receiving nation.
Environmental groups in this country, however, strongly oppose entrusting the fund to the World Bank. They contend that the bank, despite protestations of concern for the environment, continues to back ecologically destructive projects like broad-scale lumbering operations.
Larry Williams, director of the Sierra Club's international program, said, "Environmentalists all agree that a green fund is desperately needed to help the developing countries deal with their environmental problems. We are, however, extremely concerned about the World Bank's ability to manage such a fund, given its dismal environmental record."
"We would much prefer a fund be established and operated either by another existing international agency or possibly by some new entitity," Mr. Williams said. He added that the environmental groups may soon come up with their own proposals for establishing a fund for dealing with global environmental problems.
Glenn T. Prickett, a research associate of the Natural Resources Defense Council, asserted that the World Bank "has not shown a real interest in working with environmental groups and the public in the developing countries to see that its projects in those countries are environmentally sound."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
Black-Owned Media Fear Being Cut First

 BODY:
ADVERTISERS feeling the chill wind of a recession are quick to rethink where they are placing their ads, and black-owned media are worried that they will be the first to be frozen out.
Executives of black radio stations, magazines and advertising agencies say their audience is often forgotten or dismissed during a recession, largely because it is perceived as jobless, low spending and the first to clamp pocketbooks shut in economic hard times. 
 Advertisers are snipping away at their budgets while nervously harping at their media buyers to spend wisely, because they badly want to eke out the largest amount of big spenders from the least amount of placement in publications, radio and television stations.
"When the ad business suffers from a cold, black media are susceptible to pneumonia," said Bill Froelich, general sales manager for WBLS-FM, a black-owned radio station in New York. "Certain advertisers have told their agencies, 'Let's not use black radio,' and they are leaving us," he said, attributing the decision to misperceptions of black consumers. "Blacks are perceived as not having money to spend, without educational status and not in jobs, which is really not the case."
Mr. Froelich said that a recent Scarborough report that profiled WBLS listeners found that their income was on a par with other New Yorkers. He cited that as proof of value of the black marketplace.
Other black media experts agreed that large advertisers and their ad agencies seemed too quick to shift spending away from black-oriented media.
"This strategy is often used by advertisers," said Ken Smikle, president of the African American Marketing and Media Association. "People wrongly assume that a recession will hurt low-income people and that therefore all black consumers will be spending less," he said. "But blacks are not monolithic."
Mr. Smikle said that black consumers spend $263 billion a year.
Joseph Ostrow, executive vice president and worldwide media director at Foote, Cone & Belding Communications Inc. in New York, acknowledged that black media might suffer during general cutbacks in ad spending, but he maintained that in the long run they were disproportionately hurt.
"Clearly, in difficult times everyone is fair game," he said, "but what tends to happen is that you focus on target groups. I think if blacks are secondary targets, they tend to suffer."
Samuel Chisholm, president and chief executive of the Mingo Group, a black-owned advertising agency, contended that hard economic times were actually best for tapping into the black market.
"I hypothesize that the black consumer market is a bit more recession-resisitant," he said. "Because of our willingness to downgrade in the job market and the fact that we do get Government assistance, our purchases are primarily staples."
Advertisers often hope to grab black consumers through the general media, but many in advertising and publishing view that as ineffective.
"'Reaching is not selling," said Jeff Burns, vice president of the Johnson Publishing Company, which publishes Ebony magazine. "By and large, media buyers do not sit down and think of black media, and many refuse to address the fact the black consumer has the wherewithal to purchase their products. But black media is a direct invitation from the marketer to the consumer to make the purchase."
"He's absolutely right," Mr. Ostrow said. "It's really a question of mindset, and there is no question that the closer you get to where a person is coming from, the more likely" the ad will reach the intended consumer.
Mr. Froelich emphasized that black media groups could help their own cause by working to break down ignorant perceptions of black consumers. "When you speak with an agency person, what you often hear is, 'Black's don't . . .' as in: 'Black's don't drive our cars. Blacks don't eat our food.' But advertisers that we have been able to sit down with and talk to have been able to see that we have something of value."
He cited the General Foods Corporation, the Toyota Motor Corporation, and several banks as advertisers that have increased their spending. He added, "It's a long, hard process, but we're getting there."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Rises Against Yen And Mark

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
The dollar surged yesterday, jumping more than 2 percent against the Japanese yen and more than 1 percent against the German mark.
The rally, which pushed the dollar over the 1.50 level against the mark and 133 against the yen, is the first significant break upward for the dollar in months. It comes after a long decline that has pushed it to repeated lows against the mark and the Swiss franc. 
 Much of the jump yesterday, traders said, was a response to the tensions in the Middle East, which were fueled by rumors of shooting, later denied, and to reports that troops had been put on special alert status in preparation for a possible Iraqi strike. Late in the day, the United Nations Security Council, in a 12-to-2 vote, authorized the use of force against Iraq.
 
 Big Drop for Yen
 The yen, which fell the most, is also hurt from concern about the Japanese economy and from the fear of a string of business failures after the Kyowa Company announced its bankruptcy filing this week. The yen has also fallen sharply against European currencies, including the mark, reflecting a negative feeling about the currency that is temporarily overshadowing the bearish sentiment for the dollar. A prediction of an earthquake in Tokyo this weekend also unsettled the yen.
But some traders said they saw the start of a little rally for the dollar that could be supported by gulf tensions and the ususal dollar buying at the end of the year that comes with the closing of corporate books. They contended that the market had tried, but failed recently, to push the dollar even lower, and so was now considering the potential for an upward move.
"I thought the move was going to take place over a two-or-three-week period," said Robert A. White, a vice president at First Interstate Bank in Los Angeles. "But it all happened in one day."
He said the sharp jump reflected the thinness of the market; also, many people, still betting on a decline in the dollar, were caught short and had to scramble to buy to protect themselves from large losses.
Many investors and traders use stop-loss orders to avoid or limit their losses, and the American currency moved through several of these levels.
"Traders here are reacting less to negative news," added Andrew Smith, the executive vice chairman of London & Bishopsgate International Inc. in New York. "The market was looking for reasons to take the dollar up." He thinks many traders are misreading the signs pointing to a lower dollar.
Victor M. Polce, a vice president at Algemene Bank Nederland in New York, argued against yesterday's jump in the dollar being the sign of a new rally. "The fundamentals still point to the dollar going down," he said, adding that the bank advised its clients not to buy dollars in this atmosphere, despite the dollar's rise.
The United States economy is in decline, Mr. Polce said, and interest rates here are still expected to decline, while they are expected to stay level or rise abroad, making the dollar a less attractive investment.
In late trading, the dollar was at 133.20 yen, up from 130.10 on Wednesday and up from 127.15 the day before Thanksgiving. Against the mark, the American currency was at 1.5043, up from 1.4863 on Wednesday and from 1.4760 last Wednesday.
The pound, which closed at $1.9430, down from $1.9685, also dropped sharply against the dollar yesterday, partly because of comments from the new British Prime Minister, John Major, who said he opposed the imposition of a single currency for Europe.
The comment could be interpreted to mean that Mr. Major would not be as willing as thought to ease away from the adamant opposition of the former Prime Minister, Margaret Thatcher, to such a move.
