HEADLINE: GORBACHEV TAKES BLAME FOR CRISIS

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Nov. 29

 BODY:
President Mikhail S. Gorbachev pronounced himself and other Communists "guilty before the working class" today in a discussion of responsibility for the country's economic crisis and political paralysis.
Speaking to nearly 1,000 delegates to the 28th Moscow City Communist Party Conference, he said the Soviet Union faced increasing difficulties with food shortages, ethnic conflicts, crime and "a battle of laws that has led to a paralysis of power." 
 "The reasons," the Soviet leader said, are "errors in the actions of central organs, above all in the Central Committee of the Communist Party, and including the actions of the General Secretary and President."
Mr. Gorbachev is both President and General Secretary of the 18-million-member party.
He promised in his hourlong speech to take immediate steps to increase food supplies. He also reiterated his determination to hold the restive republics together.
 
Takes 'Personal Responsibility'

 Mr. Gorbachev said the party leadership's defeat in recent elections around the country could be attributed to its failure to address workers' concerns.
"We are guilty before the working class, I think, all of us, and I personally take responsibility," he said.
The speech appeared intended to disarm his critics, led by Boris N. Yeltsin, President of the Russian republic, by accepting a measure of blame while vigorously asserting that the party is on the road to renewal and can solve the country's problems.
A poll published today in Izvestia indicated the number of people who "fully trust" the party fell from 27 percent in December 1989 to 14 percent in July. Izvestia gave no details on the polling.
Mr. Gorbachev and Mr. Yeltsin differ the best methods for achieving a market economy, and the Russian Parliament has said it has the right to pass laws superseding national ones.
Nearly all of the 15 Soviet republics have taken similar steps toward autonomy. Several have stopped shipping food outside their borders, aggravating the shortages in major cities.
 
GRAIN CURB MAY BE EASED
WASHINGTON, Nov. 29 (Special to The New York Times) -- Secretary of Agriculture Clayton Yeutter said today that President Bush may ease restrictions on granting the Soviet Union credits to buy American grain this year.
"The President did indicate to me that he is prepared to have the matter fully reconsidered and I am encouraged at his willingness to do so," Mr. Yeutter told reporters after a speech to the National American Wholesale Grocers Association.
Soviet officials indicated this week that they could not fulfill their commitment under the current U.S.-U.S.S.R. Long Term Grain Agreement to buy 1.2 million tons of wheat and 600,000 tons of corn in the final quarter of 1990 without the credits.
In the past, Mr. Bush has insisted that Moscow first liberalize its emigration laws before granting agricultural credits. But Mr. Yeutter seemed to suggest the President could ask Congress to waive the Jackson-Vanik amendment to the 1974 trade law that bars normal tariff treatment to countries restricting people from leaving their countries. The amendment was passed to encourage the Soviet Union to allow Soviet Jews to emigrate.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Protecting Banks In a Severe Slump

BYLINE: By Leonard Silk

 BODY:
IN his meeting with bankers in Washington yesterday, President Bush sought to reinforce the message they have been getting from Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady and the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan: that the Government is well aware of its responsibility not only to protect the safety and soundness of commercial banks but also to insure their economic viability.
Such a commitment now would require an overhaul of the banking regulations adopted during the Depression, when the aim was to restrict commercial banks from engaging in investment banking or other functions, which they were thought to have abused. Although Mr. Bush and Secretary Brady have not yet been specific about details, the Administration is moving to support legislation next year that would enable American banks to become competitive in the functions they can perform and where they can operate. 
 However welcome the President's message may be to large money-center banks and some regional banks, if not to thousands of smaller banks around the country, he did not directly address the most immediate and very real worries of the bankers about the state of their balance sheets and the adequacy of their capital to protect them against insolvency, now that the economy has slid into what Mr. Greenspan called a "meaningful downturn."
That sounds like a euphemism for recession, but Mr. Greenspan, who said the downturn did not come until "we moved through October and into November," wants to wait a while longer to see if the slide continues before using the R-word. Michael J. Boskin, President Bush's chief economic adviser, has made recent statements in close agreement with Mr. Greenspan's appraisal, also refusing to call it a recession yet.
But euphemisms often quickly acquire the powerto alarm as much as the terms they seek to avoid. Some bankers and bankruptcy lawyers, frightened by the "meaningful downturn," are already using the D-word -- depression -- to describe the nation's economic prospects. It is worth noting that "recession" was coined in 1936 as a euphemism for "depression."
Euphemisms aside, everyone knows that a severe slump would impose heavy pressures on the banks and that a widespread banking crisis would seriously worsen the downturn. Hence, the real question cannot be avoided: How worrisome is the condition of the banking system? The markets, hailed for their efficiency by conservative politicians and economists alike, have spoken eloquently on the subject by marking down the values of bank securities. Both official regulators and private bank analysts have insisted that many of the largest American banks must strengthen their capital base or face excessive risks.
Thomas R. Hanley, managing director and chief bank analyst of Salomon Brothers Inc., finds that the sharp drop in bank stock prices since late August suggests "increasing investor caution about bank credit quality." He has used "harsher" and what he considers more realistic reserve requirements to judge their capital adequacy, including 10 percent reserves for highly leveraged transactions and 20 percent for commercial real estate. When he did so, he found that at 18 leading banks, reserve needs soared to $35.3 billion from $12.9 billion. Those 18 would need $14.3 billion of additional equity to meet a projected 1992 year-end capital ratio of 4 percent.
To raise $14.3 billion, the banks would have to shrink assets by $356 billion, cut dividends in half by $5.9 billion, or reduce their expenses by 10 percent a year to raise $35.5 billion over the next two and a half years -- or use some combination of these. Mr. Hanley said that 11 of the 18 biggest banks could reach their capital requirements by some combination of the three methods, but that the others could not and should seek mergers.
"Under regulatory encouragement," he said, "we believe that managements' attention will turn to mergers as the obvious alternative toward increasing" their efficiency of capital use.
Mergers may prove to be the real core of the Administration's effort to overhaul the American banking system. One side effect of consolidation, Mr. Hanley suggested, "should be to drive excess capacity out of the system, with eventual benefits for pricing flexibility." Some of the would-be merger candidates or providers of capital to hard-pressed American banks could be other banks or nonfinancial corporations, but that would also require regulatory change.
To gain time for regulatory overhaul and mergers to save the most threatened institutions, a steep decline in the economy must be prevented. This puts the primary burden on the Fed to supply sufficient reserves to keep the economy stable and shore up the banking system.
The Fed, with Administration and Congressional backing, stands ready to rescue leading banks threatened with sudden insolvency. That it can and will protect the financial system is the main reason for believing that the present unhappy condition -- the "meaningful downturn" -- will not lead to another Depression.

HEADLINE: San Francisco Journal;
When Skies Are Blue, So Are the Forecasters

BYLINE: By JANE GROSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Nov. 29

 BODY:
After four years of drought, with the state's reservoirs as dry as mud flats, weather forecasters here are swamped with angry calls and letters every time they say it's a beautiful day because the sun is bright and the sky is blue.
"Folks are so sensitive that you don't even dare say a day like today is 'nice,' " said Bob Haulman, the morning weatherman for KPIX-TV, the CBS affiliate in the San Francisco Bay Area. "So what do you call a day that's sunny and 60 degrees? Do you call it a terrible day? A sort-of-O.K. day? It puts limits on you." 
 Conversely, it is no longer politically correct to talk about the "threat of rain," according to Joel Bartlett, a meteorologist at the ABC station, KGO-TV. "It's the 'hope' of rain, the 'promise' of rain," Mr. Bartlett said. "You change your definitions."
The so-called rainy season has begun here, a time of wishful thinking in a state that has not seen normal precipitation since 1986, is already rationing water and is bracing for a cataclysm of Dust Bowl proportions.
While Southern California is drier, the obsession with winter storms is fiercer here in the north, where the snowpack in the Sierra Nevada feeds the rivers and reservoirs from the Oregon border to Mexico.
"The Sierra is our Federal Reserve Bank of water," Mr. Haulman said. "We've been through a recession and we're headed for a depression."
Day after day, from November to March, forecasters in the Bay Area scan satellite pictures from the National Weather Service, looking for signs that a transoceanic storm is about to slip through the weakening Pacific high pressure system and belt the North Coast.
Instead, more often than not in recent years, the approaching storms disintegrate before they reach land or get pulled in a northeasterly direction by the jet stream, drenching Oregon and Washington but leaving Northern California with no more than a dusting or a drizzle.
Then the forecasters face a different kind of wrath from friends and neighbors who demand to know why it did not rain or snow. "They think we're responsible for the weather," Mr. Bartlett said. "I tell them we're in sales, not production."
The forecasters admit they fight the impulse to tell viewers what they want to hear. "All the facts don't point in the same direction," Mr. Bartlett said, "so you have to overcome the tendency to forecast what you'd like to see happen."
Even without the wishful thinking, weather forecasting is more daunting from the West Coast than the East. Storms arrive here after crossing a vast ocean, largely untracked by the monitoring stations that follow a weather system once it reaches land.
"If a given storm is a book, we only see the title page," said Brian Hackney, another member of the weather team at KGO-TV. "We begin to write the book that the East Coast reads."
Then there is the matter of the nation's cranky geostationary weather satellites. There are supposed to be two of them, 22,300 miles above the United States. But one is broken, so the other is positioned closer to the Atlantic in the summer to track hurricanes, and nearer to the Pacific in the winter to track storms.
When there were two functioning satellites, the one relied on by California meteoroligists was positioned at 120 degrees west, near Reno. The single satellite, at this time of year, is at 90 degrees, over Indianapolis.
"We're just not seeing as much of the Pacific now," said Orman Granger, a professor of climatology at the University of California at Berkeley.
As a result, more often than not the California forecasts are plain wrong, as they have been in recent weeks.
First, in mid-November there were predictions of a storm so promising that KPIX rented a satellite truck and sent Mr. Haulman to the mountains. For two days, shivering in the cold, he broacast live from the side of Interstate 80 near Donner Pass. Only two disappointing inches of snow fell.
Again last Sunday a storm was forecast, less plausible than the last, or so KPIX thought, and so Mr. Haulman stayed home. About 10 inches of snow fell in the Sierra, with rainfall ranging from three-quarters of an inch in Kentfield in Marin County to a fifth of an inch in downtown Oakland.
Rival stations mocked KPIX for rushing to the summit at the hint of snow, but Mr. Haulman shrugged off the criticism. "This is not a puff story," he said. "This is life or death. Another year of drought and it's unthinkable what will happen."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Off in a Hectic Session

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Rumors of shooting in the Middle East, later denied, pushed down the prices of Treasury securities during a hectic session yesterday, one in which dealers had to cope with disparate pieces of information.
A report showing that personal incomes grew barely at all and that consumption was flat in October helped to push prices up early in the day. So did a speech by Richard F. Syron, the president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston, who said that the economy had fallen into recession, as well as a sharp rise in the value of the dollar. 
 "The economic data were very weak," said Robert Chandross, chief economist at Lloyds Bank. "After adjusting for inflation, consumption was lower in October than it was during any month since May. There is no strength there at all."
 
 Syron's 'Honest' Remarks
 Analysts said Mr. Syron's remarks were markedly different from those offered by other Federal Reserve officials in recent days. On Wednesday, Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, told Congress that economic output was likely to decline in the fourth quarter, but he stopped short of saying the economy had moved into recession. Earlier in the week, a Fed governor, John P. LaWare, cast doubt that the economy was all that weak.
Mr. Syron's comments were "honest," said Nancy Kimelman, chief economist at Technical Data, a Boston bond analysis and advisory firm. "He said that given the debt overhang, a potentially big recession is out there," she said. "But the market knows we are in a recession. As a consequence, I don't know how much improvement in interest rates we are going to get on that story."
Rumors of war began early in the afternoon, and prices began to slide. Subsequent denials that fighting had broken out failed to stem the decline.
 
Waiting for Vote at U.N.
 Trading ground to a close before the United Nations Security Council approved a resolution authorizing the use of force in the Persian Gulf if Iraq does not leave Kuwait by Jan. 15. But market participants said traders had expected passage.
Nervousness about the gulf situation pushed down prices in the secondary market for Treasury securities by as much as half a point.
In late trading yesterday, the 8 3/4 percent 30-year bonds of 2020 were offered at a price of 102 27/32, down by half a point, to yield 8.48 percent. Late on Wednesday, the benchmark issue was offered at a price to yield 8.43 percent.
Among Treasury note issues, the 8 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 101 5/32, down by 10/32, to yield 8.32 percent. The two new Treasury issues auctioned earlier in the week also fell in price.
By late in the day, the 7 7/8 percent five-year notes sold on Wednesday were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 7.96 percent. And the 7 3/8 percent two-year notes were offered at a price to yield 7.58 percent.
Short-term Treasury bill rates were narrowly mixed.
Three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.02 percent, down by 3 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point. But six-month bill rates rose by a basis point, to a late offered rate of 7.01 percent.
 
 Interbank Market Calms
 Meanwhile, traders said conditions in the interbank market for short-term loans calmed considerably yesterday from the chaos of recent days.
"On Wednesday, banks were paying almost any price to lock up funds," said James R. Capra, a senior vice president at Shearson Lehman Government Securities. "But now, they have adopted a much more cautious and careful stance."
Three-month Eurodollar deposit rates were largely unchanged at an offered rate of 8 1/2 percent.
In a delayed reaction to activity in the Eurodollar market, rates on certificates of deposit that mature in early January shot up by 40 basis points yesterday, to 9 percent.
In large measure, the scramble for money in the interbank market represents an effort by large banks to borrow money to dress up corporate balance sheets at the end of the year.
 
 Heavy Debt Issues
 Issuance of new debt in the investment-grade corporate bond market was heavy again yesterday.
Highlighting the issues priced was a $200 million offering of 35-year debentures by the Consolidated Edison Company of New York, the first taxable issue offered by the giant utility since 1974.
The 9.70 percent debentures, which mature on Dec. 1, 2025, were priced at $99.75, to yield 9.725 percent, or 1.25 percentage points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 8 3/4 percent bonds of 2020. The securities are noncallable for five years.
Merrill Lynch & Company acted as lead manager for the transaction. The securities are rated AA-2 by Moody's Investors Service Inc. and AA-minus by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
 
 Bonds for New York Agency
 In the tax-exempt municipal bond market, Dillon, Read & Company acted as lead manager for a negotiated sale of $175 million in securities for New York's Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority.
The issue consists of serial bonds with maturities from 1992 to 2007, and two term issues that mature in 2011 and 2021.
Yields on the serial bonds range from 5.60 percent in 1992 to 7.05 percent in 2007.
The 7 percent term bond maturing in 2011 was priced to yield 7.17 percent. The 7 percent term bond maturing in 2021 was priced to yield 7.235 percent.
The securities are rated AA by Moody's and A+ by Standard & Poor's.
 
 Corporate Bonds Down
 Participants in the secondary market for investment-grade corporate bonds said prices of most issues slipped by about three-eighths of a point.
Widely quoted issues in the secondary market for tax-exempt muncipal bonds fell by about a quarter-point, traders said.
In other developments, the Federal Reserve Board reported yesterday that two of the three measures of the nation's money supply rose during the week that ended on Nov. 19. The third, broadest measure, M-3, fell by $700 million.
The money report had no effect on prices in the credit markets.

HEADLINE: G.M. CUTS OUTPUT IN THE LATEST SIGN OF ECONOMIC SLUMP

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Nov. 29

 BODY:
The General Motors Corporation, the nation's largest industrial company, said today that it was sharply cutting back its production for the rest of the year because of the weakening American economy.
The cutbacks reflect the company's pessimistic outlook for American car and truck sales, which General Motors said had been made worse by the economic uncertainty since the Persian Gulf crisis began and dwindling consumer confidence. 
 Robert C. Stempel, the General Motors chairman, said that the weaker sales would mean the company's financial performance would "decline significantly" in the fourth quarter of 1990. Auto analysts say the company would almost surely lose money during the last three months of 1990.
 
Anticipating a Recession
 "Auto makers are paring back production in anticipation of a recession-type environment," said David Greenlaw, an economist at Morgan, Stanley & Company.
The announcement by General Motors came as the Commerce Department reported today that consumer spending, which fueled the country's eight-year economic expansion, showed no gain in October. [Page D1.] And in a speech today, President Bush said the gulf crisis was having a "very bad effect on our economy," although he said he thought it would not be "long-lasting." [Page D2.]
 
A Reduction of 9.3%
 General Motors said that it would build 111,000 fewer cars and trucks in North America in the fourth quarter of 1990 than the 1,186,000 it had previously planned, a decrease of 9.3 percent. Moreover, the previous production target had been shaved by 181,000 cars and trucks earlier in the year.
In the third quarter of the year, G.M. built 1,192,751 cars and trucks in North America; during the fourth quarter of 1989, G.M. built 1,289,252 vehicles.
The company's step suggested that it viewed a recent pickup in 10-day auto sales as an aberration, with the uptick mainly reflecting unusually weak sales in the period a year earlier. And G.M., like all the Detroit auto makers, is fighting to hold its share of the American market against continuing gains by Japanese rivals like the Toyota Motor Corporation, which announced this week that it would double its car-making capacity in Kentucky.
Stuart G. Hoffman, chief economist for the Pittsburgh National Bank, termed the General Motors announcement "a pre-emptive approach," attempting to bring production into line with falling demand for cars before costly inventories of unsold vehicles pile up. In the past, Detroit auto makers often moved slowly to cut back production, acting well after the economy began to contract and aggravating their troubles when excess inventories piled up.
 
New Production Philosophy
 Since his promotion to president earlier this year, Mr. Stempel has been an advocate of a new production philosophy at G.M. of keeping inventories lean. In the past, the company had a tendency to build more vehicles than it could sell. Consequently, G.M. was often forced to offer enormous rebates to get rid of its unsold cars.
"We are determined to keep our inventories in line with demand," Mr. Stempel said.
A G.M. spokesman said the company was also reacting to its own forecast for reduced demand for cars and trucks by retail customers.
In a speech to be delivered tonight to the Fort Wayne, Ind., Chamber of Commerce, Mr. Stempel said: "Many people have been taking a 'wait and see' attitude toward major purchase decisions. For the auto industry, this has translated into weaker automotive demand."
While Mr. Stempel did not explicitly forecast a fourth-quarter financial loss for his company, he said that "the abnormally low level of North American quarterly production, an increased mix of less profitable vehicles, high marketing costs and labor and other economic cost increases not fully recovered in price are adversely impacting our fourth-quarter North American earnings and cash flow."
 
A Significant Decline
 "As a result," he continued, "G.M. expects its 1990 overall fourth-quarter financial results will decline significantly relative to our recently released third-quarter results, excluding the special charge," he said. Auto makers record revenue and profit when they sell vehicles to dealers.
Before a $2.1 billion special charge in the third quarter covering some permanent plant closings, G.M. earned $109 million, or 2 cents a share, in the period, which was extremely weak for a company whose quarterly revenue topped $30 billion.
Ronald A. Glantz, an automotive analyst for Dean Witter Securities in San Francisco, said, "No question, G.M. will be in the red in the fourth quarter." Mr. Glantz said that G.M. would post a loss of between $180 million to $240 million.
"There's no question this action makes sense," Mr. Glantz said. "If G.M. overbuilds now, it's going to have to give them away later."
 
Early Closings Possible
 G.M. declined to say which of its more than 100 plants in North America would be affected by the production cut. Since only three more weeks remain in the fourth quarter before the company shuts down for the Christmas holiday, it is likely that some assembly plants will close early. G.M. may also reduce the work shifts in some plants from two to one, cut overtime hours or reduce the speed of production.
Most auto workers are protected against layoffs by income-security provisions of G.M.'s new three-year labor agreement with the United Automobile Workers union, signed in September.

HEADLINE: 3 Fiscal Monitors Press New York City on Budget

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
New York City's three official fiscal monitors said in separate reports yesterday that the city's current budget remained, at best, precariously balanced and that the Dinkins administration must take even more aggressive steps to confront much deeper troubles next year.
The state monitors -- the Financial Control Board, the Municipal Assistance Corporation and the State Comptroller's office -- while differing on details, said the city had only begun to make the hard choices about what programs to cut or consolidate, or how to improve productivity to keep the budget balanced. 
 The monitors agreed that expected cuts in state aid and any further weakening in the economy could throw the current budget out of balance, despite Mayor David N. Dinkins's recent cuts to close a gap that had been estimated at $388 million. State Comptroller Edward V. Regan said he believed the city still faced a gap of about $300 million in the fiscal year that ends on June 30.
Amplifying on their past warnings, the monitors said the problems would be even worse next year, when the city faces a projected overall shortfall between revenues and expenditures of at least $1.6 billion. The actions it has taken so far will close only about $300 million worth of that gap, the monitors said.
 
'The Real Problem'
 "The real problem the city has is they need $1.3 billion worth of ideas," said Allen J. Proctor, executive director of the Financial Control Board.
He said a number of the city's expectations for next year, including assumed increases in state and Federal aid and the state's taking over $165 million in local Medicaid costs, were "nonstarters" that had not been updated since July and should be abandoned.
Quentin B. Spector, executive director of the Municipal Assistance Corporation, said the question was "whether the city is acting fast enough and forcefully enough now to avoid even bigger problems later and whether the possibility of a realistic plan for next year is slipping away."
Mr. Spector's report suggested that even if $40 million in contingency cuts that have been outlined for this year's budget were not needed now, they probably would be next year, and so should be made now to save money faster. The contingencies include layoffs of teachers, closing six fire companies, cutting garbage collection and reducing parks maintenance.
"Aside from reinforcing the seriousness of the city's dilemma, making these cuts now would generate as much as $20 million of savings that would not be available if the measures were effected" next year, Mr. Spector said.
 
A Comprehensive Plan
 The city's Budget Director, Philip R. Michael, said: "We have a comprehensive plan coming in from the agencies next week. If we have to expedite some of these actions, we'll do it." But he said the city was still waiting to judge the precise effects of cuts in state aid and other uncertainties.
City agencies are to submit by Monday their recommendations for closing next year's budget gap. Mr. Michael has ordered most agencies to outline how they would trim up to 17.5 percent of their budgets, with 10 percent in cuts and 7.5 percent in contingency cuts to be made if conditions worsen. Officials have said thousands of layoffs might be needed, and Mr. Michael has told agencies to consider shutting down nonessential programs.
Asked about the monitors' concerns, First Deputy Mayor Norman Steisel said the administration was aware of the scope of the problems and was pursuing solutions for inclusion in its preliminary budget for the next fiscal year, to be issued in January.
"It ain't over yet," he said.
 
'More Dialogue' With State
 Mr. Proctor of the control board said the city should step up its efforts to persuade the state to relieve it of mandates that, in some instances, require certain numbers of workers to perform certain functions, and to coordinate whatever tax proposals it might make with the state, which is struggling to close huge budget gaps of its own.
"It's December now," Mr. Proctor said. "What's the strategy that's going to get you to January? Maybe it's time to have more dialogue with the state."
Mr. Proctor also suggested that the city should consider consolidating or eliminating some agencies that have duplicative functions, including a number of economic development agencies, or that might not be affordable in a time of retrenchment, including various planning, study and analytical offices or functions required under the new City Charter.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Quayle Says Delaying War Would Increase Risks

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Vice President Dan Quayle sought today to counter Democratic lawmakers who have urged a delay in any military action against Iraq, saying that putting off war would enable Iraqi forces to tighten their grip on Kuwait and might eventually lead to greater American military casualties.
"The longer we refrain from action against Iraq, the more time Saddam Hussein has to tighten his grip on Kuwait, and the harder it may be to break that grip, if and when war comes," Mr. Quayle told students at Seton Hall University in South Orange, N.J. "Does patience today risk greater American casualties tomorrow?" 
 In recent weeks, the Bush Administration has cited a host of reasons for forcing a resolution of the Persian Gulf crisis, including the continuing destruction in Kuwait, the holding of hostages, threats to the economy of the United States and the world and Iraq's drive to develop nuclear weapons.
Mr. Quayle's suggestion that delaying military action might increase American casualties was a new step in the Administration's effort to win public suport for its policy.
In Congressional testimony in recent days, former Defense Secretary James Schlesinger and two former Chairmen of the Joint Chiefs of Staff argued that giving sanctions a year or more to work would gradually diminish Iraq's military powers.
"I believe our relative military position improves every day," Adm. William J. Crowe, the top military official during the Reagan Administration and the first year of the Bush Administration, told the Senate Armed Services Committee on Wednesday.
Mr. Quayle's speech -- in which he cited concern over the plight of people in Kuwait, the need to minimize American casualties and concern over Iraqi nuclear weapons program as factors that limited American patience -- came as Administration officials said President Bush planned to meet this weekend at Camp David with the Joint Chiefs of Staff on the gulf situation. Officials also said today that the alert level of American and British forces in the gulf had been increased as a precaution against potential Iraqi retaliation for today's United Nations Security Council resolution. At this point, however, it appears that Baghdad seems to have settled on a strategy of outwaiting the Americans.
Mr. Quayle spoke as the Senate Armed Services Committee continued hearings on the gulf, receiving conflicting recommendations from two former Pentagon officials, a military expert and three specialists on Iraq.
Richard N. Perle, an Assistant Secretary of Defense in the Reagan Administration, told the panel it was imperative that the United States act to reduce Iraqi's military powers and argued that the multinational coalition against Iraq might unravel if the Administration waited a year or more to see if economic sanctions would work.
 
Questions on the Aftermath
 "And what of the American role in the aftermath of a settlement in which Saddam and his military survive to fight another day?" Mr. Perle asked. "Are we to remain in Saudi Arabia with military forces adequate to assure that he will not strike again?"
Mr. Perle argued, as did former Secretary of State Henry A. Kissinger, that any American military action should involve air strikes and not a ground assault against heavily fortified Iraqi positions.
But James Webb, Navy Secretary during the later part of the Reagan Administration, argued against early military action.
"Does anyone really believe that we can launch an attack in January, then secure, occupy and stabilize Kuwait and withdraw from Saudi Arabia by March?" Mr. Webb said, referring to arguments by Mr. Kissinger and others that the United States must taken decisive action before Ramadan, the Islamic month of fasting.
"The President's mistake in sending so many troops should not be compounded by a further error in using them in a premature, unprovoked ground offensive," Mr. Webb added.
Middle East specialists later gave the panel assessments of economic sanctions.
"The progressive shutdown of Iraqi industry and deterioration in the ability to provide vital services should become evident, even to distant observers, some in the second quarter of 1991," said James A. Placke, an international-affairs consultant.
He said such a shutdown could prompt Mr. Hussein to try to negotiate a compromise with Washington but added that even then Iraq might not agree to a total withdrawl of its forces from Kuwait.

HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
Excerpts From U.S., Kuwaiti, Iraqi and Chinese Remarks on the Resolution

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from statements by officials of the United States, Kuwait, Iraq and China at a meeting of the Security Council today. Remarks by the Kuwaiti, Iraqi and Chinese representatives were transcribed as they were read by United Nations translators.
 
James A. Baker 3d: Secretary of State
I would like to begin today's discussion with a quotation that I think aptly sets the context for our discussions today. The quotation is as follows:
"There is no precedent for a people being the victim of such injustice and of being at present threatened by abandonment to an aggressor. Also, there has never before been an example of any government proceeding with the systematic extermination of a nation by barbarous means in violation of the most solemn promises made to all the nations of the earth, that there should be no resort to a war of conquest and that there should not be used against innocent human beings terrible poison and harmful gases."
Those words, I think, could well have come from the Emir of Kuwait, but they do not. They were spoken instead in 1936, not in 1990; they come from Haile Selassie, the leader of Ethiopia, a man who saw his country conquered and occupied much like Kuwait has been brutalized since Aug. 2.
Sadly, colleagues, that appeal to the League of Nations fell ultimately upon deaf ears. The League's efforts to redress aggression failed and international disorder and war ensued. History has now given us another chance. With the cold war behind us, we now have the chance to build a world which was envisioned by the founders of this organization, by the founders of the United Nations.
We have the chance to make this Security Council and this United Nations true instruments for peace and for justice across the globe. We must not let the United Nations go the way of the League of Nations. We must fulfill our common vision of a peaceful and just post-cold-war world.
But if we are to do so, we must meet the threat to international peace created by Saddam Hussein's aggression. And that is why the debate that we are about to begin will, I think, rank as one of the most important in the history of the United Nations. It will surely do much to determine the future of this body.
Our aim today must be to convince Saddam Hussein that the just and humane demands of this Council, and of the international community, cannot be ignored. If Iraq does not reverse its course peacefully, then other necessary measures, including the use of force, should be authorized. We must put the choice to Saddam Hussein, in unmistakable terms.
 
Sheik Sabah al-Ahmed al-Jaber: Foreign Minister of Kuwait
It is true that Kuwait is a tiny state in terms of its size and population. But thanks to this massive and overwhelming international support, which is being rendered by you on the basis that justice is a true shield of peace now, we find that Kuwait is indeed a power that carries significant weight.
After all these efforts and all these resolutions and that time which is measured by my people in minutes as they continue to bleed profusely, no one really can now claim that the international community has not given to Iraq full opportunity to comply with the international will or for that matter that the world community has not offered good feasible grounds for a peaceful settlement and the elimination of the prospects for war and saving the region a real holocaust the consequences of which no one perhaps can predict except God the Almighty.
Nor can anyone really claim that the international community did not pay due attention to any forthcoming signals or really meaningful Iraqi initiatives in response to the Security Council resolutions. Channels of communication with the Iraqi regime are full of good prospects for achieving a just peaceful settlement.
Indeed there are many formulae and avenues that can indeed lead to peace. But the intransigence of the Iraqi regime is the real reason that is pushing the region at this point to a conflagration that we do not want to see. . . .
Mr. President, the Iraqi regime has sought to deflect attention from its crime against world peace by invoking issues that have nothing to do with its aggression against Kuwait, like citing the danger of the foreign presence in the region. Iraq is the first to know that it is the real reason for that presence.
It cited the question of hostages in order to create a humanitarian issue without paying due attention to their dignity. Indeed, it dragged them into its adventurous schemes in a desperate attempt to create a link between its aggression against Kuwait and its occupation and the expulsion of its people and the question of Palestine which is, alas, the first victim of its aggression.
It continues to exploit it for a cheap propaganda ploy. It calls for an Arab solution which it is the first to dissent from and reject later and then utilize that following sowing the seeds of dissention into Arab ranks.
Crude exploitation of these questions exposed the tactics of the Iraqi regime to gain time and to cause divisiveness and playing on emotions and sentiments. But the Arab world, the Islamic world and the international community found out the truth, defined the road, set the goals and demonstrated firm unity and resolve. And this is represented, Mr. President, in the draft resolution now before the Council.
 
Abdul Amir al-Anbari: Iraqi U.N. Representative
Mr. President, Iraq calls for peace. It desires peace not for us alone but for the entire Middle East region. Iraq advocates such a peace, we desire such a peace on the basis of the implementation of all Security Council resolutions in a just manner and in keeping with one standard based on justice and fairness.
Iraq has embodied this approach in the initiative declared by Mr. Saddam Hussein, the President of Iraq, on the 12th of August. He proposed an integrated and comprehensive manner for dealing with all the problems of the Middle East through the Security Council and on the basis of international law so as to safeguard the legitimate rights and interests of all parties so that the peoples of the region may enjoy peace, stability, prosperity now and in the future. The American Administration has rejected this initiative before even examining it. It has imposed its refusal upon others. The problems of the Middle East are not isolated one from the other. It has common historic roots. What is tangible is of that one problem is often the cause for the eruption of other problems. It could also be the result of the vestiges of a chronic problem that remains without solution. Therefore the linkage between problems is a natural and logical method. Any separation is artificial. It disregards the organic link, the historic link and the political link between these problems.
The American refusal of this reality is prompted by suspicious motivation that has no link with the interests of the people of the Middle East. It is a premeditated policy to impose domination on the fate of a region for purely imperialist designs. It is indeed an exercise in politial hypocrisy that the American position embodies a contrary or opposite linkage with the problems of the Middle East since they call for separation at the same time they try always at procrastination to avoid the resolution of the Palestinian problem in spite of the fact that Palestinian blood is shed every day as well as the continuation unimpeded of Israeli crimes. Is this because we are Arabs? Is this because the problems of the Middle East cannot be resolved by one standard, the standard of international law and the charter, which the United States alleges to espouse?
The question which must be raised now is the following -- will the Security Council rise to the level of responsibility for imposing a just and lasting peace through an integrated approach to dealing with all the problems of the region? The response to this question constitutes your historic mission. On our part, we are continuing on our road. Peace is our goal. We work for it. We desire it. If war is imposed upon us by the United States, this will be our destiny. I wish to reaffirm to you that our people will not kneel. We will rise to the level of responsibility because we are calling for a right. We are defending this right against injustice and tyranny.
 
Qian Qichen: Foreign Minister of China
Through my visits, I feel deeply that members of the international community share common grounds on the gulf crisis on two points -- namely, they all oppose the Iraqi invasion and annexation of Kuwait, and call on Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait immediately. While at the same time, they all wish to see that the gulf crisis be settled through a peaceful means. Today, when the Foreign Ministers of member states of the Security Council are meeting here once again, I believe that such common grounds are also shared by us.
Mr. President, the United Nations, as the international organization for the maintenance of peace and security, is responsible both to international security and to history. It should act with great caution and avoid taking hasty actions on such a major question as authorizing the member states to take military actions against another member state.
The reason why China has voted in favor of the previously adopted 10 relevant resolutions is that though the sanctions measures, as stipulated in those resolutions, are severe, they fall out of the domain of the use of force.
However, in this draft resolution about to be adopted, the wording "use all necessary means" is used, which in essence permits the use of military actions. This runs counter to the consistent position of the Chinese Government -- namely, to try our utmost to seek a peaceful solution. Therefore, the Chinese delegation has difficulty voting in favor of this draft resolution.
On the other hand, the gulf crisis arises as a result of the Iraqi invasion and annexation of Kuwait, and Iraq has thus far not taken any practical steps on the key question of troop withdrawal from Kuwait. In this connection, we have noted that this draft resolution has called on Iraq to fully comply with Security Council Resolution 660, and the subsequent relevant resolutions urging Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait immediately. Since China is in favor of this position, China will not cast its veto on this draft resolution either.
Mr. President, the Chinese Government still hopes the international community should maintain and strengthen its political, diplomatic and economic pressure on Iraq. As long as there is a gleam of hope for peace, efforts should be continued toward this end.
The Chinese Government wishes to once again urge Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait immediately, and Kuwait's sovereignty, independence, territorial integrity and legitimate Government should be restored. The Chinese Government once again calls on the international community to use all possible opportunities and ways and means and continue to try its best for a peaceful solution to the gulf crisis.

GRAPHIC: Photos: China's decision not to exercise its veto power paved the way for the resolution. Foreign Minister Qian Qichen, left, consulted with China's U.N. delegate, Li Daoyu, before casting an abstention. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times); Iraq's delegate to the United Nations, Abdul Amir al-Anbari, during his address to the Security Council on the resolution authorizing force against Iraq. (Chester Higgins Jr./The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Fiscal Crisis Could Handcuff Wilson in California

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Nov. 29

 BODY:
With the state's deficit expected to rise into the billions of dollars next year, California's incoming Governor, Pete Wilson, will take office in January facing a fiscal crisis that could severely restrict his adminstration's programs.
Mr. Wilson, who must present an outline of a balanced budget on Jan. 10, three days after he takes office, did not rule out the possibility of some form of new taxes in his recent campaign for the office. 
 This week, Gov. George Deukmejian called for a special session of the Legislature in an effort to cut $1 billion to balance his final budget.
But few analysts or politicians expect significant action to be taken before January to reduce the deficit, which is expected to reach $4.3 billion in Mr. Wilson's first year in office.
 
Weakening Economy Blamed
 "Clearly, these are issues best worked out with our new Governor when he takes office," said Assembly Speaker Willie Brown, a San Francisco Democrat.
In an interview today Gray Davis, the State Controller, attributed the budget gap to a weakening economy and a series of voter-imposed limits on government programs and spending. "Mr. Wilson has to confront the possibility of a tax increase during a time of a softening economy, precisely the worst time," Mr. Davis said.
But he said he foresaw the possibility of "a multiyear budget approach which would allow expected revenues in the next fiscal year to help compensate for shortfalls this year."
Although Mr. Wilson himself joined those calling for immediate action, legislative leaders, noting the political difficulties in cutting programs, expressed little enthusiasm for dealing with the departing Governor.
Indeed, even as Mr. Deukmejian held a news conference on Wednesday to call for a special session, Mr. Brown was closeted in a meeting with Mr. Wilson.
The estimate of a $4.3 billion shortfall next year came from a finance commission under the chairmanship of the State Treasurer. The commission estimated that economic growth in 1991 would be only about 5 percent, mainly because of higher gasoline prices, low consumer confidence and tightening credit.
At the same time, the commission forecast an 11 percent increase in state expenditures, including cost-of-living increases, an expanding school population and a rise in welfare and health services, along with payment of bills deferred from this year's budget.
This shortfall, combined with the need to replenish a nearly exhausted $1.3 billion budgetary reserve, could produce the greatest potential budget deficit in the state's history.
Some analysts said even that forecast by the state commission seemed overly favorable. "Frankly, it's anyone's guess right now," Mr. Davis said. "And if shooting starts in the Persian Gulf, all bets are off."
Fred Silva, fiscal adviser to David Roberti, a Los Angeles Democrat who is President pro tem of the State Senate, said, "Realistically, the gap is probably between $5 billion and $6 billion."
The total state budget for 1990-91 is $55.6 billion.
"Revenues are not growing fast enough to supply the baseline expenditure needs of the state," Mr. Silva said. "And these aren't frivolous programs and Cadillac human service delivery programs. We close hospitals in the state these days."
 
Growing Demands Expected
 Brad Williams, director of economic and revenue forecasting for the Commission on State Finance, which issued the estimate of a $4.3 billion shortfall next year, said he expected the demands for state services to grow even faster than the population in the next few years.
Part of this growth in demand comes from the current economic downturn, which makes increasing numbers of people dependent on state welfare services. But even without this, Mr. Williams said, more immigrants are using government services, more and longer prison terms are increasing the cost of prisons, and the cost of medical services are continuing to grow.
Mr. Deukmejian plans to meet with legislative leaders on Monday to talk about his call for a special session. But the Legislature is not required to take action, or even to stay in session to consider his proposals.

HEADLINE: Trying to Hold Down the Garbage Pile

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW CASTLE, N.Y.

 BODY:
Ruth Stumacher was minding her own business, doing her weekly shopping at the Gristede's here when she was caught red-handed.
No, her fingers hadn't dipped into the bin of chocolate-covered almonds. Her offense was graver: she had piled in her cart a few packs of juice boxes, each with its own straw, and all wrapped in plastic. 
 "These containers are terribly over-packaged and aren't easily recycled," Mrs. Stumacher was warned by Roberta Wiernik, a League of Women Voters member leading an "environmental shopping" tour down the aisle. The handful of women taking the tour nodded knowingly; Mrs. Stumacher, who is 66 years old, put the juice boxes back.
As garbage-disposal problems grow across the metropolitan region and much of the country, a new campaign is under way to persuade people not to produce so much trash in the first place.
 
Changing Buying Habits
 In small ways, like the League of Women Voters tours in this northern Westchester County town, as well as through county and state efforts, a new concept is being branded on the consciousness of consumers who are in many cases only starting to get the swing of recycling: pre-cycling.
Pre-cycling means being conscious of buying habits and their effect on the amount of trash going to ever-shrinking landfill space. Ways to demonstrate that awareness range from buying in bulk to avoid extravagantly packaged products to choosing recyclable containers to shunning disposable or poorly made goods, whether a diaper or a lawn chair able to endure only one season.
In December, new regulations will take effect in New York State authorizing manufacturers to use official state emblems on labels designating a product as "recycled," "reusable," or "recyclable," if the packaging meets certain standards.
Rhode Island has recently begun a similar labeling program.
In January, Suffolk County will begin a demonstration project on waste prevention that will track the shopping patterns of 50 families over three months, using a $20,000 grant from the Environmental Protection Agency.
"The state has set up a hierarchy of waste-management options, and the first is reduction, while the second is reuse and recycling," said David F. Newton, the recycling coordinator for the Suffolk County Department of General Services. "There's a tendency to jump over No. 1 toNo. 2. We've got to back up and realize that we're never going to get anywhere unless we first reduce our garbage."
The push to generate less garbage comes as recycling is taking hold in many places. In Westchester, for instance, residents last year recycled about 50,000 tons of solid waste, double the amount recycled in 1988. But in the same 12 months, as the population held steady, the actual amount of solid wasterose 3 percent.
"We have to recognize that by increasing our recycling but simultaneously increasing our generation, we're working against ourselves," said James J. Hogan, Westchester County's director of resource management.
The same is true at the state level. Last year, New Yorkers reused or recyled more than three million tons of all kinds of waste -- trash, yard debris, commercial waste and sewage sludge -- representing an estimated 15 percent to 20 percent of the state's total waste.
"Ten years ago, we were doing almost nothing with recycling," said Norman H. Nosenchuck, director of the State Department of Environmental Conservation's division of solid waste.
But the statewide garbage pile continues to grow, and New York's three-year-old Solid Waste Management Plan aims to halve the output of waste by 1997 through reduction strategies as well as reuse and recycling.
 
'A Greater Awareness'
 "What we're really talking about is a societal change from our immediate-gratification, throwaway mentality to a greater awareness of the consequences," Mr. Nosenchuck said. "As we insist on environmentally safe disposal and close landfills and dumpsites, the economics will drive a good part of this."
Nationally, the outlook is less certain. An estimated 180 million tons of household refuse and institutional waste were generated in 1988, according to the Environmental Protection Agency. That number is expected to rise to 216 million tons in 2000. Currently, about 13 percent of the waste is being recycled, and packaging accounts for more than a third of all solid waste, a share that has grown steadily for three decades.
Solid-waste experts, environmentalists and government officials recognize that consumers are, to a large extent, at the mercy of what they find in the stores. To that end, groups and individuals in New York are trying to win legislation penalizing package-happy companies while at the same time mounting public pressure against the worst offenders.
While the imposition of a tax or fee on such products has not passed the State Legislature, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo in June signed into law regulations reducing the number of heavy metals and hazardous constituents like lead, chrome and cadmium -- which are used in ink -- in packaging sold in the state.
In addition, the new state packaging regulations, going into effect Dec. 14, should bring emblems to labels by early next year. State officials hope the regulations, intended for the consumer's benefit, will serve as a carrot of sorts for manufacturers eager for the marketing appeal of the state emblems -- and willing to change their packaging ways in exchange for it.
But as environmental officials and private organizations wait for corporations to come around, they are hard at work on the other end of the equation, urging people in tours like the one here, for example, to shop only with canvas or string bags and to have their names removed from all third-class mailing lists.
"I must get one a week from Victoria's Secret," complained Lynn Pasquale, a 31-year-old mother and homemaker from Chappaqua, her 4-year-old daughter, Stephanie, squeezed in her shopping cart during the tour.
Mrs. Wiernik, the tour guide, pointed out products, most of them designed for quick cooking, that she considers extremely wasteful. There was Aunt Jemima's Pancake Express, a clear plastic bottle filled halfway with pancake mix. With water and a shake, the yield is six pancakes, an eighth of the amount in a regular-size box.
The Quaker Oats Company, which makes Pancake Express, has been criticized by environmental groups for Pancake Express, and even won the Wastemaker Award from Representative Frank Pallone Jr., Democrat of New Jersey.
But Ronald G. Bottrell, a Quaker Oats spokesman, said the company intended to keep Pancake Express on the market because of competition from similar convenience products. "There are a lot of things package designers have to consider," he said. "Right now the hot button is the environment and many people are basing their purchasing decisions on that. But it may be that we can't satisfy everyone."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Shoppers at Gristede's in New Castle, N.Y., being led on an "environmental shopping" tour by Roberta Wiernik, who pointed out recyclable products. (Joyce Dopkeen/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Neediest Cases Fund Assures New Life and Home for Child

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ

 BODY:
This year, before Anna Valentine died of AIDS, Rosa Cruz swore that she would take care of her friend's 10-year-old daughter. By the end of September, only a few days after the funeral, it appeared that the promise might be hard to keep.
The child, Desiree, had already moved into the one-room Bronx apartment where Ms. Cruz lived with her son Jesse and two cats, but city social workers threatened to take her away. 
"They were telling me I had to get a bigger place if I wanted to keep Desiree," Ms. Cruz said in an interview Wednesday night.
She knew of a two-bedroom apartment, but her welfare check had left her with no savings for a security deposit or the increased rent. Through her church, she was referred to the Catholic Charities of the Archdiocese of New York. They gave her $615, a caseworker helped her obtain additional housing benefits, and she moved into the apartment in October.
 
79th Annual Appeal
 The emergency relief was provided by The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund, which this week began its 79th annual appeal for contributions to help the sick and the poor of the New York metropolitan region. The Times pays for the campaign's overhead and gives all the money to seven charities that are to use it specifically for direct services for the needy.
The Neediest Cases money has provided shelter for homeless people, job training for the unemployed and assistance to families being torn apart by drugs and AIDS.
Ms. Cruz said she did not want to see Desiree placed with a stranger or be placed in the drug-infested housing project where an elderly and sick grandmother lives. "Anna was my girlfriend," Ms. Cruz said. "I figured that this was the least I could do for her."
The two women had hit it off from the moment they met in school eight years ago. Even though Ms. Cruz disapproved of the drugs her friend used, they had no problem finding things to talk about. They argued about The National Enquirer, compared beauty tips and traded stories about their children.
They even shared a boyfriend. When Ms. Valentine stopped seeing Desiree's father, Juan Rodriguez, Ms. Cruz went out with him, with no hard feelings.
In the last few years, as Ms. Valentine's AIDS worsened, Ms. Cruz grew worried about Desiree. She was not going to school, her hair was becoming tangled and knotted, she used "a lot of four-letter words," Ms. Cruz said.
On Wednesday night, Ms Cruz talked about the changes that have occurred.
In the last few weeks, Ms. Cruz said, Desiree has become "boy crazy." She has a crush on a neighbor. She swoons over the teen-age idols in the band New Kids on the Block. But she is still a child who loves to sing along with a tape of the music from "The Little Mermaid" and play with Barbie dolls.
Desiree is adjusting to her new life, Ms. Cruz said, and is doing better in school.
"A few weeks ago she called me 'Moms,' " Ms. Cruz said. "I asked her if she was sure that was what she wanted to call me. She said, 'Yes, you're my mom now.' "

HEADLINE: Electronic Bond Auction Founders

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
An effort to distribute a new corporate bond issue through an innovative electronic auction foundered earlier this week, when the U S West Financial Corporation chose to sell its bonds through Merrill Lynch & Company rather than Capitalink, a New York company that has been promoting the electronic system.
Capitalink, whose owners and promoters include J. P. Morgan Securities, has been trying for more than a year to find companies willing to break with tradition and sell their bonds directly and electronically to investors. 
Ordinarily, corporations sell their bonds to securities firms that act as underwriters, or middlemen.
 
 Was to Have Been New Issue
 A $100 million issue of three-year notes for U S West Financial earlier this week was the closest Capitalink has come to handling a new issue. U S West Financial asked Capitalink on Monday for an indication of what interest rate it would likely have to pay for the bonds.
Financiers involved in the deal said yesterday that Capitalink found potential buyers for the bonds, with a tentative interest rate of about 1.05 to 1.1 percentage points higher than three-year Treasury notes.
The rate was more than U S West wanted to pay, the financiers said, and 24 hours later it signed with Merrill Lynch to sell the bonds at a cost to the company of 1.04 percentage points above three-year Treasury notes.
 
 'No Firm Commitment'
 David A. Jeffrey, Capitalink's president and founder, said, "We had looked at the possibility of an auction, but there was no firm commitment to hold an auction, and no bids were ever collected."
Analysts said yesterday that when Capitalink asked investors for indications of the interest rate they would want on a new bond, the investors were likely to give a high rate, because they did not want to undercut their bargaining position.
Steven J. Rohrbach, the chief financial officer at U S West Financial, declined to comment yesterday on the financing. The company is a part of U S West, which was created in the breakup of the Bell System in 1984. Its companies serve the Rocky Mountain region and the Northwest.
 
 Others Finding It Tough
 Capitalink is not the only electronic system to encounter slow going. A new system for trading privately placed securities, called Portal and sponsored by the National Association of Securities Dealers, has also been poorly received. Analysts say issuers and investors in that market prefer to arrange their transactions privately, without being displayed for others to see.
So far, Capitalink has had more success in finding investors willing to sign up for its system than in finding corporations willing to use it. U S West Financial was one of 19 companies that have amended prospectuses filed with the Securities and Exchange Commission to allow for the possibility of selling bonds at an electronic auction, but none have done so.
The 140 investors who have signed up for Capitalink are investment advisers, pension funds and insurance companies.
 
 More Underwriting by Morgan
 Early last year, when J. P. Morgan Securities joined with Capitalink, officials at the Morgan unit saw the electronic auction idea as a way for a newcomer to break Wall Street firms' tight grip on the corporate bond underwriting business. The electronic would be best suited to good-quality corporations that are known to investors.
But since June 1989, when the Federal Reserve allowed the Morgan unit to underwrite corporate bonds, it has been very successful in joining the Wall Street firms, participating in about 100 new bond issues. Yesterday, for example, J. P. Morgan Securities joined Merrill Lynch and Goldman, Sachs & Company as one of only three firms handling a $200 million bond issue of the Consolidated Edison Company of New York.
Despite its success in joining other securities firms, an official at J. P. Morgan Securities who asked not to be identified said yesterday that the firm was not withdrawing its support of Capitalink. "We still think the idea is intellectually sound, and is a sensible alternative way of distributing some kinds of bonds," he said.

Companies
General Motors is sharply cutting back its production for the rest of the year because of the weakening American economy. Detroit's pessimistic sales outlook has been made worse by the Persian Gulf crisis and dwindling consumer confidence. [Page A1.]

North Point Press said it would stop publishing new titles after its spring 1991 list, but would continue its backlist. [D7.]
Matsushita said it would not become involved in decisions regarding the content of creative products at MCA. [D5.]
 
The Economy
Savings and loans institutions could convert to state-chartered savings banks under a proposal made by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation and supported by the industry. [D1.]
Consumer spending in October was unchanged at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $3.73 trillion, the Commerce Department said, and personal incomes rose just 0.1 percent, to a $4.71 trillion rate, far below the 0.6 percent inflation rate. [D1.]
President Bush reiterated his confidence that the economy would improve soon in a brief speech to a banking group. [D2.]
A fund to protect the global environment would be established within the World Bank under an agreement between industrial and developing countries in Paris this week. [D2.]
Twelve men from several countries were indicted on charges of taking part in an international conspiracy to distribute more than $550 million worth of stolen securities. [D8.]
Senator Alan Cranston conceded to the Senate Ethics Committee in a deposition that his involvement with Charles H. Keating Jr. was "politically stupid and unwise," but denied wrongdoing. [A23.]
 
Markets
The dollar surged more than 2 percent against the yen and more than 1 percent against the German mark. The rally is the first significant break upward for the dollar in months. [D1.]
Oil prices slipped in volatile trading, easing 37 cents a barrel, to $32.91, as traders sold hours before the U.N. Security Council authorized the use of military force to drive Iraq out of Kuwait. [D2.]

The Dow Jones average fell 16.34, to 2,518.81, because of rising interest rates and renewed war jitters. [D8.]
Treasury securities prices fell and yields rose on rumors of shooting in the Middle East, later denied. [D15.]
Corn and soybean futures prices surged on the Chicago Board of Trade on new signs of Soviet buying interest. [D16.]

Mutual fund investors are buying stock funds at a record pace, even though the year is shaping up as the worst one in a decade for the stock market. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]

HEADLINE: U.S. Sued Over Drug to Increase Milk Output

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
A public interest group sued the Department of Agriculture today, charging that it had helped begin a million-dollar public relations campaign for an experimental drug whose safety was still being investigated by the Food and Drug Administration.
The suit was filed in Federal court here by the Foundation on Economic Trends, which is waging a national campaign against the drug, a genetically engineered growth hormone intended to increase a cow's milk production. 
 The suit contends that the National Dairy Promotion and Research Board, which was set up by Congress to help increase the consumption of dairy products, had joined the drug's manufacturers and dairy trade associations in seeking to assure the public that the product was safe.
The drug, bovine somatotropin, also known as bovine growth hormone, is currently going through the Food and Drug Administration's approval process. Federal law prohibits the promotion of a new animal drug while it is still under investigation.
Yvonne Dock, a spokeswoman for the dairy board, had no immediate comment on the lawsuit. A spokesman for the Department of Agriculture, which was named as a defendant because it oversees the dairy board, said his office had not seen the lawsuit and could not comment on it.
 
Issue of Safety
 An Agriculture Department spokesman, William Grigg, denied that the review process had been tainted, saying that the safety of the product to humans had already been proved. He said the effects on the cattle and the environment were still being studied. "The substances are already present in the milk we drink anyway," he said. "The amount is in the same ball park."
The pharmaceutical companies that make the drug strenously objected to the charge that they were trying to influence the drug approval process. The four chemical manufacturers -- Upjohn Company, Monsanto, the American Cyanamid Company and Eli Lilly & Company -- have spent more than $500 million on research and development of bovine growth hormone.
The hormone is the first genetically engineered drug for use on farms to go through the review process.
Surveys show as many as 80 percent of American dairy farmers are opposed to the drug because of the public reaction to hormone-treated milk.
 
Petition Is Filed
 Jeremy Rifkin, who heads the Foundation on Economic Trends, also petitioned the F.D.A. today to stop any promotion of the product by agency officials and to end the practice of putting milk into the market from cows that have been experimentally injected with the hormone.
"This is a three-year pattern of collusion," he said. "The National Dairy Board sat in meetings with the trade associations and companies and conceived of strategies to promote the hormone."
In March, letters from the National Dairy Board to concerned farmers said the group was not promoting the drug, nor would it begin an educational campaign until after the drug was approved, because to do so would violate Federal law. But documents obtained by Mr. Rifkin's foundation through a Freedom of Information Act request show that in February the board awarded a $1 million contract to a Washington public relations firm to promote the product. Mr. Rifkin made the documents available to The New York Times.

HEADLINE: Once Again Bush Says Economy Will Improve

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
After meeting with his economic advisers, President Bush reiterated his confidence today that the nation's economy would improve soon.
In a brief speech to the Association of Bank Holding Companies, meeting here this week, the President said: "We are in a period that concerns me of a sluggish economy. Some are saying 'recession,' and some are saying 'slowdown' and some are going 'downturn.' But one positive thing is that most if not all people are suggesting that whatever it is, it won't be long lasting." 
 This is the same basic position Mr. Bush has taken in speeches, news conferences and interviews for several weeks. He has not said what if anything he intended to do to try to counteract the situation.
Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, told Congress on Wednesday that the economy was actually shrinking. He was the first top Government official to take that position. Mr. Bush referred in passing to Mr. Greenspan's testimony, calling it "rather eloquent."
 
 Effect of the Gulf Crisis
 In his off-the-cuff remarks to the bankers, Mr. Bush broke little new ground. He made these other points:
*The Administration will offer legislation next year to overhaul "outmoded" banking regulations and allow banks to enter areas of business from which they are now barred.
*He is sympathetic to the view of some bankers that "excessive zeal" of regulators was at least partly responsible for what he called a "credit crunch." Without specifying what he had in mind, he promised to try to "relieve a little pressure" on the bankers from the regulators next year.
*While the crisis in the Persian Gulf was having "a very bad effect on our economy," the effects on the economies of Eastern Europe and third world countries had been "devastating."
 
 Impact of Regulations
 The only proposal that seems certain is one to rewrite laws enacted during the Depression that limit the capacity of banks to operate across state lines and prevent them from expanding into areas like underwriting securities, selling insurance and operating mutual funds.
"The regulatory system that served us very well, indeed, from its inception in the 30's is today, in my view, increasingly outmoded, as likely to prevent banks from staying competitive as it is to allow them to serve customers and sustain confidence in the system," the President said in his speech today.
 
 Trouble With Conservatives
 Mr. Bush met this morning with Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady and the budget director, Richard G. Darman, along with other advisers, to discuss economic and domestic policy for next year. The White House would not say precisely what was discussed.
Before his State of the Union address and budget presentation early next year, Mr. Bush will have to resolve an apparent disagreement between Mr. Darman and some lower-level advisers with links to the conservative wing of the Republican Party.
The conservatives, who deplore the budget agreement Mr. Bush struck with Congress, have been advocating another push for a capital gains tax cut and other tax breaks that they feel would stimulate the economy. Under the new budget law, any tax cuts would have to be offset by tax increases elsewhere or by spending reductions.
Mr. Darman, who was one of the main architects of the budget deal, seems to prefer that attention be focused on how to make programs more effective within the agreed-on spending limits.
