HEADLINE: MIDEAST TENSIONS;
U.N. GIVES IRAQ UNTIL JAN. 15 TO RETREAT OR FACE FORCE; HUSSEIN SAYS HE WILL FIGHT

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Nov. 29

 BODY:
The United Nations Security Council voted 12 to 2 with 1 abstention today to authorize the United States and its allies to expel Iraq from Kuwait by force if President Saddam Hussein does not withdraw his forces by Jan. 15.
Security Council members plan to observe "a pause of good will" until that date by taking no further actions against Iraq and concentrating on diplomatic efforts to promote a peaceful settlement. 
 But the United States and several other Council members with forces in the region warned that this 47-day respite would end abruptly if Mr. Hussein harmed any of the thousands of foreign hostages he is holding in Iraq or Kuwait or made other provocative moves.
 
12 Support Resolution
 The resolution authorizing force was supported by 12 of the Council's 15 members, including the United States, Britain, France and the Soviet Union, which are permanent members and have a veto over decisions.
China, the fifth permanent member, abstained rather than exercise its veto. The country's Foreign Minister, Qian Qichen, condemned Iraqi aggression but maintained that the Council should avoid "hasty actions" that could lead to a war with "serious consequences" for the whole world. [Excerpts from the speeches, page A10.]
Eight of the Council's 10 rotating members supported the resolution. They are Canada, Finland, Romania, Ethiopia, the Ivory Coast, Zaire, Colombia and Malaysia. Yemen, the Council's only Arab member, joined Cuba in voting against the resolution.
 
A First for China
 It was the first time China had failed to vote for a resolution against Iraq since Mr. Hussein's forces captured Kuwait on Aug. 2. The Security Council has adopted 12 resolutions against Iraq since the Persian Gulf crisis began, including measures demanding its unconditional withdrawal from Kuwait and imposing a strict economic embargo. Among the rotating members, only Yemen and Cuba have ever voted against or abstained on any of these resolutions.
The vote marked only the second time -- the Korean war was the other instance -- that the Security Council has invoked its power to sanction the use of force to counter aggression. To underscore the importance of the vote, all the Council members were represented by their Foreign Ministers, with the exception of Yemen and the Ivory Coast.
The resolution does not explicitly authorize the use of military force against Iraq, but it empowers members "to use all necessary means" to uphold previous Security Council resolutions demanding that Iraq withdraw from Kuwait.
Iraq's United Nations representative, Abdul Amir al-Anbari, called the resolution "an act of aggression" against Iraq that reflected "America's imperialistic ambitions, which have become glaringly evident, especially in our Arab region."
"If war is imposed on us, our people will not kneel," he said.
Bush Administration officials had lobbied for weeks to gain support for the resolution, and Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d acted as chairman of the meeting. In an opening statement to a packed Security Council chamber, he reminded the members of the League of Nations' failure to combat aggression in the 1930's.
"History now has given us another chance. With the cold war behind us, we now have the chance to build a world which was envisioned by this organization, by the founders of the United Nations," Mr. Baker said, adding, "We must not let the United Nations go the way of the League of Nations."
 
'In Unmistakable Terms'
 "If Iraq does not reverse its course peacefully, then other necessary measures, including the use of force, should be authorized," he said. "We must put the choice to Saddam Hussein in unmistakable terms."
In his closing address, Mr. Baker said Mr. Hussein represented a threat to the stability of the whole Middle East because of "the vast arms he possesses, the weapons of mass destruction he seeks."
At a news conference after the vote, Mr. Baker was asked whether he had promised Soviet leaders to get their approval before using any force after Jan. 15. Mr. Baker strongly hinted that he had, saying Washington had made it clear to all its important coalition partners that it wanted "to keep them fully informed and to consult with them" at every stage.
 
Soviets See Turning Point
 The Soviet Union also cast the resolution as a turning point in international affairs that could lead to a more credible role for the United Nations in preserving international peace and resolving conflicts.
Saying the world is "entering a time of political maturity," the Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze, warned that those "who break the peace must know all available means will be used against them."
He told reporters later that Moscow was not planning to send any armed forces to the gulf despite the resolution approved today. "I'm not sure such a step would get the support of the Supreme Soviet and the Congress of People's Deputies, and without that we couldn't do it," Mr. Shevardnadze said, referring to the highest Soviet legislative entities.
But he warned that President Mikhail S. Gorbachev had the power to send forces into action without permission from these assemblies if the lives of Soviet citizens are in danger.
"If even one Soviet citizen is harmed I cannot say what the consequences might be, but they would be very, very serious," he said.
The United States, Britain, France and the Soviet Union all emphasized that the Council's members contemplated no further action against Iraq before Jan. 15. But they indicated that they would abandon such restraint if Iraq harmed any of its hostages, and they said its leaders would be held personally responsible.
They also pointedly reminded Iraq of its obligation to refrain from using chemical or biological weapons under the Geneva Protocol of 1925, which also allows nations attacked by such weapons to reply in kind.
 
Peaceful Intent Stressed
 Most of the Foreign Ministers emphasized in their statements that the resolution authorizing force was intended to promote peaceful settlement by convincing Mr. Hussein that he must give up Kuwait unconditionally.
Britain's newly reappointed Foreign Secretary, Douglas Hurd, called it "a strong bid for peace," saying the Iraqis "may have persuaded themselves the military option was a bluff." If the measure succeeds in freeing Kuwait, he said, it would represent "a very remarkable exercise in collective security."
Many Council members have said the United Nations cannot credibly punish Iraq for its aggression against Kuwait while turning a blind eye to Israel's continued occupation of the Arab lands it seized after the 1967 war.
While saying there was no formal link between the gulf crisis and the Palestinian question, Mr. Shevardnadze said that the freeing of Kuwait should lead to "a comprehensive settlement" in the rest of the Middle East and that the five permanent Council members have "a very special role to play."
 
Cuban Accuses U.S.
 Only the Cuban Foreign Minister, Isidoro Malmierca Peoli, accused the United States of deliberately shielding Israel from Security Council pressure.
Malaysia's Foreign Minister, Abu Hassan bin Haji Omar, said he would not allow "the Palestinian problem to be shunted aside."
The resolution approved today "demands" that Iraq withdraw unconditionally from Kuwait but decides to allow it "one final opportunity" to do so "as a pause of good will."
But if Iraq does not pull out by Jan. 15, the measure authorizes the countries with forces in the gulf to "use all necessary means to uphold and implement" the previous Security Council resolutions demanding such unconditional withdrawal.
While it does not speak of military force as such, it says the Council is acting under Article 7 of the Charter, which allows the United Nations to seek to reverse aggression by military action if economic boycotts and other more peaceful means do not succeed.

HEADLINE: Car-Racing Dreams Fulfilled

BYLINE: By JOSEPH SIANO

 BODY:
ON an island where the possession of a driver's license can sometimes carry a social stigma, it might come as a surprise that there is a place where you can race around in circles as fast and as long as you want and never have to worry about a summons or a cocktail-party slight.
The secret of such guilt-free motoring is to do it with a vehicle that's one-twenty-fourth the size of the real thing (or about two-thirds the size of your shoe) and to do it at a slot-car racing center. 
 Manhattan Raceway, which opened in June on Broadway at 19th Street, has four tracks, varying in length from 40 to 155 feet, on which to race the tiny electric cars. If you vaguely remember something similar from your childhood, that's because slot-car racing -- along with respectable billiard parlors -- is a bit of 1960's cultural flotsam that has washed ashore once more.
For a generation in the midst of its prime child-raising years, a rebirth of slot-car racing satisfies more than a craving for nostalgia. It's a new way to entertain children who have become bored with the two-dimensional world of video games. (And for a reporter who has followed auto racing for 25 years, it's a chance to experience his sport vicariously.)
As the name implies, the cars race around on eight-lane tracks, with a plastic rudder at the front of each car holding it in its respective slot -- up to a point. It's the ability to find out exactly how fast you can go around a turn without sending the car flying off the track that makes slot-car racing a challenge. Or a frustration, depending on how often you have to leave your seat, walk over to right your car, and sit down again.
"The only drawback is, they should have somebody putting the cars back on the tracks," said Norman Livingston, a 30-year-old Manhattan resident who was making his second visit to the raceway recently.
As they say with a shrug at the Indianapolis Motor Speedway, that's racin'.
But it wasn't discouraging Mr. Livingston, whose idea of success was much larger than one-twenty-fourth scale. "You never know," he said, "I could be the next Tom Cruise."
His sister, Joannah, may be one of his competitors when his days of thunder arrive. "I took a career test, and they said I should be a race-car driver," she said. For the time being, she works for the New York City Board of Education.
Both of them said they had owned slot cars as children, during the hobby's birth in the late 1960's.
"A lot of people who come here say the same thing," said David Da Silva, a 32-year-old real-estate entrepreneur in Manhattan who, with Pat Gray, a New Jersey car salesman, is co-owner of Manhattan Raceway. "It brings back memories."
"Some people even have 25-year-old cars," he said.
On a recent Saturday night, Jeff Siedenberg, Jody Schwarz and David Strumpf sat at a table amid the whine of tiny electric motors, waiting for some track time. (On weekend nights, the wait can be as long as 45 minutes.) "We just had some time to kill between things," said Mr. Siedenberg, a 40-year-old economic professor. "It was something I couldn't afford as a kid, and I can now."
"This is the only chance I get to have a car," added Mr. Strumpf, a 36-year-old computer specialist. "You can afford to park these."
"This is better than an after-dinner drink," said J. P. Shields of Manhasset, L.I., who was there with Denise Guarino of Port Washington.
Indeed, an unscientific sampling of raceway customers showed that many were there in the limbo between dinner and a movie (there is a multi-screen theater across Broadway).
What they find is a diversion somewhere between video games and driving a radio-controlled model car.
Unlike the latter, slot cars don't require you to steer, which makes them a lot less frustrating for young children and klutzy adults. The only thing you control is the car's speed, by pulling a trigger on a pistol-grip controller that plugs into a console at each track.
After about a half-hour of yelling "Blue, blue," or "That's in the green" to tell any unfortunate spectator which of the color-coded lanes your car belongs in, your trigger finger should be sensitive enough to keep the car on the track long enough to actually have a race with the person next to you, which is when you discover the advantage over video games.
"The difference between doing this and playing Nintendo is this is more interactive," Mr. Da Silva said. "It's more social."
 
Cappuccino, Too
 Although Manhattan Raceway isn't the only slot-car racing center in the metropolitan area (there are centers in Westchester County and Long Island), it is the only one in New York City, and the atmosphere is more Monaco than dragstrip. Television monitors show videotapes of auto races, and the walls are decorated with racing posters and prints. It also brings a singularly cosmopolitan slant to the hobby: if you like, you can sip a cappuccino or espresso as you race.
If your original slot cars went to Mom's Auto Wreckers years ago, you can still race. Rental of a car and control is $1 for 15 minutes, $2 for a half-hour and $3 for an hour. No damage waivers are necessary.
Added to that fee is track time, at $2.50 to $3 for 15 minutes, or $7 to $9 an hour, depending on the track.
The raceway's longest and most challenging track, the King, is available only to customers with their own cars. Only one other track poses more than the most rudimentary challenge, so anyone who goes there more than twice (with or without children) is going to want to own a car.
Manhattan Raceway sells stripped-down models from $19.50 to $27.50, with a controller costing $22.50. A call to a few hobby shops might find a better deal. At the Ferrari end of the scale, a hand-built racer costs $225.
As they say in the pits at Daytona, if you want to make a small fortune in auto racing, start with a big one.
Manhattan Raceway, 893 Broadway, is open from noon to 2 A.M. Monday through Saturday, and noon to midnight Sunday. Telephone: 473-5095.
 
Slot-Car Addresses
 If you are serious about slot-car racing, or live in Westchester County and want to try it, Elmsford Raceway at 380 North Saw Mill River Road in Elmsford, N.Y., is, like stock-car racing's Southern 500, the granddaddy of them all.
Opened in 1966 by Luis Del Rosario Jr., the three-track operation has survived the ebbs and flows of the hobby's popularity and is still run by Mr. Del Rosario and his wife, Barbara.
"This has a definite parallel to the way people react to real cars as a sport and a hobby," Mr. Del Rosario said in tracing the cycles in his business.
Now he is starting to see some of his original customers again. "They're bringing their kids and getting back into it themselves," he said. "Which makes me feel old, of course."
Elmsford's prime attraction is the 220-foot-long Nuvolari track, named after Tazio Nuvolari, the famous Italian driver of the 1930's who was known as the Flying Mantuan. The track, which was the site of slot-car racing's international Olympiad in 1984, includes a steeply banked turn and tight switchback corner.
Even the simplest track, a figure-eight, is long enough to offer a little challenge. A half-hour of track time, including a rental car and controller, is $4 or $5. If you have your own car, an hour of track time is $7. The phone number is (914) 592-5375, and the hours are noon to 11 P.M. Monday to Friday, 11 A.M. to 11 P.M. Saturday, and noon to 9 P.M. Sunday.
On Long Island, there are two tracks run by Islip Miniature Speedway. One is at 310 Main Street in Holbrook (516-471-4986) and the other at 3209 Horse Block Road in Medford (516-696-6967). The hours are noon to 10 P.M. Monday through Saturday, and noon to 9 P.M. Sunday. With a rental, track time costs $3 for 15 minutes, $5.50 for a half-hour and $9.75 for an hour. The longest of the four tracks at Medford is 155 feet and the longer of the two Holbrook tracks is 130 feet.
A more detailed list of tracks can be found in each issue of Scale Auto Racing magazine, which has offices at 2634 Robert Road, Aransas Pass, Tex. 78336.

HEADLINE: Guatemalan Military Makes Elections a Hoax

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your news article on Guatemala's election (Nov. 11) is another example of uncritical devotion to the United States Government interpretation of events in Guatemala. The election is called "a significant step for the United States-backed effort to end a tradition of military dictatorship and build a working democratic system."
Then you report that the only voices allowed to be heard without fear of military retribution are those of the right and far right. I have just been to Guatemala and have witnessed the hollowness of the political debate, where the most significant issues of land reform and the military's fascist control of society are never mentioned. It requires a willing suspension of disbelief to declare the process democratic. You accept the United States Government's premise that holding an election, even if it is a life-threatening proposition for anyone left of the military to participate in the process, is proof of democratic reform and a vindication of United States policy.
The United States continues to finance the army despite its participation in suppressing and killing. The most consistent theme one hears in speaking to average Guatemalans is resignation about politics. You attribute this to impatience with the parliamentary process. But no matter who wins the election, until the army is drastically reformed and reduced, electoral politics will be a cruel game perpetrated on the people of Guatemala to assuage the consciences of those who supply arms and money to the army. As long as advocates of land reform and economic reform risk their lives just to speak out, you can't call what is happening in Guatemala a victory for democracy.
To rationalize the army's control of real power in the country as "the only bureaucracy that works" is a cynical approach to Guatemala's political landscape. The same efficiency is employed in the ruthless suppression of the people.
 
STUART GOLD
Providence, R.I., Nov. 12, 1990
 
The writer is a contributing editor to Multinational Monitor magazine.

HEADLINE: Job Applicant's Lot Isn't Happy Either

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Editors Wanted; English Helpful," Ken Jacobson's commentary on the quality of editorial job applicants, based on responses to an advertisement for his publication, Metals Week (Op-Ed, Nov. 14), is unfair.
Lapses in grammar and spelling are, of course, unacceptable, but Mr. Jacobson should consider what the more competent applicant is up against.
Too many classified advertisements provide an inadequate description of the position offered, leaving the applicant to guess the employer's needs. Even then, promoting one's own virtues does not always come easily, especially if the individual is young. Small successes are invariably magnified and, very often, enthusiasm is the would-be employee's single greatest asset.
Most young job applicants understand perfectly well that their cover letter and resume should reflect their professionalism. This is why many cover letters unintentionally end up sounding pompous. Finding a job is one of the most frustrating things in life, and a cover letter may be as much an expression of desperation as an indication of potential.
In the future, it may be easier for applicants simply to tell the truth: "Look, I need a job, and your $21,000-a-year position seems just about as good as the next."
 
DAVID HILLARY
Georgetown, Conn., Nov. 15, 1990

HEADLINE: Guatemalan Military Makes Elections a Hoax;
Voice of the People

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Even if we accepted your anti democratic philosophy -- surprisingly similar to your arguments for Gov. Jim Florio's antidemocratic New Jersey tax plan -- that Guatemalans are incapable of making the right decisions at the polls, your logic in "Democracy Fails in Guatemala" (editorial, Nov. 14) is incorrect.
You agree that the outgoing Christian Democratic President, Vinicio Cerezo Arevalo, failed miserably to:
(1) control the corrupt and murderous army, and
(2) straighten out an unfair, mercantilist economic system.
Not only do I agree with you, but the Guatemalan voters agree, too. The Christian Democratic candidate (as corrupt and ill suited for the job as Mr. Cerezo) lost badly. The candidates who came in first and second, and face a January runoff, vowed:
(1) to clean up the army and get it under civilian control through the force of their leadership, and
(2) to remove statist privileges in the Guatemalan economy that have favored only the well-connected oligarchs because of its highly regulated structure.
Perhaps their economic programs were not as socialistic as you -- judging by your absurd recommendations for economic growth -- would like. But the Guatemalan people know far better than editorialists sitting in their clean offices in New York what is wrong with the Guatemalan economy. And they voted for the men they think will do the best job in cleaning it up.
 
FELIPE SANCHEZ
New York, Nov. 14, 1990

HEADLINE: Washington at Work;
An Architect of the Reagan Vision Plunges Into Inquiry on Race and I.Q.

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
One thing Charles Murray knows how to do is generate controversy. In 1984, his book "Losing Ground" argued that social programs did more harm than good and proposed, as "a thought experiment," that the country simply abolish them.
A visitor to White House offices recalls a Reagan aide brandishing the book aloft as a sort of Exhibit A when advocating program cuts. The President himself cited it in attacks on the welfare system. Liberal academics combed through census tapes seeking to discredit his thesis. 
 That may not be Mr. Murray's last uproar. Now at the American Enterprise Institute, a public policy research center here, he sits at an important crossroads of ideas and politics. Collaborating with Richard Herrnstein, a Harvard psychologist who has in the past been in the center of disputes about heredity and intelligence, he is asking one of the most explosive questions a social scientist can pose: whether there are differences in intelligence between blacks and whites that help explain differences in their economic and social standing.
 
Departure From Institute
 "There is no way to exaggerate the sensitivity of the issues we're involved in," said Mr. Murray, who argues that the issues are too complex, and his views still too subject to change, for him to publicly discuss them.
Not only is the pressure on Mr. Murray in the personal sense. His decision to wade into the subject has put him in the middle of think-tank politics. Though Mr. Murray was by far the best-known scholar at the Manhattan Institute in New York City, the conservative policy research group was reluctant to follow him into this thicket, and he left, after an eight-year association, taking with him an annual foundation grant of about $100,000 for salary, overhead and other expenses.
Other influential research institutes had reservations as well. One question is whether the enterprise institute is risking a damaging controversy, or instead adding a prominent scholar who will help it be as central to intellectual activity in the 1990's as it was in the early days of the Reagan era.
Christopher DeMuth, who worked on deregulation in the Reagan White House and is now president of the enterprise institute, says he decided to recruit Mr. Murray on the basis of his past work, without studying the details of his current project.
"I think he's a very bright social scientist who does very good work," he said.
 
Faith in Murray's Work
 As for the work on race and intelligence, "people have written responsibly and irresponsibly in the area," said Mr. DeMuth, adding that he had faith that Mr. Murray's product would fit the former description.
But many of Mr. Murray's colleagues, in Washington and elsewhere, describe themselves as torn between their regard for his intellectual integrity and their concern over where such research has led in the past. Some also worry that his concentration now on I.Q. tests is leading him away from the nonracial precepts of "Losing Ground."
"So Charles, are you a racist?" asked an editor who has published Mr. Murray's work, only half-jokingly, when the two of them met for lunch recently.
"I've started to keep a journal, because people do say that a lot," Mr. Murray said.
Defending the work in an interview, Mr. Murray said the discussion of racial differences would play only a small role in a book that would take a much broader look at the way individual characteristics, like intelligence, patience and diligence, affect success and failure.
Asked if he had concluded that differences in intelligence helped to explain the relative standings of black people and white people in America, he said: "Read the book. There is absolutely no simple answer I can give you."
William Hammett, president of the Manhattan Institute, said he thought Mr. Murray had little to gain in terms of useful knowledge as a result of his inquiry and much to lose in terms of adverse publicity.
Asked to articulate his objection, Mr. Hammett had difficulty choosing his words, saying he feared headlines like "White Scholar Suggests Genetic Inferiority," but assuring that Mr. Murray "would never fall into that."
"When you say this stuff it just sounds bad," he said. "I have to rethink this damn thing."
 
Better to Work Elsewhere
 While Mr. Murray could have stayed at the institute, he said, they both agreed it would be better if he pursued such work elsewhere.
After leaving New York, Mr. Murray also discussed the project with the Brookings Institution, which has a reputation as being more liberal than the American Enterprise Institute. "We also thought that the project was an interesting one on which no one could pass judgment until it's finished," said Bruce MacLaury, the Brookings president.
Since Mr. Murray already had a grant from the Bradley Foundation, a large Milwaukee-based foundation, Brookings could offer him an office withoutfacing the question of whether to place him on its staff, Mr. MacLaury said. The racial aspects of the research would have made the decision to offer him a staff position more difficult, he said.
Defying convention seems a habit with Mr. Murray, who said it has been the way since at least his undergraduate years. On the rare occasions when circumstances forced him to wear a necktie, he said, he took pains to make sure the narrow end was longer. "If I got it wrong, I would retie it," he said, in a tone that wobbled between pride and self-reproach.
Neckties aside, Mr. Murray, who is 47 years old, enjoyed a conventional upbringing in Newton, Iowa, where his father worked as an executive for the Maytag Corporation and his mother raised a family.
"On both sides of the family, they were really an American story of working hard and being rewarded for it," he said, describing the one kind of social program he believes in.
When Mr. Murray graduated from Harvard University in 1965 with a bachelor's degree, he said, he did not yet consider himself a conservative. He views his years in the Peace Corps, which he said he had joined more as an adventurer than a humanitarian, as the beginning of a long education in the hubris of social engineering.
 
A Pattern of Failure
 He spent two years in Thailand, telling villagers how to build toilets, but concluded that he had contributed nothing. After returning to the United States he embarked on a career as an analyst with the American Institutes for Research, a private company that works for foundations and governments in evaluating a broad range of social programs. But the more dealings he had with inner-city programs to reduce teen-age pregnancy, crime or other social ills, the more he saw a pattern of failure.
With "Losing Ground," his disillusionment became the conservatives' manifesto.
While there was nothing new in the argument that Government policy had destroyed the incentives for the poor to avoid crime and find work, the elegance and detail that Mr. Murray summoned on its behalf was. Many officials in the Reagan Administration had said the same thing, but "Losing Ground," which was published by Basic Books, gave the viewpoint its intellectual architecture.
From the start of his frontal assault on the welfare state, Mr. Murray has understood that potentially volatile racial questions lurk close to the surface. He noted in "Losing Ground" that talk of welfare dependency was often accompanied by "a good deal of mean-spirited (often racist) invective," and he took pains to insist that his was a race neutral argument.
"This book is not about blacks and whites," he says when asked about the current work. It is mostly about the tensions between America's egalitarian philosophy and the unequal way in which talents are distributed, he said.
Researchers have consistently found that groups of American blacks score on average about 15 points lower in I.Q. tests than groups of whites. But there is much disagreement about what that means. Some social scientists have argued that the gap is caused by differences in socioeconomic status and other environmental factors. Others have assailed the tests as relying on a narrow conception of intelligence.
Many argue that so-called scientific claims about racial differences in intelligence have turned out to be wrong. They say that such research has a discredited history of justifying violence and oppression.
"There is such a discrepancy in what's known by the specialists and assumed by the nonspecialists," Mr. Murray said.
Mr. Murray's collaborator in the new research, Professor Herrnstein, attracted national attention in 1971 when he predicted that as society became more meritocratic, individuals with low I.Q.'s would congregate on the bottom of the economic scale, intermarry and produce offspring with low I.Q.'s. Protesters accusing him of biological determinism -- unfairly, he argues -- disrupted his classes and tried to get him dismissed from the university.
He has largely been silent on issues of race. But this year, in The Public Interest, a conservative journal, he attacked a report by the National Academy of Sciences on the state of black America. He said the report had overemphasized racial discrimination in explaining the status of blacks and had ignored "intractable race differences" in I.Q. test results. Modern science did not know what caused the difference, he said -- inheritance or environment -- or how to cure it.
Among those unpersuaded of the value of Mr. Murray's new project is his friend and fishing partner, Ed Crane, president of the libertarian Cato Institute. Mr. Crane said that he once hoped to lure Mr. Murray to his research organization, and that he had talked to him briefly about the current project, but did not offer him a position.
"It's not an area that I wish to get involved in," said Mr. Crane, who was otherwise full of praise for Mr. Murray's work.
Asked whether he shared Mr. Murray's view that the subject had unfortunately become taboo, he said, "I think that sometimes taboos serve a legitimate social function."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Read the book," said Charles Murray of his examination of intelligence and race. "There is absolutely no simple answer I can give you" (Jose R. Lopez/The New York Times)

SECTION: Section B; Page 6; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 621 words

HEADLINE: Henry M. Caudill, Who Described Appalachia's Poverty, Dies at 68

BYLINE: By GLENN FOWLER

 BODY:
Harry M. Caudill, a Kentucky lawyer, professor and author whose eloquent description of life in poverty-ridden Appalachia inspired Federal relief efforts beginning in the 1960's, died of a self-inflicted gunshot wound yesterday at the Appalachian Regional Hospital in Whitesburg, Ky. He was 68 years old.
Mr. Caudill, who had been suffering from Parkinson's disease, shot himself in the head with a .38-caliber handgun at his home in the Cumberland Mountains near Whitesburg, said Pat Gish, an owner of The Mountain Eagle, a weekly newspaper to which Mr. Caudill frequently contributed. 
 Mrs. Gish said that in accordance with Mr. Caudill's wishes in a living will, no extraordinary life-saving measures were taken before his death.
In 1963 Mr. Caudill's first book, "Night Comes to the Cumberlands," focused nationwide attention on the plight of Appalachia's residents. President John F. Kennedy appointed a commission to investigate conditions in the region and his successor, Lyndon B. Johnson, made Appalachia a keystone of his War on Poverty.
In the succeeding quarter-century, more than $15 billion in aid was spent in the impoverished mountain region covering parts of 13 states. But Mr. Caudill and others who continued to study the region concluded in 1986 that the forecast was still bleak and that only marginal improvement had been made in the lives of millions of rural residents of Appalachia.
 
Maverick Legislator
 Mr. Caudill, who was born in the Whitesburg area, was a descendant of the earliest settlers who arrived a century and a half ago to scratch out a living on farms and in coal mines. He went to Whitesburg High School and, after serving in the Army in World War II and being wounded in combat in Italy, graduated from the University of Kentucky with a law degree in 1948.
He practiced law for nearly three decades and served three terms in the Kentucky House of Representatives, where he was a maverick legislator who fought, largely unsuccessfully, to end the state's many political fiefdoms at the county level.
A strenuous orator who liked to quote in a sonorous mountain drawl from the Bible, Shakespeare, Tennyson and Dickens, he was principally concerned with improving public education, conserving land and resources and reforming the judiciary. He co-sponsored a 1954 law that set minimum support levels for public schools and imposed a sales tax to pay the cost.
In the late 1960's Mr. Caudill became chairman of the Congress for Appalachian Development, a coalition seeking to strengthen self-government in the region. He lobbied extensively in Washington and lectured around the country to draw attention to Appalachia's problems and what he saw as its promise.
 
Compiled Own Text
 In 1977 he gave up practicing the law to become Professor of Appalachian Studies at the University of Kentucky. Having no suitable textbook on the region, he compiled his own. With his wife, Anne, as his secretary, he wrote articles that were published in The Atlantic Monthly, Reader's Digest, The Nation and Audubon Magazine, among other journals.
He wrote nine books after "Night Comes to the Cumberlands." Most of his writing dealt with problems like the ravages of surface mining, unemployment caused by automation, the inadequacies of welfare programs and the need for development of electric power to rebuild Kentucky's industrial base.
He formally retired from his chair at the university in 1985 but continued to write and lecture on issues involving Appalachia.
In addition to his wife, the former Anne Frye, he is survived by two sons, James of Louisville and Harry F. Caudill of Lexington, Ky.; a daughter, Diana Grace of New Albany, Ind., and two grandchildren.

CORRECTION-DATE: December 1, 1990, Friday, Late Edition - Final

 CORRECTION:
An obituary headline in late editions yesterday about an author who described life in Appalachia rendered his name incorrectly. He was Harry M. Caudill, not Henry. The obituary is reprinted today on page 31.
